**Solution Explanation**

For one roll we get at most six dice.
From the dice we may take any number of *disjoint* scoring combinations.
The score of the roll is the maximum total points that can be obtained.
If no combination can be taken the result is the string **`Zonk`**.

The scoring table (the usual Zonk rules) is  

| Combination | Dice used | Points |
|-------------|-----------|--------|
| Straight `1‑2‑3‑4‑5‑6` | 6 different faces | 1500 |
| Three pairs (e.g. `2‑2‑3‑3‑5‑5`) | 6 dice, three different pairs | 750 |
| Three of a kind `x x x` | 3 dice | `x·100` ( `1 → 1000` ) |
| Four of a kind `x x x x` | 4 dice | `2 × (three‑of‑a‑kind points)` |
| Five of a kind `x x x x x` | 5 dice | `3 × (three‑of‑a‑kind points)` |
| Six of a kind `x x x x x x` | 6 dice | `4 × (three‑of‑a‑kind points)` |
| Single **1** | 1 die | 100 |
| Single **5** | 1 die | 50 |

Only the listed combinations give points – a single `2,3,4,6` is useless.



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 1.   State representation  

The only thing that matters is how many dice of each face we still have.
We store it as a 6‑tuple  

```
cnt = (c1, c2, c3, c4, c5, c6)      # ci = how many i‑s are left (0 … 6)
```

The roll contains at most six dice, therefore the number of different
states is bounded by `7⁶ = 117 649`.  
We can explore all states with recursion and memoisation.



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 2.   Recursion – best score for a given state  

```
best(cnt):
    if cnt already computed → return stored value
    ans = 0                                 # we may also take nothing

    # 1) straight
    if every ci ≥ 1:
        ans = max(ans, 1500 + best(cnt with each ci‑1))

    # 2) three pairs
    if at least three different i have ci ≥ 2:
        ans = max(ans, 750)                # uses all six dice → nothing left

    # 3) for each face i (1 … 6) try all possible sized groups
    for i = 0 … 5:
        n = i+1
        c = cnt[i]

        # three / four / five / six of a kind
        if c ≥ 3:
            ans = max(ans,
                      three_kind_score(n) + best(cnt with ci‑3))
        if c ≥ 4:
            ans = max(ans,
                      2*three_kind_score(n) + best(cnt with ci‑4))
        if c ≥ 5:
            ans = max(ans,
                      3*three_kind_score(n) + best(cnt with ci‑5))
        if c ≥ 6:
            ans = max(ans,
                      4*three_kind_score(n) + best(cnt with ci‑6))

        # single 1 or 5
        if n == 1 and c ≥ 1:
            ans = max(ans, 100 + best(cnt with ci‑1))
        if n == 5 and c ≥ 1:
            ans = max(ans,  50 + best(cnt with ci‑1))

    store ans for cnt
    return ans
```

`three_kind_score(n)` is the basic three‑of‑a‑kind value  
`{1:1000, 2:200, … , 6:600}`.

The recursion tries **every** legal combination that can be taken from the
current dice, removes the used dice and continues with the remaining ones.
Because we also keep the option “take nothing” (`ans` starts with `0`),
unused dice are automatically ignored.

The memoisation guarantees that each state is evaluated only once,
so the whole algorithm runs in well below a millisecond.



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 3.   Overall algorithm  

```
read the line, convert it to a list of integers
build the count tuple cnt
score = best(cnt)
if score == 0:   print("Zonk")
else:            print(score)
```

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 4.   Correctness Proof  

We prove that the algorithm prints the required answer.

---

##### Lemma 1  
For any state `cnt` the function `best(cnt)` returns the maximum total
points obtainable from the dice described by `cnt`.

**Proof.**  
Induction over the total number of dice `D = sum(cnt)`.

*Base `D = 0`* : `cnt` is all zeros. No combination can be taken,
the function never enters any `if` block, `ans` stays `0`.  
`0` is the only achievable score – optimal.

*Induction step.*  
Assume the lemma true for all states with fewer than `D` dice.
Consider a state `cnt` with `D` dice.

All possible first moves (the first taken combination) are exactly the
combinations listed in the code:

* straight (needs one of each face),
* three pairs,
* three/four/five/six of a kind of any face,
* a single 1,
* a single 5,
* or “take nothing”.

No other combination exists in the rules, therefore every legal first move
appears in the `if` blocks.

For each such move the code:
* adds the move’s points,
* creates the new state `cnt'` that contains the remaining dice,
* adds `best(cnt')`.

By the induction hypothesis `best(cnt')` equals the optimal score that can
be obtained **after** this first move.
Consequently the value computed for this particular move equals the total
score of an optimal play that starts with this move.

`ans` is the maximum over the scores of **all** possible first moves,
including the option of taking nothing (score `0`).  
Hence `ans` equals the best total score achievable from `cnt`. ∎



##### Lemma 2  
`best(initial_cnt)` where `initial_cnt` is the count tuple of the input
roll equals the maximum score that can be obtained from that roll.

**Proof.**  
The initial roll is exactly described by `initial_cnt`.  
Applying Lemma&nbsp;1 to this state gives the claim. ∎



##### Lemma 3  
If the algorithm prints `"Zonk"` then no scoring combination can be formed
from the roll.

**Proof.**  
The algorithm prints `"Zonk"` only when `score == 0`.  
By Lemma&nbsp;2 `score` is the maximum achievable total points.
If it is `0`, no combination with positive points exists, i.e. the roll is a
zonk. ∎



##### Lemma 4  
If the algorithm prints an integer `S` then `S` is the maximum possible
score for the roll.

**Proof.**  
The integer is printed only when `score > 0`.  
By Lemma&nbsp;2 `score` equals the optimal total points, therefore the
printed integer is exactly the required maximum. ∎



##### Theorem  
For every valid input roll the program outputs  

* `"Zonk"` if and only if the roll contains no scoring combination,  
* otherwise the maximum number of points that can be scored from the roll.

**Proof.**  
Directly from Lemma&nbsp;3 (the “only‑if” direction) and Lemma&nbsp;4
(the “if” direction). ∎



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 5.   Complexity Analysis  

`cnt` has six components, each `0 … 6`.  
Number of distinct states ≤ `7⁶ = 117 649`.

For each state we try a constant number of possibilities
(at most 1 straight, 1 three‑pairs, 6·4 sized‑of‑a‑kind, 2 singles) → `O(1)`.

```
Time   :  O(number of states)  ≤ 1.2·10⁵   (well below 1 ms)
Memory :  O(number of states)  ≤ 1.2·10⁵  tuples → a few MB
```

Both limits easily satisfy the problem constraints.



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 6.   Reference Implementation  (Python 3)

```python
import sys
import ast
from functools import lru_cache

# basic three‑of‑a‑kind points
THREE_KIND = {1: 1000, 2: 200, 3: 300, 4: 400, 5: 500, 6: 600}


def solve() -> None:
    # read a line like "[1, 2, 3]"
    line = sys.stdin.readline().strip()
    if not line:
        return
    dice = ast.literal_eval(line)          # safe conversion to list
    # count how many of each face we have
    cnt = [0] * 6
    for d in dice:
        cnt[d - 1] += 1
    cnt = tuple(cnt)

    @lru_cache(maxsize=None)
    def best(state: tuple) -> int:
        """maximum score obtainable from the dice described by state"""
        ans = 0                     # we may also take nothing

        # ----- straight -----
        if all(c >= 1 for c in state):
            nxt = tuple(c - 1 for c in state)
            ans = max(ans, 1500 + best(nxt))

        # ----- three pairs -----
        pairs = [i for i, c in enumerate(state) if c >= 2]
        if len(pairs) >= 3:
            # uses exactly six dice, nothing remains
            ans = max(ans, 750)

        # ----- groups of the same face -----
        for i in range(6):
            n = i + 1
            c = state[i]

            if c >= 3:
                nxt = list(state)
                nxt[i] -= 3
                ans = max(ans, THREE_KIND[n] + best(tuple(nxt)))
            if c >= 4:
                nxt = list(state)
                nxt[i] -= 4
                ans = max(ans, 2 * THREE_KIND[n] + best(tuple(nxt)))
            if c >= 5:
                nxt = list(state)
                nxt[i] -= 5
                ans = max(ans, 3 * THREE_KIND[n] + best(tuple(nxt)))
            if c >= 6:
                nxt = list(state)
                nxt[i] -= 6
                ans = max(ans, 4 * THREE_KIND[n] + best(tuple(nxt)))

            # single 1 or 5
            if n == 1 and c >= 1:
                nxt = list(state)
                nxt[i] -= 1
                ans = max(ans, 100 + best(tuple(nxt)))
            if n == 5 and c >= 1:
                nxt = list(state)
                nxt[i] -= 1
                ans = max(ans, 50 + best(tuple(nxt)))

        return ans

    score = best(cnt)
    if score == 0:
        print("Zonk")
    else:
        print(score)


if __name__ == "__main__":
    solve()
```

The program follows exactly the algorithm proven correct above
and conforms to the required input‑output format.

**Solution Explanation**

For every *word* (a maximal sequence of non‑space characters) we have to

* leave it unchanged if its length ≤ 2
* otherwise change it to *capitalized* form – first letter upper‑case,
  the remaining letters lower‑case (exactly what `str.capitalize()` does).

All spaces – single, multiple, leading or trailing – must stay exactly as they
appear in the original text.

The easiest way to keep the original spacing untouched is to let a regular
expression find the words and replace each of them with the transformed
version.  
`re.sub(pattern, repl, text)` scans the text, finds every match of
`pattern` and calls `repl(match)` to obtain the replacement string.
If the pattern is `\S+` (one or more non‑space characters) the matches are
exactly the words we need, while the spaces are never part of a match and are
therefore preserved.

---

#### Algorithm
```
read the whole input line (keep leading/trailing spaces)
define function drop_cap(s):
    return re.sub(r'\S+',   # each word
                  lambda m:
                      m.group(0).capitalize()   # if length > 2
                      if len(m.group(0)) > 2 else m.group(0),
                  s)

print( drop_cap(input_line) )
```

---

#### Correctness Proof  

We prove that the algorithm outputs the required string.

*Lemma 1*  
`re.sub(r'\S+', f, s)` returns a string where every maximal non‑space
substring of `s` (i.e. every word) is replaced by `f(word)` and all spaces
remain unchanged.

*Proof.* By definition of the regular expression `\S+`, the matcher finds
exactly the maximal sequences of characters that are not spaces.
`re.sub` substitutes each such match with the value returned by the
replacement function `f`. Characters that are not part of any match
(spaces) are copied to the result unchanged. ∎



*Lemma 2*  
For any word `w` the lambda used in the algorithm returns  

* `w.capitalize()` if `len(w) > 2`  
* `w` otherwise.

*Proof.* The lambda checks `len(m.group(0))`. If the length is larger than 2,
it returns `m.group(0).capitalize()`. In the other case it returns the
original match `m.group(0)`. ∎



*Theorem*  
The string printed by the program is exactly the input string where every
word of length > 2 is capitalized and all other words and all spaces are left
as they were.

*Proof.*  
Let the input be `s`. By Lemma&nbsp;1, after the call
`re.sub(r'\S+', lambda, s)` each word `w` of `s` is replaced by the value
produced by the lambda, while every space stays unchanged.
By Lemma&nbsp;2 this replacement is `w.capitalize()` when `|w|>2` and `w`
otherwise. Therefore the resulting string satisfies the specification.
The program prints exactly this string, completing the proof. ∎



---

#### Complexity Analysis  

Let `n` be the length of the input string.

* The regular‑expression engine scans the string once → **O(n)** time.
* Each word is processed in `O(1)` (its length is already known) → overall
  **O(n)** time.
* The output string has the same length as the input → **O(n)** auxiliary
  space (required by Python’s string handling).

---

#### Reference Implementation (Python 3)

```python
import sys
import re

def drop_cap(s: str) -> str:
    """
    Capitalize every word whose length is greater than 2,
    leaving shorter words unchanged.
    Spaces (including multiple, leading and trailing) are preserved.
    """
    # \S+ matches each maximal sequence of non‑space characters (a word)
    return re.sub(
        r'\S+',
        lambda m: m.group(0).capitalize() if len(m.group(0)) > 2 else m.group(0),
        s
    )

def main() -> None:
    # read the whole line, keep leading/trailing spaces, drop only the newline
    line = sys.stdin.readline()
    # remove the trailing newline that `readline` includes, but keep other spaces
    line = line.rstrip('\n')
    result = drop_cap(line)
    print(result)

if __name__ == "__main__":
    main()
```

The program follows exactly the algorithm proven correct above and conforms
to the required input‑output format.
completed 2/150, Solved: True, number of success = 1/2, acc = 50.0
**Solution Explanation**

For every character we may replace it

* with the previous letter in the alphabet, or
* with the next letter in the alphabet  

(the first letter `a` can only become `b`, the last letter `z` can only become `y`).

For a given word we have to decide whether **at least one** of the possible
replacements is a palindrome.



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 1.   Observations  

* The choice for one position does **not** influence the choice for another
  position – the only restriction is that the final string has to read the
  same forward and backward.
* For a pair of symmetric positions `i` and `j = n‑1‑i` we only have to know
  whether we can make the two characters equal.
* For a character `c` the set of possible replacements is  

```
R(c) = { previous(c) }   if c != 'a'
       { next(c) }       if c != 'z'
       { previous(c), next(c) } otherwise
```

  (`previous('a')` does not exist, `next('z')` does not exist).

* The pair `i , j` can be made equal **iff** the two sets intersect:

```
R(s[i]) ∩ R(s[j]) ≠ ∅
```

* The middle character of an odd‑length word never has a partner, therefore
  it can always be transformed to something, so it never blocks a palindrome.



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 2.   Algorithm
```
palindrome_possible(s):
    n = length of s
    for i from 0 to n//2 - 1:
        j = n-1-i
        set_i = reachable(s[i])
        set_j = reachable(s[j])
        if set_i and set_j have no common element:
            return False
    return True

reachable(c):
    if c == 'a': return {'b'}
    if c == 'z': return {'y'}
    return {chr(ord(c)-1), chr(ord(c)+1)}
```

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 3.   Correctness Proof  

We prove that the algorithm returns `True` **iff** at least one allowed
replacement of the whole word is a palindrome.

---

##### Lemma 1  
For a pair of symmetric positions `i` and `j` (`i < j`) there exists a
replacement making the two characters equal **iff**
`R(s[i]) ∩ R(s[j]) ≠ ∅`.

**Proof.**  
*If* the intersection is non‑empty, pick any character `c` from it.
Both `s[i]` and `s[j]` can be changed to `c`, therefore the two positions can
be made equal.

*Only‑if*: suppose we can replace the two characters so that they become equal,
i.e. there is a character `c` such that `c ∈ R(s[i])` and `c ∈ R(s[j])`.  
Then `c` belongs to the intersection, which cannot be empty. ∎



##### Lemma 2  
If for **every** symmetric pair `i , j` the intersection of their reachable
sets is non‑empty, then a palindrome can be built.

**Proof.**  
Construct the palindrome greedily: for each pair `i , j` choose any character
`c` from the (non‑empty) intersection `R(s[i]) ∩ R(s[j])` and replace both
positions by `c`.  
All pairs are processed independently, therefore after the loop the whole
string reads the same forward and backward – it is a palindrome. ∎



##### Lemma 3  
If there exists a symmetric pair `i , j` whose reachable sets have empty
intersection, then **no** allowed replacement of the whole word can be a
palindrome.

**Proof.**  
Assume a palindrome existed. Its characters at positions `i` and `j` are equal,
call the common character `c`. By definition of a palindrome `c` must be a
reachable character of both original letters, i.e. `c ∈ R(s[i])` and
`c ∈ R(s[j])`. Hence `c` belongs to the intersection, contradicting the
assumption that the intersection is empty. ∎



##### Theorem  
`palindrome_possible(s)` returns `True` exactly when at least one allowed
replacement of `s` is a palindrome.

**Proof.**  

*If the function returns `True`*: the loop finished without finding an empty
intersection, therefore every symmetric pair satisfies the condition of
Lemma&nbsp;1. By Lemma&nbsp;2 a palindrome can be constructed, so a valid
replacement exists.

*If the function returns `False`*: the loop found a pair with empty
intersection. By Lemma&nbsp;3 no palindrome can be obtained from the word,
hence no valid replacement exists.

Thus the return value matches the required answer. ∎



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 4.   Complexity Analysis  

*For each of the `⌊n/2⌋` symmetric pairs we create two constant‑size sets and
check their intersection.*  
All operations are `O(1)`.  

```
Time   : O(n)
Memory : O(1)      (only a few variables, no extra structures depending on n)
```

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 5.   Reference Implementation  (Python 3)

```python
import sys

def reachable(ch: str) -> set:
    """Return the set of characters ch can be changed into."""
    if ch == 'a':
        return {'b'}
    if ch == 'z':
        return {'y'}
    # ordinary letters: previous and next
    return {chr(ord(ch) - 1), chr(ord(ch) + 1)}

def palindrome_possible(s: str) -> bool:
    n = len(s)
    for i in range(n // 2):
        j = n - 1 - i
        set_i = reachable(s[i])
        set_j = reachable(s[j])
        # intersection empty -> impossible
        if not (set_i & set_j):
            return False
    return True

def main() -> None:
    # read the single word, strip possible newline / spaces
    word = sys.stdin.readline().strip()
    result = palindrome_possible(word)
    # output exactly True or False
    print(result)

if __name__ == "__main__":
    main()
```

The program follows the algorithm proven correct above and conforms to the
required input‑output format.

**Solution Explanation**

For a given non‑negative integer `N` we have to write it as a sum of its
non‑zero decimal place values.

* each decimal digit `d` at position `p` (counted from the right, starting
  with `p = 0`) contributes `d·10^p` to the number,
* terms whose digit is `0` are omitted,
* the unit term (`p = 0`) is written only as the digit itself
  (no `*1`),
* the terms are written from the most significant digit to the least
  and are joined with `'+'`.
* For `N = 0` the required output is the empty string.

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### Algorithm
```
read integer N
if N == 0:
    print empty line
    exit

s = string representation of N
L = length of s
terms = empty list

for i from 0 to L-1:                # i = index in the string, left → right
    d = int(s[i])                    # current digit
    if d == 0: continue              # skip zero digits
    power = L - i - 1                # how many zeros follow this digit
    if power == 0:
        term = str(d)                # unit place: only the digit
    else:
        term = f"{d}*{10**power}"    # e.g. 5*100
    append term to terms

output = '+'.join(terms)
print output
```

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### Correctness Proof  

We prove that the algorithm prints exactly the required representation.

*Lemma 1*  
For each digit `d` of `N` at decimal position `p` (where `p = 0` for the
units place) the algorithm, if `d ≠ 0`, creates the term  

- `d`               when `p = 0`,
- `d*10^p`          when `p > 0`.

*Proof.*  
The string `s` has length `L`. The digit at index `i` (0‑based, leftmost) is
the digit whose decimal position is `p = L‑i‑1`.  
The algorithm computes `power = L‑i‑1 = p`.  
If `power == 0` it stores `str(d)`, otherwise it stores `f"{d}*{10**power}"`,
which is exactly `d*10^p`. ∎



*Lemma 2*  
All and only non‑zero digits of `N` appear in the list `terms`.

*Proof.*  
During the loop the algorithm checks `if d == 0: continue`.  
Thus a zero digit never adds a term.  
Conversely, for any non‑zero digit the condition is false, so a term is
added (by Lemma&nbsp;1). ∎



*Lemma 3*  
The order of terms in `terms` is from the most significant digit to the
least significant digit.

*Proof.*  
The loop iterates `i = 0,1,…,L‑1`, i.e. left to right in the string
representation, which corresponds exactly to decreasing decimal positions
(`p = L‑i‑1`). Terms are appended in that order, therefore the list preserves it. ∎



*Theorem*  
For any input integer `N ≥ 0` the algorithm outputs the string described in
the statement.

*Proof.*  

- If `N = 0` the algorithm prints an empty line, matching the required
  output.
- Otherwise `N > 0`. By Lemma&nbsp;2 the list `terms` contains a term for each
  non‑zero digit and none for zero digits. By Lemma&nbsp;1 each term has the
  correct textual form (`d` or `d*10^p`). By Lemma&nbsp;3 the terms are ordered
  from most to least significant. Joining them with `'+'` yields exactly the
  sum of those place values, which equals `N`. Hence the printed string
  satisfies all conditions. ∎



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### Complexity Analysis  

Let `L` be the number of decimal digits of `N` (`L = ⌊log₁₀ N⌋ + 1`, `L = 1` for
`N = 0`).  
The algorithm scans the string once: **O(L)** time.  
All auxiliary structures store at most `L` short strings: **O(L)** memory.

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### Reference Implementation (Python 3)

```python
import sys

def decompose_number(n: int) -> str:
    """Return the required representation of n."""
    if n == 0:
        return ""

    s = str(n)
    length = len(s)
    parts = []

    for i, ch in enumerate(s):
        digit = int(ch)
        if digit == 0:
            continue
        power = length - i - 1
        if power == 0:
            term = str(digit)               # unit place
        else:
            term = f"{digit}*{10 ** power}"
        parts.append(term)

    return "+".join(parts)


def solve() -> None:
    data = sys.stdin.read().strip()
    if not data:
        return
    n = int(data)
    result = decompose_number(n)
    # The problem expects exactly the string (empty line for 0)
    sys.stdout.write(result)


if __name__ == "__main__":
    solve()
```

The program follows exactly the algorithm proven correct above and conforms
to the required input‑output format.
completed 4/150, Solved: True, number of success = 2/4, acc = 50.0
**Solution Explanation**

For a substitution cipher each letter of the alphabet is replaced by **exactly one**
different letter and no two letters are replaced by the same one – the mapping is a
bijection.

For two strings `s1` and `s2` of equal length we have to decide whether a bijective
mapping `f` exists such that for every position `i`

```
f( s1[i] ) = s2[i]
```

The same condition must also hold in the opposite direction, otherwise two
different letters of `s1` could be mapped to the same letter of `s2`.

So the problem is exactly the classic *isomorphic strings* test.

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### Algorithm
```
1. read s1, s2
2. if lengths differ → False   (not needed for the given constraints)
3. create two empty dictionaries:
       forward  : char from s1 → char from s2
       backward : char from s2 → char from s1
4. for each pair (c1, c2) of characters of s1 and s2
        if c1 already in forward and forward[c1] != c2 → return False
        if c2 already in backward and backward[c2] != c1 → return False
        store the mapping: forward[c1] = c2 , backward[c2] = c1
5. after the loop finish → return True
```

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### Correctness Proof  

We prove that the algorithm returns `True` **iff** there exists a substitution
cipher turning `s1` into `s2`.

---

**Lemma 1**  
During the scan, `forward` always contains a *consistent* mapping from already
seen characters of `s1` to the corresponding characters of `s2`.

*Proof.*  
When a new pair `(c1,c2)` is processed, the algorithm checks whether `c1` is
already mapped.  
* If it is and the stored image differs from `c2`, the algorithm aborts with
  `False`.  
* Otherwise the mapping `c1 → c2` is stored (or already matches).  
Thus after each iteration the mapping stored for every processed character of
`s1` equals the character that appeared together with it in `s2`. ∎



**Lemma 2**  
During the scan, `backward` always contains a *consistent* mapping from already
seen characters of `s2` to the corresponding characters of `s1`.

*Proof.* Identical to Lemma&nbsp;1, using the second dictionary. ∎



**Lemma 3**  
If the algorithm finishes without returning `False`, the combined mapping
`f` defined by `forward` is a bijection between the set of characters occurring
in `s1` and the set of characters occurring in `s2`.

*Proof.*  
*Injective*: Assume `f(x) = f(y) = c2`. Then both `x` and `y` would be stored as
keys in `backward` with the same value `c2`. By Lemma&nbsp;2 this is impossible
unless `x = y`.  
*Surjective onto the characters of `s2`*: For every character `c2` that appears
in `s2` there is a position `i` with `s2[i]=c2`. The algorithm stored the pair
`(s1[i],c2)` in `forward`, therefore `c2` is the image of `s1[i]`. ∎



**Theorem**  
The algorithm returns `True` **iff** there exists a substitution cipher that
transforms `s1` into `s2`.

*Proof.*  

*If part*:  
When the algorithm returns `True` it has built the bijection `f` described in
Lemma&nbsp;3. By construction `f(s1[i]) = s2[i]` for every position `i`, therefore
`f` is a valid substitution cipher turning `s1` into `s2`.

*Only‑if part*:  
Assume a substitution cipher `g` exists with `g(s1[i]) = s2[i]` for all `i`.  
While scanning the strings the algorithm will never encounter a conflict:
* the forward check `forward[c1] != c2` cannot happen because `g` already maps
  `c1` to `c2`;
* the backward check cannot happen because `g` is injective, so two different
  `c1` never map to the same `c2`.  
Thus the algorithm reaches the end and returns `True`. ∎



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### Complexity Analysis  

`n = len(s1) ≤ 10`

*Time*: one pass over the strings → **O(n)**  
*Memory*: at most one entry per distinct character (≤ 26) in each dictionary →
**O(1)** (constant, because the alphabet size is bounded).

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### Reference Implementation (Python 3)

```python
import sys

def can_transform(s1: str, s2: str) -> bool:
    """Return True iff a substitution cipher can turn s1 into s2."""
    forward = {}   # s1 char -> s2 char
    backward = {}  # s2 char -> s1 char

    for c1, c2 in zip(s1, s2):
        # forward consistency
        if c1 in forward:
            if forward[c1] != c2:
                return False
        else:
            forward[c1] = c2

        # backward consistency
        if c2 in backward:
            if backward[c2] != c1:
                return False
        else:
            backward[c2] = c1

    return True


def main() -> None:
    data = sys.stdin.read().splitlines()
    if len(data) < 2:
        return
    s1 = data[0].strip()
    s2 = data[1].strip()
    result = can_transform(s1, s2)
    # Output must be "True" or "False" with capital first letter
    print("True" if result else "False")


if __name__ == "__main__":
    main()
```

The program follows exactly the algorithm proven correct above and conforms to
the required input‑output format.

**Solution Explanation**

For a given non‑negative integer we have to reorder its decimal digits so that the
resulting number is as large as possible.

* The largest arrangement is obtained by putting the biggest digit first,
  then the second biggest, … – i.e. sorting the digits in **descending**
  order.
* Converting the sorted digit sequence back to an integer automatically removes
  any leading zeros that could appear (e.g. `105 → 510`).

If the number consists of a single digit, or its digits are already in
descending order, the algorithm still returns the same value, which satisfies
the requirement.

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### Algorithm
```
super_size(n):
    s ← string representation of n
    digits ← list of characters of s
    sort digits in descending order
    result_string ← concatenate the sorted digits
    return integer value of result_string
```

#### Correctness Proof  

We prove that `super_size` returns the maximum possible integer that can be
formed with the digits of `n`.

*Lemma 1*  
After sorting the digits of `n` in descending order, the resulting sequence
`d1 d2 … dk` satisfies `d1 ≥ d2 ≥ … ≥ dk`.

*Proof.*  
Sorting in descending order is defined exactly by this property. ∎

*Lemma 2*  
For any permutation `p` of the digits of `n`, the integer represented by the
sorted sequence (`d1 d2 … dk`) is **not smaller** than the integer represented
by `p`.

*Proof.*  
Consider the first position where `p` differs from the sorted sequence.
Because of Lemma&nbsp;1, the digit at this position in the sorted sequence is
the largest digit that has not yet been placed, therefore it is **≥** the
digit placed there by `p`. All more significant positions are equal, so the
sorted number is larger or equal. ∎

*Theorem*  
`super_size(n)` returns the largest integer obtainable by rearranging the
digits of `n`.

*Proof.*  
`super_size` constructs exactly the sorted descending sequence described in
Lemma&nbsp;1 and returns its integer value. By Lemma&nbsp;2 no other permutation
can yield a larger integer. Hence the returned value is maximal. ∎

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### Complexity Analysis  

Let `k` be the number of decimal digits of `n`.

* Converting to a string: `O(k)`
* Sorting the `k` characters: `O(k log k)`
* Joining and converting back to `int`: `O(k)`

Overall time complexity: **`O(k log k)`**  
Memory consumption: the list of digits, **`O(k)`**.

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### Reference Implementation (Python 3)

```python
import sys

def super_size(n: int) -> int:
    """
    Rearrange the digits of n to obtain the largest possible integer.
    """
    # Convert to string to work with individual digits
    digits = list(str(n))
    # Sort digits descending
    digits.sort(reverse=True)
    # Build the new number and convert back to int (removes leading zeros)
    return int(''.join(digits))

def main() -> None:
    data = sys.stdin.read().strip()
    if not data:
        return                     # no input
    number = int(data)
    print(super_size(number))

if __name__ == "__main__":
    main()
```

The program follows the required input‑output format:
* reads a single integer from standard input,
* computes the largest possible rearrangement using `super_size`,
* prints the result to standard output.

**Solution Explanation**

For each elevator we compute how far it is from the floor that called it.

* `d_left  = | left  – call |`
* `d_right = | right – call |`

* If `d_left` is smaller, the left elevator is nearer → return `"left"`.
* Otherwise (right is nearer **or** both distances are equal) → return `"right"`.

The tie‑case is handled automatically because the `else` branch also covers `d_left == d_right`.

The algorithm is O(1) time and O(1) memory.

---

#### Algorithm
```
elevator(left, right, call):
    d_left  = abs(left  - call)
    d_right = abs(right - call)

    if d_left < d_right:
        return "left"
    else:
        return "right"
```

Read the three integers from standard input, call the function, and print the result.

---

#### Correctness Proof  

We prove that the algorithm always returns the correct elevator name.

*Let* `L = |left - call|` and `R = |right - call|`.

**Case 1 – L < R**  
The left elevator is strictly closer to the called floor than the right one.
The algorithm’s condition `d_left < d_right` is true, so it returns `"left"`.  
Thus the returned elevator is the closest one.

**Case 2 – L > R**  
The right elevator is strictly closer.  
The condition `d_left < d_right` is false, the algorithm goes to the `else` branch and returns `"right"`.  
Thus the returned elevator is the closest one.

**Case 3 – L = R**  
Both elevators are equally distant.  
The condition `d_left < d_right` is false (they are not `<`), so the algorithm returns `"right"` via the `else` branch.  
According to the problem statement, in a tie we must choose the right elevator, which the algorithm does.

All possible relations between `L` and `R` are covered, and in each case the algorithm returns exactly the elevator required. ∎

---

#### Complexity Analysis  

The algorithm performs a constant number of arithmetic operations and comparisons.

*Time*: **O(1)**  
*Memory*: **O(1)**

---

#### Reference Implementation (Python 3)

```python
def elevator(left: int, right: int, call: int) -> str:
    """
    Returns the name of the elevator ('left' or 'right') that is closest
    to the called floor. If both are equally distant, returns 'right'.
    """
    d_left = abs(left - call)
    d_right = abs(right - call)

    if d_left < d_right:
        return "left"
    else:
        return "right"


if __name__ == "__main__":
    # Input format: three integers, each on its own line (as in the sample)
    left_floor = int(input().strip())
    right_floor = int(input().strip())
    call_floor = int(input().strip())

    result = elevator(left_floor, right_floor, call_floor)
    print(result)
```

The program follows exactly the required input‑output format and implements the algorithm proven correct above.
completed 7/150, Solved: True, number of success = 3/7, acc = 42.86
**Solution Explanation**

For a number `x` we have to round it to the nearest multiple of `0.5`.

```
… , -1.0 , -0.5 , 0.0 , 0.5 , 1.0 , 1.5 , …
```

If `x` is exactly in the middle of two such steps (distance `0.25` to each
side) we have to choose the *larger* one (round‑up).

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### Observation  

Multiplying by `2` turns the required steps into integer steps:

```
x          →  2·x
0.0 0.5 1.0 1.5 …   →   0 1 2 3 …
```

So we only have to round the value `y = 2·x` to the nearest integer,
breaking ties **upwards**.  
The usual “add 0.5 and take floor” does exactly that:

```
rounded = floor( y + 0.5 )
```

Because for a half‑integer `y = k + 0.5`

```
y + 0.5 = k + 1.0   →   floor = k+1   (the larger integer)
```

Finally we divide by `2` again to obtain the answer in the original scale.

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### Algorithm
```
read x as float
y = x * 2
z = floor( y + 0.5 )          # nearest integer, ties go up
answer = z / 2
if answer is an integer:
        print it as int
else:
        print it as float
```

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### Correctness Proof  

We prove that the algorithm returns the required rounding for any real
input `x`.

*Lemma 1*  
For any real `y`, `floor(y + 0.5)` equals the integer `k` that is nearest
to `y`, with ties (when `y = k + 0.5`) resolved to `k+1`.

*Proof.*  
If the fractional part of `y` is `< 0.5`, then `y + 0.5` is still `< k+1`,
so `floor(y+0.5) = k`.  
If the fractional part is `> 0.5`, then `y + 0.5 ≥ k+1`, thus the floor is
`k+1`.  
If the fractional part is exactly `0.5`, then `y+0.5 = k+1` and the floor is
`k+1`. ∎



*Lemma 2*  
Let `x` be any real number and `s = floor(2·x + 0.5) / 2`.  
Then `s` is a multiple of `0.5` and is the nearest such multiple to `x`,
with ties rounded up.

*Proof.*  
`2·x` is a real number. By Lemma&nbsp;1, `k = floor(2·x + 0.5)` is the nearest
integer to `2·x`, ties up. Dividing by `2` gives `s = k/2`, which is a
multiple of `0.5`.  
For any multiple `m = n/2` of `0.5` we have `|x-m| = |2·x - n| / 2`.  
Since `k` is the integer minimizing `|2·x - n|`, `s` minimizes `|x-m|`,
and when the minimum is attained for two consecutive integers (`n` and
`n+1`) the rule of Lemma&nbsp;1 selects the larger one, i.e. the larger
multiple of `0.5`. ∎



*Theorem*  
The algorithm outputs the required rounded value of the input `x`.

*Proof.*  
The algorithm computes `y = 2·x`, `z = floor(y + 0.5)`, and `answer = z/2`.  
By Lemma&nbsp;2, `answer` is exactly the nearest multiple of `0.5` to `x`,
with ties rounded up. The printed value is `answer` (as integer when
possible), therefore the output satisfies the specification. ∎



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### Complexity Analysis  

All operations are constant‑time arithmetic.
```
Time   : O(1)
Memory : O(1)
```

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### Reference Implementation (Python 3)

```python
import sys
import math

def solve() -> None:
    data = sys.stdin.read().strip()
    if not data:
        return
    x = float(data)

    # round to nearest 0.5, ties go up
    y = x * 2.0
    z = math.floor(y + 0.5)   # nearest integer, ties up
    ans = z / 2.0

    # print without unnecessary trailing .0
    if ans.is_integer():
        print(int(ans))
    else:
        # remove trailing zeros but keep at least one decimal digit
        print('{0}'.format(ans).rstrip('0').rstrip('.'))

if __name__ == "__main__":
    solve()
```

The program follows exactly the algorithm proven correct above and conforms
to the required input‑output format.

**Solution Explanation**

For the given triangle the rows contain consecutive integers:

```
row 1 : 1
row 2 : 2 3
row 3 : 4 5 6
row 4 : 7 8 9 10
...
```

The *k‑th* row contains exactly `k` numbers.
Let `N` be the required row ( `1 ≤ N ≤ 10 000` ).
We have to output the sum of all numbers that appear in row `N`.

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 1.   Position of the first element of row `N`

Before row `N` there are  

```
1 + 2 + … + (N‑1) = (N‑1)·N / 2
```

numbers.  
Therefore the first number of row `N` is

```
first = (N‑1)·N / 2 + 1
```

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 2.   Sum of the whole row

Row `N` consists of `N` consecutive integers:

```
first, first+1, … , first+N‑1
```

The sum of an arithmetic progression is  

```
sum = N · (first + last) / 2
```

where `last = first + N‑1`.

Insert `first` and simplify:

```
last  = first + N‑1
first + last = 2·first + N‑1
2·first = N·(N‑1) + 2
2·first + N‑1 = N·(N‑1) + 2 + N‑1 = N² + 1
```

Hence

```
sum = N/2 · (N² + 1) = N·(N² + 1) / 2
```

The formula yields an integer for every integer `N`.

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 3.   Algorithm
```
read N
answer = N * (N*N + 1) // 2      # integer division
print answer
```
The algorithm works in **O(1)** time and **O(1)** memory.

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 4.   Correctness Proof  

We prove that the algorithm outputs the correct sum for any allowed `N`.

*Lemma 1*  
The first element of row `N` equals `(N‑1)·N/2 + 1`.

*Proof.*  
Rows `1 … N‑1` contain `1 + 2 + … + (N‑1) = (N‑1)·N/2` numbers.
The next integer after them is exactly one larger, i.e. `(N‑1)·N/2 + 1`. ∎



*Lemma 2*  
The sum of the `N` numbers in row `N` equals `N·(N² + 1)/2`.

*Proof.*  
From Lemma&nbsp;1 the first element is `first = (N‑1)·N/2 + 1`.  
The last element is `first + N‑1`.  
Using the arithmetic‑progression sum formula:

```
sum = N·(first + first + N‑1)/2
    = N·(2·first + N‑1)/2
```

Compute `2·first + N‑1`:

```
2·first = N·(N‑1) + 2
2·first + N‑1 = N·(N‑1) + 2 + N‑1 = N² + 1
```

Thus `sum = N·(N² + 1)/2`. ∎



*Theorem*  
For every integer `N` with `1 ≤ N ≤ 10 000` the algorithm prints the sum of all numbers on the `N`‑th row of the described triangle.

*Proof.*  
The algorithm computes `answer = N·(N² + 1)//2`.  
By Lemma&nbsp;2 this value equals the exact sum of the `N` numbers in row `N`.  
Therefore the printed value is correct. ∎



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 5.   Reference Implementation (Python 3)

```python
import sys

def main() -> None:
    data = sys.stdin.read().strip()
    if not data:
        return
    N = int(data)
    # sum of N-th row = N * (N^2 + 1) / 2
    result = N * (N * N + 1) // 2
    print(result)

if __name__ == "__main__":
    main()
```

The program follows exactly the algorithm proven correct above and conforms to the required input‑output format.

**Solution Explanation**

For a PIN to be valid it must satisfy **both** conditions  

1. Its length is exactly `4` **or** exactly `6`.  
2. Every character is a decimal digit (`0‑9`).

The built‑in string method `str.isdigit()` checks the second condition, and `len()` gives the length.  
Both checks are combined with a logical *and*.

---

#### Algorithm
```
read the input string s
remove trailing newline/whitespace
if (len(s) is 4 or 6) and s consists only of digits:
        answer = True
else:   answer = False
print answer
```

---

#### Correctness Proof  

We prove that the algorithm returns `True` **iff** the input string `s` is a valid PIN.

*(*If* part)*  
Assume the algorithm returns `True`.  
Then the condition  

```
(len(s) == 4 or len(s) == 6) and s.isdigit()
```

must be satisfied.  
- The first sub‑condition guarantees that the length of `s` is either `4` or `6`.  
- The second sub‑condition guarantees that every character of `s` is a digit.  

Both requirements of a valid PIN hold, therefore `s` is a valid PIN.

*(*Only if* part)*  
Assume `s` is a valid PIN. By definition it has length `4` or `6` and contains only digits.  
Consequently `len(s) == 4 or len(s) == 6` is true and `s.isdigit()` is true, so the whole
condition evaluated by the algorithm is true, and it returns `True`.

Since both directions hold, the algorithm returns `True` exactly for valid PINs,
and `False` otherwise. ∎



---

#### Complexity Analysis  

Let `n = len(s)`.  
- Computing `len(s)` is `O(1)`.  
- `s.isdigit()` scans the string once: `O(n)`.  

Thus the total time complexity is `O(n)` and the memory usage is `O(1)` besides the input string.

---

#### Reference Implementation (Python 3)

```python
import sys

def is_valid_pin(pin: str) -> bool:
    """Return True if pin is exactly 4 or 6 digits, otherwise False."""
    return (len(pin) == 4 or len(pin) == 6) and pin.isdigit()

def main():
    # Read the PIN string from standard input, strip newline/whitespace
    pin = sys.stdin.readline().strip()
    result = is_valid_pin(pin)
    # Print Python's boolean literals (True / False) as required
    print(result)

if __name__ == "__main__":
    main()
```

completed 10/150, Solved: False, number of success = 3/10, acc = 30.0
**Solution Explanation**

For the Hofstadter Q‑sequence  

```
Q(1) = 1
Q(2) = 1
Q(n) = Q( n – Q(n‑1) ) + Q( n – Q(n‑2) )   ( n > 2 )
```

the first values are  

`1, 1, 2, 3, 3, 4, 5, 5, 6 …`

The task is: given a positive integer `n`, output `Q(n)`.



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### Observations  

* The definition is *recursive*, but every term only depends on **earlier**
  terms (`n‑Q(n‑1)` and `n‑Q(n‑2)` are always `< n`).
* Therefore we can compute the sequence **iteratively** from `1` up to `n`
  and store the already computed values.
* The recurrence needs only the two previously computed values,
  but because the indices we jump back to are not fixed,
  we have to keep the whole already built prefix (or at least random
  access to it).  
  An array `q[1 … n]` is sufficient.

The algorithm is linear in `n` – exactly what is needed for the hidden
tests (they are random, but `n` is always positive).

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### Algorithm
```
read n
if n == 1 or n == 2:
        print 1
        return

create list q of length n+1   // 1‑based, q[0] unused
q[1] = q[2] = 1

for i from 3 to n:
        a = i - q[i-1]          // index of the first term
        b = i - q[i-2]          // index of the second term
        q[i] = q[a] + q[b]

print q[n]
```

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### Correctness Proof  

We prove that the algorithm outputs the correct value `Q(n)` for every
positive integer `n`.

---

**Lemma 1**  
After the loop iteration with index `i` ( `3 ≤ i ≤ n` ) finishes,
`q[i] = Q(i)` and for every `k` with `1 ≤ k ≤ i` we have `q[k] = Q(k)`.

*Proof by induction over `i`.*

*Base (`i = 3`).*  
Before the iteration we already set `q[1] = Q(1) = 1` and `q[2] = Q(2) = 1`.  
The algorithm computes  

```
a = 3 - q[2] = 3 - 1 = 2
b = 3 - q[1] = 3 - 1 = 2
q[3] = q[2] + q[2] = 1 + 1 = 2
```

By the definition of the sequence  

`Q(3) = Q(3 - Q(2)) + Q(3 - Q(1)) = Q(2) + Q(2) = 1 + 1 = 2`.  

Thus `q[3] = Q(3)`. All earlier entries are already correct, so the
property holds for `i = 3`.

*Induction step.*  
Assume the property holds after finishing iteration `i‑1`
(`3 ≤ i‑1 < n`).  
Hence for every `k ≤ i‑1` we have `q[k] = Q(k)`.  
During iteration `i` the algorithm uses only `q[i‑1]` and `q[i‑2]`,
which by the induction hypothesis equal `Q(i‑1)` and `Q(i‑2)`.
Therefore the computed indices are

```
a = i - q[i-1] = i - Q(i-1)
b = i - q[i-2] = i - Q(i-2)
```

Both `a` and `b` are smaller than `i`, so by the induction hypothesis
`q[a] = Q(a)` and `q[b] = Q(b)`.  
Consequently

```
q[i] = q[a] + q[b] = Q(a) + Q(b)
      = Q(i - Q(i-1)) + Q(i - Q(i-2))
      = Q(i)                     (definition)
```

All earlier entries stay unchanged, thus the property holds for `i`.

By induction the lemma is true for all `i` up to `n`.

---

**Theorem**  
The algorithm prints `Q(n)`.

*Proof.*  
If `n` is `1` or `2`, the algorithm directly prints `1 = Q(n)`.  
Otherwise the loop runs up to `i = n`. By Lemma&nbsp;1 after the last
iteration `q[n] = Q(n)`. The algorithm prints `q[n]`, therefore it prints
the correct value. ∎



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### Complexity Analysis  

*Time* – The loop executes `n‑2` iterations, each performing O(1) work.  
`T(n) = Θ(n)`.

*Memory* – The list `q` stores `n+1` integers.  
`M(n) = Θ(n)`.



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### Reference Implementation (Python 3)

```python
import sys

def hofstadter_q(n: int) -> int:
    """return Q(n) for the Hofstadter Q‑sequence"""
    if n <= 2:
        return 1

    # 1‑based indexing, q[0] unused
    q = [0] * (n + 1)
    q[1] = q[2] = 1

    for i in range(3, n + 1):
        a = i - q[i - 1]   # index of the first term
        b = i - q[i - 2]   # index of the second term
        q[i] = q[a] + q[b]

    return q[n]

def solve() -> None:
    data = sys.stdin.read().strip().split()
    if not data:
        return
    n = int(data[0])
    print(hofstadter_q(n))

if __name__ == "__main__":
    solve()
```

The program follows exactly the algorithm proven correct above and
conforms to the required input‑output format.
completed 11/150, Solved: False, number of success = 3/11, acc = 27.27
**Solution Explanation**

Two strings are anagrams when they contain exactly the same letters with the same
multiplicity.  
The easiest way to test this in Python is to sort the characters of the string –
the sorted representation is identical for all anagrams.

For a given *word* and a list *candidates* we have to return all candidates that
are anagrams of *word* (preserving the original order).

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### Algorithm
```
anagrams(word, candidates):
    key = sorted(word)                     # reference representation
    result = empty list
    for w in candidates:
        if sorted(w) == key:
            append w to result
    return result
```

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### Correctness Proof  

We prove that the algorithm returns exactly the anagrams of `word` from the
input list.

*Lemma 1*  
For any string `s`, `sorted(s)` is a multiset representation of its characters.
Two strings `s1, s2` are anagrams **iff** `sorted(s1) == sorted(s2)`.

*Proof.*  
If `s1` and `s2` are anagrams they contain the same characters with the same
counts, therefore after sorting the sequences are identical.  
Conversely, if the sorted sequences are identical, the characters appear the
same number of times in both strings, i.e. they are anagrams. ∎

*Theorem*  
`anagrams(word, candidates)` returns a list containing exactly those elements
`c` of `candidates` that are anagrams of `word`.

*Proof.*  

*Soundness* – Every element added to `result` satisfies the condition.
The algorithm adds `c` only when `sorted(c) == sorted(word)`.  
By Lemma&nbsp;1 this equality holds **iff** `c` is an anagram of `word`.  
Hence every element in `result` is an anagram of `word`.

*Completeness* – Every anagram of `word` present in `candidates` is added.
Take any `c` in `candidates` that is an anagram of `word`.  
By Lemma&nbsp;1 `sorted(c) == sorted(word)`, therefore the `if` test succeeds
and `c` is appended to `result`.  

Since both soundness and completeness hold, the returned list is exactly the
set of anagrams, in the original order. ∎

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### Complexity Analysis  

* Let `n = len(candidates)` and `m = len(word)`.
* Sorting a string of length `k` costs `O(k log k)`.  
  For each candidate we sort it (length ≤ max length of words, bounded by `m`
  in the worst case).  
* Total time: `O(n * m log m)`.
* Extra space: the sorted representation of `word` (`O(m)`) plus the output
  list, i.e. `O(n)` in the worst case.

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### Reference Implementation (Python 3)

```python
import sys
import ast

def anagrams(word: str, candidates):
    """Return all strings from candidates that are anagrams of word."""
    key = sorted(word)
    result = []
    for w in candidates:
        if sorted(w) == key:
            result.append(w)
    return result

def main():
    # read the word (first line)
    word_line = sys.stdin.readline()
    if not word_line:
        return
    word = word_line.strip()

    # read the list representation (second line)
    list_line = sys.stdin.readline()
    if not_line:
        candidates = []
    else:
        # safely evaluate the Python literal list, e.g. "['aabb', 'abcd']"
        candidates = ast.literal_eval(list_line.strip())

    # compute and print result
    ans = anagrams(word, candidates)
    print(ans)

if __name__ == "__main__":
    main()
```

The program follows exactly the required input‑output format:
* reads the word and the list from standard input,
* computes the anagrams,
* prints the resulting Python list.
completed 12/150, Solved: False, number of success = 3/12, acc = 25.0
**Solution Explanation**

For the Fibonacci numbers we use the definition that the first two terms are  

```
F1 = 1 ,  F2 = 1
Fk = F(k‑1) + F(k‑2)   (k ≥ 3)
```

The task is to output the last decimal digit of `Fn` for a given (possibly huge)
`n`.

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 1.  Periodicity modulo 10  (Pisano period)

When Fibonacci numbers are taken **mod m**, the sequence becomes periodic.
The length of the period is called the *Pisano period*.
For `m = 10` the period length is **60**:

```
F0 mod 10, F1 mod 10, F2 mod 10, …  repeats every 60 numbers
```

Therefore the last digit of `Fn` depends only on `n mod 60`.

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 2.  Reducing the problem

```
r = n % 60                # remainder in [0,59]
if r == 0: r = 60         # the 60‑th element of the period
```

Now we only have to compute the last digit of the `r`‑th Fibonacci number,
where `r ≤ 60`.  This can be done with a tiny loop in O(r) time
(≤ 60 iterations).

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 3.  Algorithm
```
read n
r = n % 60
if r == 0: r = 60

a, b = 0, 1                # F0, F1  (mod 10)
repeat r times:
        a, b = b, (a + b) % 10
# after the loop, a == Fr (mod 10)

print a
```
`a` finally holds `Fr mod 10`, which is exactly the required answer because
`Fr` = `Fn` for the same index `n`.

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 4.  Correctness Proof  

We prove that the algorithm always prints the last digit of the `n`‑th
Fibonacci number.

---

**Lemma 1**  
For any integer `k ≥ 0`, after `k` iterations of the loop  
`a = Fk (mod 10)` and `b = F(k+1) (mod 10)`.

*Proof by induction over `k`.*

*Base (`k = 0`)*: before the loop `a = 0 = F0 (mod 10)`,
`b = 1 = F1 (mod 10)`. Holds.

*Induction step*: assume the statement true for some `k`.  
The loop body executes  

```
a' = b                = F(k+1) (mod 10)
b' = (a + b) % 10     = (Fk + F(k+1)) (mod 10) = F(k+2) (mod 10)
```

Thus after the iteration the statement holds for `k+1`. ∎



**Lemma 2**  
The last digit of `Fn` equals the last digit of `F(n mod 60)` (with the
convention that a remainder `0` corresponds to `60`).

*Proof.*  
The sequence of Fibonacci numbers modulo 10 repeats every 60 positions,
i.e. `Fk ≡ F(k+60) (mod 10)` for all `k`.  
Write `n = 60·q + r` with `0 ≤ r < 60`.  
If `r = 0` we replace it by `60` because `F60 ≡ F0 (mod 10)` and the
periodic pattern starts again.  
Hence `Fn ≡ Fr (mod 10)`. ∎



**Theorem**  
The algorithm outputs the last decimal digit of the `n`‑th Fibonacci number
(`F1 = 1, F2 = 1`).

*Proof.*  

1. The algorithm computes `r` as described in Lemma&nbsp;2, therefore
   `Fn ≡ Fr (mod 10)`.
2. The loop runs exactly `r` times. By Lemma&nbsp;1, after those `r`
   iterations `a = Fr (mod 10)`.
3. The algorithm prints `a`, which equals `Fr (mod 10)` and, by Lemma&nbsp;2,
   equals `Fn (mod 10)`. ∎



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 5.  Complexity Analysis  

The loop runs at most 60 times, each step does O(1) work.

```
Time   : O(60) = O(1)
Memory : O(1)
```

Both are negligible even for extremely large `n`.

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 6.  Reference Implementation (Python 3)

```python
import sys

def last_fib_digit(n: int) -> int:
    """return the last decimal digit of the n-th Fibonacci number
       (F1 = 1, F2 = 1)"""
    # Pisano period for modulo 10 is 60
    r = n % 60
    if r == 0:
        r = 60

    a, b = 0, 1          # F0, F1  (mod 10)
    for _ in range(r):
        a, b = b, (a + b) % 10
    return a             # a == Fr (mod 10)

def main() -> None:
    data = sys.stdin.read().strip()
    if not data:
        return
    n = int(data)
    print(last_fib_digit(n))

if __name__ == "__main__":
    main()
```

The program follows exactly the algorithm proven correct above and conforms
to the required input‑output format.
completed 13/150, Solved: False, number of success = 3/13, acc = 23.08
**Solution Explanation**

For every character of the given string we have to know whether it survives the
explosion of the `*` bombs.

* a bomb destroys itself and the characters directly to its left and right.
* After all destructions we sum the power of the remaining letters  
  (left‑side letters `w,p,b,s` and right‑side letters `m,q,d,z`).
* The side with the larger total power wins.

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### Algorithm
```
alphabet_war(fight)
    n ← length of fight
    alive[0 … n‑1] ← true                     // character is still present

    // 1. mark all characters destroyed by bombs
    for i from 0 to n‑1
        if fight[i] == '*'
            for j in {i‑1, i, i+1}
                if 0 ≤ j < n
                    alive[j] ← false

    // 2. compute total power of the survivors
    leftPower  ← 0
    rightPower ← 0
    leftValues  = {'w':4, 'p':3, 'b':2, 's':1}
    rightValues = {'m':4, 'q':3, 'd':2, 'z':1}

    for i from 0 to n‑1
        if not alive[i]               // destroyed → ignore
            continue
        ch ← fight[i]
        if ch in leftValues
            leftPower  += leftValues[ch]
        else if ch in rightValues
            rightPower += rightValues[ch]

    // 3. decide the winner
    if leftPower  > rightPower   return "Left side wins!"
    if rightPower > leftPower    return "Right side wins!"
    return "Let's fight again!"
```

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### Correctness Proof  

We prove that the algorithm returns the required result.

*Lemma 1*  
After step&nbsp;1 `alive[i]` is `false` **iff** the character at position `i`
is a `*` or is adjacent (distance 1) to a `*` in the original string.

*Proof.*  
The loop iterates over every position `i`.  
If `fight[i]` is `*`, the inner loop sets `alive[i]`, `alive[i‑1]` and
`alive[i+1]` (when inside the string) to `false`. No other positions are ever
set to `false`. Therefore a position becomes `false` exactly when it is a `*`
or adjacent to a `*`. ∎



*Lemma 2*  
After step&nbsp;2 `leftPower` (resp. `rightPower`) equals the sum of the powers
of all surviving left‑side (resp. right‑side) letters.

*Proof.*  
The loop visits every index `i`.  
If `alive[i]` is `false` the character was destroyed (Lemma&nbsp;1) and is
ignored, as required.  
Otherwise the character survived. If it belongs to the left side its power
(from `leftValues`) is added to `leftPower`; similarly for the right side.
All other characters contribute `0`. Hence after the loop the two variables hold
exactly the desired sums. ∎



*Theorem*  
`alphabet_war` returns  

* `"Left side wins!"`  iff the total power of surviving left‑side letters is
  larger than that of the right‑side letters,
* `"Right side wins!"` iff the opposite,
* `"Let's fight again!"` otherwise.

*Proof.*  
By Lemma&nbsp;2 `leftPower` and `rightPower` are precisely those totals.
The final three `if` statements compare the two numbers and return the string
corresponding to the three possible relations (`>`, `<`, `=`).  
Thus the returned string matches the specification. ∎



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### Complexity Analysis  

* Let `n` be the length of the input string.
* Step 1 scans the string once and touches at most three positions per `*`:
  **O(n)** time.
* Step 2 scans the string once more: **O(n)** time.
* All auxiliary structures are arrays of length `n`: **O(n)** memory.

Overall: **O(n)** time, **O(n)** additional memory.

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### Reference Implementation (Python 3)

```python
import sys

def alphabet_war(fight: str) -> str:
    n = len(fight)
    alive = [True] * n                     # True → not destroyed

    # 1. destroy bombs and their neighbours
    for i, ch in enumerate(fight):
        if ch == '*':
            for j in (i - 1, i, i + 1):
                if 0 <= j < n:
                    alive[j] = False

    # 2. sum powers of the survivors
    left_vals = {'w': 4, 'p': 3, 'b': 2, 's': 1}
    right_vals = {'m': 4, 'q': 3, 'd': 2, 'z': 1}
    left_power = right_power = 0

    for i, ch in enumerate(fight):
        if not alive[i]:
            continue
        if ch in left_vals:
            left_power += left_vals[ch]
        elif ch in right_vals:
            right_power += right_vals[ch]

    # 3. decide the winner
    if left_power > right_power:
        return "Left side wins!"
    if right_power > left_power:
        return "Right side wins!"
    return "Let's fight again!"


def main() -> None:
    # read the whole line (the problem states a single string)
    fight = sys.stdin.readline().rstrip('\n')
    print(alphabet_war(fight))


if __name__ == "__main__":
    main()
```

The program follows exactly the algorithm proven correct above and conforms
to the required input‑output format.
completed 14/150, Solved: True, number of success = 4/14, acc = 28.57
**Solution Explanation**

For the given two strings  

* `text` – the string in which we search  
* `name` – the sequence of letters we have to find  

we have to decide whether all characters of `name` appear in `text` **in the same order**.
The characters do **not** have to be consecutive – they only have to keep their relative order.
The test is case‑insensitive.

The problem is exactly the classic *subsequence* test.



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### Algorithm
```
function nameInStr(text, name):
    convert both strings to lower case
    pos = 0                                 # current search start in text
    for each character c in name:
        pos = index of c in text starting at pos
        if c is not found (pos == -1):
            return False
        pos = pos + 1                       # continue after the found character
    return True
```

* `pos` always points to the first position in `text` that may still be used.
* `str.find(c, pos)` returns the first occurrence of `c` at or after `pos`
  or `-1` if it does not exist.

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### Correctness Proof  

We prove that the algorithm returns `True` **iff** `name` is a subsequence of `text`.

---

**Lemma 1**  
After processing the first *k* characters of `name` (`0 ≤ k ≤ len(name)`),  
`pos` equals the index **just after** the position of the *k*-th matched character
in `text`. If any of the first *k* characters cannot be matched, the algorithm
already returned `False`.

*Proof by induction over k.*

*Base (`k = 0`)*: before the loop `pos = 0`, i.e. just after the (non‑existent) 0‑th match.

*Induction step*: assume the lemma holds for `k`.  
The algorithm searches `c = name[k]` starting at `pos`.  
If `c` is not found, it returns `False`.  
Otherwise `find` returns the index `i` of the first occurrence of `c` at or after `pos`.  
The algorithm sets `pos = i + 1`, which is exactly the index just after this
(k+1)-st matched character. ∎



**Lemma 2**  
If the algorithm finishes the loop and returns `True`, then the characters of
`name` appear in `text` in order.

*Proof.*  
By Lemma&nbsp;1 with `k = len(name)`, after the last iteration `pos` is placed
just after the position of the last matched character.  
All characters were found, each at a position not earlier than the previous one,
hence they appear in order. ∎



**Lemma 3**  
If `name` is a subsequence of `text`, the algorithm returns `True`.

*Proof.*  
Consider the (unique) leftmost occurrence of each character of `name` that
realises the subsequence.  
When the loop processes the *k*-th character, the search start `pos` is exactly
the index after the (k‑1)-st matched character (Lemma&nbsp;1).  
Since the subsequence exists, the *k*-th character occurs at or after `pos`,
so `find` succeeds and the loop continues.  
Thus the algorithm never returns `False` and finally returns `True`. ∎



**Theorem**  
The algorithm returns `True` **iff** `name` is a subsequence of `text`
(case‑insensitively).

*Proof.*  
*If* part: Lemma&nbsp;3.  
*Only‑if* part: Lemma&nbsp;2. ∎



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### Complexity Analysis  

* `n = len(text)`, `m = len(name)`.
* Each iteration performs a `find` starting from the current `pos`.  
  Over the whole loop the searched region never moves backwards,
  therefore the total work is `O(n)` (each character of `text` is examined at most once).
* Memory usage is `O(1)` besides the input strings.

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### Reference Implementation (Python 3)

```python
import sys

def nameInStr(text: str, name: str) -> bool:
    """Return True iff all letters of `name` appear in `text` in order
       (case‑insensitive)."""
    text = text.lower()
    name = name.lower()
    pos = 0                     # start searching from here
    for ch in name:
        pos = text.find(ch, pos)
        if pos == -1:           # character not found
            return False
        pos += 1                # continue after the found character
    return True

def main() -> None:
    data = sys.stdin.read().splitlines()
    if len(data) < 2:
        return                     # not enough input
    text = data[0].rstrip('\n')
    name = data[1].rstrip('\n')
    result = nameInStr(text, name)
    print(result)                  # prints True or False

if __name__ == "__main__":
    main()
```

The program follows exactly the algorithm proven correct above and conforms
to the required input‑output format.
