**Solution Explanation**

For a given positive integer `n` we have to decide whether *every* decimal digit of `n`
belongs to the set `{0,1,2,3,4,5}`.

* If this is true we output  

```
Special!!
```

* otherwise we output  

```
NOT!!
```

The task is a straightforward scan of the decimal representation of the number.



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### Algorithm
```
read the input number as a string s
for each character c in s
        if c is not one of '0','1','2','3','4','5'
                print "NOT!!" and stop
print "Special!!"
```

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### Correctness Proof  

We prove that the algorithm prints the required answer.

*Lemma*: After processing the loop, the algorithm has examined every digit of `n`.

*Proof of Lemma*: The loop iterates over the characters of the string `s`,
which is exactly the decimal representation of `n`. Hence each digit is visited once. ∎

*Theorem*:  
The algorithm prints `"Special!!"` **iff** all digits of `n` are in `{0,…,5}`.

*Proof*:

* (**If**) Assume all digits of `n` belong to `{0,…,5}`.  
  By the Lemma each digit is examined.  
  For every examined digit `c` the condition `c not in allowed` is false,
  therefore the loop never triggers the early `print "NOT!!"` and termination.
  After the loop finishes, the algorithm reaches `print "Special!!"`.  
  Hence it prints `"Special!!"`.

* (**Only‑if**) Assume the algorithm prints `"Special!!"`.  
  The only way to reach that statement is to finish the loop without executing
  the early `print "NOT!!"`.  
  Consequently for every digit `c` the test `c not in allowed` was false,
  i.e. each digit `c` is one of `'0'…'5'`.  
  Therefore all digits of `n` belong to the required set.

Both directions hold, establishing the equivalence. ∎



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### Complexity Analysis  

Let `d` be the number of decimal digits of `n` (`d = ⌊log₁₀ n⌋ + 1`).  
The loop examines each digit once:

*Time*: `O(d)`  
*Memory*: `O(1)` besides the input string.

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### Reference Implementation (Python 3)

```python
import sys

def special_number(n_str: str) -> str:
    """Return the required string for the given decimal representation."""
    allowed = set('012345')
    for ch in n_str:
        if ch not in allowed:
            return "NOT!!"
    return "Special!!"

def solve() -> None:
    data = sys.stdin.read().strip().split()
    if not data:
        return
    n_str = data[0]          # the first token is the number
    # The problem guarantees a positive integer, but we keep the string as is.
    result = special_number(n_str)
    sys.stdout.write(result)

if __name__ == "__main__":
    solve()
```

The program follows exactly the algorithm proven correct above and conforms
to the required input‑output format.
completed 16/150, Solved: True, number of success = 5/16, acc = 31.25
**Solution Explanation**

For each player we are given a list of creatures.  
A creature is described by two integers  

* `power`  – the amount of damage it deals in combat  
* `toughness` – the amount of damage it can survive

When two creatures fight they deal damage to each other **simultaneously**.
A creature dies if the damage it receives is **greater than or equal to**
its toughness.  
If the two players have a different number of creatures, the extra creatures
are *unblocked* – they never fight and therefore survive automatically.

The task is to return the surviving creatures of both players.



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### Algorithm
```
battle(p1, p2)
    survivors1 ← empty list
    survivors2 ← empty list
    n ← min(len(p1), len(p2))               # number of paired fights

    for i from 0 to n‑1
        c1 ← p1[i]          # [power, toughness]
        c2 ← p2[i]

        # damage each creature receives
        dmg_to_c1 ← c2[0]   # power of opponent
        dmg_to_c2 ← c1[0]

        if dmg_to_c1 < c1[1]          # c1 lives
            survivors1.append(c1)

        if dmg_to_c2 < c2[1]          # c2 lives
            survivors2.append(c2)

    # unblocked creatures survive automatically
    survivors1.extend(p1[n:])
    survivors2.extend(p2[n:])

    return {"player1": survivors1, "player2": survivors2}
```

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### Correctness Proof  

We prove that the algorithm returns exactly the surviving creatures for both
players.

*Lemma 1*  
For a paired fight `i` (`0 ≤ i < n`) the algorithm adds `p1[i]` to
`survivors1` **iff** `p1[i]` survives the combat, and similarly for `p2[i]`.

*Proof.*  
During the fight the damage received by `p1[i]` equals the power of `p2[i]`,
i.e. `dmg_to_c1 = p2[i][0]`.  
`p1[i]` survives exactly when `dmg_to_c1 < p1[i][1]` (damage < toughness).  
The algorithm checks the same condition and appends `p1[i]` precisely in that
case. The argument for `p2[i]` is symmetric. ∎



*Lemma 2*  
All unpaired (unblocked) creatures are included in the corresponding survivor
list.

*Proof.*  
If `len(p1) > n`, the slice `p1[n:]` contains exactly those creatures that have
no opponent. The algorithm appends this slice unchanged to `survivors1`.  
The same holds for player 2. ∎



*Theorem*  
The dictionary returned by `battle(p1, p2)` contains **all and only** the
creatures that survive the whole battle for each player.

*Proof.*  
Consider player 1. Its creatures are partitioned into two disjoint groups:

1. The first `n` creatures that have an opponent.  
   By Lemma&nbsp;1 the algorithm keeps exactly those of them that survive.
2. The remaining `len(p1)‑n` creatures without an opponent.  
   By Lemma&nbsp;2 all of them are kept.

Thus `survivors1` consists of precisely the surviving creatures of player 1.
The same reasoning applies to player 2. Therefore the returned dictionary is
correct. ∎



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### Complexity Analysis  

Let `m = len(p1)` and `k = len(p2)`.  
The loop runs `n = min(m, k)` times, each iteration does O(1) work.
Appending the remaining slices is also O(m + k).  

```
Time   : O(m + k)
Memory : O(m + k)   (the lists of survivors)
```

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### Reference Implementation (Python 3)

```python
import sys
import ast
import json

def battle(player1, player2):
    """Return a dict with the surviving creatures of both players."""
    survivors1 = []
    survivors2 = []

    n = min(len(player1), len(player2))

    # paired fights
    for i in range(n):
        c1 = player1[i]          # [power, toughness]
        c2 = player2[i]

        dmg_to_c1 = c2[0]        # power of opponent
        dmg_to_c2 = c1[0]

        if dmg_to_c1 < c1[1]:
            survivors1.append(c1)
        if dmg_to_c2 < c2[1]:
            survivors2.append(c2)

    # unblocked creatures survive automatically
    survivors1.extend(player1[n:])
    survivors2.extend(player2[n:])

    return {"player1": survivors1, "player2": survivors2}


def main():
    """
    Expected input (two lines):
        <Python list literal for player 1>
        <Python list literal for player 2>

    Example:
        [[2,3],[3,3]]
        [[1,4],[4,1]]
    """
    data = sys.stdin.read().strip().splitlines()
    if len(data) < 2:
        print(json.dumps({"player1": [], "player2": []}))
        return

    # safely evaluate the list literals
    player1 = ast.literal_eval(data[0].strip())
    player2 = ast.literal_eval(data[1].strip())

    result = battle(player1, player2)
    print(json.dumps(result))


if __name__ == "__main__":
    main()
```

The program follows exactly the algorithm proven correct above, reads the two
creature lists from standard input, computes the survivors, and prints the
result as a JSON object.
completed 17/150, Solved: False, number of success = 5/17, acc = 29.41
**Solution Explanation**

For a given integer `n` we have to produce three different views of its prime
decomposition  

* `getAllPrimeFactors(n)` – a flat list of all prime factors (repeated as many
  times as they appear) in ascending order.  

* `getUniquePrimeFactorsWithCount(n)` – two parallel lists: the distinct prime
  factors (ascending) and the corresponding exponents.  

* `getPrimeFactorPotencies(n)` – the prime powers `pⁱ` for each distinct prime
  factor `p` (the same order as the previous function).

The specification also defines the behaviour for invalid input
(`n` not an integer, `n ≤ 0`) and for the special values `0`, `1` and `2`.

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 1.  Prime factorisation

The classic trial‑division algorithm is sufficient because the input size is
not limited in the statement – the algorithm works fast enough for all
reasonable test data.

```
while n is divisible by 2:
        add 2 to the factor list
        n //= 2
for odd divisor d from 3 up to sqrt(n) (step 2):
        while n % d == 0:
                add d to the factor list
                n //= d
if n > 1:               # a prime larger than sqrt(original n)
        add n to the factor list
```

The produced list is already sorted because we try the divisors in increasing
order.

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 2.  Building the three required results

* **All factors** – the list obtained directly from the algorithm, with the
  special cases handled beforehand.

* **Unique factors with their counts** – we walk through the sorted factor list
  once, counting consecutive equal values.  
  Two new lists `primes` and `powers` are filled in the same order.

* **Prime powers** – using the two lists from the previous step we simply
  compute `p ** power` for each pair.

All three functions return the exact structures demanded by the statement,
including the special return values for error cases.

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 3.  Correctness Proof  

We prove that each function satisfies the specification.

---

##### Lemma 1  
`factorise(n)` (the trial‑division routine) returns a list `F` that contains
exactly the prime factors of `n`, each repeated as many times as it occurs in
the prime factorisation of `n`, and `F` is sorted in non‑decreasing order.

**Proof.**  
The algorithm first extracts all factors `2`. Afterwards it tests every odd
integer `d` from `3` up to `⌊√n⌋`.  
*If `d` divides the current `n`* we repeatedly divide `n` by `d` and store `d`
each time – this records the exact multiplicity of `d`.  
When the loop finishes, the remaining `n` (if larger than `1`) is a prime
greater than `√original_n`; it is stored once.  
All stored numbers are prime (by construction) and every prime divisor of the
original number is discovered, because any composite divisor would have a
prime divisor ≤ its square root, which would have been removed earlier.
The divisors are examined in increasing order, therefore the stored sequence
is sorted. ∎



##### Lemma 2  
`getAllPrimeFactors(n)` returns the list required by the statement.

**Proof.**  

* If `n` is not an integer, ≤ 0 → the function returns `[]` as required.  
* If `n == 1` → returns `[1]` (special case defined by the statement).  
* If `n ≥ 2` → it calls `factorise(n)`. By Lemma&nbsp;1 the returned list is
  exactly the multiset of prime factors in ascending order, which is precisely
  the demanded output. ∎



##### Lemma 3  
`getUniquePrimeFactorsWithCount(n)` returns `[[p₁,…,p_k],[e₁,…,e_k]]`
where `p_i` are the distinct prime factors of `n` in ascending order and
`e_i` their exponents.

**Proof.**  

* Error and special cases are handled exactly as required.  
* For `n ≥ 2` the function obtains the sorted factor list `F` from Lemma 1.
  It scans `F` once, grouping equal consecutive elements.
  For each group of value `p` occurring `c` times it appends `p` to `primes`
  and `c` to `powers`.  
  Because `F` is sorted, groups appear in ascending order of `p`.  
  Every prime factor of `n` appears in exactly one group, therefore each
  distinct prime appears once in `primes` and its multiplicity is stored in
  `powers`. ∎



##### Lemma 4  
`getPrimeFactorPotencies(n)` returns the list `[p₁^{e₁}, …, p_k^{e_k}]`
with the same ordering as Lemma 3.

**Proof.**  

* The function first obtains the two lists from Lemma 3.
* It then computes `p_i ** e_i` for each index `i` and stores the results in
  order.  
  By definition of exponentiation this is exactly the product of the prime
  `p_i` taken `e_i` times, i.e. the prime power. ∎



##### Theorem  
For every integer input `n` the program prints

1. the list of all prime factors (with repetitions) in ascending order,
2. the two‑list representation of distinct primes and their exponents,
3. the list of prime powers,

or the appropriate empty/placeholder structures for invalid inputs,
exactly as required by the problem statement.

**Proof.**  
The main routine reads the input, converts it to an integer (or flags an error)
and calls the three functions described above.  
By Lemma 2, Lemma 3 and Lemma 4 each function returns the correct value for the
given `n`. The program prints those values, therefore the output satisfies the
specification. ∎



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 4.  Complexity Analysis  

Let `n` be the absolute value of the input.

*Trial division* checks at most `√n` odd numbers, each division is `O(1)`.  
Hence the factorisation runs in `O(√n)` time.

The subsequent scans of the factor list are linear in the number of factors,
which is at most `log₂ n`.  
Therefore the total time complexity is `O(√n)` and the memory consumption is
`O(log n)` (the list of factors).

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 5.  Reference Implementation  (Python 3)

```python
import sys
import math
from typing import List, Tuple

# ------------------------------------------------------------
# Helper: prime factorisation, returns sorted list of factors
# ------------------------------------------------------------
def _factorise(n: int) -> List[int]:
    """Return sorted list of prime factors of n (n >= 2)."""
    factors = []
    # factor 2
    while n % 2 == 0:
        factors.append(2)
        n //= 2
    # odd factors
    d = 3
    limit = int(math.isqrt(n)) + 1
    while d <= limit and n > 1:
        while n % d == 0:
            factors.append(d)
            n //= d
            limit = int(math.isqrt(n)) + 1   # shrink limit after division
        d += 2
    if n > 1:          # remaining prime
        factors.append(n)
    return factors


# ------------------------------------------------------------
# 1. all prime factors (with repetitions)
# ------------------------------------------------------------
def getAllPrimeFactors(n) -> List[int]:
    # error handling
    if not isinstance(n, int):
        return []
    if n <= 0:
        return []
    if n == 1:
        return [1]

    return _factorise(n)


# ------------------------------------------------------------
# 2. distinct primes + their exponents
# ------------------------------------------------------------
def getUniquePrimeFactorsWithCount(n) -> List[List[int]]:
    if not isinstance(n, int):
        return [[], []]
    if n <= 0:
        return [[], []]
    if n == 1:
        return [[1], [1]]

    factors = _factorise(n)          # sorted
    primes = []
    powers = []

    i = 0
    while i < len(factors):
        p = factors[i]
        cnt = 0
        while i < len(factors) and factors[i] == p:
            cnt += 1
            i += 1
        primes.append(p)
        powers.append(cnt)

    return [primes, powers]


# ------------------------------------------------------------
# 3. prime powers (p ** exponent)
# ------------------------------------------------------------
def getPrimeFactorPotencies(n) -> List[int]:
    if not isinstance(n, int):
        return []
    if n <= 0:
        return []
    if n == 1:
        return [1]

    uniq = getUniquePrimeFactorsWithCount(n)
    primes, powers = uniq[0], uniq[1]
    return [p ** e for p, e in zip(primes, powers)]


# ------------------------------------------------------------
# Main driver: read integer, output the three results
# ------------------------------------------------------------
def solve() -> None:
    data = sys.stdin.read().strip()
    try:
        n = int(data)
    except Exception:
        # not a number -> treat as error
        n = None

    # compute results
    all_factors = getAllPrimeFactors(n)
    uniq_counts = getUniquePrimeFactorsWithCount(n)
    potencies = getPrimeFactorPotencies(n)

    # print each result on its own line (as required by the statement)
    print(all_factors)
    print(uniq_counts)
    print(potencies)


if __name__ == "__main__":
    solve()
```

The program follows exactly the algorithm proven correct above and conforms
to the input‑output format demanded by the task.

**Solution Explanation**

For the given input string we have to

1. replace every character by its decimal ASCII code,
2. concatenate those codes – this gives the number `total1`,
3. obtain `total2` by changing every digit `7` in `total1` to `1`,
4. compute the sum of the decimal digits of `total1` and `total2`,
5. output the difference `sum(total1) – sum(total2)`.

All operations can be performed on strings – we never need the huge integer
value itself.



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### Algorithm
```
read the input line s
total1 = concatenate str(ord(ch)) for every ch in s
total2 = total1 with every '7' replaced by '1'

sum1 = sum( int(d) for each digit d in total1 )
sum2 = sum( int(d) for each digit d in total2 )

print( sum1 - sum2 )
```

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### Correctness Proof  

We prove that the algorithm prints the required difference.

*Lemma 1*  
`total1` produced by the algorithm equals the number obtained by writing the
ASCII codes of the characters of `s` one after another.

*Proof.*  
For each character `ch` the algorithm computes `ord(ch)`, which is exactly the
ASCII decimal code of `ch`. Converting it to a string yields the decimal
representation of that code. Concatenating those strings in the original order
produces precisely the required sequence of digits. ∎



*Lemma 2*  
`total2` produced by the algorithm equals the number obtained from `total1`
by replacing every occurrence of the digit `7` with the digit `1`.

*Proof.*  
`total2` is defined as `total1.replace('7','1')`. The `replace` method scans the
string and substitutes each `'7'` by `'1'`, leaving all other characters
unchanged. Hence `total2` is exactly the described transformation of `total1`. ∎



*Lemma 3*  
`sum1` (resp. `sum2`) computed by the algorithm equals the sum of the decimal
digits of `total1` (resp. `total2`).

*Proof.*  
Both sums iterate over the characters of the corresponding string, convert each
character (which is a digit) to an integer and add it to the accumulator.
Therefore each digit contributes its numeric value exactly once, which is the
definition of the digit sum. ∎



*Theorem*  
The algorithm outputs  
`(sum of digits of total1) – (sum of digits of total2)`.

*Proof.*  
By Lemma&nbsp;1 and Lemma&nbsp;2 the strings `total1` and `total2` are exactly the
numbers defined in the problem statement. By Lemma&nbsp;3 `sum1` and `sum2`
are the digit sums of those numbers. The algorithm finally prints `sum1 -
sum2`, which is precisely the required result. ∎



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### Complexity Analysis  

Let `n = len(s)`.  
Creating `total1` needs `O(n)` time (one `ord` per character) and produces at most
`3·n` characters (ASCII codes are at most three digits).  
All subsequent steps scan the resulting strings once, therefore also `O(n)`.  
Memory consumption is `O(n)` for the two strings.

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### Reference Implementation (Python 3)

```python
import sys

def solve() -> None:
    # read the whole line, keep possible spaces (strip only the trailing newline)
    s = sys.stdin.readline()
    if s.endswith('\n'):
        s = s[:-1]          # remove only the newline character

    # step 1: build total1
    total1 = ''.join(str(ord(ch)) for ch in s)

    # step 2: build total2 by replacing '7' with '1'
    total2 = total1.replace('7', '1')

    # step 3: compute digit sums
    sum1 = sum(int(d) for d in total1)
    sum2 = sum(int(d) for d in total2)

    # step 4: output the difference
    print(sum1 - sum2)


if __name__ == "__main__":
    solve()
```

completed 19/150, Solved: False, number of success = 5/19, acc = 26.32
**Solution Explanation**

For a substring to be a *valid combination* it has to

* contain only the characters `!` and `?`,
* contain at least one `!` **and** at least one `?`,
* consist of **exactly two blocks** – a block of one character followed by a
  block of the other character  

  (`!…! ?…?`  or  `?…? !…!`).

The task is to return the longest such substring.
If several substrings have the same maximal length the left‑most one is required.
If no valid substring exists the answer is the empty string.



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 1.  Observations  

*Inside the whole string* the characters `!` and `?` appear in **runs**
(maximal consecutive groups of the same character).

```
example:   !!???!?????
runs:      [!!] [???] [!] [?????]
```

A valid combination is always the concatenation of **two consecutive runs**
(one run of `!` and the next run of `?` or the opposite).
No other characters may be inside the substring, therefore a longer
valid substring can never skip a run.

Consequences  

* The answer is one of the `run[i] + run[i+1]` pairs.
* Its length is `len(run[i]) + len(run[i+1])`.
* The leftmost maximal pair is the required answer.

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 2.  Algorithm  

```
scan the whole input string
    build a list `runs` of (char, start_index, length) for only '!' and '?'
    any other character breaks the current run (it is ignored)

best_len   = 0
best_start = -1

for each i from 0 to len(runs)-2
        # runs[i] and runs[i+1] are consecutive blocks of different chars
        cur_len   = runs[i].length + runs[i+1].length
        cur_start = runs[i].start_index
        if cur_len > best_len
                best_len   = cur_len
                best_start = cur_start
        # if equal length we keep the earlier one (already stored)

if best_len == 0          # no pair found
        answer = ""
else
        answer = input_string[best_start : best_start + best_len]

output answer
```

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 3.  Correctness Proof  

We prove that the algorithm returns exactly the required substring.

---

##### Lemma 1  
Every valid combination is the concatenation of two **adjacent** runs
in the list `runs`.

**Proof.**  
A valid combination contains only `!` and `?` and has exactly one change
from one character to the other.
Therefore it consists of a maximal block of one character followed
immediately by a maximal block of the other character.
By construction of `runs` each maximal block is a run, and because the
blocks are adjacent, the two runs are consecutive in `runs`. ∎



##### Lemma 2  
For each pair of consecutive runs `runs[i] , runs[i+1]` the substring
`input[ runs[i].start : runs[i].start + runs[i].length + runs[i+1].length ]`
is a valid combination.

**Proof.**  
`runs[i]` contains only one character (`!` or `?`), `runs[i+1]` contains only
the other character (runs are maximal, therefore different).
Both runs are non‑empty, they are adjacent, and no other characters appear
between them. Hence the concatenation satisfies the definition of a valid
combination. ∎



##### Lemma 3  
The algorithm examines **all** possible valid combinations.

**Proof.**  
By Lemma&nbsp;1 every valid combination corresponds to a pair of consecutive
runs. The algorithm iterates over *all* indices `i` with `0 ≤ i < len(runs)-1`,
therefore it evaluates every such pair. ∎



##### Lemma 4  
When the algorithm finishes, `best_len` equals the maximum length of any
valid combination and `best_start` is the smallest start index among those
with maximal length.

**Proof.**  
During the loop each candidate length `cur_len` (Lemma&nbsp;2) is compared
with the current `best_len`.

*If `cur_len` is larger* – we replace `best_len` and store its start,
so the invariant “`best_len` is the greatest length seen so far” holds.

*If `cur_len` equals `best_len`* – we do **not** replace the stored start,
therefore the earlier (smaller) start index is kept.

By induction over the loop iterations the invariant holds after the last
iteration, i.e. after the loop ends `best_len` is the maximum possible
length and `best_start` the leftmost start among those maxima. ∎



##### Lemma 5  
If `best_len > 0` the substring returned by the algorithm is a valid
combination of maximal length; if `best_len = 0` no valid combination exists.

**Proof.**  
*Case `best_len > 0`*: the substring is exactly the one described by the
pair that set `best_len`. By Lemma&nbsp;2 it is a valid combination.
By Lemma&nbsp;4 its length is maximal and its start is the leftmost among
maximal ones.

*Case `best_len = 0`*: the loop never found a pair of consecutive runs,
hence there is no place where both `!` and `?` appear adjacent.
Consequently no valid combination exists (definition requires both
characters). ∎



##### Theorem  
The algorithm outputs

* the longest successive exclamation‑marks‑and‑question‑marks combination,
  choosing the leftmost one when several have the same length,
* or the empty string if no such combination exists.

**Proof.**  
Directly from Lemma&nbsp;5. ∎



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 4.  Complexity Analysis  

*Scanning* the input once builds the runs: **O(n)** time, **O(k)** memory,
where `k` is the number of runs ( ≤ n ).
The second pass over the runs is **O(k)** ≤ **O(n)**.
Overall time complexity: **O(n)**.  
Additional memory: the list of runs, at most `n` entries → **O(n)**,
well within limits.

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 5.  Reference Implementation (Python 3)

```python
import sys

def longest_combo(s: str) -> str:
    # Build runs of only '!' and '?'
    runs = []                     # each element: (char, start_index, length)
    i = 0
    n = len(s)
    while i < n:
        ch = s[i]
        if ch not in ('!', '?'):
            i += 1
            continue                # delimiter, start a new run later
        start = i
        length = 0
        while i < n and s[i] == ch:
            length += 1
            i += 1
        runs.append((ch, start, length))

    best_len = 0
    best_start = -1

    for idx in range(len(runs) - 1):
        # runs[idx] and runs[idx+1] are consecutive blocks of different chars
        cur_len = runs[idx][2] + runs[idx + 1][2]
        cur_start = runs[idx][1]
        if cur_len > best_len:
            best_len = cur_len
            best_start = cur_start
        # equal length -> keep earlier start (already stored)

    if best_len == 0:
        return ""
    return s[best_start: best_start + best_len]


def main():
    data = sys.stdin.read().splitlines()
    if not data:
        print("")          # empty input -> empty output
        return
    # The problem statement shows a single line input, but we join all lines
    # to be safe (they may contain spaces or newlines inside the string).
    input_str = "\n".join(data).rstrip("\n")
    result = longest_combo(input_str)
    print(result)


if __name__ == "__main__":
    main()
```

The program follows exactly the algorithm proven correct above
and conforms to the required input / output format.
completed 20/150, Solved: True, number of success = 6/20, acc = 30.0
**Solution Explanation**

For every element of the input list we have a string of the form  

```
"<first>-<second>"
```

Both `<first>` and `<second>` are positive integers.  
The *difference* of a couple is the absolute value `|first‑second|`.

The task is to return the couple whose difference is maximal.
If several couples have the same maximal difference we must return the one
that appears first in the list.
If every couple has difference `0` we output `false`.

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### Algorithm
```
read the whole line
parse it as a Python list → couples               # e.g. ['56-23','1-100']

max_diff   = -1
best_couple = None

for each string s in couples
        a_str, b_str = s.split('-')
        a = int(a_str)
        b = int(b_str)
        diff = abs(a - b)

        if diff > max_diff               # '>' keeps the first occurrence on ties
                max_diff   = diff
                best_couple = s

if max_diff == 0          # all differences are zero
        print('false')
else
        print(best_couple)
```

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### Correctness Proof  

We prove that the algorithm prints exactly the required output.

*Lemma 1*  
For each processed couple `s` the algorithm computes `diff = |first - second|`.

*Proof.*  
`s` is split at the single character `'-'`, producing the two substrings that
represent the two numbers. Converting them to `int` yields the original
positive integers. `abs(a-b)` is precisely the absolute difference. ∎



*Lemma 2*  
After processing the first *k* couples (`1 ≤ k ≤ n`) the variables
`max_diff` and `best_couple` store respectively  

* the greatest difference among the first *k* couples, and  
* the first couple (in the original order) that attains this greatest difference.

*Proof by induction on* *k*.

*Base (`k = 1`).*  
The loop evaluates the first couple, sets `max_diff` to its difference and
`best_couple` to the couple itself. Both statements hold.

*Induction step.*  
Assume the claim true after `k‑1` iterations.
When the `k`‑th couple is examined we have its difference `d`.

- If `d > max_diff`, the algorithm updates `max_diff` to `d` and
  `best_couple` to the current couple, which is now the unique greatest
  difference among the first `k` couples.
- If `d ≤ max_diff`, the variables stay unchanged.  
  In the case `d = max_diff` the earlier couple (already stored) remains the
  first one with that maximal difference.

Thus the claim also holds after `k` iterations. ∎



*Lemma 3*  
After the loop finishes (`k = n`) `max_diff` equals the maximal difference
among **all** input couples and `best_couple` is the first couple attaining it.

*Proof.* Directly from Lemma&nbsp;2 with `k = n`. ∎



*Theorem*  
The algorithm outputs  

* `false` iff every input couple has difference `0`; otherwise  
* the first couple with the greatest difference.

*Proof.*  

- If all differences are `0`, Lemma&nbsp;3 gives `max_diff = 0`.  
  The algorithm prints `false`, satisfying the specification.
- If at least one difference is non‑zero, Lemma&nbsp;3 yields `max_diff > 0`
  and `best_couple` is exactly the first couple with that maximal difference.
  The algorithm prints `best_couple`, which is the required answer. ∎



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### Complexity Analysis  

Let `n` be the number of couples.

* Splitting and converting each couple takes `O(1)` time.
* The loop runs `n` times.

Total time complexity: **O(n)**  
Additional space used: only a few variables → **O(1)**.

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### Reference Implementation (Python 3)

```python
import sys
import ast

def main() -> None:
    # read the whole input (it may contain newline at the end)
    data = sys.stdin.read().strip()
    if not data:
        return

    # The input format is a Python list literal, e.g. ['56-23','1-100']
    try:
        couples = ast.literal_eval(data)
    except Exception:
        # fallback: treat the line as space‑separated strings
        couples = data.split()

    max_diff = -1
    best_couple = None

    for s in couples:
        # each element is expected to be a string like "a-b"
        a_str, b_str = s.split('-')
        a = int(a_str)
        b = int(b_str)
        diff = abs(a - b)

        if diff > max_diff:          # '>' keeps the first on ties
            max_diff = diff
            best_couple = s

    if max_diff == 0:
        print('false')
    else:
        print(best_couple)


if __name__ == "__main__":
    main()
```

The program follows exactly the algorithm proven correct above and conforms
to the required input‑output format.
completed 21/150, Solved: False, number of success = 6/21, acc = 28.57
**Solution Explanation**

For every character `ch` of the given lower‑case string we have to produce a new
character according to the rules described in the statement.

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 1.   Classification of a character  

*Vowels* (Polish rule – **`y` is NOT a vowel*)  

```
vowels = { 'a', 'e', 'i', 'o', 'u' }
```

All other letters are *consonants*.

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 2.   Shift amount  

| character | shift (relative to the alphabet) |
|-----------|-----------------------------------|
| `'c'` or `'o'` | **‑1** |
| `'d'`          | **‑3** |
| `'e'`          | **‑4** |
| any other **vowel** | **‑5** |
| any other **consonant** | **+9** |

The four “exception” rows have priority – they are applied **before**
the generic vowel / consonant rule.

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 3.   Applying the shift  

Alphabet positions are numbered `0 … 25` (`a → 0`, `b → 1`, …, `z → 25`).

```
new_pos = (old_pos + shift) mod 26
new_char = chr(new_pos + ord('a'))
```

The modulo operation implements the required wrap‑around
(`z` → `a` when moving forward, `a` → `z` when moving backward).

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 4.   Forbidden results  

If after the shift the character becomes one of  

```
forbidden = { 'c', 'o', 'd', 'e' }
```

the transformation is cancelled and the **original** character is kept.

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 5.   Algorithm
```
result = empty list
for each ch in input string:
        if ch == 'c' or ch == 'o':      shift = -1
        elif ch == 'd':                shift = -3
        elif ch == 'e':                shift = -4
        elif ch in vowels:            shift = -5
        else:                          shift = +9

        shifted = shift_char(ch, shift)          # with wrap‑around
        if shifted in forbidden:
                result.append(ch)                # revert
        else:
                result.append(shifted)

print(''.join(result))
```

`shift_char` is a tiny helper that performs the modulo arithmetic described
in section&nbsp;3.

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 6.   Correctness Proof  

We prove that the algorithm outputs exactly the string required by the
statement.

---

**Lemma 1**  
For any character `ch` the algorithm chooses the shift amount prescribed by
the problem.

*Proof.*  
The algorithm checks the four special characters first (`c`, `o`, `d`, `e`).
If one matches, the corresponding shift (`-1`, `-1`, `-3`, `-4`) is taken.
Otherwise it tests whether `ch` is a vowel (`a, e, i, o, u`).  
If true, shift `-5` is used, else shift `+9`.  
These are precisely the rules given. ∎



**Lemma 2**  
`shift_char(ch, shift)` returns the character that is `shift` positions
forward (or backward if `shift` is negative) in the alphabet, with wrap‑around.

*Proof.*  
`old_pos = ord(ch) - ord('a')` is the 0‑based index of `ch`.  
Adding `shift` and taking modulo `26` yields the index of the target letter,
because modulo `26` maps any integer to the unique element of `{0,…,25}`
that is congruent to it. Converting back with `chr(... + ord('a'))` gives the
corresponding lower‑case letter. ∎



**Lemma 3**  
If the shifted character belongs to `{c,o,d,e}` the algorithm outputs the
original character, otherwise it outputs the shifted character.

*Proof.*  
After computing `shifted`, the algorithm tests `if shifted in forbidden`.
If true, it appends the original `ch`; else it appends `shifted`. ∎



**Theorem**  
For every input string `s` the algorithm outputs the string obtained by
applying **all** transformation rules of the kata.

*Proof.*  
Consider an arbitrary position `i` with character `ch = s[i]`.

1. By Lemma&nbsp;1 the algorithm selects exactly the shift prescribed for `ch`.
2. By Lemma&nbsp;2 `shift_char` computes the character that results from moving
   `ch` by that shift with the required alphabetic wrap‑around.
3. By Lemma&nbsp;3 the algorithm respects the additional rule
   “if the result becomes `c`, `o`, `d` or `e` revert to the original”.

Thus the character placed at position `i` in the output is exactly the one
defined by the problem statement.  
Since the argument holds for every position, the whole output string is
correct. ∎



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 7.   Complexity Analysis  

For a string of length `n` we perform a constant amount of work per character.

*Time*: `O(n)`  
*Memory*: `O(n)` for the resulting string (the algorithm uses only a few
additional constant‑size variables).

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 8.   Reference Implementation (Python 3)

```python
import sys

def shift_char(ch: str, shift: int) -> str:
    """Return ch shifted by `shift` positions in the alphabet with wrap‑around."""
    base = ord('a')
    pos = ord(ch) - base
    new_pos = (pos + shift) % 26
    return chr(base + new_pos)

def transform(s: str) -> str:
    vowels = {'a', 'e', 'i', 'o', 'u'}
    forbidden = {'c', 'o', 'd', 'e'}

    out_chars = []
    for ch in s:
        # 1. decide shift (exceptions first)
        if ch == 'c' or ch == 'o':
            shift = -1
        elif ch == 'd':
            shift = -3
        elif ch == 'e':
            shift = -4
        elif ch in vowels:
            shift = -5
        else:               # any other consonant
            shift = 9

        # 2. apply shift
        shifted = shift_char(ch, shift)

        # 3. revert if result is forbidden
        if shifted in forbidden:
            out_chars.append(ch)
        else:
            out_chars.append(shifted)

    return ''.join(out_chars)

def solve() -> None:
    data = sys.stdin.read().strip()
    if not data:
        return
    # the whole input is the string (no extra spaces)
    s = data
    print(transform(s))

if __name__ == "__main__":
    solve()
```

The program follows exactly the algorithm proven correct above and conforms
to the required input‑output format.
completed 22/150, Solved: True, number of success = 7/22, acc = 31.82
**Solution Explanation**

For every *token* (a piece of the input separated by blanks) we have to

1. keep the trailing punctuation unchanged,
2. decide which of the three possible transformations is applied to the
   *core* word (the part before the punctuation),
3. apply **only the first rule that matches**,
4. put the punctuation back and output the transformed tokens separated by a
   single blank.

The three rules are  

* **reverse** – if the core word has more than 6 characters **or**
  contains at least two letters `t`/`T`;
* **uppercase** – if the core word has exactly 2 characters **or**
  the token is directly followed by a comma (the first punctuation character is
  `,`);
* **replace by `0`** – if the core word has exactly 1 character.

All other words stay unchanged.



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### Algorithm
```
read the whole input line
split it by blanks → list tokens

for each token
        # 1. separate trailing punctuation
        punct = ""                     # characters after the word
        while token is not empty and token[-1] is NOT a letter, digit,
              apostrophe (') or dash (-)
                punct = token[-1] + punct
                delete last character from token
        core = token                    # word without punctuation

        # 2. decide the transformation (first rule that matches)
        if len(core) > 6 or count of 't'/'T' in core >= 2
                new_core = core reversed
        elif len(core) == 2 or (punct != "" and punct[0] == ',')
                new_core = core upper‑cased
        elif len(core) == 1
                new_core = "0"
        else
                new_core = core          # unchanged

        # 3. re‑attach punctuation
        result_token = new_core + punct
        store result_token

output the stored tokens joined by a single blank
```

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### Correctness Proof  

We prove that the algorithm outputs exactly the required transformed sentence.

---

**Lemma 1**  
For any token the algorithm extracts `core` consisting of all characters of the
token except the maximal suffix of characters that are *not* letters,
digits, apostrophe or dash.  
`punct` is exactly that suffix.

*Proof.*  
The `while` loop removes characters from the end of the token as long as the
character is not in the allowed set. It stops at the first character that *is*
in the set or when the token becomes empty. Therefore the removed characters
form the longest possible suffix of “punctuation” characters, and the remaining
prefix is `core`. ∎



**Lemma 2**  
For a given `core` the algorithm applies the first rule (in the order
*reverse → uppercase → replace by 0*) whose condition is true.

*Proof.*  
The three `if/elif/elif` statements are evaluated sequentially.
The first true condition executes its block and the remaining `elif`s are
skipped. Hence exactly the first matching rule is applied. ∎



**Lemma 3**  
If a rule’s condition holds for a word in the original problem statement,
the same condition holds for the algorithm’s `core`.

*Proof.*  
The problem’s conditions refer only to the *letters* of the word, ignoring any
trailing punctuation. By Lemma&nbsp;1 `core` is precisely the word without that
punctuation, therefore the length, the number of `t`/`T`, and the presence of a
following comma (checked by `punct[0] == ','`) are identical for the original
word and for `core`. ∎



**Theorem**  
For every input token the algorithm outputs the token transformed exactly as
required by the specification.

*Proof.*  
Consider an arbitrary token.

*Step 1* (Lemma 1) separates punctuation, which must stay unchanged.

*Step 2* uses the three conditions on `core`.  
By Lemma 3 each condition is true for `core` iff it is true for the original
word.  
By Lemma 2 the algorithm applies the first true rule, which is precisely the
rule that the problem statement demands (the statement also says “only the
first transformation applies”).  
The transformation performed (`reverse`, `upper`, or `0`) matches the rule’s
definition.

*Step 3* re‑attaches the unchanged punctuation, yielding the required final
token.

Since the algorithm processes every token independently and finally joins them
with blanks, the whole output sentence is exactly the required one. ∎



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### Complexity Analysis  

Let *n* be the total number of characters of the input line.

* Splitting the line and joining the result are `O(n)`.
* For each token we scan its characters once to separate punctuation and once
  more (constant‑time operations) to count `t`/`T`.  
  Overall work is linear in the token length.

Therefore the total time complexity is **`O(n)`** and the algorithm uses
**`O(n)`** additional space for the list of transformed tokens (the input itself
already occupies `O(n)`).

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### Reference Implementation  (Python 3)

```python
import sys
import string

def solve() -> None:
    # read the whole input (single line, but keep possible trailing newline)
    line = sys.stdin.read()
    if not line:
        return
    line = line.rstrip('\n')

    # characters that belong to a word (punctuation characters are everything else)
    word_chars = set(string.ascii_letters + string.digits + "'-")

    tokens = line.split(' ')
    out_tokens = []

    for token in tokens:
        # ---- 1. separate trailing punctuation ----
        punct = ''
        while token and token[-1] not in word_chars:
            punct = token[-1] + punct
            token = token[:-1]
        core = token

        # ---- 2. decide transformation ----
        # count t/T in core
        t_cnt = sum(1 for ch in core if ch == 't' or ch == 'T')
        new_core = core   # default unchanged

        if len(core) > 6 or t_cnt >= 2:
            new_core = core[::-1]
        elif len(core) == 2 or (punct and punct[0] == ','):
            new_core = core.upper()
        elif len(core) == 1:
            new_core = '0'

        # ---- 3. re‑attach punctuation ----
        out_tokens.append(new_core + punct)

    # output
    sys.stdout.write(' '.join(out_tokens))

if __name__ == "__main__":
    solve()
```

The program follows exactly the algorithm proven correct above and conforms to
the required input‑output format.

**Solution Explanation**

For every given phrase we have to replace **all** its occurrences

```
"probably"   → "prolly"
"i am"       → "i'm"
"instagram"  → "insta"
"do not"     → "don't"
"going to"   → "gonna"
"combination"→ "combo"
```

The replacement must be done with regular expressions (`re.sub`).  
Only lower‑case matches are required, but a correctly capitalised version
(e.g. `Probably`) has to be replaced as well (`Prolly`).  
All other capitalisations (e.g. `PROBABLY`, `prObably`) must stay unchanged.

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### Algorithm
```
read the whole input as one string  S
for each (word, slang) pair
        # 1. lower‑case version
        S = re.sub( r'\b' + escaped(word) + r'\b', slang, S )
        # 2. capitalised version (first letter upper, rest lower)
        cap_word  = word[0].upper() + word[1:]
        cap_slang = slang[0].upper() + slang[1:]
        S = re.sub( r'\b' + escaped(cap_word) + r'\b', cap_slang, S )
output S
```

* `\b` – word boundary, guarantees we replace whole words / phrases only.  
* `escaped(x)` – `re.escape(x)` so that characters like spaces are treated literally.  
* The two `re.sub` calls guarantee that both the lower‑case and the capitalised
  form are replaced, nothing else is touched.

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### Correctness Proof  

We prove that the algorithm outputs the required transformed string.

*Lemma 1*  
For a given pair `(word, slang)` the first `re.sub` call replaces **every**
occurrence of `word` that is completely lower‑case and surrounded by word
boundaries with `slang`, and does not modify any other substring.

*Proof.*  
The pattern `\bword\b` (with `word` escaped) matches exactly those substrings
that are equal to `word` and have a word boundary before the first character
and after the last character. Because the pattern is case‑sensitive, only the
lower‑case spelling `word` can match. `re.sub` replaces each match with `slang`
and leaves all non‑matching parts unchanged. ∎



*Lemma 2*  
For the same pair, the second `re.sub` call replaces **every** occurrence of
the capitalised form `CapWord` (first letter upper‑case, the rest lower‑case)
with the capitalised slang `CapSlang`, and does not modify any other substring.

*Proof.*  
`CapWord` is constructed by upper‑casing the first character of `word` and
keeping the rest unchanged, therefore it is exactly the correctly
capitalised version. The pattern `\bCapWord\b` matches only those substrings,
again because of the word boundaries and case‑sensitivity. `re.sub` replaces
each match with `CapSlang` (first letter upper‑cased, rest unchanged) and leaves
everything else untouched. ∎



*Lemma 3*  
After processing a pair `(word, slang)` the string contains the required
replacements for that pair and no other changes.

*Proof.*  
By Lemma&nbsp;1 all lower‑case occurrences are replaced, by Lemma&nbsp;2 all
capitalised occurrences are replaced. The two patterns are disjoint (they
cannot match the same substring because their first characters differ in case),
so the two `re.sub` calls do not interfere. No other part of the string is
matched, therefore it stays unchanged. ∎



*Theorem*  
After the loop finishes, the output string is exactly the input string with
all required slang replacements applied, respecting the case rules.

*Proof.*  
The loop iterates over the six given `(word, slang)` pairs.
By Lemma&nbsp;3, after each iteration the string satisfies the replacement
requirements for the processed pair while preserving all other characters.
Induction over the six iterations shows that after the last iteration the
string satisfies the requirements for **all** pairs simultaneously.
No additional modifications are performed, therefore the final string is the
desired result. ∎



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### Complexity Analysis  

Let `n` be the length of the input text.

For each of the six pairs we run two `re.sub` operations, each scanning the
whole current string once.  
Time complexity: `O(6·2·n) = O(n)`  
Space complexity: `O(n)` (the string itself and the temporary result).

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### Reference Implementation  (Python 3)

```python
import sys
import re

def gym_slang(text: str) -> str:
    # list of (original word, slang)
    pairs = [
        ("probably", "prolly"),
        ("i am", "i'm"),
        ("instagram", "insta"),
        ("do not", "don't"),
        ("going to", "gonna"),
        ("combination", "combo")
    ]

    for word, slang in pairs:
        # lower‑case version
        pattern_low = r'\b' + re.escape(word) + r'\b'
        text = re.sub(pattern_low, slang, text)

        # capitalised version (first letter upper, rest lower)
        cap_word = word[0].upper() + word[1:]
        cap_slang = slang[0].upper() + slang[1:]
        pattern_cap = r'\b' + re.escape(cap_word) + r'\b'
        text = re.sub(pattern_cap, cap_slang, text)

    return text


def main() -> None:
    # read the whole stdin (may contain newlines)
    input_text = sys.stdin.read()
    # remove possible trailing newline only for nicer output handling
    # (the problem does not forbid keeping it)
    result = gym_slang(input_text.rstrip('\n'))
    print(result)


if __name__ == "__main__":
    main()
```

The program follows exactly the algorithm proven correct above and uses
regular‑expression replacement (`re.sub`) as required.

**Solution Explanation**

For a *Madhav* array the elements after the first one must be split into
consecutive groups whose sizes are `2, 3, 4 …`.  
All those groups have to have the same sum – the value of the first
element `a[0]`.

```
a[0] = a[1] + a[2]                     (group size 2)
a[0] = a[3] + a[4] + a[5]              (group size 3)
a[0] = a[6] + a[7] + a[8] + a[9]       (group size 4)
...
```

Therefore the total length `n` of a Madhav array must be

```
n = 1 + 2 + 3 + … + k = 1 + k·(k+1)/2          (k ≥ 1)
```

If `n` does **not** have this form the array can never be Madhav.



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### Algorithm
```
is_madhav(arr):
    if len(arr) < 2:                     # 0 or 1 element → not Madhav
        return False

    # 1) find k such that 1 + k·(k+1)/2 == len(arr)
    n = len(arr)
    k = 1
    while 1 + k*(k+1)//2 < n:
        k += 1
    if 1 + k*(k+1)//2 != n:              # length does not match a triangular number
        return False

    target = arr[0]                      # value all groups must equal
    start = 1
    size  = 2

    while start < n:
        group_sum = sum(arr[start : start+size])
        if group_sum != target:
            return False
        start += size
        size  += 1                       # next group is one element larger

    return True
```

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### Correctness Proof  

We prove that `is_madhav` returns `True` **iff** the input array `arr`
is a Madhav array.

---

##### Lemma 1  
If `is_madhav` returns `True`, the length of `arr` equals  
`1 + k·(k+1)/2` for some integer `k ≥ 1`.

**Proof.**  
The algorithm searches the smallest `k` with `1 + k·(k+1)/2 ≥ n`.
If equality does not hold it returns `False`.  
When it finally returns `True` the equality must hold, therefore the
length has the required form. ∎



##### Lemma 2  
During the loop (`while start < n`) the variable `size` always equals
the size of the next group that must be checked in a Madhav array.

**Proof.**  
Initially `size = 2`, the size of the first required group.
After each iteration we increase `start` by the current `size`
(the whole group has been processed) and then increment `size` by `1`.
Thus `size` follows the sequence `2,3,4,…`, exactly the required group
sizes. ∎



##### Lemma 3  
If the algorithm finishes the loop without returning `False`,
every group defined by the Madhav rule has sum equal to `arr[0]`.

**Proof.**  
Inside the loop the algorithm computes `group_sum` of the slice
`arr[start : start+size]`. By Lemma&nbsp;2 this slice is precisely the
next required group. The algorithm compares `group_sum` with `target =
arr[0]`. If any group differed, it would return `False` immediately.
Since it never does, all examined groups have sum `arr[0]`. ∎



##### Theorem  
`is_madhav(arr)` returns `True` **iff** `arr` is a Madhav array.

**Proof.**

*(*If*)*  
Assume `is_madhav(arr)` returns `True`.  
From Lemma&nbsp;1 the length of `arr` matches the pattern
`1 + 2 + … + k`.  
From Lemma&nbsp;3 every group of sizes `2,3,…,k` has sum `arr[0]`.  
These are exactly the conditions of a Madhav array, therefore `arr`
is Madhav.

*(*Only‑if*)*  
Assume `arr` is a Madhav array.  
By definition its length is `1 + 2 + … + k` for some `k ≥ 1`,
so the search for `k` succeeds and the algorithm does **not** return
`False` at the length test.  
All required groups have sum `arr[0]`; consequently each iteration of
the loop finds `group_sum == target` and never returns `False`.  
The loop terminates after processing the last group, and the function
returns `True`. ∎



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### Complexity Analysis  

Let `n = len(arr)`.  
The loop visits each element exactly once (each belongs to exactly one
group).  
*Time*: `O(n)`  
*Memory*: `O(1)` – only a few integer variables are used.



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### Reference Implementation  (Python 3)

```python
import sys

def is_madhav(arr):
    """return True if arr is a Madhav array, else False"""
    n = len(arr)
    if n < 2:                     # 0 or 1 element cannot be Madhav
        return False

    # ---- find k such that 1 + k*(k+1)/2 == n ----
    k = 1
    while 1 + k * (k + 1) // 2 < n:
        k += 1
    if 1 + k * (k + 1) // 2 != n:
        return False

    target = arr[0]
    start = 1
    size = 2

    while start < n:
        if sum(arr[start:start + size]) != target:
            return False
        start += size
        size += 1

    return True


def solve() -> None:
    data = list(map(int, sys.stdin.read().strip().split()))
    if not data:
        return

    # Input may be either:  n  a1 a2 ... an   OR   just the array elements
    if len(data) >= 2 and data[0] == len(data) - 1:
        n = data[0]
        arr = data[1:1 + n]
    else:
        arr = data

    result = is_madhav(arr)
    # required output format: lower‑case true / false
    print(str(result).lower())


if __name__ == "__main__":
    solve()
```

The program follows exactly the algorithm proven correct above and
conforms to the required input‑output conventions.

**Solution Explanation**

A Russian bus ticket has exactly six digits.  
It is *lucky* when the sum of the first three digits equals the sum of the last three digits.

The task is to read a ticket number (as a string), decide whether it is lucky and output  
`true` / `false`.

---

#### Algorithm
1. Read the ticket string `s`.
2. Convert each character to an integer.
3. Compute  
   `sum_first = int(s[0]) + int(s[1]) + int(s[2])`  
   `sum_last  = int(s[3]) + int(s[4]) + int(s[5])`
4. The ticket is lucky iff `sum_first == sum_last`.
5. Print `"true"` if lucky, otherwise `"false"`.

The algorithm works in **O(1)** time (constant, only six characters) and **O(1)** extra space.

---

#### Correctness Proof  

We prove that the algorithm returns `true` exactly for lucky tickets.

*Let* `s` be the input string of length 6.

- `sum_first` is defined as the sum of the integer values of `s[0]`, `s[1]`, `s[2]`.  
  By construction this equals the sum of the first three digits of the ticket.
- `sum_last` is defined analogously for `s[3]`, `s[4]`, `s[5]`, i.e. the sum of the last three digits.

The algorithm returns `true` iff `sum_first == sum_last`.  
By the definition of a lucky ticket, a ticket is lucky exactly when the sum of its first three digits equals the sum of its last three digits. Therefore the condition tested by the algorithm is precisely the definition of luckiness.

Hence, the algorithm outputs `true` **iff** the ticket is lucky, proving its correctness. ∎

---

#### Reference Implementation (Python 3)

```python
def is_lucky(ticket: str) -> bool:
    """
    Returns True if the ticket is lucky, i.e. the sum of the first three digits
    equals the sum of the last three digits.
    """
    # Convert each character to an integer and sum the required parts
    sum_first = int(ticket[0]) + int(ticket[1]) + int(ticket[2])
    sum_last = int(ticket[3]) + int(ticket[4]) + int(ticket[5])
    return sum_first == sum_last


# ----- main program -----
if __name__ == "__main__":
    # read the ticket string from standard input, remove possible whitespace
    ticket_input = input().strip()
    result = is_lucky(ticket_input)
    # output must be lower‑case as required by the statement
    print("true" if result else "false")
```

**Solution Explanation**

For every element of the input array we have to check whether the element is a
multiple of its own index.

* The index `0` is ignored – division by zero is undefined.
* For an index `i ≥ 1` the element `a[i]` belongs to the answer iff  

  `a[i] % i == 0` (the remainder of the division is zero).

The required list is built by scanning the array once, therefore the algorithm
runs in `O(n)` time and uses `O(k)` extra space (`k` = size of the result).

---

#### Algorithm
```
read the line that contains the array, e.g. "[22, -6, 32, 82, 9, 25]"
parse it into a Python list `arr`

result = empty list
for i from 1 to len(arr)-1:
        if arr[i] % i == 0:
                append arr[i] to result

print result
```

---

#### Correctness Proof  

We prove that the algorithm returns exactly the elements that are multiples of
their own index.

*Lemma 1*  
For any index `i ≥ 1` the condition `arr[i] % i == 0` holds **iff**
`arr[i]` is a multiple of `i`.

*Proof.* By definition of the modulo operator, `arr[i] % i == 0` means that
there exists an integer `q` such that `arr[i] = q * i`. This is precisely the
definition of “multiple of `i`”. ∎

*Lemma 2*  
During the loop the algorithm appends an element `arr[i]` to `result`
**iff** `arr[i]` is a multiple of its index `i`.

*Proof.* The loop iterates over every `i` from `1` to `len(arr)-1`.  
Inside the loop the element is appended exactly when the `if` condition
`arr[i] % i == 0` is true. By Lemma&nbsp;1 this condition is true exactly when
`arr[i]` is a multiple of `i`. ∎

*Theorem*  
After termination `result` contains **all and only** the elements of the input
array whose value is a multiple of their own index.

*Proof.*  
- *All*: For any index `i ≥ 1` such that `arr[i]` is a multiple of `i`,
  Lemma&nbsp;2 tells us that the algorithm appends `arr[i]` to `result`. Hence
  every qualifying element appears in `result`.
- *Only*: If an element appears in `result`, it was appended by the loop.
  By Lemma&nbsp;2 this can happen only when the element is a multiple of its
  index. Hence no non‑qualifying element can be in `result`.

Index `0` is never considered, matching the problem statement. Therefore the
final `result` is exactly the desired array. ∎

---

#### Complexity Analysis  

The loop examines each of the `n` elements once, performing only constant‑time
operations.  

- **Time:** `O(n)`  
- **Additional Space:** `O(k)` where `k` is the number of selected elements
  (the output list). No other significant memory is used.

---

#### Reference Implementation (Python 3)

```python
import sys
import ast

def multiples_of_index(arr):
    """Return a list of elements that are multiples of their own index."""
    res = []
    # start from 1 because division by zero is not defined
    for i in range(1, len(arr)):
        if arr[i] % i == 0:
            res.append(arr[i])
    return res

def main():
    # read the whole line, strip trailing newline/spaces
    line = sys.stdin.readline().strip()
    if not line:
        print([])          # empty input -> empty output
        return

    # safely evaluate the list literal, e.g. "[22, -6, 32]"
    try:
        arr = ast.literal_eval(line)
    except Exception:
        # if parsing fails, treat as empty list
        arr = []

    # ensure we really got a list
    if not isinstance(arr, list):
        arr = []

    result = multiples_of_index(arr)
    print(result)

if __name__ == "__main__":
    main()
```

The program follows exactly the algorithm proven correct above and conforms
to the required input‑output format.
completed 27/150, Solved: False, number of success = 9/27, acc = 33.33
**Solution Explanation**

For a given string `s` we have to compute the value described in the statement.

| Symbol | Meaning |
|--------|---------|
| `a`    | sum of the ASCII codes of all characters of `s` |
| `b`    | sum of the differences between consecutive characters  <br>`b = Σ (ord(s[i]) – ord(s[i‑1]))` for `i = 1 … len(s)-1` |
| `c`    | `(a | b) & ((~a) << 2)`  (bitwise **OR**, **AND**, **NOT**, left‑shift) |
| `d`    | `c ^ (32 * (spaces + 1))`  (bitwise **XOR**) where `spaces` is the number of `' '` characters in `s` |
| result | `d` |

All operations are performed with Python’s integer type, which has unlimited precision, so overflow is not a problem.

The algorithm is implemented directly, following the definition step‑by‑step.

---

#### Algorithm
```
hash(s):
    a = sum(ord(ch) for ch in s)

    b = 0
    for i from 1 to len(s)-1:
        b += ord(s[i]) - ord(s[i-1])

    c = (a | b) & ((~a) << 2)

    spaces = s.count(' ')
    d = c ^ (32 * (spaces + 1))

    return d
```

---

#### Correctness Proof  

We prove that the algorithm returns exactly the value defined in the problem.

*Lemma 1*  
`a` computed by the algorithm equals the sum of ASCII values of all characters of `s`.

*Proof.*  
The generator `ord(ch) for ch in s` iterates over every character of `s` once and `sum` adds the results. ∎

*Lemma 2*  
`b` computed by the algorithm equals the sum of the differences between each consecutive pair of characters.

*Proof.*  
The loop runs for every index `i` with `1 ≤ i < len(s)`. In each iteration it adds `ord(s[i]) - ord(s[i-1])`. This is precisely the required difference, and the loop accumulates all of them. If `len(s) ≤ 1` the loop body never executes, yielding `b = 0`, which matches the definition. ∎

*Lemma 3*  
`c` computed by the algorithm equals `(a OR b) AND ((NOT a) shifted left by 2)`.

*Proof.*  
Python’s operators `|`, `&`, `~`, and `<<` are the bitwise OR, AND, NOT, and left‑shift respectively. The expression `(a | b) & ((~a) << 2)` therefore implements exactly the formula given. ∎

*Lemma 4*  
`d` computed by the algorithm equals `c XOR (32 * (total_number_of_spaces + 1))`.

*Proof.*  
`spaces = s.count(' ')` counts the occurrences of the space character, i.e. `total_number_of_spaces`. The term `32 * (spaces + 1)` is built exactly as required, and the `^` operator is Python’s bitwise XOR. ∎

*Theorem*  
`hash(s)` returned by the algorithm is the hash value defined in the problem statement.

*Proof.*  
By Lemma 1, Lemma 2, Lemma 3 and Lemma 4 each intermediate variable (`a`, `b`, `c`, `d`) matches the specification. The function finally returns `d`, which is the required hash value. ∎

---

#### Complexity Analysis  

Let `n = len(s)`.

* Computing `a` – O(n) time.  
* Computing `b` – O(n) time (single pass over consecutive pairs).  
* Counting spaces – O(n) time.  

All other operations are O(1).  
Total time complexity: **O(n)**.  
The algorithm stores only a few integer variables, so the memory consumption is **O(1)**.

---

#### Reference Implementation (Python 3)

```python
import sys

def compute_hash(s: str) -> int:
    # a : sum of ASCII codes
    a = sum(ord(ch) for ch in s)

    # b : sum of differences between consecutive characters
    b = 0
    for i in range(1, len(s)):
        b += ord(s[i]) - ord(s[i - 1])

    # c : (a | b) & ((~a) << 2)
    c = (a | b) & ((~a) << 2)

    # number of spaces in the original string
    spaces = s.count(' ')

    # d : c ^ (32 * (spaces + 1))
    d = c ^ (32 * (spaces + 1))

    return d

def main() -> None:
    # read the whole line (it may contain spaces)
    s = sys.stdin.readline()
    # remove the trailing newline but keep any other spaces
    s = s.rstrip('\n')
    result = compute_hash(s)
    print(result)

if __name__ == "__main__":
    main()
```

The program follows exactly the algorithm proven correct above and conforms to the required input‑output format.
completed 28/150, Solved: False, number of success = 9/28, acc = 32.14
**Solution Explanation**

For the given input we have to decide whether it is

* an HTML colour name, or
* a six‑digit hexadecimal colour code (always beginning with `#`).

If it is a *name* we replace only the seven “yellow” names that are listed in the
statement, otherwise we return the original string unchanged.

If it is a *hex code* we look at the three colour components  

`R` – the first two hex digits,  
`G` – the next two,  
`B` – the last two.

The code is considered *yellow* when **both** `R` and `G` are **strictly larger**
than `B`.  
For such a code we reorder the three component values:

```
smallest  → R
largest   → G
middle    → B
```

The new three two‑digit values are written back as a hex string.
The case of the letters (`a‑f` or `A‑F`) must be the same as in the input,
so we output the whole string in lower case if the original contained only
lower‑case letters, otherwise in upper case (the usual way hex colours are
written).

The algorithm works in *O(1)* time and memory.



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### Algorithm
```
read line, strip whitespace → s

if s starts with '#' and length is 7 and all following chars are hex digits:
        # ----- HEX CODE -----
        R = int(s[1:3], 16)
        G = int(s[3:5], 16)
        B = int(s[5:7], 16)

        if R > B and G > B:                     # "yellow"
                values = [R, G, B]
                values.sort()                  # [small, middle, large]
                newR, newB, newG = values[0], values[1], values[2]

                # decide output case
                if any(c.isupper() for c in s[1:]):
                        fmt = "{:02X}"        # upper case
                else:
                        fmt = "{:02x}"        # lower case

                result = "#" + fmt.format(newR) + fmt.format(newG) + fmt.format(newB)
        else:
                result = s                       # unchanged
else:
        # ----- HTML COLOUR NAME -----
        mapping = {
            "gold"               : "ForestGreen",
            "khaki"              : "LimeGreen",
            "lemonchiffon"       : "PaleGreen",
            "lightgoldenrodyellow": "SpringGreen",
            "lightyellow"        : "MintCream",
            "palegoldenrod"      : "LightGreen",
            "yellow"             : "Lime"
        }

        low = s.lower()
        if low in mapping:
                result = mapping[low]
        else:
                result = s                       # unchanged

print result
```

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### Correctness Proof  

We prove that the algorithm prints exactly the required output.

---

##### Lemma 1  
If the input string `s` is a valid six‑digit hex colour code, the algorithm
recognises it as such.

**Proof.**  
The algorithm checks three conditions:

1. `s[0] == '#'`
2. `len(s) == 7`
3. every character `s[1:]` is a hexadecimal digit (`0‑9`, `a‑f`, `A‑F`).

These are precisely the definition of a six‑digit hex colour code, therefore
the test succeeds **iff** `s` is such a code. ∎



##### Lemma 2  
For a recognised hex colour code the algorithm returns the original code
iff the colour is *not* yellow (i.e. `R ≤ B` or `G ≤ B`).

**Proof.**  
The algorithm computes the integer values `R, G, B`.  
If the condition `R > B and G > B` is false, the `else` branch is taken and
`result = s`.  
Thus the output equals the input exactly when the colour is not yellow. ∎



##### Lemma 3  
For a recognised *yellow* hex colour code the algorithm returns a code whose
components are a permutation of the original components with the smallest
value in the red position, the largest in the green position and the middle
in the blue position.

**Proof.**  
When `R > B` and `G > B` hold, the algorithm builds the list
`values = [R, G, B]` and sorts it, obtaining
`values[0] ≤ values[1] ≤ values[2]`.  
It then assigns  

* `newR = values[0]` (smallest)  
* `newG = values[2]` (largest)  
newB = values[1]` (middle)

and formats them back to two‑digit hex numbers, preserving the case of the
original string.  
Therefore the output hex code contains exactly the same three component
values, reordered as required. ∎



##### Lemma 4  
If the input string `s` is an HTML colour name, the algorithm outputs the
mapped green name **iff** `s` (case‑insensitively) equals one of the seven
yellow names listed in the statement; otherwise it outputs `s` unchanged.

**Proof.**  
The algorithm lower‑cases `s` to `low` and looks it up in the dictionary
`mapping` that contains exactly the seven required keys (the lower‑cased
yellow names) with the prescribed green names as values.
* If `low` is a key, `result = mapping[low]`, i.e. the required replacement.
* If `low` is not a key, the `else` branch returns the original `s`.

Thus the behaviour matches the specification. ∎



##### Theorem  
For every possible input the algorithm prints exactly the colour that the
problem statement requires.

**Proof.**  
Two exhaustive cases:

1. **Input is a hex colour code** – by Lemma&nbsp;1 the algorithm treats it as
   a hex code.  
   *If it is not yellow* – Lemma&nbsp;2 shows the original code is printed.  
   *If it is yellow* – Lemma&nbsp;3 shows the correctly reordered green/blue‑green
   code is printed.

2. **Input is not a hex code** – therefore it is an HTML colour name.
   By Lemma&nbsp;4 the algorithm either replaces it with the required green name
   (when it is one of the seven yellow names) or leaves it unchanged.

All possibilities are covered, so the algorithm is correct. ∎



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### Complexity Analysis  

All operations are simple arithmetic, dictionary look‑ups and a sort of three
numbers.

*Time* – `O(1)` (constant, independent of input size)  
*Memory* – `O(1)` (only a few integer variables and a constant‑size dictionary)

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### Reference Implementation (Python 3)

```python
import sys

def is_hex_color(s: str) -> bool:
    """Return True if s is a 6‑digit hex colour code like '#1A2b3C'."""
    if len(s) != 7 or s[0] != '#':
        return False
    for ch in s[1:]:
        if ch not in "0123456789abcdefABCDEF":
            return False
    return True

def solve() -> None:
    s = sys.stdin.readline().strip()

    # ----- case 1: hex colour code -----
    if is_hex_color(s):
        # parse components
        r = int(s[1:3], 16)
        g = int(s[3:5], 16)
        b = int(s[5:7], 16)

        if r > b and g > b:                     # "yellow"
            vals = [r, g, b]
            vals.sort()                         # [small, middle, large]
            new_r, new_b, new_g = vals[0], vals[1], vals[2]

            # decide output case: upper if any letter in original is upper
            if any(ch.isupper() for ch in s[1:]):
                fmt = "{:02X}"
            else:
                fmt = "{:02x}"

            result = "#" + fmt.format(new_r) + fmt.format(new_g) + fmt.format(new_b)
        else:
            result = s

    # ----- case 2: HTML colour name -----
    else:
        mapping = {
            "gold": "ForestGreen",
            "khaki": "LimeGreen",
            "lemonchiffon": "PaleGreen",
            "lightgoldenrodyellow": "SpringGreen",
            "lightyellow": "MintCream",
            "palegoldenrod": "LightGreen",
            "yellow": "Lime"
        }

        low = s.lower()
        result = mapping.get(low, s)

    print(result)


if __name__ == "__main__":
    solve()
```

The program follows exactly the algorithm proven correct above and conforms

completed 29/150, Solved: True, number of success = 10/29, acc = 34.48
**Solution Explanation**

A rectangle is completely determined by  

* two different horizontal lines (its top and bottom) and  
* two different vertical lines (its left and right).

If we have `n` horizontal lines we can choose the top and bottom in  

\[
\binom{n}{2}= \frac{n\,(n-1)}{2}
\]

ways.  
If we have `m` vertical lines we can choose the left and right in  

\[
\binom{m}{2}= \frac{m\,(m-1)}{2}
\]

ways.

Both choices are independent, therefore the total number of rectangles is  

\[
\text{rectangles}= \binom{n}{2}\times\binom{m}{2}
                = \frac{n\,(n-1)}{2}\times\frac{m\,(m-1)}{2}.
\]

If `n < 2` or `m < 2` the binomial coefficient is zero, so the answer is `0`.

The formula works for all `0 ≤ n,m ≤ 100` and needs only O(1) time and O(1) memory.

---

#### Algorithm
```
read n, m
if n < 2 or m < 2:
    answer = 0
else:
    answer = (n * (n-1) // 2) * (m * (m-1) // 2)
print answer
```

---

#### Correctness Proof  

We prove that the algorithm returns the exact number of rectangles.

*Lemma 1*  
Choosing any two distinct horizontal lines determines the top and bottom sides of a rectangle, and there are exactly `C(n,2)` such unordered pairs.

*Proof.* There are `n` horizontal lines. An unordered pair of distinct lines can be chosen in `C(n,2)` ways, and each pair uniquely defines the top and bottom of a rectangle. ∎

*Lemma 2*  
Choosing any two distinct vertical lines determines the left and right sides of a rectangle, and there are exactly `C(m,2)` such unordered pairs.

*Proof.* Analogous to Lemma&nbsp;1. ∎

*Lemma 3*  
For any fixed pair of horizontal lines and any fixed pair of vertical lines there is exactly one rectangle bounded by them.

*Proof.* The four lines intersect at four points forming a single axis‑aligned rectangle; no other rectangle can be formed with the same four lines. ∎

*Theorem*  
The algorithm outputs the total number of axis‑aligned rectangles formed by `n` horizontal and `m` vertical lines.

*Proof.*  
- If `n < 2` or `m < 2` no pair of distinct horizontal (or vertical) lines exists, so by Lemma 1 or Lemma 2 the number of rectangles is zero; the algorithm returns `0`.
- Otherwise, by Lemma 1 there are `C(n,2)` possible horizontal pairs, by Lemma 2 there are `C(m,2)` possible vertical pairs. By Lemma 3 each combination of a horizontal pair with a vertical pair yields a unique rectangle, and every rectangle arises from exactly one such combination. Hence the total number of rectangles equals `C(n,2)·C(m,2)`, which the algorithm computes.

Thus the algorithm is correct. ∎

---

#### Complexity Analysis  

The algorithm performs a constant amount of arithmetic operations.

- **Time:** `O(1)`
- **Memory:** `O(1)`

---

#### Reference Implementation (Python 3)

```python
import sys

def count_rectangles(n: int, m: int) -> int:
    """Return the number of rectangles formed by n horizontal and m vertical lines."""
    if n < 2 or m < 2:
        return 0
    horiz_pairs = n * (n - 1) // 2   # C(n,2)
    vert_pairs = m * (m - 1) // 2    # C(m,2)
    return horiz_pairs * vert_pairs

def solve() -> None:
    data = sys.stdin.read().strip().split()
    if not data:
        return
    # The problem statement shows each number on its own line,
    # but we accept any whitespace separation.
    n = int(data[0])
    m = int(data[1]) if len(data) > 1 else 0
    result = count_rectangles(n, m)
    print(result)

if __name__ == "__main__":
    solve()
```
