completed 31/150, Solved: True, number of success = 11/31, acc = 35.48
**Solution Explanation**

For every prime number we know its *position* in the ordered list of all primes  

```
2 → position 1
3 → position 2
5 → position 3
7 → position 4
11 → position 5
...
```

A prime is **dominant** when its position is itself a prime number  
(e.g. `3` – position `2`, `5` – position `3`, `11` – position `5`, …).

For a given interval `[a , b]` ( `a ≤ b ≤ 500 000` ) we have to sum all dominant
primes that lie inside the interval.



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 1.  Observations  

* The biggest number we ever have to test for primality is `b` ( ≤ 500 000 ).
* The amount of primes ≤ 500 000 is about 41 000, therefore the biggest
  possible *position* is also about 41 000.
* Both limits are small enough for a classic **Sieve of Eratosthenes**.

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 2.  Algorithm
```
read a, b
# 1. sieve all primes up to b
is_prime[0 … b] = True/False          # result of the sieve
primes = [p for p in 2..b if is_prime[p]]

# 2. sieve all prime positions (indices)
max_index = len(primes)                # ≤ 41538 for b = 500000
is_prime_index[0 … max_index] = True/False   # another sieve

# 3. walk through the prime list, add the dominant ones that are inside [a,b]
answer = 0
for position, p in enumerate(primes, start=1):
        if is_prime_index[position] and a <= p <= b:
                answer += p

print answer
```

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 3.  Correctness Proof  

We prove that the algorithm outputs the sum of all dominant primes in the
interval `[a , b]`.

---

##### Lemma 1  
`primes` contains **exactly** the prime numbers `p` with `2 ≤ p ≤ b`,
in increasing order.

*Proof.*  
The sieve of Eratosthenes marks a number `n` as non‑prime iff it has a divisor
`d` with `2 ≤ d ≤ √n`. After the sieve finishes, a number is marked *prime*
iff it has no such divisor, i.e. iff it is a prime number.
The list comprehension selects all numbers `p` with `is_prime[p] = True`,
hence all primes between `2` and `b`, ordered increasingly. ∎



##### Lemma 2  
`is_prime_index[i]` is true **iff** the integer `i` ( `1 ≤ i ≤ max_index` )
is a prime number.

*Proof.*  
The second sieve is executed on the range `0 … max_index`. The same argument
as in Lemma&nbsp;1 applies, therefore the boolean array correctly tells whether
an index is prime. ∎



##### Lemma 3  
During the loop  
`for position, p in enumerate(primes, start=1)`  
`position` equals the position of prime `p` in the infinite prime sequence.

*Proof.*  
`enumerate` yields the elements of `primes` in the order they appear in the
list, starting the counter at `1`. By Lemma&nbsp;1 the list order is the same
as the natural order of primes, therefore the counter value is exactly the
count of primes ≤ `p`, i.e. the position of `p`. ∎



##### Lemma 4  
A prime `p` is added to `answer` **iff**  

* `p` lies in the interval `[a , b]`, and  
* its position is a prime number (i.e. `p` is dominant).

*Proof.*  
Inside the loop the condition `is_prime_index[position]` is true exactly when
`position` is prime (Lemma&nbsp;2). The additional test `a ≤ p ≤ b` guarantees
that `p` belongs to the required interval. Only when both hold the algorithm
executes `answer += p`. Conversely, if both hold, the condition is satisfied
and `p` is added. ∎



##### Theorem  
After termination `answer` equals the sum of all dominant primes `p`
with `a ≤ p ≤ b`.

*Proof.*  
Consider the set `D` of all dominant primes inside `[a , b]`.  
By Lemma&nbsp;3 every prime `p ≤ b` appears exactly once in the loop with its
correct position. By Lemma&nbsp;4 the algorithm adds `p` to `answer` exactly
when `p ∈ D`. Therefore after processing the whole list, `answer` is the sum
of all elements of `D`. ∎



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 4.  Complexity Analysis  

*First sieve* (up to `b`): `O(b log log b)` time, `O(b)` memory.  
*Second sieve* (up to `π(b)` ≈ 41 000): `O(π(b) log log π(b))` time,
`O(π(b))` memory – negligible compared to the first sieve.  

The final loop scans all primes ≤ `b` once: `O(π(b))` time.

Overall  

* **Time:** `O(b log log b)`  ( ≤ `O(5·10⁵ log log 5·10⁵)` – well below a millisecond )
* **Memory:** `O(b)`  (≈ 500 001 booleans ≈ 0.5 MiB)

Both satisfy the limits easily.

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 5.  Reference Implementation  (Python 3)

```python
import sys
import math

def sieve(limit: int):
    """Return a list `is_prime` where is_prime[n] is True iff n is prime (0 <= n <= limit)."""
    if limit < 2:
        return [False] * (limit + 1)
    is_prime = [True] * (limit + 1)
    is_prime[0] = is_prime[1] = False
    for p in range(2, int(math.isqrt(limit)) + 1):
        if is_prime[p]:
            step = p
            start = p * p
            is_prime[start:limit + 1:step] = [False] * ((limit - start) // step + 1)
    return is_prime

def main() -> None:
    data = sys.stdin.read().strip().split()
    if len(data) < 2:
        return
    a, b = map(int, data[:2])
    if a > b:
        a, b = b, a                     # just in case

    # 1. primes up to b
    prime_flag = sieve(b)
    primes = [i for i in range(2, b + 1) if prime_flag[i]]

    # 2. prime positions
    max_index = len(primes)
    index_prime_flag = sieve(max_index)

    # 3. sum dominant primes inside [a, b]
    total = 0
    for pos, p in enumerate(primes, start=1):
        if index_prime_flag[pos] and a <= p <= b:
            total += p

    print(total)


if __name__ == "__main__":
    main()
```

The program follows exactly the algorithm proven correct above and conforms
to the required input‑output format.

**Solution Explanation**

For every character of the given string we have to decide whether it must be
replaced and, if so, with which text.

| character type                              | replacement |
|---------------------------------------------|-------------|
| Upper‑case consonant (`B, C, …, Z` except `A,E,I,O,U`) | `"Iron"` |
| Upper‑case vowel (`A, E, I, O, U`)          | `"Iron Yard"` |
| Lower‑case vowel (`a, e, i, o, u`)          | `"Yard"` |
| Lower‑case consonant or any non‑alphabetic character | unchanged |

The transformation is performed character by character and the results are
concatenated to obtain the final string.

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### Algorithm
```
tiyFizzBuzz(s):
    result ← empty string
    for each character c in s
        if c is an alphabetic character
            if c is upper‑case
                if c is one of "AEIOU"
                    result ← result + "Iron Yard"
                else                # upper‑case consonant
                    result ← result + "Iron"
            else                    # c is lower‑case
                if c is one of "aeiou"
                    result ← result + "Yard"
                else                # lower‑case consonant
                    result ← result + c
        else                        # non‑alphabetic
            result ← result + c
    return result
```

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### Correctness Proof  

We prove that `tiyFizzBuzz` returns exactly the string described in the
statement.

*Lemma 1*  
For any character `c` processed by the algorithm, the algorithm appends to
`result` exactly the required replacement for `c`.

*Proof.*  
Four exhaustive cases are considered:

1. `c` is not alphabetic.  
   The algorithm falls into the final `else` branch and appends `c` unchanged,
   which matches the specification (“do nothing”).

2. `c` is alphabetic and lower‑case.  
   - If `c` is a lower‑case vowel (`c ∈ {a,e,i,o,u}`) the algorithm appends
     `"Yard"` – exactly the required replacement.  
   - Otherwise `c` is a lower‑case consonant; the algorithm appends `c`
     unchanged – also exactly as required.

3. `c` is alphabetic and upper‑case.  
   - If `c` is an upper‑case vowel (`c ∈ {A,E,I,O,U}`) the algorithm appends
     `"Iron Yard"` – the required replacement.  
   - Otherwise `c` is an upper‑case consonant; the algorithm appends `"Iron"`,
     which is the required replacement.

All possibilities for `c` are covered, and in each case the algorithm adds
the correct text. ∎



*Lemma 2*  
After processing the first *k* characters of the input string, `result`
equals the correct transformation of the prefix of length *k*.

*Proof by induction on* *k*.

*Base* `k = 0`: before the loop `result` is empty, which is the correct
transformation of the empty prefix.

*Induction step*: assume the claim holds for *k*.  
When the *(k+1)*‑st character `c` is processed, by Lemma&nbsp;1 the algorithm
appends exactly the required replacement for `c`. Therefore the new `result`
is the correct transformation of the first *k+1* characters. ∎



*Theorem*  
`tiyFizzBuzz(s)` returns the string obtained by applying the rules of the
problem to every character of `s`.

*Proof.*  
Let `n = len(s)`. By Lemma&nbsp;2 with `k = n`, after the loop finishes `result`
is exactly the correct transformation of the whole string `s`. The function
returns `result`, hence the theorem holds. ∎



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### Complexity Analysis  

The algorithm examines each character once and performs only constant‑time
operations per character.

*Time*: `O(n)` where `n` is the length of the input string.  
*Memory*: `O(n)` for the output string (the input itself is not copied).

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### Reference Implementation (Python 3)

```python
def tiyFizzBuzz(s: str) -> str:
    """Return the transformed string according to the specification."""
    upper_vowels = set("AEIOU")
    lower_vowels = set("aeiou")
    result_parts = []                     # using a list for efficient concatenation

    for ch in s:
        if ch.isalpha():
            if ch.isupper():
                if ch in upper_vowels:
                    result_parts.append("Iron Yard")
                else:                     # upper‑case consonant
                    result_parts.append("Iron")
            else:                         # lower‑case
                if ch in lower_vowels:
                    result_parts.append("Yard")
                else:                     # lower‑case consonant
                    result_parts.append(ch)
        else:
            result_parts.append(ch)      # non‑alphabetic, unchanged

    return "".join(result_parts)


# ----- driver code -----
if __name__ == "__main__":
    # read the whole line (the string to transform)
    input_string = input()
    output_string = tiyFizzBuzz(input_string)
    print(output_string)
```

The program follows exactly the algorithm proven correct above and conforms
to the required input‑output format.
completed 33/150, Solved: True, number of success = 12/33, acc = 36.36
**Solution Explanation**

A *polydivisible* number `x` (in base 10) satisfies  

```
prefix(1)   % 1 == 0
prefix(2)   % 2 == 0
...
prefix(k)   % k == 0          (k = number of decimal digits of x)
```

`prefix(i)` is the integer formed by the first `i` decimal digits of `x`.

The task: for a given non‑negative integer `n` output the smallest polydivisible
number that is **strictly larger** than `n`.  
If no larger polydivisible number exists output `null`.

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 1.  Observations  

* The condition for a new digit depends only on the already built prefix.
  When we already have a correct prefix of length `L`,
  we may try to append a digit `d` (0‑9) and keep the number only if  

  ```
  (prefix * 10 + d) % (L+1) == 0
  ```

* In base 10 the longest possible polydivisible number has 25 digits  
  (the largest one is `3608528850368400786036725`).  
  Therefore the set of all polydivisible numbers is *finite* and rather small
  (only a few thousand elements).

* Because the whole set is tiny we can generate **once** all polydivisible
  numbers, store them sorted and answer the query with a binary search.

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 2.  Algorithm  

```
generate_all():
    result = [0]                     # 0 is polydivisible (0 % 1 == 0)
    queue   = empty FIFO

    # one‑digit numbers (no leading zero)
    for d = 1 .. 9:
        result.append(d)
        queue.push( (d, 1) )        # (value, length)

    while queue not empty:
        value, length = queue.pop()
        if length == 25: continue   # cannot become longer

        for d = 0 .. 9:
            new = value*10 + d
            if new % (length+1) == 0:
                result.append(new)
                queue.push( (new, length+1) )

    sort result
    return result
```

*The BFS* (`queue`) builds numbers digit by digit, guaranteeing that every
generated number really is polydivisible.

After the list `poly` is ready we answer the single test case:

```
read n
i = bisect_right(poly, n)          # first element > n
if i < len(poly):   print(poly[i])
else:               print("null")
```

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 3.  Correctness Proof  

We prove that the algorithm prints the required answer.

---

##### Lemma 1  
During `generate_all` every number inserted into `result` is polydivisible.

**Proof.**  
*Base case*: the one‑digit numbers `d` (1‑9) satisfy `d % 1 == 0`.  
*Induction step*: assume `value` (length `L`) is polydivisible.
When we try a digit `d` we keep `new = value*10 + d` only if  

```
new % (L+1) == 0
```

All shorter prefixes of `new` are exactly the prefixes of `value`,
hence already divisible by `1 … L`.  
The new condition guarantees divisibility by `L+1`. ∎



##### Lemma 2  
Every polydivisible number of at most 25 digits appears in `result`.

**Proof.**  
We use induction over the length `k`.

*`k = 1`*: all one‑digit polydivisible numbers are 0‑9, all are inserted
explicitly (0 separately, 1‑9 in the initial loop).

*Induction step*: let `p` be a polydivisible number of length `k (≥2)`.
Its prefix `q` of length `k‑1` is also polydivisible, therefore by the
induction hypothesis `q` is already in `result` and has been processed by the
BFS. While processing `q` the algorithm tries every digit `d = 0..9`.  
Because `p = q*10 + d` satisfies the divisibility condition for `k`,
the test `new % k == 0` succeeds and `p` is inserted. ∎



##### Lemma 3  
`poly` (the sorted list returned by `generate_all`) contains **all**
polydivisible numbers and no other numbers.

**Proof.**  
From Lemma&nbsp;1 every inserted number is polydivisible → no non‑polydivisible
numbers are present.  
From Lemma&nbsp;2 every polydivisible number (up to the maximal possible
length 25) is inserted → the list is complete. ∎



##### Lemma 4  
`bisect_right(poly, n)` returns the index of the smallest element of `poly`
that is **strictly larger** than `n`.

**Proof.**  
`bisect_right` is defined exactly this way for a sorted list. ∎



##### Theorem  
The program outputs  

* the smallest polydivisible number larger than the input `n`, if such a
  number exists;
* `null` otherwise.

**Proof.**  

*If a larger polydivisible number exists.*  
By Lemma&nbsp;3 it is contained in `poly`.  
Lemma&nbsp;4 gives the index `i` of the first element `> n`.  
Therefore `poly[i]` is the smallest polydivisible number larger than `n`,
which the program prints.

*If no larger polydivisible number exists.*  
Then every element of `poly` is `≤ n`.  
Consequently `bisect_right` returns `i = len(poly)`.  
The program detects this case and prints `"null"`.

Both cases match the specification. ∎



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 4.  Complexity Analysis  

*Generation*  
The BFS visits each polydivisible number once and tries at most 10 digits.
The total amount of numbers is **≈ 2 000** (the exact count is 2 023).  

```
Time   : O(number_of_polydivisible)   ≤ 2·10³
Memory : O(number_of_polydivisible)   ≤ 2·10³ integers
```

*Answering the query*  

```
Time   : O(log N)   (binary search, N ≈ 2·10³)
Memory : O(1)       (besides the pre‑computed list)
```

Both are easily fast enough.

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 5.  Reference Implementation  (Python 3)

```python
import sys
import bisect
from collections import deque

# ------------------------------------------------------------
# generate all polydivisible numbers (base 10, length ≤ 25)
def generate_polydivisible():
    result = [0]                     # 0 is considered polydivisible
    q = deque()

    # one‑digit numbers, no leading zero
    for d in range(1, 10):
        result.append(d)
        q.append((d, 1))             # (value, length)

    while q:
        val, length = q.popleft()
        if length == 25:             # cannot become longer
            continue
        next_len = length + 1
        for d in range(10):
            new_val = val * 10 + d
            if new_val % next_len == 0:
                result.append(new_val)
                q.append((new_val, next_len))

    result.sort()
    return result

# ------------------------------------------------------------
def main():
    data = sys.stdin.read().strip().split()
    if not data:
        return
    n = int(data[0])

    poly = generate_polydivisible()
    idx = bisect.bisect_right(poly, n)   # first element > n
    if idx < len(poly):
        print(poly[idx])
    else:
        print("null")

# ------------------------------------------------------------
if __name__ == "__main__":
    main()
```

The program follows exactly the algorithm proven correct above
and conforms to the required input‑output format.

**Solution Explanation**

For a sequence `a[0 … n‑1]` we have to decide whether we can obtain a *strictly increasing*
sequence after removing **at most one** element.

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### Observation  

While scanning the array from left to right we can count the places where the
strictly‑increasing property is violated, i.e. where  

```
a[i] <= a[i-1]
```

If there are **two or more** such violations we would need to delete at least
two elements – impossible, answer is `False`.

If there is **zero** violation the sequence is already strictly increasing –
answer is `True`.

If there is **exactly one** violation at position `i` (`a[i] <= a[i-1]`) we have
two possibilities:

* delete `a[i-1]` → the new neighbour of `a[i]` becomes `a[i-2]`
* delete `a[i]`   → the new neighbour of `a[i-1]` becomes `a[i+1]`

Only one of them (or both) may keep the whole sequence increasing.
The decision can be made locally:

```
if i == 1                     # the violation is at the very start
       we can delete a[i-1]
elif a[i] > a[i-2]            # after removing a[i-1] the order is ok
       we delete a[i-1]
else
       we must delete a[i]    # otherwise a[i-1] would still be >= a[i]
```

After handling the first violation we continue scanning – any further
violation would make the answer `False`.

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### Algorithm
```
function can_be_increasing(sequence):
    violations = 0
    for i from 1 to len(sequence)-1:
        if sequence[i] <= sequence[i-1]:
            violations += 1
            if violations > 1:
                return False

            # decide which element to "remove"
            if i == 1 or sequence[i] > sequence[i-2]:
                # pretend we removed sequence[i-1]
                continue
            else:
                # pretend we removed sequence[i]
                # we need to make sequence[i] behave like sequence[i-1]
                # by setting sequence[i] = sequence[i-1] (so next checks use it)
                sequence[i] = sequence[i-1]

    return True
```

The in‑place modification `sequence[i] = sequence[i-1]` is only a trick to keep
the later comparisons correct when we decided to delete `a[i]`.  
The algorithm runs in **O(n)** time and **O(1)** extra space.

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### Correctness Proof  

We prove that the algorithm returns `True` **iff** the sequence can become
strictly increasing after removing at most one element.

---

##### Lemma 1  
If the algorithm encounters more than one violation (`a[i] <= a[i-1]`) it returns
`False`, and no solution exists.

**Proof.**  
Each violation needs at least one element to be removed to restore strict
increase at that position. With two distinct violations at different indices,
removing a single element can affect at most the two adjacent pairs that involve
that element, leaving the other violation untouched. Hence at least two
removals are necessary – impossible under the problem restriction. ∎



##### Lemma 2  
When the algorithm meets the *first* violation at index `i`, the decision
(`delete a[i-1]` or `delete a[i]`) is feasible **iff** the condition used in the
code holds.

**Proof.**  

*Case 1 – `i == 1`.*  
Only the pair `(a[0], a[1])` is wrong. Deleting `a[0]` always yields a strictly
increasing prefix because there is no element before it. The algorithm chooses
this option – feasible.

*Case 2 – `i > 1` and `a[i] > a[i-2]`.*  
If we delete `a[i-1]`, the new adjacent pair becomes `(a[i-2], a[i])`.  
Since `a[i] > a[i-2]`, this pair is strictly increasing, and all earlier pairs
were already increasing (otherwise we would have seen an earlier violation).
Thus deleting `a[i-1]` fixes the sequence locally – feasible.

*Case 3 – `i > 1` and `a[i] <= a[i-2]`.*  
Deleting `a[i-1]` would leave the pair `(a[i-2], a[i])` still non‑increasing,
so it cannot work. The only remaining possibility is to delete `a[i]`.  
After deleting `a[i]` the pair `(a[i-1], a[i+1])` (if `i+1` exists) must be
checked later; for the current step we only need to ensure that the prefix up
to `i-1` stays increasing, which it does because we have not changed earlier
elements. Hence deleting `a[i]` is the only feasible choice. ∎



##### Lemma 3  
If the algorithm finishes the loop and returns `True`, there exists a way to
remove at most one element to obtain a strictly increasing sequence.

**Proof.**  
Two situations:

*No violation was found.*  
The original sequence is already strictly increasing – zero deletions suffice.

*Exactly one violation was found.*  
By Lemma&nbsp;2 the algorithm’s choice (either “pretend to delete `a[i-1]`” or
“pretend to delete `a[i]`”) is feasible, i.e. after that deletion the whole
prefix up to the current index is strictly increasing. The algorithm never
encounters another violation (otherwise it would have returned `False`).  
Therefore the whole array, after applying the chosen deletion, is strictly
increasing. ∎



##### Lemma 4  
If a solution exists (the sequence can become strictly increasing after at most
one deletion), the algorithm returns `True`.

**Proof.**  
Assume a solution exists.

*If the original sequence is already increasing* the algorithm sees no violation
and returns `True`.

*Otherwise* there is at least one violation. Let `i` be the first index where
`a[i] <= a[i-1]`. Any valid solution must delete either `a[i-1]` or `a[i]`,
otherwise the pair at `i-1,i` would stay non‑increasing.  

- If the solution deletes `a[i-1]`, then necessarily `a[i] > a[i-2]` (or `i=1`);
  otherwise the pair `(a[i-2], a[i])` would still be non‑increasing.
  The algorithm will take the same branch (Lemma&nbsp;2) and continue without
  counting a second violation.

- If the solution deletes `a[i]`, then `a[i] <= a[i-2]` (or `i=1`), so the
  algorithm also chooses to delete `a[i]` (again Lemma&nbsp;2).

After this deletion the remainder of the array must be strictly increasing,
hence the algorithm will encounter no further violation and will finish with
`True`. ∎



##### Theorem  
The algorithm returns `True` **iff** the given sequence can be turned into a
strictly increasing sequence by removing at most one element.

**Proof.**  
*If* part: Lemma&nbsp;3.  
*Only‑if* part: Lemma&nbsp;4. ∎



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### Complexity Analysis  

The loop scans the array once.

*Time*: `O(n)` where `n = len(sequence)` ( ≤ 1000 ).  
*Memory*: `O(1)` extra (the input list is modified in place, which is allowed).

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### Reference Implementation  (Python 3)

```python
import sys
import ast

def can_be_increasing(seq):
    """Return True if seq can become strictly increasing after removing at most one element."""
    violations = 0
    i = 1
    while i < len(seq):
        if seq[i] <= seq[i - 1]:
            violations += 1
            if violations > 1:
                return False

            # decide which element to "remove"
            if i == 1 or seq[i] > seq[i - 2]:
                # pretend we removed seq[i-1]; nothing to change
                pass
            else:
                # pretend we removed seq[i]; make seq[i] equal to seq[i-1]
                # so the next comparison uses the larger value
                seq[i] = seq[i - 1]
        i += 1
    return True


def main():
    # read a line like "[1, 3, 2, 1]"
    line = sys.stdin.readline().strip()
    # safely evaluate the list literal
    try:
        sequence = ast.literal_eval(line)
    except Exception:
        # fallback: split by spaces/comma
        sequence = list(map(int, line.replace('[', '').replace(']', '').split(',')))
    # ensure we have a list of ints
    if not isinstance(sequence, list):
        sequence = list(sequence)

    result = can_be_increasing(sequence)
    print(result)


if __name__ == "__main__":
    main()
```

The program follows the required input‑output format, implements the algorithm
proved correct above, and conforms to the given constraints.

**Solution Explanation**

For a number `factor` to be a factor of `base` the division `base / factor` must leave no remainder.  
In Python the remainder of an integer division is obtained with the modulo operator `%`.

```
base % factor == 0   →   factor divides base exactly
```

The problem guarantees:

* `base` is a non‑negative integer (0, 1, 2, …)
* `factor` is a positive integer (1, 2, …)

So we never have to worry about division by zero.

The required steps are:

1. Read the two integers from standard input.
2. Define a function `is_factor(base, factor)` that returns `True` if `base % factor == 0`, otherwise `False`.
3. Call the function with the read values.
4. Print the Boolean result (`True` or `False`).

The algorithm runs in **O(1)** time and uses **O(1)** extra space.

---

**Reference Implementation (Python 3)**

```python
import sys

def is_factor(base: int, factor: int) -> bool:
    """
    Return True if `factor` is a factor of `base`,
    i.e., base is divisible by factor without remainder.
    """
    return base % factor == 0

def main() -> None:
    data = sys.stdin.read().strip().split()
    if len(data) < 2:
        # Not enough input; nothing to do
        return
    # Convert the first two tokens to integers
    base = int(data[0])
    factor = int(data[1])

    result = is_factor(base, factor)
    # Printing the boolean prints exactly "True" or "False"
    print(result)

if __name__ == "__main__":
    main()
```

The program follows the required input‑output format, reads the two numbers, checks the factor relationship, and prints `True` or `False` accordingly.

**Solution Explanation**

For a given price `p` ( 1 ≤ p ≤ 1000 ) and a rating `r` ( -1, 0, 1 ) we have to compute the tip `T` exactly as Dee does.

The algorithm consists of three ordered steps.

---

### 1. Round the price to the nearest ten  

```
rounded = ((p + 5) // 10) * 10
```

Adding 5 and integer‑dividing by 10 implements the usual “round half up” rule
(5 → up, 4 → down).

---

### 2. Base tip  

The rounded amount is always a multiple of ten, therefore dropping the
units digit is the same as dividing by 10.

```
T = rounded // 10
```

---

### 3. Apply the satisfaction rating  

```
if r == 1:          # satisfied
        T = T + 1
elif r == 0:        # unsatisfied
        T = T - 1
else:               # r == -1, appalled
        T = (T // 2) - 1          # integer division = round down
```

The problem requires a non‑negative result, therefore we clamp the value:

```
if T < 0:
        T = 0
```

---

### Correctness Proof  

We prove that the algorithm described in the program returns exactly the tip
defined in the statement.

*Lemma 1* – *Rounding*  
`rounded = ((p+5)//10)*10` is the nearest multiple of ten to `p`, rounding
half up.  
*Proof.* Adding 5 shifts the interval `[10k‑5, 10k+4]` to `[10k,10k+9]`. Integer division by 10
therefore yields `k`. Multiplying by 10 gives `10k`, the nearest ten. ∎

*Lemma 2* – *Base tip*  
`T = rounded // 10` equals the rounded price with its units digit removed.  
*Proof.* `rounded` is a multiple of ten, i.e. `rounded = 10·k`. Integer division by 10
returns `k`, which is exactly the number formed by deleting the trailing zero. ∎

*Lemma 3* – *Rating adjustment*  
The three cases in the program implement the three rules:

* `r = 1` → `T+1` (add 1) – matches “add 1”.
* `r = 0` → `T‑1` (subtract 1) – matches “subtract 1”.
* `r = -1` → `T//2 - 1` – integer division is “round down”, then subtract 1,
  exactly as required.

*Lemma 4* – *Non‑negativity*  
If the computed value is negative we output `0`. This satisfies the
requirement “return value T should be a non‑negative integer”.

*Theorem* – The function `calc_tip(p, r)` returns the tip defined by the
statement.  
*Proof.* By Lemma 1 the first line of the program produces the required rounded
price. By Lemma 2 the second line yields the required base tip `T`.  
By Lemma 3 the third step transforms `T` exactly as the rating prescribes.
Finally Lemma 4 guarantees the required non‑negative output. Hence the
function’s result coincides with the specification. ∎



---

### Complexity Analysis  

All operations are constant‑time arithmetic on integers.
Time complexity: **O(1)**  
Memory usage: **O(1)**

---

### Reference Implementation (Python 3)

```python
import sys

def calc_tip(p: int, r: int) -> int:
    # 1. round to nearest ten (half up)
    rounded = ((p + 5) // 10) * 10

    # 2. base tip = rounded without its units digit
    tip = rounded // 10

    # 3. apply rating
    if r == 1:          # satisfied
        tip += 1
    elif r == 0:        # unsatisfied
        tip -= 1
    else:               # r == -1, appalled
        tip = (tip // 2) - 1

    # ensure non‑negative
    if tip < 0:
        tip = 0
    return tip


if __name__ == "__main__":
    # input format: two integers, price and rating, separated by whitespace
    data = sys.stdin.read().strip().split()
    if len(data) != 2:
        raise ValueError("Expected exactly two integers as input")
    p = int(data[0])
    r = int(data[1])
    print(calc_tip(p, r))
```

The program follows the required input / output format, implements the
algorithm step‑by‑step, and prints the resulting tip.

**Solution Explanation**

For every character of the input string we have to change its case:

* if the character is an upper‑case letter → make it lower‑case  
* if the character is a lower‑case letter → make it upper‑case  
* any other character (digits, punctuation, spaces …) stays unchanged  

The operation must not modify the original string – we create a new one.

---

#### Algorithm
```
to_alternating_case(s):
    result = empty list
    for each character c in s
        if c is upper‑case letter      → append lower(c) to result
        else if c is lower‑case letter → append upper(c) to result
        else                           → append c unchanged
    return the concatenation of all characters in result
```

`str.isupper()` and `str.islower()` tell us whether a character is an upper‑ or lower‑case letter.
`str.upper()` / `str.lower()` perform the conversion.

---

#### Correctness Proof  

We prove that `to_alternating_case(s)` returns exactly the required alternating‑case string.

*Lemma 1*  
For any character `c`:
- if `c.isupper()` is true, the algorithm appends `c.lower()`, which is the lower‑case version of `c`;
- if `c.islower()` is true, the algorithm appends `c.upper()`, which is the upper‑case version of `c`;
- otherwise it appends `c` unchanged.

*Proof.* The algorithm checks the three mutually exclusive conditions in the given order and appends the corresponding value, matching the three cases above. ∎

*Theorem*  
For every input string `s`, `to_alternating_case(s)` equals the string obtained by swapping the case of each alphabetic character of `s` and leaving all other characters unchanged.

*Proof.* Consider the characters of `s` one by one.
By Lemma 1 the algorithm appends exactly the required transformed character for each position.
Since the algorithm processes the characters in their original order and concatenates the produced characters, the final string has the same length as `s` and each position contains the correctly transformed character.
Therefore the whole output string is precisely the desired alternating‑case version of `s`. ∎

---

#### Complexity Analysis  

Let `n = len(s)`.  
The loop visits each character once and performs O(1) work, so the time complexity is **O(n)**.  
A new list of `n` characters is built, requiring **O(n)** additional memory.

---

#### Reference Implementation (Python 3)

```python
import sys

def to_alternating_case(s: str) -> str:
    """
    Return a new string where each lower‑case letter of `s` becomes upper‑case
    and each upper‑case letter becomes lower‑case. All other characters are
    left unchanged.
    """
    transformed = []
    for ch in s:
        if ch.isupper():
            transformed.append(ch.lower())
        elif ch.islower():
            transformed.append(ch.upper())
        else:
            transformed.append(ch)
    return ''.join(transformed)


def main() -> None:
    # Read the whole input (single line, but keep possible trailing newline)
    input_text = sys.stdin.read()
    # Remove only the final newline that `read()` may include,
    # but keep other whitespace exactly as in the original string.
    if input_text.endswith('\n'):
        input_text = input_text[:-1]

    result = to_alternating_case(input_text)
    print(result)


if __name__ == "__main__":
    main()
```

The program follows the algorithm proven correct above, conforms to the required input‑output format, and runs in linear time.
completed 38/150, Solved: True, number of success = 13/38, acc = 34.21
**Solution Explanation**

For a list that should contain every integer from `1` to `n` exactly once,
exactly one value is missing.
If we know `n`, the missing value can be obtained from the well‑known
formula for the sum of the first `n` natural numbers  

```
S(n) = n·(n+1) / 2
```

The list we receive already has `len(arr)` elements, therefore  

```
n = len(arr) + 1                # one element is missing
missing = S(n) – sum(arr)
```

The algorithm works in linear time `O(n)` (only one pass to compute the
sum) and constant extra memory `O(1)`.  
It also works for very large inputs because Python integers have arbitrary
precision and we never store anything besides the running sum.

---

#### Algorithm
```
1. Read the whole input line, e.g. "[1, 2, 3]".
2. Remove the surrounding brackets and split by commas.
3. Convert the resulting strings to integers → list `a`.
4. n = len(a) + 1
5. total = n * (n + 1) // 2          # integer arithmetic
6. missing = total - sum(a)
7. Output missing
```

---

#### Correctness Proof  

We prove that the algorithm always outputs the missing number.

*Lemma 1*  
For any positive integer `n`, the sum of the numbers `1 … n` equals
`n·(n+1)/2`.

*Proof.* Classic arithmetic series formula, proved by induction or by
pairing first and last elements. ∎

*Lemma 2*  
Let `A` be the given list, `k = len(A)`.  
The original complete set is `{1,…,k+1}` and exactly one element `m`
is absent from `A`.

*Proof.* By problem statement the list is a permutation of the numbers
`1 … n` with one element missing. Since `|A| = k`, the original size must be
`k+1`; denote it `n = k+1`. ∎

*Theorem*  
The algorithm returns the missing element `m`.

*Proof.*  
From Lemma&nbsp;2 the complete set is `{1,…,n}` with `n = k+1`.  
By Lemma&nbsp;1 its total sum is `S = n·(n+1)/2`.  
The algorithm computes `total = S` (step 5) and `present = sum(A)` (step 6).  
Because `A` contains every element of the complete set except `m`,
`present = S – m`.  
Therefore `missing = total – present = S – (S – m) = m`.  
The algorithm outputs `missing`, which equals the missing number. ∎

---

#### Complexity Analysis  

* Let `N = n = len(A) + 1` be the size of the original full set.  
* The algorithm scans the list once to compute `sum(A)` → **O(N)** time.  
* Only a few integer variables are stored → **O(1)** extra space.

---

#### Reference Implementation (Python 3)

```python
import sys

def find_missing(arr):
    """Return the missing number from 1..n given arr (list of unique ints)."""
    n = len(arr) + 1                     # original size
    total = n * (n + 1) // 2             # sum 1..n
    return total - sum(arr)

def solve() -> None:
    data = sys.stdin.read().strip()
    # Expected format: [1, 2, 3] (possibly with spaces / newlines)
    if not data:
        return

    # Remove surrounding brackets
    data = data.strip()
    if data[0] == '[' and data[-1] == ']':
        data = data[1:-1]

    # Split by commas, ignore empty parts (e.g., empty list)
    parts = [p.strip() for p in data.split(',') if p.strip()]
    numbers = list(map(int, parts)) if parts else []

    missing = find_missing(numbers)
    print(missing)

if __name__ == "__main__":
    solve()
```

The program follows exactly the algorithm proven correct above and conforms
to the required input‑output format.
completed 39/150, Solved: False, number of success = 13/39, acc = 33.33
**Solution Explanation**

For two vectors `A` and `B` we have to count how many elements are equal **and**
appear at the same index in both vectors.  
If `c` is that count and `n` is the length of the vectors, the required affinity
value is  

\[
\text{affinity}= \frac{c}{n}
\]

The result is a real number between `0.0` and `1.0`.  
Both input vectors are given on separate lines in the usual Python list
syntax, e.g. `[1, 2, 3]`.  

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### Algorithm
```
read line1, line2
parse them into Python lists A, B               (using ast.literal_eval)

matches = 0
for each pair (a, b) from A and B (zip):
        if a == b:
                matches += 1

affinity = matches / len(A)          # vectors are guaranteed to have the same length
print(affinity)
```

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### Correctness Proof  

We prove that the algorithm outputs the required affinity.

*Lemma 1*  
After the loop finishes, `matches` equals the number of indices `i`
(0 ≤ i < n) such that `A[i] == B[i]`.

*Proof.*  
The loop iterates over all index‑wise pairs `(A[i], B[i])` because `zip`
produces exactly one pair for each index `i`.  
For each pair the algorithm increments `matches` **iff** the two elements are
equal. Therefore `matches` is increased once for every index where the
elements are equal and never otherwise. ∎

*Lemma 2*  
`affinity = matches / n` equals the proportion of equal‑at‑same‑index elements.

*Proof.*  
By Lemma&nbsp;1, `matches` is the count of equal‑at‑same‑index positions.
Dividing this count by the total number of positions `n` yields exactly the
fraction of positions that match, which is the definition of affinity. ∎

*Theorem*  
The algorithm prints the correct affinity value for the two input vectors.

*Proof.*  
The algorithm computes `matches` as in Lemma&nbsp;1, then computes
`affinity` as in Lemma&nbsp;2, and finally prints it. Hence the printed value
is precisely the required affinity. ∎

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### Complexity Analysis  

Let `n` be the length of the vectors.

* Time: the loop examines each of the `n` positions once → **O(n)**
* Memory: only a few scalar variables besides the input lists → **O(1)**
  extra space.

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### Reference Implementation (Python 3)

```python
import ast
import sys

def vector_affinity(v1, v2):
    """Return the proportion of equal elements at the same indices."""
    if not v1:                     # empty vectors – defined as 0.0 affinity
        return 0.0
    matches = sum(1 for a, b in zip(v1, v2) if a == b)
    return matches / len(v1)

def solve() -> None:
    # read two lines, each containing a list like "[1, 2, 3]"
    line1 = sys.stdin.readline().strip()
    line2 = sys.stdin.readline().strip()

    # safely convert the textual representation to Python lists
    vec1 = ast.literal_eval(line1)
    vec2 = ast.literal_eval(line2)

    # compute and output the affinity
    result = vector_affinity(vec1, vec2)
    print(result)

if __name__ == "__main__":
    solve()
```

The program follows exactly the algorithm proven correct above and conforms
to the required input‑output format.
completed 40/150, Solved: False, number of success = 13/40, acc = 32.5
**Solution Explanation**

For every element `arr[i]` we consider the product of all *other* elements.
The element whose removal gives the largest product is the answer.
If several removals give the same maximal product we return the smallest
removed value.

The array length is at most `15`, therefore a direct `O(n²)` enumeration is
more than fast enough.



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### Algorithm
```
best_product = -infinity
best_removed = None

for each index i in [0 … n-1]:
        prod = 1
        for each index j in [0 … n-1], j != i:
                prod *= arr[j]

        if prod > best_product:
                best_product = prod
                best_removed = arr[i]
        elif prod == best_product and arr[i] < best_removed:
                best_removed = arr[i]

output best_removed
```

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### Correctness Proof  

We prove that the algorithm returns exactly the element that must be removed.

*Lemma 1*  
For a fixed index `i` the inner loop computes  
`P_i = ∏_{j ≠ i} arr[j]`, the product of all elements except `arr[i]`.

*Proof.*  
The loop multiplies `prod` by every `arr[j]` with `j ≠ i` and never touches
`arr[i]`. Starting from `1`, after processing all such `j` the value of
`prod` equals the required product. ∎



*Lemma 2*  
After processing the first `k` indices (`0 … k-1`) the variables
`best_product` and `best_removed` store:

* the maximum product among `{P_0, … , P_{k-1}}`
* the smallest element whose removal yields that maximum product.

*Proof by induction on `k`.*

*Base `k = 1`* : after the first iteration the algorithm sets
`best_product = P_0` and `best_removed = arr[0]`. The statement holds.

*Induction step* : assume it holds after `k` iterations.
During iteration `k` we compute `P_k` (Lemma&nbsp;1) and compare it with the
current `best_product`.

* if `P_k` is larger, the maximum among the first `k+1` products becomes
  `P_k`; the algorithm stores it together with `arr[k]`.
* if `P_k` equals the current maximum, the algorithm keeps the smaller of
  the two candidate removed values, exactly the required tie‑break rule.
* if `P_k` is smaller, nothing changes.

Thus after the `(k+1)`‑st iteration the invariant is preserved. ∎



*Theorem*  
When the loop finishes, `best_removed` is the element whose removal yields
the maximal possible product of the remaining array elements; if several
elements achieve that product, it is the smallest among them.

*Proof.*  
After the last iteration (`k = n`) Lemma&nbsp;2 holds for all indices
`0 … n-1`. Hence `best_product` is the maximum of the whole set
`{P_i | i = 0 … n-1}` and `best_removed` is the smallest element that
produces this maximum. This is exactly the required answer. ∎



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### Complexity Analysis  

*Outer loop*: `n` iterations (`n ≤ 15`).  
*Inner loop*: `n‑1` multiplications per iteration.  

Time   `O(n²)` ≤ `225` operations – negligible.  
Memory `O(1)` besides the input array.

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### Reference Implementation  (Python 3)

```python
import sys
import ast

def element_to_remove(arr):
    best_product = None          # will hold the maximal product found
    best_removed = None          # element whose removal gives that product

    n = len(arr)
    for i in range(n):
        prod = 1
        for j in range(n):
            if j == i:
                continue
            prod *= arr[j]

        if best_product is None or prod > best_product:
            best_product = prod
            best_removed = arr[i]
        elif prod == best_product and arr[i] < best_removed:
            best_removed = arr[i]

    return best_removed


def main():
    # read the whole input, it is expected to be something like "[1, 2, 3]"
    data = sys.stdin.read().strip()
    # safely evaluate the list literal
    arr = ast.literal_eval(data)

    result = element_to_remove(arr)
    print(result)


if __name__ == "__main__":
    main()
```

The program follows exactly the algorithm proven correct above and conforms
to the required input‑output format.

**Solution Explanation**

From the starting value `1` we may

* add `1`                      `x → x+1`
* multiply by `2`                 `x → 2·x`

All operations have the same cost (one *step*).  
We have to reach a given `num ( 1 ≤ num ≤ 10000 )` with the smallest possible
number of steps.

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 1.  Reversing the process  

Both operations are reversible:

* `x → x+1` ⇔ `x → x‑1`
* `x → 2·x` ⇔ `x → x/2`  (only possible when `x` is even)

If we start from `num` and go **backwards** to `1` using the reverse
operations, every backward step corresponds to one forward step.
Therefore the length of a shortest backward path equals the length of a
shortest forward path.

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 2.  Greedy choice in the backward direction  

While we are moving backwards:

* If the current value `v` is **even**, we can divide it by `2`.
  Dividing reduces the number much faster than subtracting `1`,
  and it never makes the solution longer – any optimal forward sequence
  that reaches `v` must have performed a multiplication just before `v`,
  so we can safely undo it now.
* If `v` is **odd**, division is impossible, the only possible previous
  value is `v‑1`. So we must subtract `1`.

Thus the optimal backward algorithm is simply:

```
steps = 0
while v > 1:
    if v is even: v = v // 2
    else:         v = v - 1
    steps += 1
```

The loop runs at most `log2(num) + number_of_ones_in_binary(num)` times,
well below the limits (`num ≤ 10000`).

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 3.  Correctness Proof  

We prove that the algorithm returns the minimal number of steps.

---

**Lemma 1**  
For any even integer `v > 1`, there exists an optimal forward sequence
ending at `v` whose last operation is a multiplication by `2`.

*Proof.*  
If the last operation were `+1`, the previous value would be `v‑1`,
which is odd. From an odd number we cannot obtain an even number by a
single `+1` (it would stay odd) – we would need at least one extra
multiplication later, making the sequence longer. Replacing the final
`+1` by a division by `2` (the reverse of a multiplication) shortens the
sequence, contradicting optimality unless the last operation already
was a multiplication. ∎



**Lemma 2**  
When the current backward value `v` is even, any optimal backward path
from `v` to `1` must start with the operation `v → v/2`.

*Proof.*  
By Lemma&nbsp;1 the corresponding forward optimal path ends with a
multiplication, therefore the backward optimal path must undo that
multiplication first, i.e. divide by `2`. ∎



**Lemma 3**  
When the current backward value `v` is odd (`v>1`), any optimal backward
path from `v` to `1` must start with the operation `v → v‑1`.

*Proof.*  
Division by `2` is impossible for odd `v`. The only legal reverse operation
is subtracting `1`. Hence every backward path (optimal or not) must begin
with `v‑1`. ∎



**Theorem**  
The algorithm described in Section&nbsp;2 produces a shortest sequence of
steps from `1` to `num`.

*Proof.*  
We use induction on the number of iterations of the algorithm.

*Base.*  
If `num = 1`, the algorithm performs zero iterations and returns `0`,
which is clearly optimal.

*Induction step.*  
Assume the algorithm is optimal for all numbers `< v`.  
Consider the first iteration with current value `v > 1`.

*Case 1 – `v` even.*  
By Lemma&nbsp;2 every optimal backward path must start with `v → v/2`.
The algorithm does exactly this, then continues optimally from `v/2`
by the induction hypothesis. Hence the whole path is optimal.

*Case 2 – `v` odd.*  
By Lemma&nbsp;3 every optimal backward path must start with `v → v‑1`.
The algorithm does this, then proceeds optimally from `v‑1`
by the induction hypothesis. Hence the whole path is optimal.

Thus by induction the algorithm yields an optimal (shortest) sequence for
any `num`. ∎



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 4.  Complexity Analysis  

The loop reduces the current value at least by half every time it is even,
otherwise by one.  
The number of iterations is bounded by `O(log₂ num)`.  
For `num ≤ 10000` this is at most 14 iterations.

*Time*: `O(log num)`  
*Memory*: `O(1)` (only a few integer variables)

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 5.  Reference Implementation (Python 3)

```python
import sys

def min_steps_to_target(num: int) -> int:
    """Return the minimal number of steps to reach `num` from 1
    using operations +1 and *2."""
    steps = 0
    while num > 1:
        if num % 2 == 0:
            num //= 2          # reverse of a multiplication
        else:
            num -= 1           # reverse of an addition
        steps += 1
    return steps

def main() -> None:
    data = sys.stdin.read().strip()
    if not data:
        return
    n = int(data)
    print(min_steps_to_target(n))

if __name__ == "__main__":
    main()
```

The program follows exactly the algorithm proven correct above and conforms
to the required input‑output format.

**Solution Explanation**

For a given integer `N`

* we may use only prime numbers `p , q` with `0 < p , q < N`
* a *prime‑prime* is the rational number `p / q`
* we only keep the fractions that are **smaller than 1**, i.e. `p < q`

For all admissible fractions we have to output  

* `cnt` – how many such fractions exist  
* `int_sum` – the integer part (floor) of the sum of all those fractions



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 1.   Observations  

* Let  

```
P = [p1 , p2 , … , pk]      (p1 < p2 < … < pk)
```

be the list of all primes `< N`.  
`k = |P|`.

* For a fixed denominator `pj` every smaller prime `pi (i < j)` gives a
  fraction `pi / pj`.  
  Therefore the contribution of `pj` to the total sum is  

```
   (p1 + p2 + … + p{j‑1}) / pj
```

* The number of fractions is simply the number of unordered pairs of
  different primes, because each pair `{pi , pj}` (`i < j`) yields exactly
  one fraction `pi / pj` that is `< 1`.

```
cnt = C(k,2) = k·(k‑1) / 2
```

* The sum can be built in **linear** time using a running prefix sum
  of the primes:

```
prefix = 0
total  = 0
for each prime q in P (in increasing order):
        total  += prefix / q          # Fraction, not float
        prefix += q
```

The fraction `total` is kept as a `Fraction` (exact rational arithmetic),
so the integer part is simply `total.numerator // total.denominator`.

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 2.   Algorithm
```
read N
primes = all primes < N               (sieve of Eratosthenes)
k      = len(primes)

cnt = k*(k-1)//2                       # number of fractions

from fractions import Fraction
total   = Fraction(0,1)
prefix  = 0
for q in primes:                      # increasing order
        total  += Fraction(prefix, q)
        prefix += q

int_sum = total.numerator // total.denominator

print([cnt, int_sum])
```

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 3.   Correctness Proof  

We prove that the algorithm outputs the required pair  
`[cnt, int_sum]`.

---

##### Lemma 1  
For a fixed denominator `q` (prime `< N`) the sum of all admissible
fractions having denominator `q` equals  

\[
\frac{\displaystyle\sum_{p<q,\;p\text{ prime}} p}{q}.
\]

**Proof.**  
All admissible fractions with denominator `q` are exactly the numbers
`p/q` where `p` is a prime `< q`. Adding them gives  

\[
\sum_{p<q} \frac{p}{q}= \frac{1}{q}\sum_{p<q}p .
\qquad\blacksquare
\]

---

##### Lemma 2  
Let `P = [p1,…,pk]` be the sorted list of primes `< N`.  
After processing the first `j` primes in the loop (`j ≥ 1`) we have  

* `prefix = p1 + … + pj`  
* `total  = Σ_{i=2..j} ( Σ_{t=1}^{i-1} pt ) / pi`

**Proof.**  
Induction over `j`.

*Base (`j = 1`).*  
Before the first iteration `prefix = 0`.  
The loop adds `Fraction(0, p1)` to `total` (still `0`) and then
updates `prefix ← 0 + p1 = p1`.  
Thus the invariant holds.

*Induction step.*  
Assume it holds after iteration `j‑1`.  
During iteration `j` the algorithm adds `Fraction(prefix, pj)` to `total`.
By the induction hypothesis `prefix = Σ_{t=1}^{j-1} pt`, therefore the
added term is exactly the contribution described in Lemma&nbsp;1 for
denominator `pj`. Afterwards `prefix` becomes `Σ_{t=1}^{j} pt`.  
Hence the invariant holds for `j`. ∎



---

##### Lemma 3  
When the loop finishes (`j = k`)  

\[
total = \sum_{p<q,\;p,q\text{ prime}<N} \frac{p}{q}.
\]

**Proof.**  
By Lemma&nbsp;2 with `j = k` the expression for `total` contains, for every
prime `pi` (`i ≥ 2`), the term  

\[
\frac{\displaystyle\sum_{t=1}^{i-1} pt}{pi},
\]

which by Lemma&nbsp;1 is precisely the sum of all fractions whose denominator
is `pi`. Adding those contributions for all `i = 2 … k` yields the sum over
*all* admissible fractions. ∎



---

##### Lemma 4  
`cnt = k·(k‑1)/2` equals the number of admissible fractions.

**Proof.**  
Every unordered pair `{p_i , p_j}` with `i < j` gives exactly one fraction
`p_i / p_j` that is `< 1`. The number of unordered pairs of a set of size `k`
is the binomial coefficient `C(k,2) = k·(k‑1)/2`. ∎



---

##### Theorem  
The program prints  

* the exact number of prime‑prime fractions `< 1` that can be formed with
  primes `< N`, and  
* the integer part of the sum of all those fractions.

**Proof.**  

*Counting.*  
The program computes `k = |P|` and `cnt = k·(k‑1)//2`.  
By Lemma&nbsp;4 this equals the required count.

*Summation.*  
During the loop the variable `total` is a `Fraction`.  
By Lemma&nbsp;3 after the loop `total` equals the exact rational sum of all
admissible fractions.  
The integer part of a rational number `a/b` (`b>0`) is `a // b`.  
The program outputs `total.numerator // total.denominator`, which is exactly
that integer part.

Both requested values are therefore produced correctly. ∎



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 4.   Complexity Analysis  

*Generating primes* – sieve up to `N‑1`  

```
Time :  O(N log log N)
Memory: O(N)
```

*Counting & summation* – one linear scan over the `k` primes  

```
Time :  O(k)   (k ≤ N / log N)
Memory: O(1)   besides the prime list
```

Overall the algorithm runs in `O(N log log N)` time and `O(N)` memory,
easily fast enough for the usual limits (`N` up to a few 10⁶).

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 5.   Reference Implementation  (Python 3)

```python
import sys
from fractions import Fraction

def sieve_primes(limit: int):
    """Return list of all primes < limit using Eratosthenes sieve."""
    if limit <= 2:
        return []
    is_prime = bytearray(b'\x01') * limit
    is_prime[0:2] = b'\x00\x00'          # 0 and 1 are not prime
    for p in range(2, int(limit ** 0.5) + 1):
        if is_prime[p]:
            step = p
            start = p * p
            is_prime[start:limit:step] = b'\x00' * ((limit - start - 1) // step + 1)
    return [i for i, val in enumerate(is_prime) if val]

def solve() -> None:
    data = sys.stdin.read().strip()
    if not data:
        return
    N = int(data)

    primes = sieve_primes(N)          # all primes < N
    k = len(primes)

    # number of fractions p/q with p<q
    cnt = k * (k - 1) // 2

    # sum of all fractions, kept as exact Fraction
    total = Fraction(0, 1)
    prefix = 0
    for q in primes:                  # increasing order
        total += Fraction(prefix, q)
        prefix += q

    int_sum = total.numerator // total.denominator

    print([cnt, int_sum])

if __name__ == "__main__":
    solve()
```

The program follows exactly the algorithm proven correct above and
conforms to the required input‑output format.
completed 43/150, Solved: False, number of success = 13/43, acc = 30.23
**Solution Explanation**

For a non‑negative integer `x`

```
digitSum(x) = sum of its decimal digits
```

For a given `n` we have to choose two non‑negative integers `a , b`

```
a + b = n
```

so that  

`digitSum(a) + digitSum(b)` is as large as possible.
Only the value of this maximum has to be printed.



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 1.   Adding digit by digit  

Write `n` in decimal, least significant digit first  

```
n = … d2 d1 d0          (di  ∈ {0,…,9})
```

When we add `a` and `b` we also work digit by digit, keeping the
carry from the previous (lower) position.

```
ai + bi + ci   =   di + 10·ci+1          (ci  – incoming carry,
                                          ci+1 – outgoing carry)
```

`c0 = 0` (no carry into the units) and after the most significant
position we must have `clen = 0` (otherwise the sum would be larger
than `n`).

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 2.   What does the answer look like?

From the equation above

```
ai + bi = di + 10·ci+1 – ci                (0 ≤ ai , bi ≤ 9)
```

Summation over all positions gives

```
 Σ(ai+bi) = Σdi  +  Σ(10·ci+1 – ci)
          = digitSum(n) + 9· Σ ci+1          (c0 = 0)
```

`Σ ci+1` is the **number of carries that appear while adding**.
Every carry contributes `+9` to the wanted value.
Therefore

```
maximum digitSum(a)+digitSum(b) = digitSum(n) + 9·(maximum possible number of carries)
```

So the whole problem is reduced to

*“How many carries can we create while adding two non‑negative numbers
that sum to `n`?”*



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 3.   When is a carry possible?

For a fixed incoming carry `ci` (0 or 1) and a chosen outgoing carry
`ci+1` (0 or 1) the equality

```
ai + bi = di + 10·ci+1 – ci
```

must be feasible, i.e. the right hand side has to be a value that can be
written as the sum of two decimal digits:

```
0 ≤ di + 10·ci+1 – ci ≤ 18                (because 0 ≤ ai , bi ≤ 9)
```

All four possibilities are easy to test:

```
ci = 0 , ci+1 = 0   →  0 ≤ di          ≤ 18   (always true)
ci = 0 , ci+1 = 1   →  0 ≤ di + 10     ≤ 18   →  di ≤ 8
ci = 1 , ci+1 = 0   →  0 ≤ di - 1      ≤ 18   →  di ≥ 1
ci = 1 , ci+1 = 1   →  0 ≤ di + 9      ≤ 18   →  di ≤ 9
```

Thus a carry can be *generated* (`ci+1 = 1`) only if

```
ci = 0  →  di ≤ 8
ci = 1  →  di ≤ 9
```

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 4.   Dynamic programming over the digits  

The number of decimal digits of `n` is at most 12 (`n ≤ 10^11`), therefore a
tiny DP is sufficient.

`dp[i][c]` – maximum number of carries that can be produced after processing
the first `i` least‑significant digits,
**ending with an incoming carry `c` for the next digit** (`c = 0 or 1`).

Transition for the `i`‑th digit `di` :

```
for each possible incoming carry  c (0 or 1)
    for each possible outgoing carry c' (0 or 1)
        if 0 ≤ di + 10·c' – c ≤ 18          // feasible ?
            dp[i+1][c'] = max( dp[i+1][c'],
                               dp[i][c] + c' )   // we count the new carry c'
```

Initial state: `dp[0][0] = 0` (no digits processed, no incoming carry).  
After the most significant digit we must have **no final carry**,
therefore the answer is `dp[len][0]`.

The DP works in `O(number_of_digits)` time and `O(1)` memory.



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 5.   Computing the final answer  

```
digitSumN = Σ di
maxCarries = dp[len][0]
answer = digitSumN + 9 * maxCarries
```

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 6.   Correctness Proof  

We prove that the algorithm prints the required maximum.

---

##### Lemma 1  
For any addition of two non‑negative integers `a , b` with sum `n`,
let `c1 , c2 , … , c_len` be the carries generated at the digit positions
( `c_i` is the carry *out* of position `i-1` ).
Then  

```
digitSum(a) + digitSum(b) = digitSum(n) + 9·(c1 + … + c_len)
```

**Proof.**  
From the digitwise addition equation  

`ai + bi + ci = di + 10·ci+1`  

we obtain  

`ai + bi = di + 10·ci+1 – ci`.  

Summation over all positions `i = 0 … len-1` gives  

`Σ(ai+bi) = Σdi + Σ(10·ci+1 – ci)`.  

All intermediate carries appear twice with opposite signs, except the
first (`c0 = 0`) and the last (`c_len = 0`).  
Hence the sum reduces to `Σdi + 9·Σ ci+1`. ∎



##### Lemma 2  
A carry `ci+1 = 1` is feasible for digit `di` and incoming carry `ci`
iff  

```
ci = 0  →  di ≤ 8
ci = 1  →  di ≤ 9
```

**Proof.**  
Feasibility means `0 ≤ di + 10·ci+1 – ci ≤ 18`.  
Insert `ci+1 = 1` :

```
0 ≤ di + 10 – ci ≤ 18   ⇔   di ≤ 8 + ci
```

If `ci = 0` we obtain `di ≤ 8`; if `ci = 1` we obtain `di ≤ 9`. ∎



##### Lemma 3  
The DP described in Section&nbsp;4 computes, for every `i` and incoming
carry `c`, the maximum possible number of carries among all ways to
choose the first `i` digits of `a` and `b` that respect the addition
rules.

**Proof.**  
Induction over `i`.

*Base (`i = 0`).*  
No digit processed, only the state `c = 0` is reachable with `0` carries.
Thus `dp[0][0] = 0` is optimal.

*Induction step.*  
Assume the statement true for `i`.  
Consider any feasible construction for the first `i+1` digits that ends
with outgoing carry `c'`.  
Its first `i` digits end with some incoming carry `c`.  
By the induction hypothesis the best possible number of carries for the
prefix is `dp[i][c]`.  
If the transition `(c → c')` is feasible, the construction adds exactly
`c'` new carries (the carry generated at position `i`).  
Therefore the total number of carries is at most `dp[i][c] + c'`,
and the DP stores the maximum of those values in `dp[i+1][c']`.  
Conversely, every value written by the DP corresponds to a concrete
choice of digits (choose `ai , bi` so that their sum equals
`di + 10·c' – c`), hence is attainable. ∎



##### Lemma 4  
`maxCarries = dp[len][0]` equals the maximum possible number of carries
in any addition `a + b = n`.

**Proof.**  
By Lemma&nbsp;3, `dp[len][c]` is the best achievable number of carries
when the *final* (most significant) incoming carry equals `c`.  
A final carry `c = 1` would mean that the sum of `a` and `b` has an extra
most‑significant digit `1`, i.e. it would be larger than `n`.  
Thus only `c = 0` is admissible, and the optimum is `dp[len][0]`. ∎



##### Lemma 5  
The algorithm outputs the maximum possible value of
`digitSum(a) + digitSum(b)`.

**Proof.**  
Let `Cmax = dp[len][0]`.  
By Lemma&nbsp;4 there exists a pair `(a,b)` with exactly `Cmax` carries,
and no pair can have more carries.  
Using Lemma&nbsp;1,

```
digitSum(a)+digitSum(b) = digitSum(n) + 9·Cmax .
```

The algorithm prints exactly this expression, therefore it prints the
largest achievable sum of digit sums. ∎



##### Theorem  
For every input integer `n (0 ≤ n ≤ 10^11)` the program prints

```
max_{a,b ≥ 0, a+b=n}  ( digitSum(a) + digitSum(b) )
```

**Proof.**  
Directly from Lemma&nbsp;5. ∎



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 7.   Complexity Analysis  

* `L` – number of decimal digits of `n` (`L ≤ 12`).

*Time* : `O(L)` – one constant‑size DP transition per digit.  
*Memory*: `O(1)` – only two arrays of size `2` are kept.

Both limits are easily satisfied for the given constraints.



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 8.   Reference Implementation  (Python 3)

```python
import sys

def max_digit_sum_pair(n_str: str) -> int:
    """return max digitSum(a)+digitSum(b) for a+b = int(n_str)"""
    # digit sum of n
    digit_sum_n = sum(int(ch) for ch in n_str)

    # digits of n, least significant first
    digits = [int(ch) for ch in reversed(n_str)]

    # dp[c] = best number of carries after processing current prefix,
    #         ending with incoming carry = c (c = 0 or 1)
    NEG = -10**9
    dp = [0, NEG]          # dp for i = 0, only c=0 is possible

    for d in digits:
        ndp = [NEG, NEG]
        for c_in in (0, 1):
            if dp[c_in] < 0:
                continue
            for c_out in (0, 1):
                total = d + 10 * c_out - c_in
                if 0 <= total <= 18:          # feasible
                    ndp[c_out] = max(ndp[c_out], dp[c_in] + c_out)
        dp = ndp

    max_carries = dp[0]          # final carry must be 0
    return digit_sum_n + 9 * max_carries


def solve() -> None:
    data = sys.stdin.read().strip()
    if not data:
        return
    n_str = data.split()[0]      # single integer
    print(max_digit_sum_pair(n_str))


if __name__ == "__main__":
    solve()
```

The program follows exactly the algorithm proven correct above
and conforms to the required input‑output format.
completed 44/150, Solved: False, number of success = 13/44, acc = 29.55
**Solution Explanation**

We have two squares with side lengths `a` and `b`.  
The suitcase is a rectangle with width `m` and height `n`.

The squares may be placed only with their sides parallel to the suitcase
(sides are not allowed to be rotated at an angle).  
Therefore a placement can be of only two kinds  

* **horizontally** – the squares are placed side‑by‑side along the width  

  * required width `a + b` must not exceed the suitcase width `m`
  * required height `max(a, b)` must not exceed the suitcase height `n`

* **vertically** – the squares are stacked along the height  

  * required height `a + b` must not exceed the suitcase height `n`
  * required width  `max(a, b)` must not exceed the suitcase width `m`

If either of the two possibilities fits, the answer is `True`,
otherwise it is `False`.

---

#### Algorithm
```
fit_in(a, b, m, n):
    if (a + b <= m) and (max(a, b) <= n):
        return True                     # horizontal placement
    if (a + b <= n) and (max(a, b) <= m):
        return True                     # vertical placement
    return False
```

Read the four integers from standard input, call `fit_in`, and print the
result.

---

#### Correctness Proof  

We prove that the algorithm returns `True` **iff** the two squares can be
packed into the rectangle.

*Lemma 1*  
If the squares can be placed horizontally, then  
`a + b ≤ m` and `max(a, b) ≤ n`.

*Proof.*  
In a horizontal placement the total width equals the sum of the two side
lengths, therefore it must not exceed the suitcase width `m`.  
The height of the arrangement equals the larger of the two squares,
hence it must not exceed the suitcase height `n`. ∎

*Lemma 2*  
If the squares can be placed vertically, then  
`a + b ≤ n` and `max(a, b) ≤ m`.

*Proof.* Identical to Lemma&nbsp;1, swapping the roles of width and height. ∎

*Theorem*  
`fit_in(a,b,m,n)` returns `True` exactly when the two squares can be packed
into the `m × n` rectangle.

*Proof.*  

*→* (algorithm returns `True`)  
The algorithm returns `True` only in one of the two `if` branches.
If the first branch is taken, the conditions `a + b ≤ m` and
`max(a, b) ≤ n` hold, which by Lemma&nbsp;1 guarantee a feasible horizontal
placement.  
If the second branch is taken, the conditions `a + b ≤ n` and
`max(a, b) ≤ m` hold, which by Lemma&nbsp;2 guarantee a feasible vertical
placement. Hence a packing exists.

*←* (packing exists)  
Assume a packing exists. It is either horizontal or vertical
(because squares are axis‑aligned).  
* If it is horizontal, Lemma&nbsp;1 gives `a + b ≤ m` and `max(a, b) ≤ n`,
  so the first `if` condition of the algorithm is satisfied and it returns
  `True`.  
* If it is vertical, Lemma&nbsp;2 gives `a + b ≤ n` and `max(a, b) ≤ m`,
  so the second `if` condition is satisfied and the algorithm returns
  `True`.

Thus the algorithm returns `True` exactly in the cases where a packing is
possible. ∎



---

#### Complexity Analysis  

The algorithm performs a constant amount of arithmetic and comparisons,
so its time complexity is **O(1)** and its memory usage is **O(1)**.

---

#### Reference Implementation (Python 3)

```python
import sys

def fit_in(a: int, b: int, m: int, n: int) -> bool:
    """
    Return True if two squares of side lengths a and b can be placed
    inside a rectangle of size m (width) by n (height) without overlap.
    """
    # horizontal placement
    if (a + b <= m) and (max(a, b) <= n):
        return True
    # vertical placement
    if (a + b <= n) and (max(a, b) <= m):
        return True
    return False


def main() -> None:
    # read four numbers, one per line (or space‑separated)
    data = sys.stdin.read().strip().split()
    if not data:
        return
    a, b, m, n = map(int, data[:4])
    print(fit_in(a, b, m, n))


if __name__ == "__main__":
    main()
```
