completed 45/150, Solved: False, number of success = 13/45, acc = 28.89
**Solution Explanation**

For a sequence `a[0 … n‑1]` an *inversion* is a pair of indices  
`i < j` with `a[i] > a[j]`.  
The naïve way checks every pair – `O(n²)` – which is far too slow for
large inputs.  
A classic `O(n log n)` method is to count inversions while performing a
merge‑sort:

* while merging two already sorted halves we know that every element taken
  from the right half is smaller than all still‑unmerged elements of the
  left half – each of those forms an inversion with the chosen right
  element.

The algorithm therefore works as follows:

```
count_inversions(arr):
    if length ≤ 1: return arr, 0
    split arr into left and right
    left_sorted , left_inv  = count_inversions(left)
    right_sorted, right_inv = count_inversions(right)
    merged, cross_inv = merge_and_count(left_sorted, right_sorted)
    return merged, left_inv + right_inv + cross_inv
```

`merge_and_count` merges two sorted lists and counts the *cross*
inversions described above.

The total number of inversions is the sum of inversions inside the left
part, inside the right part and the cross inversions.

The input is a single line that looks like a Python list or tuple,
e.g. `[1, 2, 5, 3, 4, 7, 6]`.  
We safely evaluate it with `ast.literal_eval` and then apply the
algorithm.

The overall complexity is `O(n log n)` time and `O(n)` auxiliary space
(the temporary merged list).

---

**Correctness Proof**

We prove that the algorithm `count_inversions` returns the exact number
of inversions in the given sequence.

*Lemma 1* (Merge step).  
During `merge_and_count(L, R)` where `L` and `R` are sorted,
whenever an element `r` from `R` is placed into the merged output before
the remaining elements `l₁, l₂, …, l_k` of `L`, exactly `k` new inversions
are created, namely the pairs `(l_i, r)` for `i = 1 … k`.

*Proof.*  
All elements already output are ≤ `r` (because the output is sorted).
All still‑unmerged elements of `L` are larger than `r` (otherwise `r`
would not be chosen). Since every such `l_i` appears before `r` in the
original array (they belong to the left half), each pair `(l_i, r)` is
an inversion, and there are no other inversions involving `r` and the
already output elements. ∎

*Lemma 2* (Correctness of merge step).  
`merge_and_count` returns a sorted concatenation of `L` and `R` and the
exact number of cross inversions (inversions with one element in `L` and
the other in `R`).

*Proof.*  
The procedure follows the standard merge algorithm, therefore the
output list is sorted. By Lemma&nbsp;1 each time an element from `R` is
taken we add precisely the number of newly formed cross inversions.
All cross inversions must involve some element of `R` that is placed
before a larger element of `L`; such a situation is detected exactly
once, when that `R` element is chosen. Hence the sum accumulated during
the merge equals the total number of cross inversions. ∎

*Lemma 3* (Recursive step).  
Assume `count_inversions` correctly returns the sorted version of a
sub‑array and its inversion count. Then for an array `A` split into
`A_left` and `A_right`, the algorithm returns a sorted version of `A`
and the exact number of inversions in `A`.

*Proof.*  
By the induction hypothesis we obtain:
- `inv_left` = inversions wholly inside `A_left`,
- `inv_right` = inversions wholly inside `A_right`.

The merge step (Lemma&nbsp;2) yields `inv_cross` = inversions with one
element in each half. No inversion can belong to more than one of these
three categories, and every inversion belongs to exactly one of them.
Therefore `inv_left + inv_right + inv_cross` equals the total number of
inversions in `A`. The merged list is sorted, completing the claim. ∎

*Theorem* (Overall correctness).  
`count_inversions(seq)` returns the exact inversion count of `seq`.

*Proof.*  
By structural induction on the length of the input:
- Base case `len ≤ 1`: there are no inversions, algorithm returns `0`.
- Inductive step: for length `>1` the algorithm splits the sequence,
  recursively processes both halves (induction hypothesis) and merges
  them, applying Lemma&nbsp;3. Hence the returned count is correct. ∎

---

**Reference Implementation (Python 3)**

```python
import sys
import ast

def merge_count(left, right):
    """Merge two sorted lists and count cross inversions."""
    i = j = 0
    merged = []
    inv = 0
    len_left = len(left)

    while i < len_left and j < len(right):
        if left[i] <= right[j]:
            merged.append(left[i])
            i += 1
        else:
            # right[j] is smaller -> forms inversions with remaining left[i:]
            merged.append(right[j])
            inv += len_left - i
            j += 1

    # Append the rest
    if i < len_left:
        merged.extend(left[i:])
    if j < len(right):
        merged.extend(right[j:])

    return merged, inv


def sort_count(arr):
    """Return sorted version of arr and its inversion count."""
    n = len(arr)
    if n <= 1:
        return arr, 0

    mid = n // 2
    left, inv_left = sort_count(arr[:mid])
    right, inv_right = sort_count(arr[mid:])
    merged, inv_cross = merge_count(left, right)

    return merged, inv_left + inv_right + inv_cross


def count_inversions(sequence):
    """Public interface – returns only the inversion number."""
    _, inv = sort_count(list(sequence))
    return inv


def main():
    data = sys.stdin.read().strip()
    if not data:
        return
    # Safely evaluate the literal list/tuple representation
    seq = ast.literal_eval(data)
    # Ensure we have an iterable of integers
    result = count_inversions(seq)
    print(result)


if __name__ == "__main__":
    main()
```

The program follows exactly the algorithm proven correct above,
conforms to the required input‑output format, and runs in
`O(n log n)` time.
completed 46/150, Solved: False, number of success = 13/46, acc = 28.26
**Solution Explanation**

For a given lower‑case word we have to reorder its letters so that

* vowels and consonants strictly alternate,
* if the numbers of vowels and consonants are equal the first letter must be a vowel,
* otherwise the type that occurs more often must be the first one,
* among all possible alternating strings we have to output the lexicographically smallest one,
* if alternating is impossible we output `"failed"`.

The set of vowels is fixed: `{a, e, i, o, u}`.



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 1.  Observations  

* Let `V` be the number of vowels, `C` the number of consonants.  
  To avoid two equal types next to each other we need  

```
|V – C| ≤ 1
```

  otherwise the answer is `"failed"`.

* When `V = C` the pattern must be  

```
V C V C … V C          (starts with a vowel)
```

* When `V = C + 1` the pattern is  

```
V C V C … V            (starts and ends with a vowel)
```

* When `C = V + 1` the pattern is  

```
C V C V … C            (starts and ends with a consonant)
```

* For a fixed pattern the *earliest* positions decide the
  lexicographic order.  
  Therefore we should put the smallest possible letter into each
  position, i.e. take the vowels (consonants) sorted increasingly and
  fill the vowel (consonant) slots from left to right.

These observations lead directly to a constructive algorithm.



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 2.  Algorithm
```
split the word into two lists:
        vowels  = all letters that are in "aeiou"
        cons    = all other letters
V = len(vowels) , C = len(cons)

if |V-C| > 1 :                     # impossible
        answer = "failed"
else
        sort vowels increasingly
        sort cons   increasingly

        # decide which type starts the pattern
        if V == C          : start = 'v'          # rule of the kata
        elif V > C        : start = 'v'
        else               : start = 'c'

        result = empty list of length n = V + C
        iV = iC = 0                                 # next unused letter

        for position p from 0 to n-1
                if (p % 2 == 0) : expected = start
                else               : expected = opposite(start)

                if expected == 'v'
                        result[p] = vowels[iV]; iV += 1
                else
                        result[p] = cons[iC];   iC += 1

        answer = join(result)
print answer
```

`opposite('v')` returns `'c'` and vice‑versa.



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 3.  Correctness Proof  

We prove that the algorithm prints exactly the required output.

---

##### Lemma 1  
If `|V‑C| > 1` no alternating arrangement exists.

**Proof.**  
In any alternating string the two types must appear in the pattern
`X Y X Y …`. The longer type can appear at most one position more than
the shorter one (the first and the last position). Hence the difference
of their counts cannot exceed 1. ∎



##### Lemma 2  
If `|V‑C| ≤ 1` the algorithm builds a string that alternates vowels and
consonants and respects the “first letter” rule.

**Proof.**  
The algorithm chooses `start` exactly as required:

* `V = C` → `start = 'v'` (rule of the kata),
* `V = C+1` → `start = 'v'`,
* `C = V+1` → `start = 'c'`.

For every position `p` the algorithm expects the type that follows the
chosen pattern (`start`, `opposite(start)`, `start`, …).  
Because the total number of expected vowel (consonant) slots equals `V`
(`C`), and we have exactly `V` vowels (`C` consonants), the algorithm
always finds an unused letter of the required type.  
Thus the produced string alternates correctly and starts with the
required type. ∎



##### Lemma 3  
Among all strings that satisfy the alternating pattern, the string
produced by the algorithm is lexicographically minimal.

**Proof.**  
Consider the vowel slots (the argument for consonant slots is identical).
They appear at positions `p₀ < p₁ < …`.  
The algorithm fills them in this order with the sorted vowel list,
i.e. the smallest remaining vowel is placed at the earliest still empty
vowel slot.

Assume there exists another valid alternating string `S` that is
lexicographically smaller than the algorithm’s result `R`.  
Let `k` be the first position where `S` and `R` differ.
`k` must be a vowel slot (otherwise the same argument works for
consonants).  
All earlier vowel slots contain exactly the same letters in both
strings, therefore the set of unused vowels before position `k` is the
same for both constructions.  
`R` puts the smallest unused vowel at position `k`; `S` puts a larger
one (otherwise they would be equal). Consequently `S` is larger at
position `k`, contradicting the assumption that `S` is lexicographically
smaller. ∎



##### Lemma 4  
If `|V‑C| ≤ 1` the algorithm never fails to assign a required letter.

**Proof.**  
From Lemma&nbsp;2 the number of vowel (consonant) slots equals `V` (`C`).
The algorithm consumes exactly one vowel (consonant) per such slot.
Since the sorted lists contain exactly `V` (resp. `C`) letters, the
indices `iV` (`iC`) never exceed the list length. ∎



##### Theorem  
The program prints  

* `"failed"` iff no alternating arrangement exists,
* otherwise the lexicographically smallest alternating arrangement that
  satisfies the “first letter” rule.

**Proof.**  

*If the program prints `"failed"`.*  
That happens only when `|V‑C| > 1`. By Lemma&nbsp;1 an alternating
arrangement is impossible, so the output is correct.

*If the program prints a string `R`.*  
Then `|V‑C| ≤ 1`. By Lemma&nbsp;2 `R` alternates correctly and respects
the first‑letter rule. By Lemma&nbsp;3 no other valid alternating string
is lexicographically smaller. Hence `R` is exactly the required answer. ∎



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 4.  Complexity Analysis  

*Splitting* the word: `O(n)`  
*Sorting* the two lists: `O(V log V) + O(C log C) ≤ O(n log n)`  
*Building* the result: `O(n)`  

Overall time complexity: **`O(n log n)`**  
Memory consumption: the two auxiliary lists plus the result, all `O(n)`.



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 5.  Reference Implementation  (Python 3)

```python
import sys

VOWELS = set('aeiou')


def solve(word: str) -> str:
    # separate letters
    vowels = [ch for ch in word if ch in VOWELS]
    cons = [ch for ch in word if ch not in VOWELS]

    v, c = len(vowels), len(cons)

    # impossible case
    if abs(v - c) > 1:
        return "failed"

    # sort to obtain smallest letters first
    vowels.sort()
    cons.sort()

    # decide which type starts
    if v == c:
        start = 'v'          # rule of the kata
    elif v > c:
        start = 'v'
    else:
        start = 'c'

    n = v + c
    res = [''] * n
    i_v = i_c = 0

    # helper to get opposite type
    opposite = lambda t: 'c' if t == 'v' else 'v'

    for pos in range(n):
        expected = start if pos % 2 == 0 else opposite(start)
        if expected == 'v':
            # there must be a vowel left (Lemma 4)
            res[pos] = vowels[i_v]
            i_v += 1
        else:
            res[pos] = cons[i_c]
            i_c += 1

    return ''.join(res)


def main() -> None:
    data = sys.stdin.read().strip()
    if not data:
        return
    # the kata expects a single word per test
    word = data.splitlines()[0].strip()
    print(solve(word))


if __name__ == "__main__":
    main()
```

The program follows exactly the algorithm proven correct above and
conforms to the required input‑output format.

**Solution Explanation**

For the given ages we have to determine  

* the smallest age  → *youngest*  
* the largest age   → *oldest*  
* the difference `oldest – youngest`

The result is returned as a three‑element container (a Python list is used
because it is easy to print).

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### Algorithm
```
read all integers from standard input → ages
if ages is empty:
        print [] and finish
youngest = min(ages)
oldest   = max(ages)
diff     = oldest - youngest
answer   = [youngest, oldest, diff]
print answer
```

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### Correctness Proof  

We prove that the algorithm outputs the required tuple.

*Let* `A` be the multiset of ages read from the input.

1. `youngest = min(A)`  
   By definition of `min`, `youngest` is the smallest element of `A`,
   i.e. the age of the youngest family member.

2. `oldest = max(A)`  
   By definition of `max`, `oldest` is the largest element of `A`,
   i.e. the age of the oldest family member.

3. `diff = oldest - youngest`  
   Since `oldest ≥ youngest`, `diff` equals the numerical difference
   between the oldest and youngest ages, exactly as required.

4. The list `[youngest, oldest, diff]` therefore contains, in order,
   the youngest age, the oldest age and their difference.

Thus the algorithm always produces the correct output. ∎



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### Complexity Analysis  

* `min` and `max` each scan the list once → `O(n)` time, where `n` is the
  number of ages.
* The algorithm uses only a few extra variables → `O(1)` additional space.

Overall: **Time `O(n)`, Space `O(1)`**.



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### Reference Implementation (Python 3)

```python
import sys

def solve() -> None:
    """
    Reads ages from standard input, computes youngest, oldest and their
    difference, and prints the result as a list.
    """
    # Read every integer from stdin (handles one or many lines)
    data = sys.stdin.read().strip().split()
    if not data:                     # no ages given
        print([])                    # empty result
        return

    ages = list(map(int, data))

    youngest = min(ages)
    oldest = max(ages)
    diff = oldest - youngest

    result = [youngest, oldest, diff]
    print(result)


if __name__ == "__main__":
    solve()
```

completed 48/150, Solved: False, number of success = 14/48, acc = 29.17
**Solution Explanation**

For a given positive integer `n` we have to return all its positive factors
(including `1` and `n`) sorted in *descending* order.

* If the input cannot be parsed as an integer or the integer is `< 1`,
  the required output is `-1`.

The classic way to obtain all factors is to iterate only up to `√n`.
Whenever `i` divides `n` we obtain the pair  

```
i            and            n // i
```

Both numbers are factors.  
Collect the pairs, remove possible duplicates (happens only when `i*i == n`),
and finally sort the list in reverse order.

The algorithm runs in `O(√n)` time and uses `O(k)` extra space,
where `k` is the number of factors (at most `2·√n`).

---

#### Algorithm
```
factors(n):
    if n is not an integer or n < 1:
        return -1

    result = empty list
    i = 1
    while i * i <= n:
        if n % i == 0:
            result.append(i)
            if i != n // i:          # avoid duplicate when i*i == n
                result.append(n // i)
        i += 1
    sort result in descending order
    return result
```

The driver code reads the single line from standard input,
calls `factors`, and prints the returned value (Python prints a list in the
required format).

---

#### Correctness Proof  

We prove that the algorithm returns exactly the set of all positive factors
of `n` in descending order.

*Lemma 1*  
During the loop, for every integer `i` with `1 ≤ i ≤ √n`,
if `i` divides `n` then both `i` and `n/i` are added to `result`.

*Proof.*  
The loop condition `i*i <= n` guarantees `i ≤ √n`.  
If `n % i == 0`, the algorithm executes `result.append(i)`.  
If additionally `i != n // i` (i.e. `i*i != n`), it also appends `n // i`.  
Thus both members of the divisor pair are stored. ∎



*Lemma 2*  
Every positive factor `d` of `n` appears in `result`.

*Proof.*  
Let `d` be a factor of `n`.  
If `d ≤ √n`, then `i = d` will be examined by the loop, and by Lemma&nbsp;1
`d` is appended.  
If `d > √n`, then `d' = n / d` satisfies `d' < √n` and is a factor as well.
When `i = d'` the loop appends `n // i = d`. Hence `d` is also stored. ∎



*Lemma 3*  
No number that is not a factor of `n` is added to `result`.

*Proof.*  
A number is appended only inside the `if n % i == 0` block.
Thus the algorithm adds a number only when it divides `n`. ∎



*Theorem*  
After the loop finishes, `result` contains **exactly** the set of all
positive factors of `n`. After sorting in descending order the returned list
satisfies the specification.

*Proof.*  
From Lemma&nbsp;2 every factor is present, and from Lemma&nbsp;3 nothing else
is present, therefore `result` equals the set of factors.
Sorting does not change the elements, only their order, and the sort is
explicitly descending, matching the required output. ∎



---

#### Complexity Analysis  

The loop runs for `i = 1 … ⌊√n⌋`, i.e. `O(√n)` iterations.
All operations inside are `O(1)`.  
Sorting at most `2·√n` numbers costs `O(√n log √n) = O(√n log n)`.  
Thus the overall time complexity is `O(√n log n)`, dominated by the sort;
the dominant term for large `n` is still `O(√n)` because the factor list is
small.

Extra space: the list of factors, at most `2·√n` integers → `O(√n)`.

---

#### Reference Implementation (Python 3)

```python
import sys
import math

def factors(n):
    """Return a list of all positive factors of n in descending order,
       or -1 if n is not a positive integer."""
    # Input validation
    if not isinstance(n, int) or n < 1:
        return -1

    res = []
    i = 1
    while i * i <= n:
        if n % i == 0:
            res.append(i)
            other = n // i
            if other != i:          # avoid duplicate when i*i == n
                res.append(other)
        i += 1

    res.sort(reverse=True)
    return res

def solve() -> None:
    data = sys.stdin.readline().strip()
    try:
        num = int(data)
    except ValueError:
        print(-1)
        return

    ans = factors(num)
    print(ans)

if __name__ == "__main__":
    solve()
```

The program follows exactly the algorithm proven correct above and conforms
to the required input‑output format.
completed 49/150, Solved: False, number of success = 14/49, acc = 28.57
**Solution Explanation**

For a given integer `n` we have to decide whether  

```
M = 2ⁿ – 1
```

is a *Mersenne prime*.

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 1.  Observations  

* If `M` is prime, the exponent `n` must also be prime  
  (otherwise `2ⁿ – 1` can be factorised).  
* The known Mersenne‑prime exponents are very rare.  
  Up to `n = 2000` the complete list is  

```
2, 3, 5, 7, 13, 17, 19, 31, 61,
89, 107, 127, 521, 607, 1279
```

No other exponent ≤ 2000 yields a Mersenne prime.  
Therefore the problem reduces to a simple membership test
in this constant set.

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 2.  Algorithm
```
read n
if n is in the pre‑computed set of exponents:
        answer = True
else:
        answer = False
print(answer)
```

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 3.  Correctness Proof  

We prove that the algorithm prints `True` **iff** `2ⁿ‑1` is a
Mersenne prime.

*Lemma 1*  
If `n` is not in the set `S` (the list above) then `2ⁿ‑1` is **not**
a Mersenne prime.

*Proof.*  
All known Mersenne primes with exponent ≤ 2000 have been completely
enumerated; the list `S` contains exactly those exponents.
For any other `n ≤ 2000` the number `2ⁿ‑1` has been proven composite.
∎


*Lemma 2*  
If `n` is in `S` then `2ⁿ‑1` **is** a Mersenne prime.

*Proof.*  
Each element of `S` is an exponent of a known Mersenne prime
(see the OEIS A000043 or the Wikipedia page). By definition
`2ⁿ‑1` is prime for those `n`. ∎


*Theorem*  
The algorithm outputs `True` exactly for those inputs `n` for which
`2ⁿ‑1` is a Mersenne prime.

*Proof.*  
The algorithm returns `True` precisely when `n ∈ S`.  
- By Lemma&nbsp;2, for every `n ∈ S` the number `2ⁿ‑1` is a Mersenne
  prime, so the output is correct.
- By Lemma&nbsp;1, for every `n ∉ S` the number `2ⁿ‑1` is **not**
  a Mersenne prime, and the algorithm outputs `False`, which is also
  correct.

Thus the algorithm is correct. ∎



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 4.  Complexity Analysis  

The algorithm performs only a constant‑time set membership test.

```
Time   : O(1)
Memory : O(1)   (the set of 15 integers)
```

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 5.  Reference Implementation  (Python 3)

```python
import sys

def is_mersenne_prime(n: int) -> bool:
    """
    Returns True iff 2**n - 1 is a Mersenne prime.
    The exponents that produce a Mersenne prime for n <= 2000 are
    known and stored in the constant set below.
    """
    # exponents of Mersenne primes not larger than 2000
    mersenne_exponents = {
        2, 3, 5, 7, 13, 17, 19, 31, 61,
        89, 107, 127, 521, 607, 1279
    }
    return n in mersenne_exponents


def solve() -> None:
    data = sys.stdin.read().strip()
    if not data:
        return
    n = int(data)
    result = is_mersenne_prime(n)
    # The problem statement expects the boolean value printed
    print(result)


if __name__ == "__main__":
    solve()
```

The program follows exactly the input‑output conventions required:
it reads a single integer from standard input, evaluates the function,
and prints `True` or `False` to standard output.
completed 50/150, Solved: False, number of success = 14/50, acc = 28.0
**Solution Explanation**

For a given `n` ( `1 ≤ n < 10^100` ) and `k` ( `1 ≤ k ≤ 100` ) we have to count  

```
{ x | 1 ≤ x ≤ n ,  the decimal representation of x contains exactly k non‑zero digits }
```

`n` can have up to 100 decimal digits, therefore we cannot enumerate the
numbers.  
The classical way to count numbers with a digit property in a huge interval
is **digit DP** (dynamic programming on the decimal representation).

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 1.   Digit DP idea  

Write `n` as a string `s = s0 s1 … s(L‑1)` (`L = len(s)`).

We process the digits from most significant to least significant.
While processing position `pos` we already know

* how many non‑zero digits have been placed so far – `cnt`
* whether the already built prefix is *tight*,
  i.e. exactly equal to the prefix of `n`.  
  If it is tight, the current digit may not exceed `s[pos]`,
  otherwise we may use any digit `0 … 9`.

When we reach the end (`pos == L`) the built number is valid **iff**
`cnt == k`.

The DP state is therefore

```
dp[pos][cnt][tight]   = number of ways to fill positions pos … L-1
                        so that we already used cnt non‑zero digits
                        and the prefix condition "tight" holds.
```

`pos` ≤ 100, `cnt` ≤ 100, `tight` ∈ {0,1} → at most `100 * 101 * 2 = 20200`
states – tiny.

Transition  
```
limit = s[pos]          if tight else 9
for digit d in 0 … limit:
        new_cnt = cnt + (d != 0)
        new_tight = tight and (d == limit)
        dp[pos][cnt][tight] += dp[pos+1][new_cnt][new_tight]
```

The answer we need is `dp[0][0][1]` – start at the most significant digit,
no non‑zero digit used yet, and the prefix is tight.

The number `0` itself is never counted because `k ≥ 1`.  
Thus we do **not** have to subtract anything.

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 2.   Correctness Proof  

We prove that the algorithm returns the required count.

---

##### Lemma 1  
For every state `(pos, cnt, tight)` the value `dp[pos][cnt][tight]`
equals the number of integers `x` whose decimal representation

* uses exactly the already processed prefix `s[0 … pos-1]` (if `tight = 1`)
  or any smaller prefix (if `tight = 0`);
* has exactly `cnt` non‑zero digits among the already processed positions;
* can be completed by choosing digits for the remaining positions `pos … L-1`
  (including possible leading zeros) so that the whole number does not exceed `n`.

**Proof.** By induction on `pos` from `L` down to `0`.

*Base (`pos = L`).* No positions left.
`dp[L][cnt][tight]` is defined as `1` if `cnt == k` else `0`.  
Exactly one way (the already built number) satisfies the condition
iff it already contains `k` non‑zero digits – matches the definition.

*Induction step.* Assume the statement holds for `pos+1`.  
From state `(pos,cnt,tight)` we may choose any digit `d` allowed by the
tightness (`0 … limit`).  
`new_cnt` correctly reflects the additional non‑zero digit contributed by `d`.  
`new_tight` is true exactly when the prefix stays equal to `n` after placing `d`.  
All completions of the suffix are counted by `dp[pos+1][new_cnt][new_tight]`
by the induction hypothesis. Summation over all admissible `d` therefore
counts **all** numbers that satisfy the definition for `(pos,cnt,tight)`,
and none else. ∎



##### Lemma 2  
`dp[0][0][1]` equals the number of integers `x` with `1 ≤ x ≤ n`
that contain exactly `k` non‑zero digits.

**Proof.**  
State `(0,0,1)` corresponds to:

* no digit processed yet (`cnt = 0`);
* the prefix is exactly the empty prefix of `n` (`tight = 1`);
* we are about to fill all `L` positions.

By Lemma&nbsp;1, `dp[0][0][1]` counts all completions of the whole length
that do not exceed `n` and end with exactly `k` non‑zero digits.
Every integer `x` with `0 ≤ x ≤ n` has a unique length‑`L` representation
(with possible leading zeros) and is counted once.
Since `k ≥ 1`, the representation of `0` (all zeros) has `0` non‑zero digits
and is **not** counted.  
Thus the counted numbers are precisely those with `1 ≤ x ≤ n`
and exactly `k` non‑zero digits. ∎



##### Theorem  
The algorithm outputs the required answer.

**Proof.**  
The algorithm computes `dp` using the recurrence of Lemma&nbsp;1,
starting from the base case and memoising results.
Finally it prints `dp[0][0][1]`.  
By Lemma&nbsp;2 this value equals the number of integers between `1` and `n`
(inclusive) that contain exactly `k` non‑zero digits. ∎



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 3.   Complexity Analysis  

*`L = len(n) ≤ 100`*  

Number of DP states: `L * (k+1) * 2 ≤ 100 * 101 * 2 = 20 200`.  
For each state we iterate over at most `10` digits.

```
Time   : O(L * k * 10)  ≤ 2·10⁵ operations
Memory : O(L * k * 2)   ≤ 2·10⁴ integers
```

Both are negligible.

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 4.   Reference Implementation  (Python 3)

```python
import sys
sys.setrecursionlimit(10000)

def count_numbers_with_k_nonzero(n_str: str, k: int) -> int:
    """
    Returns the amount of integers x (1 <= x <= n) whose decimal
    representation contains exactly k non‑zero digits.
    n_str : decimal representation of n (no leading zeros, length <= 100)
    k     : required number of non‑zero digits (1 <= k <= 100)
    """
    L = len(n_str)
    digits = list(map(int, n_str))

    # memo[pos][cnt][tight] -> int, use dict for simplicity
    from functools import lru_cache

    @lru_cache(maxsize=None)
    def dfs(pos: int, cnt: int, tight: int) -> int:
        """Number of ways for suffix starting at position pos."""
        if cnt > k:                     # already too many non‑zero digits
            return 0
        if pos == L:                    # all positions processed
            return 1 if cnt == k else 0

        limit = digits[pos] if tight else 9
        total = 0
        for d in range(limit + 1):
            new_cnt = cnt + (1 if d != 0 else 0)
            new_tight = tight and (d == limit)
            total += dfs(pos + 1, new_cnt, new_tight)
        return total

    # start from most significant digit, 0 non‑zero used, tight = True
    return dfs(0, 0, 1)


def main():
    data = sys.stdin.read().strip().split()
    if not data:
        return
    n_str = data[0].strip()
    k = int(data[1])
    ans = count_numbers_with_k_nonzero(n_str, k)
    print(ans)


if __name__ == "__main__":
    main()
```

The program follows exactly the algorithm proven correct above
and conforms to the required input‑output format.

**Solution Explanation**

For a given base `b` ( `2 ≤ b ≤ 62` ) a *polydivisible* number satisfies  

```
value( first 1 digit )   is divisible by 1
value( first 2 digits )  is divisible by 2
value( first 3 digits )  is divisible by 3
...
value( whole number )    is divisible by length
```

The value of a prefix is the ordinary integer value of that prefix when it is
interpreted in base `b`.

The task consists of two parts  

* `is_polydivisible(s, b)` – test whether the digit string `s` is polydivisible
  in base `b`.  
* `get_polydivisible(n, b)` – return the `n`‑th polydivisible number (0‑based)
  written in base `b`.

The program reads a digit string and a base from *stdin*,
calls `is_polydivisible` and prints `True` or `False`.



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 1.  Common utilities  

The characters used for bases up to 62 are

```
CHARS = "0123456789ABCDEFGHIJKLMNOPQRSTUVWXYZabcdefghijklmnopqrstuvwxyz"
```

* `char_to_val[c]` – dictionary that maps a character to its numeric value.
* `int_to_base(x, b)` – converts a non‑negative integer `x` to a string in base
  `b` using the same character set.

Both are straightforward O(1) operations.



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 2.  `is_polydivisible(s, b)`

We walk through the string once, building the value of the current prefix
exactly as we would when converting a number from base `b` to decimal.

```
value = 0
for i, ch in enumerate(s, start=1):          # i = length of current prefix
        value = value * b + char_to_val[ch]   # add next digit
        if value % i != 0:                   # divisibility test fails
                return False
return True
```

*Time* – `O(L)` where `L = len(s)`  
*Memory* – `O(1)`



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 3.  `get_polydivisible(n, b)`

All polydivisible numbers can be built digit by digit:

* a 1‑digit polydivisible number is any digit `d` (`0 ≤ d < b`);
* a `k`‑digit polydivisible number is a `(k‑1)`‑digit polydivisible number
  `p` followed by a digit `d` such that  

  ` (p * b + d) % k == 0 `.

We generate them breadth‑first (by increasing length) until we have
`n+1` numbers (the 0‑th one is `"0"`).

```
queue = [ (0, "0") ]                # (value, representation)
result = ["0"]                      # already have the 0‑th number

while len(result) <= n:
        value, rep = queue.pop(0)   # take the smallest already generated number
        length = len(rep) + 1       # length of the new numbers we will create

        for d in range(b):         # try every possible next digit
                if length > 1 and rep == "0":   # avoid leading zeroes
                        continue
                new_val = value * b + d
                if new_val % length == 0:
                        new_rep = rep + CHARS[d]
                        queue.append( (new_val, new_rep) )
                        result.append(new_rep)
                        if len(result) > n:
                                break
        # the inner loop may have already produced enough numbers
```

The queue always contains numbers in the order they were discovered,
which is exactly the order required for the sequence.

*Time* – proportional to the amount of generated numbers, which is unavoidable
for this problem.  
*Memory* – stores the frontier of the BFS, also proportional to the number of
generated numbers.



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 4.  Main program

```
read the digit string s
read the base b (as int)
print(is_polydivisible(s, b))
```

Only the first function is needed for the given I/O format, but the second
function is implemented as required by the statement.



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 5.  Correctness Proof  

We prove that the provided implementations satisfy the specification.

---

##### Lemma 1  
During the execution of `is_polydivisible`, after processing the first `i`
characters of `s`, the variable `value` equals the integer value of the prefix
`s[0:i]` interpreted in base `b`.

**Proof.** By induction over `i`.

*Base (`i = 1`).*  
`value` is initialised to `0`. After the first iteration  

```
value = 0 * b + digit(s[0]) = digit(s[0])
```

which is exactly the value of the 1‑digit prefix.

*Induction step.* Assume the lemma holds for `i-1`.  
When the `i`‑th character `c` is processed,

```
value_new = value_old * b + digit(c)
```

`value_old` is the value of the first `i-1` digits (induction hypothesis);
multiplying by `b` shifts it left by one digit, adding `digit(c)` appends the
new digit. Hence `value_new` is the value of the first `i` digits. ∎



##### Lemma 2  
`is_polydivisible(s, b)` returns `True` **iff** every prefix of `s` of length `i`
is divisible by `i`.

**Proof.**  
From Lemma&nbsp;1, at the moment the `i`‑th character is processed,
`value` equals the integer value of the length‑`i` prefix.
The algorithm tests `value % i == 0`.  
If any test fails, the function returns `False` immediately.
If all tests succeed, the loop finishes and the function returns `True`. ∎



##### Lemma 3  
In `get_polydivisible`, every string ever inserted into `queue` (and therefore
into `result`) is a polydivisible number.

**Proof.** By construction.  
*Base case*: the initial element `"0"` is trivially polydivisible.  
*Induction step*: assume `rep` is polydivisible of length `k-1`.  
When a digit `d` is appended, the algorithm checks  

```
new_val % k == 0
```

where `new_val` is the value of the new `k`‑digit string.
All shorter prefixes are unchanged, thus still divisible by their lengths,
and the new full length satisfies the required divisibility.
Therefore the new string is polydivisible. ∎



##### Lemma 4  
`result` contains the polydivisible numbers in strictly increasing order of
their length, and for equal length in lexicographic order of their digit
strings.

**Proof.**  
The BFS processes numbers level by level: all numbers of length `1` are taken
from the queue before any number of length `2` is generated, etc.
Within a level the loop iterates digits `0 … b-1` in that order, appending each
valid extension to the end of the queue. Consequently extensions are discovered
in lexicographic order. Whenever a new number is discovered it is appended to
`result` immediately, preserving this order. ∎



##### Lemma 5  
After the `while` loop finishes, `result[n]` is the `n`‑th polydivisible number
(0‑based).

**Proof.**  
`result[0]` is `"0"` – the first polydivisible number.  
By Lemma&nbsp;3 every later entry is a polydivisible number, and by Lemma&nbsp;4
the entries appear exactly in the order defined by the sequence.
The loop stops only after `len(result) > n`, i.e. after the `(n)`‑th index has
been filled. Hence `result[n]` is precisely the required number. ∎



##### Theorem  
* `is_polydivisible(s, b)` returns `True` exactly when `s` is a polydivisible
  number in base `b`.  
* `get_polydivisible(n, b)` returns the `n`‑th polydivisible number (as a
  string) in base `b`.

**Proof.**  
The first claim follows directly from Lemma&nbsp;2.  
The second claim follows from Lemma&nbsp;5. ∎



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 6.  Complexity Analysis  

*`is_polydivisible`*  
`O(L)` time, `O(1)` extra memory (`L = len(s)`).

*`get_polydivisible`*  
Let `P` be the amount of polydivisible numbers generated until the `n`‑th one.
Both time and memory are `O(P)`. This is optimal because each of those numbers
must be examined at least once to know whether it belongs to the sequence.



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 7.  Reference Implementation  (Python 3)

```python
import sys

# ------------------------------------------------------------
#  character set for bases up to 62
CHARS = "0123456789ABCDEFGHIJKLMNOPQRSTUVWXYZabcdefghijklmnopqrstuvwxyz"
char_to_val = {c: i for i, c in enumerate(CHARS)}


def int_to_base(x: int, b: int) -> str:
    """convert non‑negative integer x to a string in base b"""
    if x == 0:
        return "0"
    digits = []
    while x:
        x, r = divmod(x, b)
        digits.append(CHARS[r])
    return "".join(reversed(digits))


def is_polydivisible(s: str, b: int) -> bool:
    """return True iff the digit string s is polydivisible in base b"""
    value = 0
    for i, ch in enumerate(s, start=1):
        value = value * b + char_to_val[ch]
        if value % i != 0:
            return False
    return True


def get_polydivisible(n: int, b: int) -> str:
    """return the n‑th (0‑based) polydivisible number in base b as a string"""
    # start with the only 0‑digit number: "0"
    result = ["0"]
    # queue holds tuples (value, representation)
    queue = [(0, "0")]

    while len(result) <= n:
        value, rep = queue.pop(0)
        next_len = len(rep) + 1

        for d in range(b):
            # avoid leading zeroes for numbers longer than 1 digit
            if next_len > 1 and rep == "0":
                continue
            new_val = value * b + d
            if new_val % next_len == 0:
                new_rep = rep + CHARS[d]
                queue.append((new_val, new_rep))
                result.append(new_rep)
                if len(result) > n:
                    break
    return result[n]


# ------------------------------------------------------------
def main() -> None:
    data = sys.stdin.read().strip().splitlines()
    if len(data) < 2:
        return
    s = data[0].strip()
    b = int(data[1].strip())
    print(is_polydivisible(s, b))


if __name__ == "__main__":
    main()
```

The program follows exactly the algorithm proven correct above and conforms
to the required input / output format.
completed 53/150, Solved: False, number of success = 14/53, acc = 26.42
**Solution Explanation**

For a natural number `n`

```
d(i) = number of positive divisors of i
S(n) = d(1) + d(2) + … + d(n)
```

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 1.  From divisors to a double‑counting problem  

For every divisor `a` of a number `b` we have a pair  

```
(a , b / a)      with   a·(b/a) = b ≤ n
```

Conversely, every pair of natural numbers `(x , y)` with `x·y ≤ n`
produces a divisor `x` of the number `x·y`.  
Therefore the total number of divisor occurrences in the whole interval
`[1 … n]` equals the number of pairs `(x , y)` satisfying `x·y ≤ n`.

```
S(n) = #{ (x , y) ∈ ℕ² | x·y ≤ n }
```

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 2.  A simple formula  

Fix the first component `x`.  
All admissible `y` are `1 … ⌊ n / x ⌋`.  
Hence

```
S(n) = Σ_{x=1..n} ⌊ n / x ⌋                (1)
```

The right‑hand side is called the *divisor summatory function*.
A naïve evaluation needs `O(n)` steps – far too slow for `n ≤ 10¹⁵`.

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 3.  Grouping equal quotients  

When `x` grows, the value `⌊ n / x ⌋` stays constant for a whole interval.
If for a current `x`

```
q = ⌊ n / x ⌋
```

then the next `x` where the quotient changes is

```
next = ⌊ n / q ⌋ + 1
```

All `x` in `[x , next-1]` have the same contribution `q`.
The whole block contributes `q * (next - x)` to the sum.

Repeating this step jumps over whole blocks, therefore the number of
iterations is only about `2·√n` (the well known “hyperbola method”).

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 4.  Algorithm
```
total = 0
x = 1
while x ≤ n:
        q = n // x
        next = n // q + 1          # first x with a smaller quotient
        total += q * (next - x)    # block contribution
        x = next
output total
```
All operations are integer, Python’s arbitrary length integers handle the
range up to `10¹⁵` easily.

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 5.  Correctness Proof  

We prove that the algorithm returns `S(n)`.

---

##### Lemma 1  
For any integer `x (1 ≤ x ≤ n)` let `q = ⌊ n / x ⌋`.  
All integers `y` with `x ≤ y < n // q + 1` satisfy `⌊ n / y ⌋ = q`,
and for `y = n // q + 1` the quotient is strictly smaller.

**Proof.**  
`⌊ n / y ⌋ = q`  ⇔  `q ≤ n / y < q+1` ⇔ `n/(q+1) < y ≤ n/q`.  
Since `x` itself fulfills `⌊ n / x ⌋ = q`, we have `x ≤ n/q`.  
The largest integer `y` with the same quotient is `⌊ n/q ⌋ = n // q`.  
Thus the interval of equal quotients is `[x , n // q]`. ∎



##### Lemma 2  
During one loop iteration the algorithm adds exactly the sum of
`⌊ n / y ⌋` for all `y` in the current block `[x , next-1]`.

**Proof.**  
By Lemma&nbsp;1 the block is precisely the set of `y` with the same
quotient `q = ⌊ n / x ⌋`.  
The algorithm adds `q * (next - x)`, i.e. `q` once for each element of
the block. ∎



##### Lemma 3  
After the `k`‑th iteration the variable `x` equals the first integer
greater than all numbers already processed.

**Proof.**  
Initially `x = 1`, no number is processed.  
Assume before an iteration `x` is the smallest not yet processed integer.
The algorithm computes `next = n // q + 1`, which by Lemma&nbsp;1 is the
first integer whose quotient differs from the current block.
All numbers `< next` belong to the block and are processed now.
The assignment `x = next` makes `x` the smallest not yet processed
integer for the next iteration. ∎



##### Lemma 4  
When the loop terminates (`x > n`) every integer `1 … n` has been
processed exactly once.

**Proof.**  
By Lemma&nbsp;3 the loop invariant “all numbers `< x` are already processed”
holds.  
Termination condition `x > n` therefore means “all numbers `≤ n` are
processed”. No number can be processed twice because each iteration
advances `x` beyond the whole current block (Lemma&nbsp;2). ∎



##### Theorem  
The algorithm outputs `S(n) = Σ_{i=1}^{n} ⌊ n / i ⌋`.

**Proof.**  
By Lemma&nbsp;2 each iteration adds the exact contribution of its block to
the running total.  
By Lemma&nbsp;4 the union of all blocks is the whole set `{1,…,n}`.
Consequently after the last iteration `total` equals the sum of
`⌊ n / i ⌋` over all `i = 1 … n`, i.e. `S(n)`. ∎



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 6.  Complexity Analysis  

Let `m = ⌊√n⌋`.  
The hyperbola method creates at most `2·m` blocks, therefore

```
Time   :  O(√n)          ( ≤ 2·10⁷ operations for n = 10¹⁵ )
Memory :  O(1)           only a few integer variables
```

Both limits satisfy the problem requirements.

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 7.  Reference Implementation  (Python 3)

```python
import sys

def divisor_summatory(n: int) -> int:
    """return S(n) = sum_{i=1..n} floor(n / i) using the hyperbola method"""
    total = 0
    x = 1
    while x <= n:
        q = n // x                     # current quotient
        nxt = n // q + 1               # first x with a smaller quotient
        total += q * (nxt - x)         # block contribution
        x = nxt
    return total

def solve() -> None:
    data = sys.stdin.read().strip().split()
    if not data:
        return
    n = int(data[0])
    print(divisor_summatory(n))

if __name__ == "__main__":
    solve()
```

The program follows exactly the algorithm proven correct above,
conforms to the required input‑output format and runs in `O(√n)` time
with constant extra memory.
completed 54/150, Solved: False, number of success = 14/54, acc = 25.93
**Solution Explanation**

For the two given strings `s1` and `s2`

* only the lower‑case letters `a … z` are relevant,
* for every letter we look at the number of its occurrences in each string,
* the *maximum* of the two numbers is kept **only if it is larger than 1**,
* the kept letters are written as a substring  

```
prefix + (letter repeated maximum times)
```

where  

```
prefix = "1:"   if the maximum comes from s1 only
prefix = "2:"   if the maximum comes from s2 only
prefix = "=:"   if both strings contain the same maximum
```

All substrings are finally ordered

1. decreasing by their length (i.e. by the maximum count),
2. for equal length – ascending lexicographically (ordinary string order, which is the same as ordering by Unicode code points).

The ordered substrings are joined with the character `'/'` and printed.



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### Algorithm
```
read line s1
read line s2

cnt1 = frequency of a‑z in s1
cnt2 = frequency of a‑z in s2

result = empty list
for each letter ch from 'a' to 'z':
        c1 = cnt1[ch] (0 if absent)
        c2 = cnt2[ch] (0 if absent)
        m  = max(c1, c2)
        if m > 1:
                if   c1 > c2 : prefix = "1:"
                elif c2 > c1 : prefix = "2:"
                else          : prefix = "=:"
                result.append( prefix + ch * m )

sort result by key = (-len(substring), substring)

print '/'.join(result)
```

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### Correctness Proof  

We prove that the algorithm prints exactly the string required in the statement.

---

##### Lemma 1  
For every lower‑case letter `ch` the algorithm adds a substring to `result`
iff the maximum of its occurrences in `s1` and `s2` is **strictly larger than 1**,
and the added substring has the required prefix and repeats `ch`
exactly that maximum number of times.

**Proof.**  
The algorithm computes `c1` and `c2`, the exact frequencies of `ch` in the two
strings (by construction of `cnt1` and `cnt2`).  
`m = max(c1,c2)` is therefore the required maximum.
The `if m > 1` guard adds a substring exactly when the maximum is larger than 1.
The three `if/elif/else` branches set the prefix according to the comparison of
`c1` and `c2`, which matches the specification (`1:`, `2:` or `=:`).  
Finally `ch * m` repeats the letter `m` times. ∎



##### Lemma 2  
After the loop, `result` contains **all** substrings that have to appear in the
final answer, and **no other** substrings.

**Proof.**  
*All required substrings* – By Lemma&nbsp;1 a required substring is added for
every letter whose maximum count is > 1, therefore every required substring is
present.  
*No extra substrings* – Lemma&nbsp;1 also shows that a substring is added only
when the maximum count of the corresponding letter is > 1, i.e. exactly the
condition from the statement. Hence no substring that must not appear can be
added. ∎



##### Lemma 3  
The sorting key `(-len, substring)` orders the substrings exactly as demanded:
first by decreasing length, then by increasing lexicographic order for equal
length.

**Proof.**  
`-len` makes longer strings have a smaller key, therefore they appear first
when sorting in ascending order.  
If two substrings have the same length, their `-len` parts are equal, so the
second component `substring` decides the order; Python’s default string order is
lexicographic by Unicode code point, which is precisely the required order. ∎



##### Theorem  
The string printed by the algorithm is exactly the string described in the
problem statement.

**Proof.**  
By Lemma&nbsp;2 the list `result` contains precisely the needed substrings,
each correctly formed (Lemma&nbsp;1).  
Lemma&nbsp;3 guarantees that after sorting they appear in the required order.  
Finally the algorithm joins the ordered substrings with `'/'`, which is the
required separator. Therefore the printed string coincides with the specification. ∎



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### Complexity Analysis  

*Counting* each string scans it once: `O(|s1| + |s2|)` time.  
The loop over the 26 letters is `O(1)`.  
Sorting at most 26 substrings costs `O(26 log 26) = O(1)`.  

Overall time complexity: **`O(|s1| + |s2|)`**  
Memory consumption: two counters of size 26 → **`O(1)`** additional memory.



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### Reference Implementation (Python 3)

```python
import sys
from collections import Counter

def mix(s1: str, s2: str) -> str:
    # count only lower‑case letters
    cnt1 = Counter(ch for ch in s1 if 'a' <= ch <= 'z')
    cnt2 = Counter(ch for ch in s2 if 'a' <= ch <= 'z')

    parts = []
    for code in range(ord('a'), ord('z') + 1):
        ch = chr(code)
        c1 = cnt1.get(ch, 0)
        c2 = cnt2.get(ch, 0)
        m = max(c1, c2)
        if m > 1:
            if c1 > c2:
                prefix = "1:"
            elif c2 > c1:
                prefix = "2:"
            else:
                prefix = "=:"
            parts.append(prefix + ch * m)

    # sort: decreasing length, then lexicographically
    parts.sort(key=lambda s: (-len(s), s))
    return "/".join(parts)


def main() -> None:
    data = sys.stdin.read().splitlines()
    if len(data) < 2:
        return
    s1 = data[0]
    s2 = data[1]
    print(mix(s1, s2))


if __name__ == "__main__":
    main()
```

**Solution Explanation**

For the given expression  

```
[number][op][number]=[number]
```

all characters are decimal digits, ‘‑’, ‘?’, one of the operators ‘+’, ‘‑’, ‘*’
and exactly one ‘=’.
Every ‘?’ stands for the *same* unknown decimal digit.
The unknown digit

* is not already used as a known digit in the expression,
* may be `0`,
* after the substitution every number must be a legal decimal representation
  (no leading zero, except the single digit `0`).

The task is to find the smallest digit that makes the whole equation true,
or `-1` if no digit works.



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 1.   Parsing the expression  

```
left = part before '='
right = part after  '='
```

`left` has the form `firstNumber op secondNumber`.
Only a leading ‘‑’ can belong to a number, therefore the operator is the first
character after the first (possibly signed) block of digits / ‘?’ :

```
i = 0
if left[0] == '-': i = 1                # skip leading minus
while i < len(left) and left[i] in '0123456789?':
        i += 1
op      = left[i]                       # '+', '-', '*'
first   = left[0 : i]                   # string of the first number
second  = left[i+1 : ]                  # string of the second number
```

Now we have three strings `first`, `second`, `right` that may contain ‘?’.



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 2.   Helper – is a substituted number legal ?

```
def legal(s):
        # s already contains only digits and possibly a leading '-'
        if s[0] == '-':
                core = s[1:]
                # "-0" is not allowed, nor "-01", …
                return len(core) == 1 or core[0] != '0'
        else:
                return len(s) == 1 or s[0] != '0'
```

The function returns `True` iff the string is a correct decimal representation
according to the statement.



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 3.   Trying all possible digits  

* `used` – set of all known digits appearing in the original expression  
  (`{'0','1',…}` – only the characters that are already digits)
* For every digit `d = 0 … 9`
    * skip it if `str(d)` is in `used`
    * substitute `?` → `str(d)` in the three strings
    * check the three numbers with `legal`
    * evaluate the left side with the operator and compare with the right side
    * if they are equal, store `d`

The smallest stored digit is the answer, otherwise `-1`.



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 4.   Correctness Proof  

We prove that the algorithm returns exactly the required digit.

---

##### Lemma 1  
`legal(s)` returns `True` **iff** `s` is a valid decimal integer according to
the problem statement.

**Proof.**  
Two cases.

*`s` starts with ‘-’* – the integer part after the sign must not have a leading
zero unless it consists of the single digit `0`.  
`legal` checks exactly this (`len(core)==1` or `core[0]!='0'`).

*`s` does not start with ‘-’* – the whole string must not start with `0` unless
its length is one.  
`legal` checks exactly this (`len(s)==1` or `s[0]!='0'`). ∎



##### Lemma 2  
For a fixed digit `d` the algorithm accepts `d`
*iff* after replacing every ‘?’ by `d`

* all three numbers are legal, and
* the arithmetic equation holds.

**Proof.**  
The algorithm performs exactly those three tests:

1. substitution,
2. `legal` on each of the three resulting strings,
3. evaluation of `first op second` and comparison with `right`.

If all three succeed the algorithm stores `d`; otherwise it discards `d`. ∎



##### Lemma 3  
If a digit `d` satisfies the conditions of the problem,
the algorithm will store `d`.

**Proof.**  
Such a digit `d` is not a known digit, therefore `str(d)` is not in `used`
and the algorithm does **not** skip it.
After substitution the three numbers are legal (by the problem statement),
so `legal` returns `True` for each of them.
The equation is true, therefore the evaluation test succeeds.
Consequently, by Lemma&nbsp;2 the algorithm stores `d`. ∎



##### Lemma 4  
If the algorithm stores a digit `d`, then `d` satisfies the conditions of the
problem.

**Proof.**  
A stored digit passed all three tests of Lemma&nbsp;2, i.e.

* it is not a known digit,
* after substitution the three numbers are legal (Lemma&nbsp;1),
* the arithmetic equation holds.

Thus `d` fulfills exactly the required conditions. ∎



##### Theorem  
The algorithm outputs  

* the smallest digit that makes the expression correct, or  
* `-1` if no such digit exists.

**Proof.**  

*Existence.*  
If at least one digit works, by Lemma&nbsp;3 every such digit is stored.
The algorithm returns the minimum of the stored digits, therefore the smallest
working digit.

*Non‑existence.*  
If no digit works, Lemma&nbsp;4 shows that the algorithm stores nothing.
Consequently it returns `-1`.

Both cases match the specification. ∎



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 5.   Complexity Analysis  

*Parsing* – `O(L)` where `L` ≤ length of the expression ( ≤ 30 ).
*Trying digits* – at most 10 candidates, each needs three substitutions and a
constant‑time evaluation: `O(10·L) = O(L)`.
Overall time `O(L)`, memory `O(L)` for the three substrings.



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 6.   Reference Implementation  (Python 3)

```python
import sys

def legal(num: str) -> bool:
    """return True iff num is a legal decimal integer (no leading zeros)."""
    if num[0] == '-':
        core = num[1:]
        # "-0" or "-01" … are illegal
        return len(core) == 1 or core[0] != '0'
    else:
        return len(num) == 1 or num[0] != '0'


def solve_expression(expr: str) -> int:
    expr = expr.strip()

    # split at '='
    eq_pos = expr.find('=')
    left = expr[:eq_pos]
    right = expr[eq_pos + 1:]

    # find operator in left part
    i = 0
    if left[0] == '-':
        i = 1
    while i < len(left) and (left[i].isdigit() or left[i] == '?'):
        i += 1
    op = left[i]                     # '+', '-', '*'
    first = left[:i]
    second = left[i + 1:]

    # digits already known in the expression
    used_digits = {ch for ch in expr if ch.isdigit()}

    possible = []

    for d in range(10):
        dch = str(d)
        if dch in used_digits:
            continue

        a = first.replace('?', dch)
        b = second.replace('?', dch)
        c = right.replace('?', dch)

        if not (legal(a) and legal(b) and legal(c)):
            continue

        ai = int(a)
        bi = int(b)
        ci = int(c)

        if op == '+':
            ok = (ai + bi == ci)
        elif op == '-':
            ok = (ai - bi == ci)
        else:               # '*'
            ok = (ai * bi == ci)

        if ok:
            possible.append(d)

    return min(possible) if possible else -1


def main() -> None:
    data = sys.stdin.read().strip()
    if not data:
        return
    # the whole line is the expression
    result = solve_expression(data)
    print(result)


if __name__ == "__main__":
    main()
```

The program follows exactly the algorithm proven correct above and conforms
to the required input‑output format.

**Solution Explanation**

The input line contains an English representation of a non‑negative integer.
Only the words listed below can appear  

```
zero, one, two, three, four, five, six, seven, eight, nine,
ten, eleven, twelve, thirteen, fourteen, fifteen, sixteen,
seventeen, eighteen, nineteen,
twenty, thirty, forty, fifty, sixty, seventy, eighty, ninety,
hundred, thousand, million
```

The word *and* may be present – it has no influence on the value.
Hyphens (e.g. *eighty‑three*) are also possible.

The task is to convert the words into the corresponding integer
(0 … 1 000 000).

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 1.  Observations  

* All numbers are built from *units* (zero … ninety) and *multipliers*
  (`hundred`, `thousand`, `million`).
* While scanning the words from left to right we can keep two
  accumulators  

  *`current`* – the value of the part that has not yet been multiplied by a
  larger unit (`thousand`, `million`).  
  *`total`*   – the sum of already finished parts.

* When we meet a multiplier

  * `hundred` multiplies the *current* part by 100.
  * `thousand` or `million` finishes the current part:
    `total += current * multiplier` and resets `current` to 0.

* At the end we add the remaining `current` to `total`.

The algorithm works for every valid input up to one million.

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 2.  Algorithm
```
read line
replace '-' by ' '
split line into tokens
remove every token equal to "and"

number = {
    "zero":0, "one":1, ..., "nineteen":19,
    "twenty":20, "thirty":30, ..., "ninety":90
}
multiplier = {"thousand":1000, "million":1000000}

current = 0
total   = 0

for token in tokens:
    if token in number:
        current += number[token]
    elif token == "hundred":
        current *= 100
    elif token in multiplier:
        total   += current * multiplier[token]
        current = 0
    # (no other tokens appear)

total += current
print(total)
```

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 3.  Correctness Proof  

We prove that the algorithm outputs the integer represented by the input
string.

---

**Lemma 1**  
After processing any prefix of the token list, `current` equals the
numeric value of the last *unfinished* group (the part that has not yet
been multiplied by `thousand` or `million`), and `total` equals the sum
of all *finished* groups that appear earlier in the string.

*Proof by induction over the processed tokens.*

*Base*: before any token is processed, both groups are empty,
`current = 0`, `total = 0`. The statement holds.

*Induction step*: assume the lemma holds after processing a prefix.
Consider the next token `t`.

*Case 1 – `t` is a basic number (e.g. "seven")*  
`current` is increased by its value, exactly extending the unfinished
group. `total` stays unchanged – the invariant holds.

*Case 2 – `t` is "hundred"*  
The unfinished group must already contain a number `x` (by the grammar
of English numbers). Multiplying it by 100 yields the correct value of
the group after the word "hundred". The algorithm does `current *= 100`,
so the invariant holds.

*Case 3 – `t` is "thousand" or "million"*  
The unfinished group now becomes a finished group whose value must be
multiplied by the corresponding multiplier `m`. The algorithm adds
`current * m` to `total` and resets `current` to 0, exactly matching the
definition of finishing that group. The invariant holds.

Thus the lemma is true for every prefix. ∎



**Lemma 2**  
When the loop terminates, `total + current` equals the numeric value of
the whole input string.

*Proof.* By Lemma&nbsp;1 after the last token has been processed,
`total` contains the sum of all groups that were finished by a
multiplier, while `current` holds the value of the final group (which
has no following multiplier). Adding them yields the complete number. ∎



**Theorem**  
The algorithm prints the integer represented by the input string.

*Proof.* After the loop the algorithm executes `total += current`,
so the variable `total` becomes exactly the value described in
Lemma&nbsp;2. The printed value is therefore the correct integer. ∎



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 4.  Complexity Analysis  

Let *n* be the number of tokens (at most a few dozen).  
All operations inside the loop are O(1).  

*Time*: **O(n)** – linear in the length of the input.  
*Memory*: **O(1)** – only a few integer variables and constant‑size
dictionaries.

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 5.  Reference Implementation  (Python 3)

```python
import sys

def word_to_int(s: str) -> int:
    # replace hyphens, split, drop the optional "and"
    tokens = s.replace('-', ' ').split()
    tokens = [t for t in tokens if t != 'and']

    # basic numbers
    numbers = {
        "zero": 0, "one": 1, "two": 2, "three": 3, "four": 4,
        "five": 5, "six": 6, "seven": 7, "eight": 8, "nine": 9,
        "ten": 10, "eleven": 11, "twelve": 12, "thirteen": 13,
        "fourteen": 14, "fifteen": 15, "sixteen": 16,
        "seventeen": 17, "eighteen": 18, "nineteen": 19,
        "twenty": 20, "thirty": 30, "forty": 40, "fifty": 50,
        "sixty": 60, "seventy": 70, "eighty": 80, "ninety": 90,
    }

    multipliers = {
        "thousand": 1000,
        "million": 1000000,
    }

    current = 0
    total = 0

    for token in tokens:
        if token in numbers:
            current += numbers[token]
        elif token == "hundred":
            current *= 100
        elif token in multipliers:
            total += current * multipliers[token]
            current = 0
        # no other tokens appear (input is guaranteed valid)

    total += current
    return total


def solve() -> None:
    line = sys.stdin.readline().strip()
    if not line:
        return
    result = word_to_int(line)
    print(result)


if __name__ == "__main__":
    solve()
```

conforms to the required input‑output format.
completed 57/150, Solved: False, number of success = 15/57, acc = 26.32
**Solution Explanation**

For a given `n` we have to count how many ordered `k`‑tuples  

```
(a1 , a2 , … , ak)      (ai ≥ 1)
```

satisfy  

```
a1 · a2 · … · ak = n
```

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 1.  Prime factorisation

Write `n` as a product of primes  

```
n = p1^e1 · p2^e2 · … · pm^em          (ei ≥ 1)
```

`m` is at most 9 for `n ≤ 5·10^8` (because 2·3·5·7·11·13·17·19·23 > 5·10^8).

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 2.  Distributing the exponents

For a fixed prime `pi` we have to decide how many copies of `pi`
appear in each component of the tuple.
If we denote these numbers by  

```
xi,1 , xi,2 , … , xi,k   (xi,j ≥ 0)
```

they must satisfy  

```
xi,1 + xi,2 + … + xi,k = ei
```

The number of non‑negative integer solutions of this equation is the
classical *stars‑and‑bars* result  

```
C( ei + k – 1 , k – 1 )
```

The choices for different primes are independent, therefore the total
number of ordered `k`‑tuples is

```
answer =   ∏  C( ei + k – 1 , k – 1 )
           i=1..m
```

If `n = 1` the product over an empty set is defined as `1`
(the only possible tuple is `(1,1,…,1)`).

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 3.  Computing the binomial coefficients

`k` can be as large as `1000`, while `ei` is at most `29`
(`2^29 > 5·10^8`).  
The arguments of the binomial are at most `ei + k – 1 ≤ 1028`,
so the values easily fit into Python’s arbitrary length integers.
The standard library function `math.comb` (available from Python 3.8)
computes them directly and efficiently.

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 4.  Algorithm
```
read n, k
factorise n by trial division up to sqrt(n)          // O(√n)
result = 1
for each exponent e found in the factorisation:
        result *= comb(e + k - 1, k - 1)
print result
```

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 5.  Correctness Proof  

We prove that the algorithm outputs exactly the number of ordered
`k`‑tuples of positive integers whose product equals `n`.

---

##### Lemma 1  
For a fixed prime `p` with exponent `e` in `n`,
the number of ways to assign non‑negative exponents
`x1,…,xk` to the `k` tuple components such that  
`x1 + … + xk = e` equals `C(e + k – 1 , k – 1)`.

**Proof.** Classic stars‑and‑bars: write `e` stars and insert `k‑1`
bars to split them into `k` groups (empty groups allowed). The number
of positions for the bars among `e + k – 1` slots is the binomial
coefficient. ∎



##### Lemma 2  
For a given prime factorisation `n = ∏ pi^ei`,
the number of ordered `k`‑tuples whose product is `n` equals  

```
∏  C( ei + k – 1 , k – 1 )
```

**Proof.**  
For each prime `pi` we must decide how many copies of `pi` go into each
tuple component; by Lemma&nbsp;1 there are `C(ei + k – 1 , k – 1)` possibilities.
Choices for different primes are independent because the exponent of a
prime in a component is determined solely by the choice for that prime.
Multiplying the independent counts yields the total number of tuples. ∎



##### Lemma 3  
If `n = 1` the algorithm returns `1`.

**Proof.** The factorisation loop finds no prime factors, therefore the
product over the empty set leaves `result = 1`. The only tuple of
positive integers with product `1` is `(1,…,1)`. ∎



##### Theorem  
The algorithm prints the exact number of ordered `k`‑tuples of positive
integers whose product equals the given `n`.

**Proof.**  
The algorithm obtains the prime exponents `ei` of `n`.  
For each exponent it multiplies `result` by `C(ei + k – 1 , k – 1)`,
exactly the factor appearing in Lemma&nbsp;2.
Consequently after processing all primes `result` equals the product
stated in Lemma&nbsp;2, which by that lemma is precisely the desired
count. The special case `n = 1` is handled correctly by Lemma&nbsp;3.
Thus the printed value is correct. ∎



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 6.  Complexity Analysis  

*Factorisation* – trial division up to `√n`  
`O(√n)` time, `O(1)` extra space.

*Binomial multiplications* – at most the number of distinct primes of `n`
(`≤ 9` for the given limits) → negligible.

Overall  

```
Time   : O(√n)   ≤ O(2·10^4)   (fast for n ≤ 5·10^8)
Memory : O(1)    (apart from Python big integers for the answer)
```

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 7.  Reference Implementation  (Python 3)

```python
import sys
import math

def count_ordered_factorisations(n: int, k: int) -> int:
    """Return the number of ordered k‑tuples of positive integers whose product is n."""
    if n == 1:
        # only (1,1,...,1)
        return 1

    result = 1
    temp = n
    p = 2
    # trial division up to sqrt(temp)
    while p * p <= temp:
        if temp % p == 0:
            exp = 0
            while temp % p == 0:
                temp //= p
                exp += 1
            # multiply by C(exp + k - 1, k - 1)
            result *= math.comb(exp + k - 1, k - 1)
        p += 1 if p == 2 else 2   # after 2 test only odd numbers
    # if a prime factor > sqrt(original n) remains
    if temp > 1:
        # exponent is 1
        result *= math.comb(1 + k - 1, k - 1)   # = k
    return result

def solve() -> None:
    data = sys.stdin.read().strip().split()
    if not data:
        return
    n = int(data[0])
    k = int(data[1]) if len(data) > 1 else 0
    ans = count_ordered_factorisations(n, k)
    print(ans)

if __name__ == "__main__":
    solve()
```
