completed 95/150, Solved: False, number of success = 18/95, acc = 18.95
completed 96/150, Solved: False, number of success = 18/96, acc = 18.75
completed 97/150, Solved: False, number of success = 18/97, acc = 18.56
completed 98/150, Solved: False, number of success = 18/98, acc = 18.37
**Solution Explanation**

For a polynomial  

```
aₙ·xⁿ + aₙ₋₁·xⁿ⁻¹ + … + a₁·x + a₀
```

the derivative is  

```
n·aₙ·xⁿ⁻¹ + (n‑1)·aₙ₋₁·xⁿ⁻² + … + 1·a₁
```

The task is:

1. read a polynomial given as a *string* (e.g. `"12x+2"` or `"x^2+3x+2"`),
2. read an integer `x₀`,
3. compute the derivative of the polynomial,
4. evaluate the derivative at `x = x₀`,
5. output the resulting integer.

Only the following term forms can appear (as guaranteed by the statement)

* constant                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                  
completed 99/150, Solved: False, number of success = 18/99, acc = 18.18
**Solution Explanation**

For a string `s` we have to return the longest *contiguous* palindrome.
If several palindromes have the same maximal length we must return the one
that appears first.

The well known linear‑time algorithm for this task is **Manacher’s algorithm**.
It computes for every possible centre the radius of the longest palindrome
that is centred there.  
From those radii we can obtain the length and the start position of every
palindrome and keep the best one.

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 1.   Definitions  

* `n = len(s)`  

* `d1[i]` – radius of the longest **odd** length palindrome with centre `i`  

  `d1[i] = k`  means  

  ```
  s[i‑k+1 … i … i+k‑1]   is a palindrome
  length = 2·k‑1
  start  = i‑k+1
  ```

* `d2[i]` – radius of the longest **even** length palindrome whose centre is
  between `i‑1` and `i`  

  `d2[i] = k` means  

  ```
  s[i‑k … i‑1]  and  s[i … i+k‑1]   are mirrored
  length = 2·k
  start  = i‑k
  ```

Both arrays are built in `O(n)` using the classic “left‑right” trick of
Manacher.

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 2.   Computing `d1` (odd palindromes)

```
l = 0, r = -1                     # current rightmost palindrome [l … r]
for i = 0 … n‑1
        if i > r:   k = 1
        else:       k = min( d1[l+r-i] , r-i+1 )
        while i‑k ≥ 0 and i+k < n and s[i‑k] == s[i+k]:
                k += 1
        d1[i] = k
        k -= 1
        if i+k > r:
                l = i‑k
                r = i+k
```

The same idea (with a slightly different initial `k`) gives `d2`.

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 3.   Selecting the answer  

While scanning the two radius arrays we keep the best palindrome seen so far.

```
best_len   = 0
best_start = 0

for i = 0 … n‑1
        # odd
        cur_len = 2*d1[i] - 1
        start   = i - d1[i] + 1
        update_if_better(cur_len, start)

        # even
        cur_len = 2*d2[i]
        start   = i - d2[i]
        update_if_better(cur_len, start)
```

`update_if_better` replaces the current best when

* `cur_len > best_len`, or
* `cur_len == best_len` **and** `start < best_start`
  (earlier occurrence).

Finally the answer is `s[best_start : best_start + best_len]`.

The algorithm works in `O(n)` time and `O(n)` additional memory,
which satisfies the required linear performance.

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 4.   Correctness Proof  

We prove that the algorithm returns exactly the first longest palindromic
substring.

---

##### Lemma 1  
For every position `i` the value `d1[i]` computed by the algorithm equals the
maximum `k` such that `s[i‑k+1 … i … i+k‑1]` is a palindrome.

**Proof.**  
The algorithm maintains the invariant that `[l … r]` is the rightmost palindrome
found so far.  
If `i` lies outside this interval (`i > r`) we start with `k = 1`, i.e. the
trivial palindrome consisting of the centre character only – this is the
largest possible radius we can guarantee without any knowledge.

If `i` lies inside `[l … r]` we know that the palindrome centred at the
mirrored position `j = l+r-i` is already computed (`d1[j]`).  
The part of the palindrome around `i` that is guaranteed to be inside
`[l … r]` has radius `min(d1[j], r-i+1)`.  
Thus `k` is at least that value, and the subsequent `while` loop expands `k`
as long as the characters match. When the loop stops, the next expansion would
break the palindrome property, therefore the final `k` is maximal. ∎



##### Lemma 2  
Analogously, for every `i` the value `d2[i]` equals the maximum `k` such that
`s[i‑k … i‑1]` and `s[i … i+k‑1]` are mirrored (i.e. the longest even palindrome
with centre between `i‑1` and `i`).

*Proof.* Identical to Lemma&nbsp;1, only the initial radius is `0` because an
even‑length palindrome may be empty. ∎



##### Lemma 3  
For each palindrome `p` occurring in `s` there exists an index `i` such that
`p` is exactly the palindrome described by `d1[i]` (if `|p|` is odd) or by
`d2[i]` (if `|p|` is even).

*Proof.*  
Take the centre of `p`.  
If `|p|` is odd, the centre is a character at position `c`; the palindrome
radius around `c` is precisely `d1[c]`.  
If `|p|` is even, the centre lies between positions `c‑1` and `c`; the radius
is `d2[c]`. By Lemma&nbsp;1 and Lemma&nbsp;2 those radii are maximal, therefore
the palindrome reconstructed from the radius equals `p`. ∎



##### Lemma 4  
During the final scan the pair `(best_len, best_start)` is always the longest
palindrome seen so far, and among those of equal length the earliest one.

*Proof.*  
The scan examines every palindrome described by `d1[i]` and `d2[i]`.  
When a palindrome `(len, start)` is examined, `update_if_better` replaces the
current best exactly when the new palindrome is longer, or when it has the
same length but a smaller start index. Hence after processing any prefix of
the scan, the stored pair satisfies the claim for that prefix. By induction
the claim holds after the whole scan. ∎



##### Lemma 5  
`best_len` equals the length of the longest palindromic substring of `s`,
and `best_start` is the smallest start index among all substrings of that
length.

*Proof.*  
By Lemma&nbsp;3 every palindromic substring appears in the scan, therefore the
set of examined candidates is exactly the set of all palindromic substrings.
Lemma&nbsp;4 tells us that after the complete scan the stored pair is the
maximum‑length element of this set, breaking ties by smallest start index.
∎



##### Theorem  
The algorithm outputs the first (leftmost) longest contiguous palindromic
substring of the input string `s`.

*Proof.*  
From Lemma&nbsp;5 the algorithm determines `best_start` and `best_len` that
describe precisely the leftmost longest palindrome. The returned slice
`s[best_start : best_start+best_len]` is therefore exactly the required
substring. ∎



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 5.   Complexity Analysis  

*Building `d1`* – one linear pass, each character examined at most twice:  
`O(n)` time, `O(n)` memory.

*Building `d2`* – same: `O(n)` time, `O(n)` memory.

*Final scan* – another linear pass: `O(n)` time, `O(1)` extra memory.

Overall  

* **Time:** `O(n)`  
* **Memory:** `O(n)` (two integer arrays of length `n`)

Both satisfy the required linear performance for strings up to 100 000
characters.

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 6.   Reference Implementation  (Python 3)

```python
import sys

def longest_palindromic_substring(s: str) -> str:
    n = len(s)
    if n == 0:
        return ""

    # ---------- odd length ----------
    d1 = [0] * n
    l = 0
    r = -1
    for i in range(n):
        k = 1 if i > r else min(d1[l + r - i], r - i + 1)
        while i - k >= 0 and i + k < n and s[i - k] == s[i + k]:
            k += 1
        d1[i] = k
        k -= 1
        if i + k > r:
            l = i - k
            r = i + k

    # ---------- even length ----------
    d2 = [0] * n
    l = 0
    r = -1
    for i in range(n):
        k = 0 if i > r else min(d2[l + r - i + 1], r - i + 1)
        while i - k - 1 >= 0 and i + k < n and s[i - k - 1] == s[i + k]:
            k += 1
        d2[i] = k
        k -= 1
        if i + k > r:
            l = i - k - 1
            r = i + k

    # ---------- choose the best ----------
    best_len = 0
    best_start = 0

    for i in range(n):
        # odd palindrome
        cur_len = 2 * d1[i] - 1
        start = i - d1[i] + 1
        if cur_len > best_len or (cur_len == best_len and start < best_start):
            best_len = cur_len
            best_start = start

        # even palindrome
        cur_len = 2 * d2[i]
        start = i - d2[i]
        if cur_len > best_len or (cur_len == best_len and start < best_start):
            best_len = cur_len
            best_start = start

    return s[best_start:best_start + best_len]


def main() -> None:
    data = sys.stdin.read().splitlines()
    # the problem statement gives a single line input, but we are robust:
    s = data[0] if data else ""
    print(longest_palindromic_substring(s))


if __name__ == "__main__":
    main()
```

The program follows exactly the algorithm proven correct above and conforms
to the required input‑output format.
completed 100/150, Solved: True, number of success = 19/100, acc = 19.0
**Solution Explanation**

For every element we know

* its current position `i` ( `1 … n` )
* its value `a[i]`

If the whole array is sorted increasingly we obtain another array  

`b[1] < b[2] < … < b[n]` – the same numbers, only ordered.

For each value we can write down where it has to be after the whole array is
sorted.

```
pos[value] = index of this value in the sorted array b
```

Because all numbers are different `pos` is a permutation of `{1 … n}`.

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 1.   What does sorting one subsequence do ?

Take a subsequence `S = {i1 < i2 < … < ik}`.
Before sorting the values on these positions are  

`a[i1] , a[i2] , … , a[ik]`.

After sorting the subsequence they become the same numbers,
but ordered increasingly and placed again on the same positions:

```
sorted( a[i1] , … , a[ik] )  →  positions i1 , i2 , … , ik
```

So **only the elements whose original positions belong to `S` can move
inside `S`** – nothing can leave the set and nothing from outside can enter.

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 2.   When is a partition feasible ?

Consider a position `i`.  
The element that finally has to stay at `i` is the value `b[i]`.  
Its original position is `orig[i] = index of b[i]` in the initial array.

For the whole array to become sorted we need

```
i and orig[i] must belong to the same subsequence
```

otherwise the needed element could never be moved to `i`.

Define the permutation  

```
p[i] = the final position of the element that is currently at i
     = pos[ a[i] ]                (1‑based)
```

`p[i]` tells where the element of the current position `i` has to go.
The condition above is exactly

```
i and p[i] must be in the same subsequence
```

Therefore a subsequence has to be **closed under the permutation `p`**:
if it contains a position, it must also contain the image of this position,
the image of the image, … – the whole cycle of `p`.

Consequences  

* a feasible subsequence is a union of whole cycles of `p`;
* a cycle cannot be split, otherwise some element would need to leave the
  subsequence;
* to obtain the *maximum* number of subsequences we simply take **one
  subsequence per cycle**.

Thus the answer is:  

`k = number of cycles of the permutation p`  
and the required subsequences are exactly those cycles.

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 3.   Algorithm
```
read n and the array a[1…n]

# 1. build the permutation p
make list pairs = (value, original_index) for all positions
sort pairs by value → this is the sorted array b
for sorted_position = 1 … n:
        original_index = pairs[sorted_position-1].index
        p[original_index] = sorted_position          # where this element must go

# 2. find all cycles of p
visited[1…n] = false
cycles = empty list
for i = 1 … n:
        if not visited[i]:
                cur = i
                cycle = empty list
                while not visited[cur]:
                        visited[cur] = true
                        cycle.append(cur)
                        cur = p[cur]                # follow the permutation
                cycles.append(cycle)

# 3. output
print number of cycles
for each cycle in cycles:
        print length_of_cycle, followed by its indices (any order)
```

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 4.   Correctness Proof  

We prove that the algorithm outputs a partition with the maximum possible
number of subsequences.

---

##### Lemma 1  
For any feasible partition, each subsequence is a union of whole cycles of
the permutation `p`.

**Proof.**  
Take a subsequence `S` of a feasible partition and a position `x ∈ S`.  
The element currently at `x` must finally be at position `p[x]`.  
Because after all operations the whole array is sorted, this element must be
able to move to `p[x]`; therefore `p[x]` also belongs to `S`.  
Applying the same argument repeatedly we obtain that the whole orbit
`x, p[x], p[p[x]], …` – i.e. the whole cycle of `p` containing `x` – is
contained in `S`. ∎



##### Lemma 2  
If a set of indices is a whole cycle of `p`, then sorting exactly this set
makes all its elements appear at their correct final positions.

**Proof.**  
Let the cycle be `c1 → c2 → … → ck → c1`.  
The values originally at these positions are `a[c1], … , a[ck]`.  
Because `p[ci] = ci+1` (indices modulo `k`), after sorting the set the
smallest value among them will be placed at the smallest index,
the second smallest at the second smallest index, … .
But the order of the values inside the cycle is exactly the order of the
sorted array `b`, therefore each `a[ci]` ends up at position `p[ci]`,
its required final position. ∎



##### Lemma 3  
The partition produced by the algorithm (one subsequence per cycle) is
feasible.

**Proof.**  
By construction each subsequence is a whole cycle of `p`.  
By Lemma&nbsp;2 sorting a cycle puts every element of the cycle to its
required final position.  
All cycles are disjoint, therefore after sorting every cycle the whole
array becomes the sorted array `b`. ∎



##### Lemma 4  
No feasible partition can contain more subsequences than the number of
cycles of `p`.

**Proof.**  
By Lemma&nbsp;1 each subsequence must contain whole cycles.
Different subsequences are disjoint, hence each cycle can belong to at most
one subsequence.  
Consequently the number of subsequences is bounded by the number of cycles. ∎



##### Theorem  
The algorithm outputs a feasible partition with the maximum possible number
of subsequences.

**Proof.**  
The algorithm outputs exactly one subsequence for each cycle, therefore the
number of subsequences equals the number of cycles.
By Lemma&nbsp;3 the partition is feasible.
By Lemma&nbsp;4 no feasible partition can have more subsequences.
Hence the algorithm achieves the optimum. ∎



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 5.   Complexity Analysis  

*Sorting* `n` pairs – `O(n log n)` time.  
*Finding cycles* – each index is visited once – `O(n)` time.  

Total time: `O(n log n)`.

Additional memory:

* the array `p` – `O(n)`
* the visited flag array – `O(n)`
* the list of cycles – together `O(n)` indices.

So `O(n)` extra memory.

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 6.   Reference Implementation  (Python 3)

```python
import sys

def solve() -> None:
    data = sys.stdin.buffer.read().split()
    if not data:
        return
    it = iter(data)
    n = int(next(it))
    a = [int(next(it)) for _ in range(n)]

    # 1. build permutation p (1‑based indices)
    pairs = [(a[i], i + 1) for i in range(n)]          # (value, original_index)
    pairs.sort(key=lambda x: x[0])                    # sorted by value -> array b
    p = [0] * (n + 1)                                 # p[original_index] = final_position
    for final_pos, (_, orig_idx) in enumerate(pairs, start=1):
        p[orig_idx] = final_pos

    # 2. find cycles
    visited = [False] * (n + 1)
    cycles = []
    for i in range(1, n + 1):
        if not visited[i]:
            cur = i
            cycle = []
            while not visited[cur]:
                visited[cur] = True
                cycle.append(cur)
                cur = p[cur]
            cycles.append(cycle)

    # 3. output
    out_lines = [str(len(cycles))]
    for cyc in cycles:
        out_lines.append(str(len(cyc)) + ' ' + ' '.join(map(str, cyc)))
    sys.stdout.write('\n'.join(out_lines))

if __name__ == "__main__":
    solve()
```

The program follows exactly the algorithm proven correct above and conforms
to the required input‑output format.
completed 101/150, Solved: False, number of success = 19/101, acc = 18.81
**Solution Explanation**

For every voter we know  

* `p[i]` – the party he will vote for now (`1 … m`)  
* `c[i]` – how many bytecoins we have to pay to make him vote for any
  other party (we will always change his vote to party **1**).

The United Party has index `1`.  
We have to spend the smallest possible total amount of money so that after
the bribery

```
votes for party 1  >  votes for every other party
```

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 1.   Observations  

* Bribing a voter that already votes for party `1` is useless – it can only
  decrease the number of votes of the United Party.
* If we bribe a voter of another party, we will always give his vote to
  party `1`.  
  (Giving it to a third party never helps the inequality.)
* After bribing a voter of party `x (x>1)`  

  * party `1` gets **+1** vote,
  * party `x` gets **‑1** vote.

So the whole problem is: choose a set of voters (all from parties `2 … m`)
to move to party `1` with minimum total cost, such that finally

```
votes1  >  max_j (votesj) ,   j = 2 … m
```

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 2.   Fix the final number of votes of party 1  

Assume after all bribery party 1 will have exactly `k` votes  
(`k` is at least the current number of its votes and at most `n`).

*For a fixed `k` we can compute the cheapest possible total cost.*

* For every other party `j`

```
cntj = current number of its voters
```

  In the final situation it may have at most `k‑1` votes,
  therefore we **must** bribe at least  

```
needj = max(0 , cntj - (k-1))
```

  of its voters (the cheapest ones).  
  Those `needj` voters are *forced* – we have no choice.

* After bribing all forced voters

```
forced_bribed = Σ needj                (total number of forced voters)
forced_cost   = Σ (sum of the cheapest needj costs of party j)
```

  Party 1 now has  

```
cur_votes = current_votes_of_party1 + forced_bribed
```

*If `cur_votes` is already `≥ k` we are done – the forced bribery already
gives party 1 at least `k` votes and all other parties have `≤ k‑1` votes.*

*Otherwise we still need*

```
extra = k - cur_votes
```

  additional voters.  
  They may be taken from **any** still un‑bribed voter (the ones we did not
  use as forced).  
  To minimise the cost we simply take the `extra` cheapest among them.

The total cost for this `k` is

```
forced_cost + (cost of the extra cheapest voters, if needed)
```

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 3.   Searching the best `k`

The answer is the minimum of the above cost over all feasible `k`.

`k` can be any integer from  

```
max(1 , current_votes_of_party1)   …   n
```

(`k = 0` is impossible because party 1 could never have strictly more votes).

`n ≤ 3000`, therefore trying all `k` is completely fast enough.

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 4.   Data structures  

* `party[v]` – list of costs of voters that currently support party `v`
  (`v = 1 … m`).  
  After reading the input each list is sorted increasingly.
* `pref[v][t]` – prefix sums of the sorted list of party `v`  

  `pref[v][t] = sum of the t cheapest costs of this party` (`pref[v][0]=0`).

Both are built once, in `O(n log n)` time.

During the loop over `k`

* for every party we read `needj` and add `pref[j][needj]` to `forced_cost`
* the *optional* voters (those not forced) are collected into a temporary
  Python list `optional`. Its total size over all parties is at most `n`.
* if we need extra voters we sort `optional` ( `O(n log n)` ) and take the
  first `extra` elements.

Overall complexity  

```
for each k ( ≤ n ) we scan all parties ( ≤ m )   →   O(n·m)   ≤ 9·10⁶
plus a sort of at most n numbers for each k      →   O(n² log n)   ≤ 3·10⁷
```

With `n = 3000` this easily fits into the time limit in Python.

Memory consumption: the lists of costs – `O(n)`.

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 5.   Correctness Proof  

We prove that the algorithm outputs the minimum possible total amount of
bytecoins.

---

##### Lemma 1  
For a fixed final number of votes `k` of party 1, any feasible bribery must
bribe at least `needj = max(0, cntj-(k-1))` voters of every other party `j`.

**Proof.**  
After bribery party `j` may have at most `k‑1` votes.
It starts with `cntj` votes, each bribed voter reduces its vote count by one.
Therefore at least `cntj-(k-1)` voters have to be bribed, but never a negative
number. ∎



##### Lemma 2  
For a fixed `k` the cheapest way to satisfy the mandatory numbers
`needj` is to take, for each party `j`, the `needj` cheapest voters of that
party.

**Proof.**  
The voters of different parties are independent – bribing a voter of party
`a` does not influence the number of voters we have to bribe in party `b`.
Inside one party we have to choose exactly `needj` voters, therefore the
minimum possible sum of costs is the sum of the `needj` smallest costs. ∎



##### Lemma 3  
After bribing all mandatory voters (the *forced* ones) the cheapest way to
obtain any additional required votes for party 1 is to take the cheapest
still un‑bribed voters, regardless of their original parties.

**Proof.**  
All remaining voters are equivalent: bribing any of them adds exactly one
vote to party 1 and does not increase any other party’s vote count
(because they are taken away from their original party).  
Consequently the total cost is minimised by picking the voters with the
smallest individual costs. ∎



##### Lemma 4  
For a fixed `k` the algorithm computes the minimum possible total cost
among all bribery plans that give party 1 at least `k` votes.

**Proof.**  
The algorithm

1. forces exactly `needj` voters of each party `j` (Lemma&nbsp;1) and pays
   the sum of the cheapest such voters (Lemma&nbsp;2).  
   This is the cheapest possible way to satisfy the mandatory condition.
2. If after step&nbsp;1 party 1 already has `≥ k` votes, the plan is already
   feasible and its cost is optimal (nothing else can be cheaper).
3. Otherwise it needs `extra = k - cur_votes` more votes.
   By Lemma&nbsp;3 the cheapest way to obtain them is to bribe the `extra`
   cheapest among the still free voters, which is exactly what the
   algorithm does.

Thus no other bribery achieving at least `k` votes can be cheaper. ∎



##### Lemma 5  
Let `Ans` be the minimum value output by the algorithm over all
`k`. Then `Ans` equals the minimum total amount of money that makes party 1
the winner.

**Proof.**  
*Upper bound.*  
For the `k` that gives `Ans` the algorithm constructs a concrete bribery
with total cost `Ans`. By construction party 1 ends with `k` votes and every
other party has at most `k‑1` votes, therefore party 1 wins. Hence a cost of
`Ans` is sufficient.

*Lower bound.*  
Consider any optimal bribery `B` (minimum possible total cost).  
Let `kB` be the number of votes party 1 has after applying `B`.  
`B` is a feasible plan for the value `k = kB`.  
By Lemma&nbsp;4 the algorithm’s cost for this `k` is **not larger** than the
cost of `B`. Consequently `Ans`, the minimum over all `k`, is also not
larger than the optimal cost.

Both bounds give `Ans` = optimal cost. ∎



##### Theorem  
The program prints the minimum number of bytecoins the United Party has to
spend to win the election.

**Proof.**  
The program enumerates every integer `k` from `max(1, current_votes_of_party1)`
to `n`, computes for each `k` the cheapest possible cost (Lemma&nbsp;4) and
keeps the smallest of them. By Lemma&nbsp;5 this smallest value equals the
overall optimum. Therefore the printed number is exactly the required
minimum. ∎



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 6.   Reference Implementation  (Python 3)

```python
import sys

def solve() -> None:
    data = sys.stdin.read().strip().split()
    if not data:
        return
    it = iter(data)
    n = int(next(it))
    m = int(next(it))

    # voters per party
    parties = [[] for _ in range(m + 1)]
    for _ in range(n):
        p = int(next(it))
        c = int(next(it))
        parties[p].append(c)

    # sort and build prefix sums
    pref = [None] * (m + 1)
    for p in range(1, m + 1):
        parties[p].sort()
        ps = [0]
        s = 0
        for cost in parties[p]:
            s += cost
            ps.append(s)
        pref[p] = ps

    cur1 = len(parties[1])          # current votes of United Party
    INF = 10 ** 20
    answer = INF

    # k = final number of votes of party 1 (must be at least 1)
    start_k = max(1, cur1)
    for k in range(start_k, n + 1):
        forced_cost = 0
        forced_bribed = 0
        optional = []                # costs of voters that are not forced

        # handle parties 2 .. m
        for p in range(2, m + 1):
            cnt = len(parties[p])
            need = cnt - (k - 1)
            if need < 0:
                need = 0
            # forced voters: cheapest 'need' of this party
            forced_cost += pref[p][need]
            forced_bribed += need
            # remaining voters of this party become optional
            optional.extend(parties[p][need:])

        cur_votes = cur1 + forced_bribed
        if cur_votes >= k:
            total = forced_cost
        else:
            extra = k - cur_votes
            optional.sort()
            extra_cost = sum(optional[:extra])
            total = forced_cost + extra_cost

        if total < answer:
            answer = total

    print(answer)


if __name__ == "__main__":
    solve()
```

The program follows exactly the algorithm proven correct above
and conforms to the required input‑output format.

**Solution Explanation**

For every game we have  

* `n` piles, pile `i` contains `a[i]` stones (`a[i] ≥ 1`);
* players move alternately, T moves first;
* in one move a player removes **exactly one** stone from a **non‑empty** pile,
  but the pile used in the previous move is forbidden;
* a player that cannot move loses.

Both players play perfectly.  
We have to decide who wins.



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 1.  Observations  

* Only the **total number of stones** `S = Σ a[i]` matters for the length of the game.
* The *restriction* “cannot use the same pile as in the previous turn’’ only matters
  when one pile is *much larger* than all the others together.
  If the biggest pile is not larger than the rest, the players can always
  alternate between two different piles and the restriction never blocks a move.

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 2.  When does the restriction become decisive ?

Let  

```
mx = max_i a[i]          # size of the biggest pile
rest = S - mx            # stones in all other piles together
```

*If `mx > rest`*  

The biggest pile contains more stones than all the other piles together.
T can start by taking a stone from that biggest pile.
Afterwards HL **must** take a stone from a *different* pile (otherwise the rule is broken).
Because the other piles together have only `rest` stones, after `rest` rounds they become empty,
while the biggest pile still has at least one stone left.
Now it is HL’s turn, the previous move was made on a *different* pile,
but **all other piles are empty**, therefore HL cannot move and loses.
So **T wins** whenever `mx > rest`.

*If `mx ≤ rest`*  

The biggest pile is not dominant. The players can always keep the game going
by alternating between two different piles (for example the two biggest ones).
Thus the only thing that decides the winner is the parity of the total number of moves,
i.e. the parity of `S`.

*   If `S` is odd, the first player (T) makes the last move → **T wins**.
*   If `S` is even, the second player (HL) makes the last move → **HL wins**.

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 3.  Final decision rule  

```
if mx > rest:                winner = "T"
else:
        if S % 2 == 1:       winner = "T"
        else:                winner = "HL"
```

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 4.  Correctness Proof  

We prove that the algorithm above outputs the correct winner for every game.

---

##### Lemma 1  
If `mx > rest` then T has a winning strategy.

**Proof.**  
T’s first move: take a stone from the biggest pile (size `mx`).  
After this move the biggest pile still contains `mx‑1` stones,
the other piles together contain `rest` stones.

From now on HL must take a stone from a *different* pile (otherwise the rule is broken).
Consequently HL can only take stones from the `rest` stones that are not in the biggest pile.
After exactly `rest` further moves (alternating players) all those `rest` stones are removed,
so the biggest pile still has at least one stone left (`mx‑1‑rest ≥ 0` because `mx > rest`).

Now it is HL’s turn, the previous move was made on a *different* pile,
but every pile except the biggest one is empty, therefore HL has no legal move and loses.
∎



##### Lemma 2  
If `mx ≤ rest` then the game can be played for exactly `S` moves
without ever being blocked by the “different pile” rule.

**Proof.**  
Because `mx ≤ rest`, the total number of stones outside the biggest pile is at least
as large as the biggest pile itself.
Consider the following simple strategy for the two players together:

*Whenever a player removes a stone from a pile, the next player removes a stone
from any other non‑empty pile (such a pile always exists because the total number of
stones in the other piles is never smaller than the stones left in the current pile).*

Formally, after each move the number of stones in the pile just used is at most
the number of stones in the union of the remaining piles, therefore a different
non‑empty pile exists. Repeating this argument shows that the game can continue
until all `S` stones are removed. ∎



##### Lemma 3  
If `mx ≤ rest` then the winner is determined solely by the parity of `S`:
odd → T, even → HL.

**Proof.**  
By Lemma&nbsp;2 the game lasts exactly `S` moves, i.e. all stones are taken.
The players move alternately, T moves on odd-numbered turns, HL on even-numbered turns.
If `S` is odd, the last (the `S`‑th) move is made by T, after which HL has no move → T wins.
If `S` is even, the last move is made by HL, after which T has no move → HL wins. ∎



##### Theorem  
For every game the algorithm outputs the winner under optimal play.

**Proof.**  
Two cases.

*Case 1:* `mx > rest`.  
The algorithm outputs **T**. By Lemma&nbsp;1 T indeed has a winning strategy,
so the output is correct.

*Case 2:* `mx ≤ rest`.  
The algorithm looks at the parity of `S`.  
By Lemma&nbsp;3 the parity decides the winner exactly as the algorithm does.
Thus the output is correct.

Both cases cover all possible inputs, therefore the algorithm is always correct. ∎



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 5.  Complexity Analysis  

For each test case we compute `S` and `mx` by scanning the `n` numbers once.

```
Time   :  O(n) per test case   ( ≤ 100 )
Memory :  O(1)                 (only a few integers)
```

Both limits are easily within the given constraints.

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 6.  Reference Implementation  (Python 3)

```python
import sys

def solve() -> None:
    data = sys.stdin.read().strip().split()
    if not data:
        return
    it = iter(data)
    t = int(next(it))
    out_lines = []
    for _ in range(t):
        n = int(next(it))
        a = [int(next(it)) for _ in range(n)]
        total = sum(a)
        mx = max(a)
        rest = total - mx
        if mx > rest:
            out_lines.append("T")
        else:
            if total % 2 == 1:
                out_lines.append("T")
            else:
                out_lines.append("HL")
    sys.stdout.write("\n".join(out_lines))

if __name__ == "__main__":
    solve()
```

and conforms to the required input‑output format.
completed 103/150, Solved: True, number of success = 21/103, acc = 20.39
**Solution Explanation**

For every employee we know the languages he already knows.
Two employees can directly correspond if they have a common language.
If employee `A` can correspond with `B` and `B` with `C`,
then `A` can also correspond with `C` (the correspondence may be indirect).

So we have a graph

* vertices – employees `1 … n`
* an (undirected) edge between two vertices if the two employees share at least one
  language.

The whole company can correspond iff this graph is **connected**.
Teaching one language to one employee costs `1`.  
We have to add the cheapest set of such lessons that makes the graph connected.



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 1.   Observations  

*All employees that already share a language belong to the same connected
component.*  
If several employees know the same language we can join them into one component.

*If at least one employee knows a language*  
let `c` be the number of connected components of the graph described above.
By teaching **one** employee of a component a language that already exists in
another component we merge the two components.
Repeating this `c‑1` times we obtain a single component.
It is impossible to need fewer lessons, because each lesson can reduce the
number of components by **at most one**.  
Therefore  

```
answer = components – 1          (if somebody already knows a language)
```

*If nobody knows any language* (`k_i = 0` for all `i`) the graph has `n`
isolated vertices and there is **no** language at all.
Every employee must learn a language, otherwise he can never talk to anyone.
Teaching the same language to all `n` employees costs `n`, and this is optimal.

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 2.   Algorithm
```
read n, m
make an empty list for each language   lang_emp[1 … m]
total_known = 0

for each employee i = 0 … n-1
        read k and the k languages
        total_known += k
        for each language L
                lang_emp[L].append(i)

create a Disjoint Set Union (DSU) of size n

for each language L = 1 … m
        let employees = lang_emp[L]
        for every employee e in employees[1 …]
                DSU.union(employees[0], e)      # join all who know L

components = number of different DSU roots among 0 … n-1

if total_known == 0
        answer = n
else
        answer = components - 1

print answer
```

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 3.   Correctness Proof  

We prove that the algorithm outputs the minimum possible amount of money.

---

##### Lemma 1  
After processing all languages, two employees are in the same DSU set **iff**
they belong to the same connected component of the original “share‑a‑language”
graph.

**Proof.**  
*If* two employees share a language `L`, the algorithm puts both of them into
`lang_emp[L]` and unions them, therefore they end up in the same DSU set.  
Because DSU union is transitive, any chain of shared languages also puts the
endpoints into the same set, i.e. every edge of the graph is respected.

*Only‑if*: DSU unions are performed **only** between employees that share a
language, therefore two employees can become united only via a sequence of
such unions, i.e. via a path of shared languages in the graph. ∎



##### Lemma 2  
If at least one employee knows a language, the minimum number of lessons needed
to make the whole company connected equals  
`components – 1`, where `components` is the number of DSU sets after the
initialisation.

**Proof.**  

*Upper bound* – we can achieve connectivity with `components‑1` lessons:  
pick one arbitrary employee from each component, call them `rep[1] … rep[c]`.
Teach `rep[2]` any language known by `rep[1]`. Now components 1 and 2 merge.
Teach `rep[3]` a language known by the (now merged) component, etc.
Each lesson reduces the number of components by exactly one, after
`c‑1` lessons only one component remains.

*Lower bound* – a single lesson can involve only one employee, therefore it can
connect at most one previously separate component to the rest.
Consequently at least `c‑1` lessons are necessary.

Thus the optimum equals `c‑1`. ∎



##### Lemma 3  
If no employee knows any language, the minimum number of lessons needed equals
`n`.

**Proof.**  
When nobody knows a language, after any set of lessons the only possible
communication paths are those that use the newly taught languages.
If an employee never learns a language, he cannot be part of any path, so all
`n` employees must learn at least one language → at least `n` lessons.

Teaching the same language to every employee uses exactly `n` lessons and
makes the whole graph a single clique, therefore `n` lessons are sufficient and
optimal. ∎



##### Theorem  
The algorithm prints the minimum possible total cost.

**Proof.**  

Two cases.

*Case 1 – `total_known > 0`*  
By Lemma&nbsp;1 the DSU components equal the connected components of the
original graph.  
The algorithm outputs `components‑1`.  
By Lemma&nbsp;2 this number is both achievable and optimal.  

*Case 2 – `total_known = 0`*  
The algorithm outputs `n`.  
By Lemma&nbsp;3 this is the optimal cost.

In both cases the printed value is the optimum. ∎



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 4.   Complexity Analysis  

*Reading input* – `O(n + Σ k_i) ≤ O(n·m)`  
*Union operations* – each language processes its list once, total `O(Σ k_i)`
  DSU operations, each `α(n)` (inverse Ackermann), i.e. practically constant.  

Overall time `O(n·m)` ≤ `10⁴` operations for the given limits.  
Memory consumption:  

* language → employee lists: `O(n·m)` worst case (but ≤ 10⁴ integers)  
* DSU arrays: `O(n)`  

Both well within limits.

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 5.   Reference Implementation  (Python 3)

```python
import sys

# ---------- Disjoint Set Union ----------
class DSU:
    def __init__(self, n):
        self.parent = list(range(n))
        self.rank = [0] * n

    def find(self, x):
        # path compression
        while self.parent[x] != x:
            self.parent[x] = self.parent[self.parent[x]]
            x = self.parent[x]
        return x

    def union(self, a, b):
        ra = self.find(a)
        rb = self.find(b)
        if ra == rb:
            return
        # union by rank
        if self.rank[ra] < self.rank[rb]:
            self.parent[ra] = rb
        elif self.rank[ra] > self.rank[rb]:
            self.parent[rb] = ra
        else:
            self.parent[rb] = ra
            self.rank[ra] += 1


# ---------- Solver ----------
def solve() -> None:
    data = sys.stdin.read().strip().split()
    if not data:
        return
    it = iter(data)
    n = int(next(it))
    m = int(next(it))

    # language -> list of employees
    lang_emp = [[] for _ in range(m + 1)]
    total_known = 0

    for emp in range(n):
        k = int(next(it))
        total_known += k
        for _ in range(k):
            lang = int(next(it))
            lang_emp[lang].append(emp)

    dsu = DSU(n)

    # union employees that share a language
    for lst in lang_emp:
        if len(lst) > 1:
            first = lst[0]
            for other in lst[1:]:
                dsu.union(first, other)

    # count components
    roots = set()
    for i in range(n):
        roots.add(dsu.find(i))
    components = len(roots)

    if total_known == 0:
        ans = n
    else:
        ans = components - 1

    print(ans)


if __name__ == "__main__":
    solve()
```

The program follows exactly the algorithm proven correct above and conforms
to the required input‑output format.
completed 104/150, Solved: True, number of success = 22/104, acc = 21.15
**Solution Explanation**

For every flight one of the airports is the home airport `H`.

* `H -> X` – a flight from home to a contest place `X`
* `X -> H` – a flight back home from the contest place `X`

During the whole year Jinotega always returns home after a competition,
except possibly for the *last* competition that is still in progress.
Therefore for every contest place `X`

```
#flights H -> X  =  #flights X -> H      (if Jinotega is already home)
#flights H -> X  =  #flights X -> H + 1  (if Jinotega is now at X)
```

So for each place we can keep the difference  

`diff[X] = (# H -> X) – (# X -> H)`

* `diff[X] = 0` – all trips to `X` are already finished
* `diff[X] = 1` – Jinotega is currently at `X`
* any other value cannot appear because the input is guaranteed to be
  consistent.

The whole list of flights is given in arbitrary order, therefore we only
need to count these differences.

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### Algorithm
```
read n
read home airport H
diff = empty map from string to integer

repeat n times:
        read line "A->B"
        if A == H:          # flight from home to contest
                diff[B] += 1
        else:               # then B must be H, flight back home
                diff[A] -= 1

if there exists a key with diff[key] != 0:
        answer = "contest"
else:
        answer = "home"

print answer
```

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### Correctness Proof  

We prove that the algorithm prints `"home"` iff Jinotega is at home,
otherwise it prints `"contest"`.

---

**Lemma 1**  
For every contest airport `X` after processing all flights,
`diff[X] = (# H -> X) – (# X -> H)`.

*Proof.*  
The algorithm adds `+1` to `diff[X]` exactly for each flight `H -> X`,
and adds `-1` exactly for each flight `X -> H`. No other flights modify
`diff[X]`. Hence after all flights are processed the value equals the
desired difference. ∎



**Lemma 2**  
If Jinotega is at home, then for every `X` we have `diff[X] = 0`.

*Proof.*  
When Jinotega is at home, every trip to a contest place `X` consists of a
pair of flights `H -> X` and `X -> H`. Their contributions to `diff[X]`
cancel out (`+1` and `-1`). Since all trips are complete, the total
difference is zero for each `X`. ∎



**Lemma 3**  
If Jinotega is at a contest place `C`, then `diff[C] = 1` and for all
other places `X ≠ C` we have `diff[X] = 0`.

*Proof.*  
All finished trips to any place `X` contribute equally many outbound and
inbound flights, giving zero difference (as in Lemma&nbsp;2).  
For the current place `C` there is one more outbound flight `H -> C`
than inbound flight `C -> H`, because the return flight has not happened
yet. Therefore `diff[C] = 1`. ∎



**Theorem**  
The algorithm outputs `"home"` exactly when Jinotega is at home,
otherwise it outputs `"contest"`.

*Proof.*  

*If the algorithm outputs `"home"`*:  
All stored differences are zero, i.e. `diff[X] = 0` for every `X`.  
By Lemma&nbsp;2 this can happen only when Jinotega is at home.

*If the algorithm outputs `"contest"`*:  
There exists a key with `diff[key] ≠ 0`.  
Because the input is consistent, the only possible non‑zero value is `1`
(Lemma&nbsp;3). Hence Jinotega must be at the corresponding contest
airport, not at home.

Both directions hold, therefore the theorem is proved. ∎



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### Complexity Analysis  

`n ≤ 100`.  
The algorithm processes each flight once and uses a hash map.

* Time complexity: `O(n)`
* Memory consumption: at most one entry per distinct contest airport,
  i.e. `O(n)` (worst case each flight goes to a different airport).

Both limits are easily satisfied.

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### Reference Implementation  (Python 3)

```python
import sys

def solve() -> None:
    data = sys.stdin.read().strip().splitlines()
    if not data:
        return
    n = int(data[0].strip())
    home = data[1].strip()
    diff = {}                     # airport -> int

    for i in range(2, 2 + n):
        line = data[i].strip()
        # format: XXX->YYY
        a, b = line.split('->')
        if a == home:             # home -> contest
            diff[b] = diff.get(b, 0) + 1
        else:                     # contest -> home (b must be home)
            diff[a] = diff.get(a, 0) - 1

    # check if any difference is non‑zero
    for v in diff.values():
        if v != 0:
            print("contest")
            return
    print("home")

if __name__ == "__main__":
    solve()
```
