completed 105/150, Solved: True, number of success = 23/105, acc = 21.9
**Solution Explanation**

For every citizen we have a current amount of money.

```
type 1  p x :   a[p] = x                     (receipt)
type 2  x   :   for all i   if a[i] < x : a[i] = x   (payout)
```

`n , q ≤ 2·10⁵`, therefore an `O(n·q)` simulation is impossible.



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 1.   Observations  

* A payout only **increases** money, never decreases it.
* For a fixed citizen only the **last** receipt matters – everything that
  happened before that receipt is overwritten.
* After the last receipt of a citizen, only payouts that appear **later**
  in the log (i.e. *after* that receipt) can still raise his money.

So we have to know, for every citizen

```
max( value written by his last receipt,
     maximal x of all payouts that are placed after that receipt )
```

If a citizen never receives a receipt, the “last receipt value” is his
initial amount.



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 2.   Process the log backwards  

Scanning the events from the end to the beginning gives us exactly the
information we need:

* while moving backwards we keep the variable  

  `cur_max = maximal x of all type‑2 events already seen`
  (these are the payouts that are **later** in the original order).

* when we meet a type‑1 event `1 p x`

  *if* citizen `p` has not been fixed yet, his final amount is  

  `max( x , cur_max )`  

  because `cur_max` contains all later payouts.
  Afterwards the citizen is marked as *resolved* – an earlier receipt
  (closer to the beginning) is irrelevant.

* when we meet a type‑2 event `2 x` we only update  

  `cur_max = max(cur_max , x)`.



After the backward scan every citizen is either

* already resolved (had a receipt after the last relevant payout) – we
  already stored his final amount, or
* still unresolved – his final amount is  

  `max( initial_a[i] , cur_max )`



All operations are `O(1)`, the whole algorithm is `O(n + q)`.



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 3.   Correctness Proof  

We prove that the algorithm outputs the correct final balances.

---

##### Lemma 1  
During the backward scan `cur_max` always equals the maximum `x`
among all type‑2 events that are **later** (in forward order) than the
currently processed event.

**Proof.**  
Initially no event has been processed, therefore “later” means *all*
type‑2 events – `cur_max` is `0`, the correct maximum.  
Induction step: assume the invariant holds before processing the next
event (going backwards).

*If the event is type‑1* – `cur_max` does not change, and the set of
later type‑2 events is unchanged, so the invariant stays true.

*If the event is type‑2 with value `x`* – this event becomes the newest
one among the “later” events for all previously processed positions.
We set `cur_max = max(cur_max, x)`, which is exactly the maximum of the
old later events and the new one. ∎



##### Lemma 2  
When the algorithm processes a type‑1 event `1 p x` and citizen `p` is
still unresolved, the value stored for `p`

```
final[p] = max( x , cur_max )
```

equals the amount of money of citizen `p` after **all** events
that are later (in forward order) than this receipt.

**Proof.**  
All later events consist of

* the receipt itself (`x`);
* some set `S` of type‑2 events that appear after it.

By Lemma&nbsp;1 `cur_max` is the maximum `x` among exactly the set `S`.
A payout raises the balance only if it is larger than the current
balance, therefore after applying all payouts from `S` the balance
becomes `max( x , max_{y∈S} y ) = max( x , cur_max )`. ∎



##### Lemma 3  
If a citizen never becomes resolved during the backward scan, the
algorithm finally assigns

```
final[i] = max( initial_a[i] , cur_max )
```

which equals his balance after all events.

**Proof.**  
No receipt for this citizen occurs after the last payout that influences
him, i.e. the whole forward log consists only of the initial amount and
some type‑2 events.  
`cur_max` after the complete backward scan is the maximum `x` among **all**
type‑2 events (Lemma&nbsp;1).  
Applying all payouts to the initial amount yields exactly
`max( initial_a[i] , cur_max )`. ∎



##### Lemma 4  
Each citizen is resolved at most once and the stored value is his
correct final balance.

**Proof.**  
The first (from the back) receipt of a citizen is the *last* receipt in
forward order. After processing it we mark the citizen resolved, so any
earlier receipt is ignored – it cannot affect the final balance because
it is overwritten by the later one.  
By Lemma&nbsp;2 the stored value equals the balance after all later
events, i.e. after the whole log. ∎



##### Theorem  
The algorithm outputs for every citizen the amount of money he has after
all `q` events.

**Proof.**  
Consider an arbitrary citizen `i`.

*If `i` becomes resolved* – by Lemma&nbsp;4 the stored value is his
correct final balance.

*If `i` never becomes resolved* – by Lemma&nbsp;3 the value assigned at
the end is his correct final balance.

All citizens belong to one of the two cases, therefore the whole output
is correct. ∎



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 4.   Complexity Analysis  

*Reading input* : `O(n + q)`  
*Backward scan* : each event processed once → `O(q)`  
*Final pass over citizens* : `O(n)`

Total time   `O(n + q) ≤ 4·10⁵` operations.  

Additional memory:

* the list of events – `O(q)`
* three arrays of length `n` (`initial`, `final`, `resolved`) – `O(n)`

Overall `O(n + q)` memory, well within limits.



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 5.   Reference Implementation  (Python 3)

```python
import sys

def solve() -> None:
    data = list(map(int, sys.stdin.buffer.read().split()))
    it = iter(data)

    n = next(it)
    init = [next(it) for _ in range(n)]

    q = next(it)
    events = []                     # store as tuples
    for _ in range(q):
        typ = next(it)
        if typ == 1:
            p = next(it) - 1        # zero‑based index
            x = next(it)
            events.append((1, p, x))
        else:                       # typ == 2
            x = next(it)
            events.append((2, x))

    cur_max = 0                     # maximal x of already seen payouts
    final = [0] * n
    resolved = [False] * n

    # scan backwards
    for ev in reversed(events):
        if ev[0] == 1:               # receipt
            _, p, x = ev
            if not resolved[p]:
                final[p] = max(x, cur_max)
                resolved[p] = True
        else:                        # payout
            _, x = ev
            if x > cur_max:
                cur_max = x

    # citizens without a later receipt
    for i in range(n):
        if not resolved[i]:
            final[i] = max(init[i], cur_max)

    sys.stdout.write(' '.join(map(str, final)))

if __name__ == "__main__":
    solve()
```

The program follows exactly the algorithm proven correct above
and conforms to the required input‑output format.
completed 106/150, Solved: True, number of success = 24/106, acc = 22.64
**Solution Explanation**

For every position `i` we know the two bits  

* `a[i]` – the bit of the first number  
* `b[i]` – the bit of the second number  

The original bitwise OR at this position is  

```
c[i] = a[i] OR b[i]
```

Only the bits of `a` may be swapped.  
After swapping positions `i` and `j`

```
a'[i] = a[j] ,   a'[j] = a[i]
c'[i] = a'[i] OR b[i] = a[j] OR b[i]
c'[j] = a'[j] OR b[j] = a[i] OR b[j]
```

All other positions keep the same OR value.  
The whole OR changes **iff** at least one of the two positions changes,
i.e. `c'[i] ≠ c[i]` or `c'[j] ≠ c[j]`.

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 1.  When does a single position change its OR ?

*If `b[i] = 1`*  
`c[i] = 1` no matter what `a[i]` is, therefore swapping never changes the
OR at this position.

*If `b[i] = 0`*  
`c[i] = a[i]`. After the swap the new OR becomes `a[j]`.  
So the OR at `i` changes **iff** `a[i] ≠ a[j]`.

Consequences  

* a position with `b = 1` is *inactive* – its OR never changes,
* a position with `b = 0` is *active* – its OR changes exactly when the
  swapped `a`‑bits are different.

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 2.  Condition for a pair `(i , j)`

The whole OR changes **iff**

```
a[i] ≠ a[j]          (the two bits are different)
and
not (b[i] = 1 and b[j] = 1)   (at least one of them has b = 0)
```

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 3.  Classify positions

Four possible combinations of `(a , b)` :

| type | a | b | description |
|------|---|---|-------------|
| 00   | 0 | 0 | active, a = 0 |
| 01   | 0 | 1 | inactive, a = 0 |
| 10   | 1 | 0 | active, a = 1 |
| 11   | 1 | 1 | inactive, a = 1 |

Let  

```
cnt00 = number of positions of type 00
cnt01 = number of positions of type 01
cnt10 = number of positions of type 10
cnt11 = number of positions of type 11
```

We need unordered pairs with different `a` values (`0` vs `1`) and
*not* both `b = 1`.  
All admissible type pairs are:

| first type | second type | a different? | both b = 1? | allowed? |
|------------|-------------|--------------|-------------|----------|
| 00         | 10          | yes          | no          | yes |
| 00         | 11          | yes          | no (b of first = 0) | yes |
| 01         | 10          | yes          | no (b of second = 0) | yes |
| 01         | 11          | yes          | **yes** (both b = 1) | **no** |

Therefore the number of good swaps is

```
answer = cnt00 * cnt10          # (00,10)
        + cnt00 * cnt11        # (00,11)
        + cnt01 * cnt10        # (01,10)
```

All other pairs either have equal `a` bits or both `b = 1` and do not
change the OR.

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 4.  Algorithm
```
read n, strings a, b
cnt00 = cnt01 = cnt10 = cnt11 = 0
for i = 0 … n-1
        if a[i] == '0' and b[i] == '0': cnt00 += 1
        if a[i] == '0' and b[i] == '1': cnt01 += 1
        if a[i] == '1' and b[i] == '0': cnt10 += 1
        if a[i] == '1' and b[i] == '1': cnt11 += 1
answer = cnt00*cnt10 + cnt00*cnt11 + cnt01*cnt10
print answer
```

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 5.  Correctness Proof  

We prove that the algorithm outputs exactly the number of swaps that
change the bitwise OR.

---

##### Lemma 1  
If `b[i] = 1` then swapping any bits of `a` never changes the OR at position
`i`.

**Proof.**  
`c[i] = a[i] OR 1 = 1`. After any swap `a'[i]` is still either `0` or `1`,
but `a'[i] OR 1 = 1`. ∎



##### Lemma 2  
If `b[i] = 0` then swapping bits of `a` changes the OR at position `i`
iff the two swapped bits are different.

**Proof.**  
With `b[i]=0` we have `c[i] = a[i]`. After swapping with position `j`,
`c'[i] = a[j]`. Hence `c'[i] ≠ c[i]` ⇔ `a[j] ≠ a[i]`. ∎



##### Lemma 3  
A pair of indices `{i , j}` changes the whole OR **iff**
`a[i] ≠ a[j]` and not both `b[i] = 1` and `b[j] = 1`.

**Proof.**  
*If* the condition holds, at least one of the two positions has `b = 0`
(active). By Lemma&nbsp;2 the OR at that active position changes because
the two `a` bits differ. Hence the whole OR changes.

*Only‑if*: suppose the whole OR changes after swapping `i` and `j`.  
Then at least one of the two positions changes its OR.
By Lemma&nbsp;1 a position with `b = 1` cannot change, therefore the
changing position must have `b = 0`. By Lemma&nbsp;2 its OR changes only
when the two `a` bits differ, i.e. `a[i] ≠ a[j]`. Consequently not both
`b` are `1`. ∎



##### Lemma 4  
The number of unordered index pairs satisfying Lemma&nbsp;3 equals  

```
cnt00·cnt10 + cnt00·cnt11 + cnt01·cnt10 .
```

**Proof.**  
Pairs must have different `a` values, therefore one index belongs to a
type with `a = 0` (`00` or `01`) and the other to a type with `a = 1`
(`10` or `11`).  
All four combinations are examined:

* `00` with `10` – allowed (both `b = 0`);
* `00` with `11` – allowed (first `b = 0`);
* `01` with `10` – allowed (second `b = 0`);
* `01` with `11` – forbidden because both `b = 1`.

Counting unordered pairs gives exactly the three products above. ∎



##### Lemma 5  
The algorithm outputs the value given in Lemma&nbsp;4.

**Proof.**  
The algorithm counts how many positions belong to each of the four
types, obtaining `cnt00, cnt01, cnt10, cnt11`.  
It then computes `cnt00*cnt10 + cnt00*cnt11 + cnt01*cnt10` and prints it.
Thus it outputs the number from Lemma&nbsp;4. ∎



##### Theorem  
The algorithm prints the exact number of ways to swap two bits of `a`
so that the bitwise OR of `a` and `b` changes.

**Proof.**  
By Lemma&nbsp;3 a swap changes the OR precisely for the pairs described
in Lemma&nbsp;4. Lemma&nbsp;4 gives the count of such pairs, and Lemma&nbsp;5
shows that the algorithm outputs this count. Therefore the algorithm’s
output equals the required number of swaps. ∎



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 6.  Complexity Analysis  

*Counting the four types* – one pass over `n` characters: `O(n)` time.  
*Memory* – only four integer counters: `O(1)` extra space.

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 7.  Reference Implementation  (Python 3)

```python
import sys

def solve() -> None:
    data = sys.stdin.read().strip().split()
    if not data:
        return
    n = int(data[0])
    a = data[1].strip()
    b = data[2].strip()

    cnt00 = cnt01 = cnt10 = cnt11 = 0

    for i in range(n):
        ai = a[i]
        bi = b[i]
        if ai == '0' and bi == '0':
            cnt00 += 1
        elif ai == '0' and bi == '1':
            cnt01 += 1
        elif ai == '1' and bi == '0':
            cnt10 += 1
        else:               # ai == '1' and bi == '1'
            cnt11 += 1

    ans = cnt00 * cnt10 + cnt00 * cnt11 + cnt01 * cnt10
    print(ans)


if __name__ == "__main__":
    solve()
```

The program follows exactly the algorithm proven correct above
and conforms to the required input‑output format.
completed 107/150, Solved: True, number of success = 25/107, acc = 23.36
**Solution Explanation**

For every column `i ( 1 … n )` we have `h[i]` unit blocks.
A block is *internal* iff it has a neighbour on the four sides

* top – another block,
* bottom – another block or the floor,
* left  – a block in column `i‑1` on the same height,
* right – a block in column `i+1` on the same height.

All *boundary* blocks are destroyed **simultaneously** in one operation.
The process is repeated until no block is left.
The required answer is the number of performed operations.



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 1.   Height after one operation  

For a fixed column `i` a block on height `j ( 1 ≤ j ≤ h[i] )` survives the
first operation iff

```
j < h[i]                (there is a block above)
j = 1   or   j > 1      (bottom is floor or a block)
h[i-1] ≥ j              (left neighbour)
h[i+1] ≥ j              (right neighbour)
```

Therefore the highest surviving block in column `i` after the first
operation is

```
new_h[i] = max( 0 ,  min( h[i] - 1 , h[i-1] , h[i+1] ) )
```

(For the first and the last column one of the neighbours does not exist,
hence `new_h = 0`.)

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 2.   Height after *t* operations  

Apply the same rule repeatedly.
After `t` operations the height of column `i` is

```
h_t[i] = max( 0 ,  min_{k = -t … t} ( h[i+k] - (t - |k|) ) )          (1)
```

*Proof by induction*  

* `t = 1` – formula (1) is exactly the rule written above.
* Assume it holds for `t`.  
  For `t+1`

```
h_{t+1}[i] = max(0 , min( h_t[i] - 1 , h_t[i-1] , h_t[i+1] ))
```

Insert the induction hypothesis for the three heights,
the minimum becomes the minimum over the interval `[-t-1 … t+1]`
with the appropriate subtraction `t+1-|k|`. ∎



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 3.   When does something stay after *t* operations ?  

`h_t[i] > 0`  ⇔  the minimum in (1) is at least `1`

```
∀ k ∈ [ -t , t ] :   h[i+k] ≥ (t - |k|) + 1                (2)
```

The right‑hand side is a **V‑shaped** required height:
the centre needs height `t+1`, one step left/right needs `t`,
two steps needs `t-1`, …  

So after `t` operations a block can survive **iff**
there exists a centre column `i` (with enough neighbours, i.e. `t+1 ≤ i ≤ n-t`)
such that the whole interval `[i-t , i+t]` lies **above** that V‑shape.



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 4.   Transforming the condition  

Rewrite (2) separately for the left and the right side.

```
left side (p ≤ i) :   h[p] + (i-p) ≥ t+1      →   (h[p] - p) ≥ t+1 - i
right side(p ≥ i) :   h[p] + (p-i) ≥ t+1      →   (h[p] + p) ≥ t+1 + i
```

Define two auxiliary arrays  

```
L[p] = h[p] - p          (left‑influence)
R[p] = h[p] + p          (right‑influence)
```

For a fixed `t` and a centre `i`

```
min_{p ∈ [i-t , i]}   L[p]   ≥   t+1 - i          (3)
min_{p ∈ [i , i+t]}   R[p]   ≥   t+1 + i          (4)
```

Both are ordinary **range minima** queries.



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 5.   Checking a given `t` in O(n)

*Window size* = `t+1`.

*   `left_min[i]` – minimum of `L` on the interval `[i-t , i]`
    (only defined for `i ≥ t+1`).
*   `right_min[i]` – minimum of `R` on the interval `[i , i+t]`
    (only defined for `i ≤ n-t`).

Both arrays are obtained in linear time with a sliding window
(monotone deque).

Now we simply test all possible centres:

```
for i = t+1 … n-t
        if left_min[i]  ≥ t+1 - i   and
           right_min[i] ≥ t+1 + i
                →   some block survives after t operations
```

If at least one centre satisfies the two inequalities, the answer for
this `t` is **true**, otherwise **false**.

The whole test works in `O(n)`.



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 6.   Binary search for the maximal `t`

The number of operations needed is

```
answer = (largest t with a non‑empty shape after t operations) + 1
```

` t = 0 ` is always possible (the initial shape is non‑empty).

Upper bound for `t`

* a block cannot survive more steps than its height → `t ≤ max(h)`
* it also cannot be farther from the border than the distance to the
  nearest edge → `t ≤ (n-1)/2`

So we can binary search `t` in the interval `[0 ,  min(max(h), (n-1)//2) ]`
(about `30` iterations, each `O(n)`).

Overall complexity  

```
O( n log max_h )      ≤  O( 1e5 * 30 )
```

Memory consumption `O(n)`.



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 7.   Correctness Proof  

We prove that the algorithm prints the required number of operations.

---

##### Lemma 1  
After `t` operations the height of column `i` is given by formula (1).

*Proof.* By induction, see Section&nbsp;2. ∎



##### Lemma 2  
`h_t[i] > 0`  **iff** condition (2) holds.

*Proof.*  
`h_t[i] > 0` means the minimum in (1) is at least `1`.  
The minimum is taken over all `k ∈ [-t,t]` of the expression
`h[i+k] - (t-|k|)`.  
Rearranging gives exactly (2). ∎



##### Lemma 3  
For a fixed `t` and centre `i` (with `t+1 ≤ i ≤ n-t`)  
condition (2) is equivalent to the two inequalities (3) and (4).

*Proof.*  
Split the interval `[i-t , i+t]` into the left part `p ≤ i`
and the right part `p ≥ i`.  
For `p ≤ i` :

```
h[p] ≥ (t - (i-p)) + 1   ⇔   h[p] - p ≥ t+1 - i
```

which is (3).  
The right part is analogous and gives (4). ∎



##### Lemma 4  
For a given `t` the procedure **Check(t)** (sliding minima + test)
returns *true*  **iff** after `t` operations at least one block remains.

*Proof.*  

*If part* – Assume **Check(t)** finds a centre `i` satisfying (3) and (4).
By Lemma&nbsp;3 the whole interval `[i-t , i+t]` fulfills (2),
hence by Lemma&nbsp;2 `h_t[i] > 0`. So a block survives.

*Only‑if part* – Assume after `t` operations some block survives.
Take its column `i`. By Lemma&nbsp;2 condition (2) holds,
hence by Lemma&nbsp;3 both (3) and (4) hold.
The sliding‑window minima computed by the algorithm are exactly the
left‑hand sides of (3) and (4), therefore the algorithm will recognise
this centre and return *true*. ∎



##### Lemma 5  
Let `T` be the largest integer such that **Check(T)** is *true*.
Then after `T` operations the shape is non‑empty and after `T+1`
operations it is empty.

*Proof.*  
By Lemma&nbsp;4, **Check(T)** true ⇒ non‑empty after `T` ops.
If the shape were still non‑empty after `T+1` ops,
then **Check(T+1)** would also be true (again Lemma&nbsp;4),
contradicting maximality of `T`. ∎



##### Lemma 6  
The algorithm outputs `T+1`.

*Proof.*  
Binary search finds the maximal `T` with **Check(T)** true.
The program finally prints `T+1`. ∎



##### Theorem  
The program prints the exact number of operations needed to destroy all
towers.

*Proof.*  
By Lemma&nbsp;5 the process needs exactly `T+1` operations.
By Lemma&nbsp;6 the program outputs `T+1`. ∎



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 8.   Reference Implementation  (Python 3)

```python
import sys
from collections import deque

def solve() -> None:
    data = sys.stdin.read().strip().split()
    if not data:
        return
    it = iter(data)
    n = int(next(it))
    h = [0] * (n + 1)          # 1‑based
    for i in range(1, n + 1):
        h[i] = int(next(it))

    # auxiliary arrays
    L = [0] * (n + 1)          # h[i] - i
    R = [0] * (n + 1)          # h[i] + i
    max_h = 0
    for i in range(1, n + 1):
        L[i] = h[i] - i
        R[i] = h[i] + i
        if h[i] > max_h:
            max_h = h[i]

    # upper bound for t
    upper = min(max_h, (n - 1) // 2)

    INF = 10 ** 20

    # ---------- check(t) ----------
    def check(t: int) -> bool:
        if t == 0:
            # at least one block exists initially (h_i >= 1)
            return True
        if t > (n - 1) // 2:
            return False
        w = t + 1                     # window size

        # left minima: min L on [i-w+1 .. i]
        left_min = [INF] * (n + 1)
        dq = deque()
        for i in range(1, n + 1):
            # push i
            while dq and L[dq[-1]] >= L[i]:
                dq.pop()
            dq.append(i)
            # remove out of window
            while dq and dq[0] < i - w + 1:
                dq.popleft()
            if i >= w:
                left_min[i] = L[dq[0]]

        # right minima: min R on [i .. i+w-1]
        right_min = [INF] * (n + 1)
        dq.clear()
        for i in range(n, 0, -1):
            while dq and R[dq[-1]] >= R[i]:
                dq.pop()
            dq.append(i)
            while dq and dq[0] > i + w - 1:
                dq.popleft()
            if i + w - 1 <= n:
                right_min[i] = R[dq[0]]

        # test all possible centres
        for i in range(t + 1, n - t + 1):
            if left_min[i] >= (t + 1 - i) and right_min[i] >= (t + 1 + i):
                return True
        return False

    # ---------- binary search ----------
    lo = 0
    hi = upper + 1          # exclusive, invariant: check(lo) true, check(hi) false
    while lo + 1 < hi:
        mid = (lo + hi) // 2
        if check(mid):
            lo = mid
        else:
            hi = mid

    # answer = lo + 1
    print(lo + 1)


if __name__ == "__main__":
    solve()
```

The program follows exactly the algorithm proven correct above
and conforms to the required input‑output format.
completed 108/150, Solved: True, number of success = 26/108, acc = 24.07
**Solution Explanation**

For every cell we know whether it is *ground* (free) or *lava* (blocked).
The grid has only two rows, therefore a path from the left‑upper corner  
`(1,1)` to the right‑lower corner `(2,n)` can be blocked only by a very
specific situation:

*if a blocked cell in one row touches (in the same column or in a neighbour
column) a blocked cell in the other row, the two rows become “linked” by
lava and the whole passage is cut.*

```
row 1 :  .  X  .
row 2 :  X  .  .
          ^  ^  ^
          columns c-1 , c , c+1
```

If at least one such touching pair exists, the girl can never cross from
the upper row to the lower row (or vice‑versa) after that column, therefore
the whole way is impossible.
If no touching pair exists, she can always walk around the lava cells
(e.g. stay in the upper row as long as possible, cross to the lower row at
the first free column, …).  
So the answer after each update is simply:

```
possible  <=>  there is no touching pair of blocked cells
```

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 1.   Data structures

* `blocked[2][n+2]` – boolean array, `blocked[r][c]` is `True` iff the cell
  `(r,c)` (0‑based row) is lava.  
  Two extra columns (`0` and `n+1`) are added only to avoid bound checks.
* `conflicts` – integer, the current number of touching pairs.
  (`conflicts == 0`  →  answer *Yes*).

Both structures need `O(n)` memory.

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 2.   Updating one cell

When the cell `(r,c)` is toggled we have to update `conflicts`.

*Turning the cell **on** (ground → lava)*  

```
for each dc in {-1,0,1}:
        if blocked[other_row][c+dc] is True:
                conflicts += 1
blocked[r][c] = True
```

*Turning the cell **off** (lava → ground)*  

```
for each dc in {-1,0,1}:
        if blocked[other_row][c+dc] is True:
                conflicts -= 1
blocked[r][c] = False
```

Only the three neighbour columns of the opposite row can create or delete
touching pairs, therefore the update is `O(1)`.

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 3.   Correctness Proof  

We prove that the algorithm prints “Yes” exactly when a path exists.

---

##### Lemma 1  
A touching pair of blocked cells (one in each row, columns differ by at most
1) makes any path from `(1,1)` to `(2,n)` impossible.

**Proof.**  
Consider the leftmost column `x` that belongs to a touching pair.
All cells in columns `< x` are free, therefore the girl can reach both
cells `(1,x)` and `(2,x)`.  
Because the pair blocks the two rows in columns `x-1, x, x+1`,
there is no free side‑adjacent move that leaves this set of columns.
Consequently she cannot go from the left side of the pair to the right
side, and the destination `(2,n)` (which is to the right) is unreachable. ∎



##### Lemma 2  
If there is **no** touching pair of blocked cells, a path from `(1,1)` to
`(2,n)` always exists.

**Proof.**  
Traverse columns from left to right while staying in the upper row as long
as possible.  
Whenever the current cell `(1,c)` is blocked, the cell `(2,c)` must be free
(because otherwise a touching pair would exist).  
We can move down to `(2,c)` and continue moving right in the lower row.
Symmetrically, if the lower row becomes blocked we can move up.
Since no touching pair exists, such a move is always possible at the
first blocked cell we meet, and after the move we are again in a free cell.
Repeating this yields a monotone walk that finally reaches column `n`,
and because `(2,n)` itself is never blocked, the walk ends at the goal. ∎



##### Lemma 3  
`conflicts` maintained by the algorithm equals the number of touching
pairs of blocked cells in the current grid.

**Proof.**  
Initially all cells are free, so both numbers are `0`.  
Consider a toggle of cell `(r,c)`.

*If the cell becomes blocked*:  
For each neighbour column `c+dc` (`dc∈{-1,0,1}`) that already contains a
blocked cell in the opposite row, a new touching pair appears.
The algorithm adds exactly one to `conflicts` for each such neighbour,
hence `conflicts` increases by the number of newly created pairs.

*If the cell becomes free*:  
All touching pairs that involved this cell disappear, and they are exactly
the neighbours counted above. The algorithm subtracts one for each of them,
so `conflicts` decreases by the number of removed pairs.

No other pairs are affected, therefore after the update `conflicts`
still equals the true number of touching pairs. By induction the invariant
holds after every query. ∎



##### Lemma 4  
After each query the algorithm outputs “Yes” **iff** `conflicts = 0`.

**Proof.**  
The algorithm prints “Yes” exactly when `conflicts == 0` by construction. ∎



##### Theorem  
For every moment (after each toggle) the algorithm prints “Yes” exactly
when a path from `(1,1)` to `(2,n)` exists.

**Proof.**  
From Lemma&nbsp;3, `conflicts` is the number of touching pairs.
By Lemma&nbsp;1, any touching pair makes a path impossible.
By Lemma&nbsp;2, the absence of touching pairs guarantees a path.
Therefore a path exists **iff** `conflicts == 0`.
By Lemma&nbsp;4 the algorithm prints “Yes” exactly in this case. ∎



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 4.   Complexity Analysis

*Memory* : `2 × (n+2)` booleans → `O(n)`.

*Time* : each query touches at most three neighbour cells → `O(1)` per
query, `O(q)` total.

Both limits satisfy `n, q ≤ 10^5`.

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 5.   Reference Implementation  (Python 3)

```python
import sys

def solve() -> None:
    input = sys.stdin.readline
    n, q = map(int, input().split())

    # blocked[row][col] , rows 0 and 1, columns 0..n+1 (extra borders)
    blocked = [[False] * (n + 2) for _ in range(2)]

    conflicts = 0          # number of touching pairs
    out = []

    for _ in range(q):
        r, c = map(int, input().split())
        r -= 1               # to 0‑based row (0 or 1)

        if not blocked[r][c]:          # turn ON (ground -> lava)
            for dc in (-1, 0, 1):
                if blocked[1 - r][c + dc]:
                    conflicts += 1
            blocked[r][c] = True
        else:                           # turn OFF (lava -> ground)
            for dc in (-1, 0, 1):
                if blocked[1 - r][c + dc]:
                    conflicts -= 1
            blocked[r][c] = False

        out.append("Yes" if conflicts == 0 else "No")

    sys.stdout.write("\n".join(out))

if __name__ == "__main__":
    solve()
```

The program follows exactly the algorithm proven correct above and
conforms to the required input‑output format.
completed 109/150, Solved: True, number of success = 27/109, acc = 24.77
**Solution Explanation**

For every subarray `a[l … r]` we need the value  

```
f(l,r) = a[l] | a[l+1] | … | a[r]          (bitwise OR)
```

and we have to count how many different numbers appear among all `n·(n+1)/2`
subarrays.

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 1.  Observations  

* `x | y` never clears a bit – the set of `1`‑bits can only grow.
* The numbers are ≤ 10⁶, i.e. they use at most **20 bits** (`2²⁰ = 1 048 576`).

Because of the monotonicity, while we extend a subarray to the right,
the OR value can change only a limited number of times:

```
start with some value v
next value = v | a[i]          (adds some new bits)
next value = (v | a[i]) | a[i+1]   (adds more bits)
...
```

After at most 20 steps all bits that can ever become `1` are already set,
so the OR value stops changing.  
Therefore for a fixed right end `i` there are at most 20 *different* OR
values of subarrays that end at `i`.

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 2.  Dynamic programming over the right end  

Let  

```
cur = set of all different OR values of subarrays that end at position i
```

When we move to the next element `a[i+1]`

```
new_cur = { a[i+1] }                     // subarray consisting of only this element
          ∪ { v | a[i+1]  for v in cur } // extend every previous subarray by a[i+1]
```

`new_cur` again contains at most 20 numbers.
All values that ever appear are stored in a global set `all_vals`.

The algorithm:

```
all_vals = empty set
cur = empty set
for each element x in the array:
        nxt = {x}
        for v in cur:
                nxt.add(v | x)
        cur = nxt
        all_vals.update(cur)
answer = |all_vals|
```

The inner loop iterates over at most 20 elements, therefore the total
complexity is `O(n · 20) = O(n)`.

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 3.  Correctness Proof  

We prove that the algorithm returns exactly the number of distinct values
`f(l,r)`.

---

##### Lemma 1  
For a fixed right end `i` the set `cur` after processing `a[i]` equals  
`{ f(l,i) | 1 ≤ l ≤ i }`.

**Proof.** By induction over `i`.

*Base (`i = 1`).*  
`cur = { a[1] }`. The only subarray ending at 1 is `[1,1]`, its OR is `a[1]`.
So the statement holds.

*Induction step.* Assume the statement true for `i-1`.  
When processing `a[i]` the algorithm builds  

```
nxt = {a[i]} ∪ { v | a[i]  | v ∈ cur_{i-1} }
```

* `a[i]` is exactly `f(i,i)`.
* For any `l ≤ i-1` we have `v = f(l,i-1)` by the induction hypothesis,
  therefore `v | a[i] = f(l,i)`.

Thus `nxt` contains precisely all `f(l,i)` (`l = i` and `l ≤ i-1`).
∎



##### Lemma 2  
During the whole execution `all_vals = { f(l,r) | 1 ≤ l ≤ r ≤ current i }`,
i.e. after processing the first `i` elements it contains the OR of **all**
subarrays completely inside the prefix `a[1…i]`.

**Proof.** By induction over `i`.

*Base (`i = 1`).* After the first iteration `cur = {f(1,1)}` and we add it to
`all_vals`. So the claim holds.

*Induction step.* Assume the claim true after iteration `i-1`.  
In iteration `i` we compute `cur = { f(l,i) | 1 ≤ l ≤ i }` (Lemma&nbsp;1) and
add all its elements to `all_vals`. The set already contained every
`f(l,r)` with `r ≤ i-1`; now it also contains every `f(l,i)`. Hence after the
update it contains all subarrays whose right end ≤ `i`. ∎



##### Lemma 3  
At the end of the algorithm `all_vals` contains **every** value `f(l,r)` of the
whole array.

**Proof.** After the last iteration `i = n`, Lemma&nbsp;2 tells us that
`all_vals` contains all subarrays whose right end ≤ `n`, i.e. all subarrays of
the array. ∎



##### Theorem  
The algorithm outputs the number of distinct values of `f(l,r)` for the given
sequence.

**Proof.** By Lemma&nbsp;3 the set `all_vals` is exactly the set of all possible
OR results of subarrays. Its cardinality `|all_vals|` is therefore the required
answer, which the algorithm prints. ∎



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 4.  Complexity Analysis  

*Each iteration* processes at most `B = 20` elements (the number of bits).  

```
Time   : O(n · B) = O(n)   (n ≤ 10⁵)
Memory : O(|all_vals|) ≤ 2^B = 1 048 576  (fits easily in RAM)
```

Both limits satisfy the problem constraints.

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 5.  Reference Implementation  (Python 3)

```python
import sys

def solve() -> None:
    data = sys.stdin.read().strip().split()
    if not data:
        return
    n = int(data[0])
    a = list(map(int, data[1:]))

    all_vals = set()
    cur = set()                     # OR values of subarrays ending at previous position

    for x in a:
        nxt = {x}                   # subarray consisting only of x
        for v in cur:
            nxt.add(v | x)          # extend previous subarrays
        cur = nxt
        all_vals.update(cur)

    print(len(all_vals))

if __name__ == "__main__":
    solve()
```

**Solution Explanation**

For a non‑negative integer `m`

```
P(2) = a0 + a1·2 + a2·2² + … + ak·2^k          ( 0 ≤ ai ≤ 7 )
```

The coefficients `ai` are the *digits* of `m` written in base `2`
but the allowed digit set is `{0,1,…,7}` (instead of only `{0,1}`).
The task is to count how many different digit strings  
`a0 , a1 , … , ak` give the value `m`.

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 1.   Carry formulation  

While we read the binary representation of `m` from the least
significant bit to the most significant one we keep a **carry**
`c ( ≥ 0 )` produced by the lower positions.

For one position  

```
ai + c  =  biti  (mod 2)                (biti – i‑th binary digit of m)
new_c   = (ai + c – biti) / 2
```

`ai` may be any number `0 … 7` that satisfies the parity condition.
The new carry is an integer, therefore the whole process is a
finite state automaton whose state is the current carry.

*Maximum possible carry*  
`ai ≤ 7`. Starting with `c = 0` the sequence of carries is

```
0 → 3 → 5 → 6 → 6 → …
```

so the carry never exceeds `6`.  
The automaton has only the states `{0,1,…,6}`.

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 2.   Transition matrices for one binary digit  

For a fixed binary digit `b ∈ {0,1}` define a `7 × 7` matrix `M[b]`

```
M[b][c][c'] = number of choices of ai (0…7) with
               (ai + c) % 2 = b   and   (ai + c - b)/2 = c'
```

`M[b][c][c']` is `0 … 4` (at most four different `ai` give the same
`c'`).  
All arithmetic is performed modulo `MOD = 1 000 000 007`.

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 3.   Processing many bits at once – 16‑bit blocks  

The binary representation of `m` ( ≤ 10¹⁸ ) contains at most 60 bits.
Processing them one by one would need `≈ 60·7·7` elementary operations
per test case – far too many for `t = 5·10⁵`.

We group the bits into blocks of **16** bits.
For every possible 16‑bit pattern `p (0 … 2¹⁶‑1)` we pre‑compute the
combined transition matrix

```
T[p] = M[ b0 ] · M[ b1 ] · … · M[ b15 ]          (b0 – least significant bit)
```

`T[p]` is again a `7 × 7` matrix.
The pre‑computation is done once:

```
for every pattern p:
        for start_c = 0 … 6:
                vec = unit vector with 1 at start_c
                for i = 0 … 15:
                        b = (p >> i) & 1
                        vec = vec * M[b]          (vector‑matrix product)
                store vec as the start_c‑th row of T[p]
```

The work for one pattern is `7·16·4 ≈ 900` elementary operations,
overall about `6·10⁷` – easily fast enough.

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 4.   Answering one query  

```
vec = [1,0,0,0,0,0,0]          # start with carry = 0
while there are still bits of m or we have not processed an extra block:
        block = lowest 16 bits of m   (0 if m already exhausted)
        vec   = vec * T[block]        (vector‑matrix product, 7·7 ops)
        m    >>= 16
answer = vec[0]          # finally the carry must be 0
```

Why is one extra zero block sufficient?
The maximal remaining carry after all real bits is `6`.  
A block of 16 zero bits can represent any number `≤ 6·2¹⁶`,
hence it certainly contains all possibilities to eliminate the carry.
All higher bits are forced to be `0`, therefore we do **not** miss any
polynomial.

The number of processed blocks is at most  

```
ceil( (bitlen(m) + 16) / 16 ) ≤ 5
```

so each query needs at most `5·7·7 = 245` modular additions/multiplications.

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 5.   Correctness Proof  

We prove that the algorithm returns exactly the number of polynomials
`P` with coefficients in `{0,…,7}` satisfying `P(2)=m`.

---

##### Lemma 1  
For a fixed binary digit `b` and a carry `c` the set of admissible
coefficients `ai` is exactly the set used in the definition of `M[b]`,
and each such `ai` leads to the new carry `c' = (ai + c - b)/2`.

**Proof.**  
The definition of `P(2)` gives the equation  

```
ai + c = b  (mod 2)          (because the i‑th binary digit of m is b)
```

and the integer division by two yields the carry for the next position.
∎



##### Lemma 2  
For any binary digit `b` and any carries `c , c'`

```
M[b][c][c'] = | { ai ∈ {0,…,7}  :  (ai + c) % 2 = b  and  (ai + c - b)/2 = c' } |
```

**Proof.** Directly from Lemma&nbsp;1 and the construction of `M[b]`. ∎



##### Lemma 3  
Let `v` be a row‑vector where `v[c]` equals the number of ways to obtain
carry `c` after processing some prefix of the binary representation of
`m`. After reading the next binary digit `b` the new vector is `v·M[b]`.

**Proof
completed 111/150, Solved: False, number of success = 28/111, acc = 25.23
**Solution Explanation**

For every object `v ( 0 … n‑1 )`

* `v = 0` – the black hole  
* `v > 0` – a planet, the monster can be there

For every player  

* player `0` – Rick, his move set `S0` (`k0` numbers)  
* player `1` – Morty, his move set `S1` (`k1` numbers)

A *state* is a pair  

```
(player , position)      player = 0 or 1 ,  position = 0 … n‑1
```

When it is `player`’s turn and the monster is on `position`

* the player chooses any `x` from his set,
* the monster moves clockwise `x` steps,
* new position = `(position + x) mod n`.

If after the move the monster reaches the black hole (`0`) the player
who just moved **wins** immediately.

The task: for every start position `v = 1 … n‑1` and for both possible
first players decide whether the starter

* can force a win – **Win**
* will inevitably lose – **Lose**
* can avoid losing forever but cannot force a win – **Loop**  

(If a player can either lose or make the game infinite, he prefers the
infinite one.)

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 1.   Game theory view – reverse graph

The game is a finite directed graph:

```
edge  (p , v)  -->  (1-p , (v + x) mod n)   for every x in Sp
```

A state is **terminal** when the monster already sits in the black hole.
If it is your turn and the monster is already there, you have already
lost, therefore

```
state (0 , 0) = Lose
state (1 , 0) = Lose
```

All other states are initially **unknown** (Loop).

The classic way to solve such impartial games is a **reverse BFS**
starting from the known terminal states.

*If a state is Lose for the player to move, every predecessor state
(where the opponent could move to it) is Win for the opponent,
because the opponent can move to a losing state of his rival.*

*If a state is Win for the player to move, a predecessor state becomes
Lose for the opponent **only when all its outgoing moves lead to Win
states** (the opponent has no safe move).*

To apply this we need for every state the number of still‑unprocessed
outgoing moves – the **degree**.

```
degree[0][v] = k0   (size of Rick's set)   for all v
degree[1][v] = k1   (size of Morty's set) for all v
```

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 2.   BFS algorithm

```
queue ← (0,0) , (1,0)                // both are Lose
state[p][v] =  0   for all p,v       // 0 = Loop (unknown)
state[0][0] = state[1][0] = -1       // -1 = Lose,  1 = Win
while queue not empty
        (p , v) = queue.pop()
        cur = state[p][v]            // -1 or 1
        opp = 1-p
        for every move x in S_opp               // predecessor moves
                u = (v - x) mod n                // position before the move
                if u == 0 : continue            // black hole never appears as start
                if state[opp][u] != 0 : continue // already decided
                if cur == -1                    // we reached a Lose state
                        state[opp][u] = 1       // opponent can win
                        queue.push(opp,u)
                else   // cur == 1  (Win)
                        degree[opp][u] -= 1
                        if degree[opp][u] == 0
                                state[opp][u] = -1   // all moves go to Win → Lose
                                queue.push(opp,u)
```

When the queue becomes empty, every state that stayed `0` is a **Loop**
(the game can be forced to stay forever).

The algorithm touches each edge at most once, therefore  

```
time   :  O( n * (k0 + k1) )   ≤ 2·7000·7000  < 10⁸   (well inside limits)
memory :  O( n )
```

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 3.   Producing the answer

For player `0` (Rick) output `state[0][v]` for `v = 1 … n‑1`

```
-1 → "Lose"
 1 → "Win"
 0 → "Loop"
```

The same for player `1` (Morty).

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 4.   Correctness Proof  

We prove that the algorithm outputs the correct result for every state.

---

##### Lemma 1  
`state[p][0] = Lose` for both players is correct.

**Proof.**  
If the monster is already in the black hole before a player moves,
the previous move has already placed it there, therefore the previous
player has won and the current player has lost. ∎



##### Lemma 2  
Whenever the algorithm sets `state[opp][u] = Win`,
the opponent really has a winning strategy from `(opp , u)`.

**Proof.**  
The assignment happens only when we pop a state `(p , v)` that is already
known to be **Lose** for player `p`.  
`u` is a predecessor: the opponent can move from `(opp , u)` to `(p , v)`
by choosing the move `x` that satisfies `v = (u + x) mod n`.  
Since `(p , v)` is Lose for the player who must move there,
the opponent can move to a losing state of his rival and therefore wins
immediately. ∎



##### Lemma 3  
Whenever the algorithm sets `state[opp][u] = Lose`,
the opponent really has **no** winning move from `(opp , u)`.

**Proof.**  
The assignment is performed only after all outgoing moves of `(opp , u)`
have been examined and each of them leads to a state already known to be
**Win** for the player who will move next (i.e. for `p = 1‑opp`).  
The algorithm keeps a counter `degree[opp][u]` that is decreased each time
one of those successors becomes Win. When the counter reaches `0`,
*every* possible move from `(opp , u)` goes to a Win state for the opponent,
hence the current player cannot avoid giving the opponent a winning
position. Consequently the current player loses. ∎



##### Lemma 4  
All states that the algorithm finally marks **Win** (resp. **Lose**) are
indeed winning (resp. losing) positions for the player to move.

**Proof.**  
By induction on the order in which states are taken from the BFS queue.

*Base.* The two initial states `(0,0)` and `(1,0)` are Lose by Lemma&nbsp;1.

*Induction step.*  
Assume every state already removed from the queue is correctly classified.
Consider the next removed state `(p , v)`.

* If it is Lose, Lemma&nbsp;2 shows that every predecessor we newly mark
  Win is indeed winning.
* If it is Win, Lemma&nbsp;3 shows that every predecessor we newly mark
  Lose is indeed losing.

Thus the newly marked states are correct, completing the induction. ∎



##### Lemma 5  
If after the BFS a state remains **Loop**, then the game can be forced to
continue forever from that state (i.e. the player can avoid losing).

**Proof.**  
A Loop state never became Win or Lose, therefore:

* It is **not** Win – otherwise it would have been discovered by Lemma&nbsp;2.
* It is **not** Lose – otherwise all its moves would have been proven Win,
  decreasing its degree to zero and triggering Lemma&nbsp;3.

Hence the state has at least one outgoing move to another Loop state.
Repeating this choice forever yields an infinite play, never reaching a
terminal Lose state. ∎



##### Lemma 6  
For every state `(p , v)` the algorithm outputs the optimal result
according to the problem statement (Win > Loop > Lose).

**Proof.**  

* If the algorithm marks Win – by Lemma&nbsp;4 the player can force a win,
  which is optimal.
* Else if it marks Lose – by Lemma&nbsp;4 the player inevitably loses,
  no better outcome exists.
* Else it stays Loop – by Lemma&nbsp;5 the player can avoid losing forever,
  and no winning move exists (otherwise it would have been marked Win).
  Therefore Loop is the best achievable result. ∎



##### Theorem  
The program prints, for each starting planet and each first player,
the correct word **Win**, **Lose** or **Loop**.

**Proof.**  
Directly from Lemma&nbsp;6 applied to all states `(0 , v)` and `(1 , v)`
with `v = 1 … n‑1`. ∎



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 5.   Reference Implementation  (Python 3)

```python
import sys
from collections import deque

def solve() -> None:
    data = sys.stdin.read().strip().split()
    if not data:
        return
    it = iter(data)
    n = int(next(it))

    k0 = int(next(it))
    s0 = [int(next(it)) for _ in range(k0)]

    k1 = int(next(it))
    s1 = [int(next(it)) for _ in range(k1)]

    # state[p][v] :  0 = Loop (unknown),  1 = Win, -1 = Lose
    state = [[0] * n for _ in range(2)]
    # degree[p][v] = number of moves still not known to lead to opponent Win
    degree = [[k0] * n, [k1] * n]

    q = deque()
    # black hole is Lose for both players
    state[0][0] = -1
    state[1][0] = -1
    q.append((0, 0))
    q.append((1, 0))

    sets = [s0, s1]

    while q:
        p, v = q.popleft()
        cur = state[p][v]          # -1 (Lose) or 1 (Win)
        opp = 1 - p
        for x in sets[opp]:
            u = (v - x) % n        # predecessor position
            if u == 0:             # we never need results for the black hole as start
                continue
            if state[opp][u] != 0:
                continue
            if cur == -1:          # we reached a Lose -> predecessor is Win
                state[opp][u] = 1
                q.append((opp, u))
            else:                  # cur == 1 (Win)
                degree[opp][u] -= 1
                if degree[opp][u] == 0:
                    state[opp][u] = -1
                    q.append((opp, u))

    # translate to required words
    words = {1: "Win", -1: "Lose", 0: "Loop"}
    out0 = [words[state[0][i]] for i in range(1, n)]
    out1 = [words[state[1][i]] for i in range(1, n)]

    sys.stdout.write(' '.join(out0) + '\n')
    sys.stdout.write(' '.join(out1) + '\n')


if __name__ == "__main__":
    solve()
```

The program follows exactly the algorithm proven correct above
and conforms to the required input‑output format.
completed 112/150, Solved: True, number of success = 29/112, acc = 25.89
**Solution Explanation**

For every of the 7 time slots we have to know how many groups have a class
in that slot.
All classes that happen at the same time need different rooms, therefore
the number of rooms that must exist at that moment equals the number of
simultaneous classes.
The whole day can be covered with the *maximum* of those numbers, because
rooms may be reused in the next time slot.

So the answer is  

```
max over slots ( number of groups with a class in this slot )
```

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### Algorithm
```
read n
cnt[0 … 6] = 0                     // counters for the 7 slots
repeat n times:
        s = input line (string of length 7)
        for i = 0 … 6:
                if s[i] == '1':
                        cnt[i] += 1
answer = max(cnt)
print answer
```

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### Correctness Proof  

We prove that the algorithm outputs the minimum number of rooms.

*Lemma 1*  
For each time slot `t` (1 ≤ t ≤ 7) the algorithm stores in `cnt[t‑1]`
exactly the number of groups that have a class in slot `t`.

*Proof.*  
During the processing of a group we examine every character of its
7‑character schedule. Whenever the character for slot `t` is `'1'` we
increase `cnt[t‑1]` by one. Hence after all groups are processed,
`cnt[t‑1]` equals the total number of groups with `'1'` in that position,
i.e. the number of classes in slot `t`. ∎



*Lemma 2*  
At any time slot `t` at least `cnt[t‑1]` rooms are necessary.

*Proof.*  
All `cnt[t‑1]` groups have a class simultaneously in slot `t`.  
A single room can host at most one class at a time, therefore we need at
least one distinct room for each of those classes, i.e. at least
`cnt[t‑1]` rooms. ∎



*Lemma 3*  
Using `R = max(cnt)` rooms is sufficient for the whole day.

*Proof.*  
Consider any slot `t`. By Lemma&nbsp;1 the number of simultaneous classes
is `cnt[t‑1] ≤ max(cnt) = R`.  
We can assign each of those classes to a different room among the `R`
available rooms. After the slot ends the rooms become free again, so the
same `R` rooms can be reused for the next slot. Thus `R` rooms are enough
for every slot, and consequently for the whole day. ∎



*Theorem*  
The algorithm outputs the minimum possible number of rooms.

*Proof.*  
Let `R_alg = max(cnt)` be the algorithm’s output.

- By Lemma&nbsp;2 any feasible schedule needs at least `cnt[t‑1]` rooms for
  each slot `t`; therefore it needs at least `max(cnt) = R_alg` rooms.
- By Lemma&nbsp;3 `R_alg` rooms are sufficient.

Hence no schedule can use fewer than `R_alg` rooms and a schedule with
exactly `R_alg` rooms exists. So `R_alg` is the minimum number of rooms.
∎



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### Complexity Analysis  

The algorithm processes `n` groups, each with 7 characters.

```
Time   : O(n·7) = O(n)   (n ≤ 1000)
Memory : O(7) = O(1)
```

Both limits are easily satisfied.

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### Reference Implementation (Python 3)

```python
import sys

def solve() -> None:
    data = sys.stdin.read().strip().split()
    if not data:
        return
    n = int(data[0])
    cnt = [0] * 7                     # counters for the 7 time slots
    idx = 1
    for _ in range(n):
        s = data[idx]
        idx += 1
        # safety: ignore possible whitespace, consider only first 7 chars
        for i, ch in enumerate(s[:7]):
            if ch == '1':
                cnt[i] += 1
    answer = max(cnt)
    print(answer)

if __name__ == "__main__":
    solve()
```

The program follows exactly the algorithm proven correct above and
conforms to the required input‑output format.
completed 113/150, Solved: True, number of success = 30/113, acc = 26.55
**Solution Explanation**

For every value `x` let  

* `first[x]` – first position of `x` in the array  
* `last[x]`  – last  position of `x` in the array  
* `cnt[x]`   – how many times `x` occurs  

The whole segment `[first[x] , last[x]]` is called the **interval of `x`**.
All occurrences of `x` lie inside this interval, of course.

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 1.   When can a value stay unchanged ?

If we decide to keep the value `x` (i.e. we do **not** change any occurrence of `x`),
all positions that belong to the interval of `x` must finally contain the same
value – otherwise the final array would contain the value `x` in two separated
blocks.

All other values that appear inside the interval of `x` have to be changed
**to `x`**.  
Therefore we are allowed to keep `x` together with another value `y`
*only if the intervals of `x` and `y` are disjoint* – otherwise one of them
would be split by the other one.

Consequences  

* the intervals of all kept values are pairwise disjoint;
* a value whose interval contains another value’s interval can be kept **alone**
  (the inner value is changed to it);
* a value whose interval is completely inside another interval can **never**
  be kept together with the outer one.

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 2.   Values whose interval already consists of a single block  

For a value `x`

```
last[x] - first[x] + 1 == cnt[x]
```

holds **iff** inside its interval there is no other value – the whole interval
is already a single block of `x`.  
Such a value can be kept together with any other value whose interval does not
touch it, because it never forces a change of another element.

All those “already‑contiguous’’ values are completely independent.
Let  

```
base = sum of cnt[x] over all already‑contiguous values
```

`base` elements can stay unchanged for free.

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 3.   Keeping one non‑contiguous value  

Assume we keep a non‑contiguous value `v`.  
All values whose intervals lie **inside** `[first[v] , last[v]]` must be changed
to `v`, therefore they cannot be kept.
All other already‑contiguous values (those completely outside the interval of
`v`) may stay unchanged.

Hence, if we keep `v`

```
kept(v) = cnt[v] + (base – sum of cnt of already‑contiguous intervals that lie inside v’s interval)
```

Only one non‑contiguous value can be kept together with the outside
already‑contiguous ones – keeping two different non‑contiguous values would
require an interval that contains a part of the other one, which is impossible
(their intervals would intersect).

The optimal answer is therefore

```
max_keep = max( base ,  max over all non‑contiguous v of kept(v) )
```

and the required difficulty (minimal number of changed elements) is

```
answer = n – max_keep
```

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 4.   Computing the sums fast  

All already‑contiguous intervals are disjoint.
Store them as triples `(L,R,cnt)` and sort by `L`.

* `starts[i] = L_i`
* `ends[i]   = R_i`
* `pref[i]   = sum_{j < i} cnt_j`   (prefix sums)

For a non‑contiguous value `v` with interval `[L,R]`

* intervals completely **inside** `[L,R]` are those with  
  `L_i ≥ L` **and** `R_i ≤ R`.
  Because the intervals are disjoint, they form a contiguous sub‑array in the
  sorted list.
* using binary search (`bisect`) we find the first index `i` with `L_i ≥ L`
  and the last index `j` with `R_j ≤ R`.  
  The sum of their counts is `pref[j+1] – pref[i]`.

All operations are `O(log m)` where `m` is the number of already‑contiguous
values, therefore the whole algorithm works in `O(n log n)` time
(`n ≤ 2·10⁵`) and `O(n)` memory.

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 5.   Correctness Proof  

We prove that the algorithm outputs the minimal possible number of changed
elements.

---

##### Lemma 1  
If a value `x` is kept, every other kept value has an interval disjoint from
`[first[x],last[x]]`.

**Proof.**  
If another kept value `y` had an interval intersecting `x`’s interval, then at
least one occurrence of `x` would lie between two occurrences of `y` (or the
other way round). After the changes the final array would contain two blocks
of the same value separated by a different value – contradicting the definition
of a nice sequence. ∎



##### Lemma 2  
All already‑contiguous values can be kept simultaneously.

**Proof.**  
For an already‑contiguous value `x` the whole interval `[first[x],last[x]]`
contains only `x`. Therefore it never forces a change of any other element.
Two such intervals cannot intersect (otherwise a position would contain two
different values), consequently by Lemma&nbsp;1 they may be kept together. ∎



##### Lemma 3  
If a non‑contiguous value `v` is kept, no other value whose interval lies
inside `[first[v],last[v]]` can be kept.

**Proof.**  
All positions of the inner interval belong to the interval of `v`.  
If the inner value were kept, its occurrences would stay different from `v`,
splitting the block of `v`. This contradicts Lemma&nbsp;1. ∎



##### Lemma 4  
For any solution let `K` be the set of kept values.
`K` contains either  

* only already‑contiguous values, or  
* exactly one non‑contiguous value together with all already‑contiguous values
  whose intervals are outside that value’s interval.

**Proof.**  
If `K` contains two different non‑contiguous values `v₁ , v₂`,
their intervals intersect (otherwise they would be disjoint and each would
contain the other’s interval, contradicting Lemma&nbsp;3).  
Intersecting intervals cannot both belong to `K` by Lemma&nbsp;1.
Hence at most one non‑contiguous value can be kept.
All already‑contiguous values whose intervals intersect the kept non‑contiguous
interval must be discarded (Lemma&nbsp;3); the others may stay (Lemma&nbsp;2). ∎



##### Lemma 5  
For every non‑contiguous value `v` the algorithm computes exactly the total
number of elements that can stay unchanged when `v` is the (only) non‑contiguous
kept value.

**Proof.**  
When `v` is kept, all its `cnt[v]` elements stay.
Every already‑contiguous interval completely inside `[first[v],last[v]]`
must be changed (Lemma&nbsp;3) and therefore does **not** contribute.
All other already‑contiguous intervals are outside and may stay
(Lemma&nbsp;2).  
The algorithm subtracts the sum of the inside intervals from `base`,
which is precisely the number of outside already‑contiguous elements.
Adding `cnt[v]` gives the total number of unchanged elements. ∎



##### Lemma 6  
`max_keep` computed by the algorithm equals the maximum possible number of
unchanged elements in any feasible final nice sequence.

**Proof.**  
*Upper bound.*  
By Lemma&nbsp;4 any feasible solution belongs to one of the two families
described there.
*   Family 1 (only already‑contiguous values) keeps at most `base` elements.
*   Family 2 (one non‑contiguous `v` plus outside already‑contiguous) keeps
    at most `kept(v)` elements, and Lemma&nbsp;5 shows the algorithm evaluates
    exactly this number.
Therefore any solution keeps ≤ `max_keep`.

*Achievability.*  
*   Keeping all already‑contiguous values yields `base` unchanged elements.
*   For any non‑contiguous `v`, changing all values of the inside intervals
    to `v` and leaving the outside already‑contiguous values unchanged produces
    a nice sequence with exactly `kept(v)` unchanged elements (construction
    follows Lemma&nbsp;5).  
Thus the algorithm’s maximum is attainable. ∎



##### Lemma 7  
The answer printed by the program, `n – max_keep`, equals the minimum possible
number of changed elements.

**Proof.**  
`max_keep` is the maximal number of elements that can stay unchanged
(Lemma&nbsp;6).  
Consequently the minimal number that must be changed is `n – max_keep`. ∎



##### Theorem  
For the given input (with `q = 0`) the program outputs the difficulty of the
initial sequence, i.e. the minimum possible number of elements that have to be
changed to obtain a nice sequence under the stated global‑change rule.

**Proof.**  
Directly from Lemma&nbsp;7. ∎



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 6.   Complexity Analysis  

*Scanning the array* – `O(n)` time, `O(n)` memory for the maps.  
*Collecting already‑contiguous intervals* – `O(m)` where `m ≤ n`.  
*Sorting them* – `O(m log m)`.  
*Processing every non‑contiguous value* – each needs two binary searches,
`O(log m)`. The number of such values is at most the number of distinct
values, ≤ `n`.  

Overall  

```
Time   : O(n log n)
Memory : O(n)
```

Both satisfy the limits (`n ≤ 200 000`).

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 7.   Reference Implementation  (Python 3)

```python
import sys
import bisect

def solve() -> None:
    data = sys.stdin.read().strip().split()
    if not data:
        return
    it = iter(data)
    n = int(next(it))
    q = int(next(it))          # q is always 0, we ignore it
    a = [int(next(it)) for _ in range(n)]

    # first, last, count for each value
    first = {}
    last = {}
    cnt = {}
    for idx, val in enumerate(a, 1):
        if val not in first:
            first[val] = idx
        last[val] = idx
        cnt[val] = cnt.get(val, 0) + 1

    # intervals that are already contiguous
    contig_intervals = []          # (L,R,weight)
    base = 0
    for val in cnt:
        L = first[val]
        R = last[val]
        c = cnt[val]
        if R - L + 1 == c:        # no other value inside
            contig_intervals.append((L, R, c))
            base += c

    # sort contiguous intervals by left endpoint
    contig_intervals.sort()
    m = len(contig_intervals)
    starts = [iv[0] for iv in contig_intervals]
    ends   = [iv[1] for iv in contig_intervals]
    pref   = [0] * (m + 1)
    for i, (_, _, w) in enumerate(contig_intervals, 1):
        pref[i] = pref[i-1] + w

    max_keep = base   # case: keep only contiguous values

    # evaluate each non‑contiguous value
    for val in cnt:
        L = first[val]
        R = last[val]
        c = cnt[val]
        if R - L + 1 == c:          # already contiguous, already counted
            continue

        # find intervals completely inside [L,R]
        i = bisect.bisect_left(starts, L)          # first with start >= L
        j = bisect.bisect_right(ends, R) - 1       # last with end <= R
        inside = 0
        if i <= j:
            inside = pref[j+1] - pref[i]

        outside = base - inside
        keep = c + outside
        if keep > max_keep:
            max_keep = keep

    answer = n - max_keep
    print(answer)

if __name__ == "__main__":
    solve()
```

The program follows exactly the algorithm proven correct above and conforms
to the required input‑output format.
completed 114/150, Solved: False, number of success = 30/114, acc = 26.32
**Solution Explanation**

For one test case we are given  

* an integer `x` (`1 ≤ x ≤ 10^6`)
* a string `s` consisting only of the characters `1 , 2 , 3` (`|s| ≤ 500`)

The cursor starts left of the first character (`ℓ = 0`).  
The following loop is executed

```
while ℓ < x
        move cursor one step to the right                (ℓ ← ℓ+1)
        cut the part on the right of the cursor
        paste the clipboard   sℓ   times
```

` sℓ ` is the digit (`1,2,3`) that is **currently** at the cursor position.
The loop stops exactly after the iteration with `ℓ = x`.

We have to output the final length of the string, modulo  

```
MOD = 1 000 000 007
```

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 1.   What really happens in one iteration ?

Assume before the iteration the whole string has length `L`
and the cursor is at position `i` (`i = ℓ` after the *move* step, `1‑based`).

```
prefix   = s[1 … i]                length = i
suffix   = s[i+1 … L]              length = L-i
```

*cut* removes the suffix, the clipboard becomes `suffix`.  
*paste* appends the clipboard `d = s_i` times.

```
new length = i + d·(L-i)
```

Only the length is needed for the answer, but we also need the
characters `s_i` for all `i = 1 … x`.  
`x ≤ 10^6`, therefore we can really store the first `x` characters of the
ever‑growing string – no more are ever required.

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 2.   Updating the length (modulo)

Let  

```
len_mod   – current length modulo MOD
i         – 0‑based index of the cursor (i = ℓ-1)
d         – integer value of s[i]   (1,2 or 3)
```

The real length before the iteration is `len_mod` (modulo only).
The part *after* the cursor has length `len_mod - i - 1` (again only modulo
is needed).

```
extra = (len_mod - i - 1)               // length of the suffix (mod MOD)
len_mod = (len_mod + extra·(d-1)) % MOD // because i + d·extra = i+extra + extra·(d-1)
```

All operations are `O(1)`.

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 3.   Keeping the first `x` characters

The real (non‑modulo) length of the already built prefix is `len(s)`.
If it is already `≥ x` we do **nothing** – we already know all needed digits.

Otherwise we have to simulate the *paste* operation, but only until the
stored string reaches length `x`.

```
suffix = s[i+1 : current_len]          // the part that has to be repeated
repeat = d-1                           // we already have one copy (the prefix)
for repeat times
        copy as many characters of suffix as still needed
```

Every character that is appended becomes part of the final string,
therefore each character is copied **at most once**.
Overall work for one test case is `O(x)`.

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 4.   Correctness Proof  

We prove that the algorithm outputs the correct final length for every
test case.

---

##### Lemma 1  
During the processing of position `i` (`0‑based`) the variable `len_mod`
equals the current length of the string modulo `MOD`.

**Proof.**  
Induction over `i`.

*Base (`i = -1` before the loop)*:  
`len_mod` is initialised with `|s|`, the true length, modulo `MOD`.

*Induction step.*  
Assume the statement true before processing position `i`.  
Let `L` be the real length before the iteration, `d = s_i`.  
The algorithm computes  

```
extra = (len_mod - i - 1)  (mod MOD) = (L - i - 1) (mod MOD)
len_mod' = (len_mod + extra·(d-1)) (mod MOD)
```

Because `len_mod ≡ L (mod MOD)` and `extra ≡ L-i-1 (mod MOD)`

```
len_mod' ≡ L + (L-i-1)·(d-1) ≡ i + d·(L-i-1)   (mod MOD)
```

The right hand side is exactly the new real length after the iteration,
hence `len_mod'` equals the new length modulo `MOD`. ∎



##### Lemma 2  
After finishing the iteration for position `i` the list `s`
contains the first `min(|S_i|, x)` characters of the current string,
where `S_i` denotes the whole string after processing position `i`.

**Proof.**  
Induction over `i`.

*Base (`i = -1`)*: `s` is the initial string, therefore the claim holds.

*Induction step.*  
Assume the claim true after position `i-1`.  
During the iteration for `i` the algorithm copies the suffix
`S_{i-1}[i+1 …]` exactly `d-1` times, but stops as soon as `|s| = x`.
Thus the characters appended are precisely the characters that appear
in the real string after the cursor, in the correct order, until either
the whole suffix repetitions are finished or `x` characters have been
generated. Consequently after the iteration `s` again stores the first
`min(|S_i|, x)` characters. ∎



##### Lemma 3  
For every `i = 0 … x-1` the algorithm uses the correct digit `d = s_i`
(the digit that the real process would see at that moment).

**Proof.**  
By Lemma&nbsp;2, after processing position `i-1` the list `s` already
contains the first `i` characters of the real string.
Therefore `s[i]` equals the real character at the cursor before the
iteration for `i`. ∎



##### Lemma 4  
The loop executes exactly `x` iterations.

**Proof.**  
The loop variable `i` starts at `0` and is increased by one each iteration
until `i = x`. The condition `while i < x` therefore performs precisely
the iterations for `i = 0,1,…,x-1`. This matches the definition of the
original procedure, which stops after the cursor has been moved to
position `x`. ∎



##### Theorem  
For each test case the algorithm outputs the length of the string after
the described procedure stops, modulo `10^9+7`.

**Proof.**  

*Length:*  
By Lemma&nbsp;1, after the last iteration (`i = x-1`) the variable
`len_mod` equals the true final length modulo `MOD`. The algorithm prints
exactly this value.

*Termination:*  
The loop runs `x` times (Lemma&nbsp;4). In each iteration the required
digit is correct (Lemma&nbsp;3), therefore the simulated transformation
is identical to the real one, and the final length is the same.

Thus the printed number is the required answer. ∎



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 5.   Complexity Analysis  

For one test case  

* the loop runs `x` times → `O(x)` iterations
* each character that is ever stored in `s` is copied at most once,
  total number of stored characters ≤ `x`

```
Time   :  O(x)
Memory :  O(x)   (the first x characters, at most 10^6)
```

The sum of all `x` over the whole input is ≤ `10^6`,
so the program easily fits into the limits.

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 6.   Reference Implementation  (Python 3)

```python
import sys

MOD = 1_000_000_007

def solve() -> None:
    data = sys.stdin.read().split()
    it = iter(data)
    t = int(next(it))
    out_lines = []

    for _ in range(t):
        x = int(next(it))
        s_str = next(it).strip()
        s = list(s_str)                 # real characters, we keep at most x of them
        length_mod = len(s) % MOD       # current length modulo MOD
        i = 0

        while i < x:
            d = int(s[i])               # digit at current cursor position (1,2 or 3)

            # ----- update length (modulo) -----
            extra = (length_mod - i - 1) % MOD          # length of the suffix (mod MOD)
            length_mod = (length_mod + extra * (d - 1)) % MOD

            # ----- extend the stored prefix if we still need characters -----
            if len(s) < x:
                cur_len = len(s)                         # length before extension
                suffix = s[i + 1:cur_len]                # the part that will be repeated
                repeat = d - 1
                for _ in range(repeat):
                    if len(s) >= x:
                        break
                    need = x - len(s)
                    # copy as many as possible from suffix
                    if need >= len(suffix):
                        s.extend(suffix)
                    else:
                        s.extend(suffix[:need])

            i += 1

        out_lines.append(str(length_mod))

    sys.stdout.write("\n".join(out_lines))


if __name__ == "__main__":
    solve()
```

The program follows exactly the algorithm proven correct above
and conforms to the required input‑output format.
completed 115/150, Solved: True, number of success = 31/115, acc = 26.96
**Solution Explanation**

For a triple of positions `i < j < k`

* `s[i]` must be `'Q'`
* `s[j]` must be `'A'`
* `s[k]` must be `'Q'`

The number of required subsequences is therefore the number of such triples.

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### Observation  

For a fixed position `j` that contains `'A'`

* any `'Q'` **before** `j` can be used as the first character,
* any `'Q'` **after** `j` can be used as the last character.

If  

* `left[j]` = number of `'Q'` in the prefix `s[0 … j‑1]`
* `right[j]` = number of `'Q'` in the suffix `s[j+1 … n‑1]`

then the number of triples that use this particular `'A'` is  

```
left[j] * right[j]
```

Summation over all positions `j` with `s[j] == 'A'` gives the answer.

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### Algorithm
```
read string s, n = len(s)

# prefix count of Q
left = [0] * n
cnt = 0
for i in range(n):
    left[i] = cnt
    if s[i] == 'Q':
        cnt += 1

# suffix count of Q
right = [0] * n
cnt = 0
for i in range(n-1, -1, -1):
    right[i] = cnt
    if s[i] == 'Q':
        cnt += 1

ans = 0
for i in range(n):
    if s[i] == 'A':
        ans += left[i] * right[i]

print(ans)
```

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### Correctness Proof  

We prove that the algorithm outputs exactly the number of subsequences
`"QAQ"`.

*Lemma 1*  
For any position `j` (`0 ≤ j < n`) `left[j]` equals the number of indices
`i` with `i < j` and `s[i] = 'Q'`.

*Proof.*  
The loop that fills `left` processes the string from left to right.
Before processing `s[j]` the variable `cnt` holds the number of `'Q'`
seen so far, i.e. exactly the number of `'Q'` in positions `< j`.  
`left[j]` is assigned this value, then `cnt` is possibly increased if
`s[j]` itself is `'Q'`. ∎



*Lemma 2*  
For any position `j` (`0 ≤ j < n`) `right[j]` equals the number of indices
`k` with `k > j` and `s[k] = 'Q'`.

*Proof.*  
Analogous to Lemma&nbsp;1, but the loop runs from right to left, therefore
`cnt` contains the number of `'Q'` already seen to the right of `j`. ∎



*Lemma 3*  
For a fixed position `j` with `s[j] = 'A'` the number of triples
`(i, j, k)` that form a subsequence `"QAQ"` equals `left[j] * right[j]`.

*Proof.*  
By Lemma&nbsp;1 there are `left[j]` possible choices for `i` (any `'Q'`
before `j`).  
By Lemma&nbsp;2 there are `right[j]` possible choices for `k` (any `'Q'`
after `j`).  
Every pair `(i, k)` together with the fixed `j` yields a distinct triple,
and all such triples are valid because the order `i < j < k` holds.
Thus the total number is the product. ∎



*Theorem*  
The algorithm outputs the total number of subsequences `"QAQ"` in `s`.

*Proof.*  
The algorithm sums `left[j] * right[j]` over all positions `j` where
`s[j] = 'A'`.  
By Lemma&nbsp;3 each summand equals the number of triples that use this
particular `'A'`.  
Different `'A'` positions are disjoint in the triples they generate, so
the sum counts **every** valid triple exactly once.  
Therefore the printed value equals the required number of subsequences. ∎



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### Complexity Analysis  

* Computing `left` and `right` each scans the string once: `O(n)`.
* The final loop is another `O(n)`.

Memory usage: three arrays of length `n` (`left`, `right`, the string) → `O(n)`.

With `n ≤ 100` this easily satisfies the limits.

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### Reference Implementation (Python 3)

```python
import sys

def solve() -> None:
    s = sys.stdin.readline().strip()
    n = len(s)

    # left[i] = number of 'Q' before position i
    left = [0] * n
    cnt = 0
    for i in range(n):
        left[i] = cnt
        if s[i] == 'Q':
            cnt += 1

    # right[i] = number of 'Q' after position i
    right = [0] * n
    cnt = 0
    for i in range(n - 1, -1, -1):
        right[i] = cnt
        if s[i] == 'Q':
            cnt += 1

    ans = 0
    for i in range(n):
        if s[i] == 'A':
            ans += left[i] * right[i]

    print(ans)


if __name__ == "__main__":
    solve()
```
