completed 116/150, Solved: True, number of success = 32/116, acc = 27.59
**Solution Explanation**

For every psycho we look at the neighbour on the left.

* a psycho dies in a step **iff** the left neighbour has a larger id  
  (the left neighbour kills him).

All killings of one step are simultaneous, therefore after a step we only
keep the psychos that are **not** killed, i.e. the ids form a *non‑decreasing*
sequence.

The process is repeated until the whole line becomes non‑decreasing.
The required answer is the number of performed steps.



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 1.   Reformulation

For the array `a[0 … n‑1]`

```
in one step   remove every i ( i > 0 ) with a[i‑1] > a[i]
```

The same problem is known as **Poisonous Plants** (there a plant dies if
its value is larger than the left neighbour).  
Only the comparison sign is reversed, therefore the same algorithm works
with the sign changed.



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 2.   Observations

*If a psycho never dies* – there is no larger element on his left that can
ever become his immediate neighbour.  
*If a psycho dies* – the day on which he dies depends only on the elements
to his left, never on the elements to his right.

Consequently we can process the array from left to right and, for each
position, compute the day on which this psycho disappears.
The answer is the maximum of those days.



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 3.   Stack algorithm  

The stack keeps pairs **(value , death_day)** for a suffix of the already
processed part of the line.

For the current element `x = a[i]`

```
days = 0
while stack not empty and x >= stack.top.value:
        # the top element cannot kill x (its value is not larger)
        days = max(days, stack.top.death_day)
        pop the top
# after the loop:
#   - if the stack is empty, there is no larger element on the left → x never dies
#   - otherwise the element now on the top is the nearest left neighbour larger than x
#     therefore x will die one day after the maximal day of all popped elements
if stack empty:
        days = 0
else:
        days += 1
push (x , days) onto the stack
answer = max(answer , days)
```

Why does it work?

* The `while` loop removes all elements that are **not larger** than `x`.
  They can never be the neighbour that kills `x`.  
  While removing them we remember the largest death day among them –
  those elements may disappear earlier and expose a larger element that is
  farther left.
* After the loop the new stack top (if any) is the closest element on the
  left that is larger than `x`.  
  `x` will die exactly one day after the day when that larger element becomes
  the neighbour, i.e. after `max(popped_death_days) + 1`.
* If the stack became empty, there is no larger element on the left,
  therefore `x` survives forever (`days = 0`).

Each array element is pushed once and popped at most once → **O(n)** time,
**O(n)** memory.



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 4.   Correctness Proof  

We prove that the algorithm outputs the required number of steps.

---

##### Lemma 1  
For every processed position `i` the value `days` computed by the algorithm
equals the day on which psycho `i` dies (or `0` if he never dies).

**Proof.**  
Induction over `i`.

*Base (`i = 0`).*  
The stack is empty, the algorithm sets `days = 0`.  
The first psycho has no left neighbour, therefore he never dies. ✓

*Induction step.*  
Assume the statement true for all positions `< i`.  
Consider psycho `i` with id `x = a[i]`.

All elements left of `i` are already in the stack, each stored together with
its true death day (induction hypothesis).

The `while` loop removes exactly those left elements whose value `≤ x`.
None of them can ever kill `x` (they are not larger), therefore they are
irrelevant for the death of `x`.  
During removal we keep `mx = max` of their death days.

After the loop the stack top (if it exists) is the nearest left element
with value `> x`. Call it `L`.  
All elements between `L` and `x` have already been removed, consequently
after day `mx` they are gone and `L` becomes the immediate left neighbour of
`x`. Because `L` is larger, `x` will be killed exactly on the next day,
i.e. on day `mx + 1`.

If the stack is empty, there is no larger element on the left, thus `x`
never dies (`days = 0`).

The algorithm sets `days` exactly to these values, proving the lemma. ∎



##### Lemma 2  
`answer = max_i days_i` (the maximum death day among all psychos) equals the
total number of steps after which no more killings occur.

**Proof.**  
All psychos that die do so on the day stored in `days_i`.  
After day `answer` every psycho with `days_i ≤ answer` is already dead,
and every remaining psycho has `days_i = 0` (never dies).  
Hence from day `answer` onward no psycho has a larger left neighbour,
so no further killings happen.  
Conversely, before day `answer` at least one psycho with `days_i = answer`
is still alive and will die on that day, therefore the process is not yet
stable. ∎



##### Theorem  
The algorithm outputs the exact number of steps after which the line of
psychos stops changing.

**Proof.**  
By Lemma&nbsp;1 the algorithm computes for each psycho its true death day.
By Lemma&nbsp;2 the maximum of those days is precisely the required number
of steps. The algorithm prints this maximum, therefore it is correct. ∎



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 5.   Complexity Analysis  

*Time* : each element is pushed once and popped at most once → `O(n)`  
*Memory*: the stack holds at most `n` pairs → `O(n)`



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 6.   Reference Implementation  (Python 3)

```python
import sys

def solve() -> None:
    data = sys.stdin.read().strip().split()
    if not data:
        return
    n = int(data[0])
    a = list(map(int, data[1:1 + n]))

    stack = []                     # each element is (value, death_day)
    answer = 0

    for x in a:
        days = 0
        # remove all left elements that are not larger than x
        while stack and x >= stack[-1][0]:
            days = max(days, stack[-1][1])
            stack.pop()
        if not stack:              # no larger element on the left
            days = 0
        else:                      # nearest larger element exists
            days += 1
        stack.append((x, days))
        if days > answer:
            answer = days

    print(answer)


if __name__ == "__main__":
    solve()
```

The program follows exactly the algorithm proven correct above and conforms
to the required input‑output format.
completed 117/150, Solved: True, number of success = 33/117, acc = 28.21
**Solution Explanation**

For a rooted tree we have a parent array `p[1 … n]`

* exactly one vertex `r` satisfies `p[r] = r` – the root,
* for every other vertex `v` the edge `{v , p[v]}` exists,
* the undirected graph formed by those edges is a tree  
  (connected and without cycles).

If we look at the directed edges `v → p[v]`

* every vertex has out‑degree `1`,
* the root has a self‑loop,
* all other vertices belong to a directed tree whose edges are
  directed towards the root.

So a *valid* parent array is a **functional graph** that contains

* one self‑loop,
* no other directed cycles.

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 1.   What is wrong with an arbitrary array `a` ?

In a functional graph each connected component contains **exactly one**
directed cycle (maybe a self‑loop).  
Let  

* `C` – number of connected components ( = number of cycles ),
* `S` – number of components whose cycle is a self‑loop.

If `S ≥ 1` we can keep one of those self‑loops as the root,
all other components have to be attached to this root.
If `S = 0` we have to create a root by turning one vertex of some
component into a self‑loop.

For every component **except the one that will become the root**
we have to break its cycle by changing **one** edge, e.g. make one
vertex of the cycle point to the root.
For the component that becomes the root we also have to change
exactly one edge – either the existing self‑loop (if it already is a
self‑loop we keep it, otherwise we turn a vertex into a self‑loop).

Therefore the minimal number of changes is  

```
answer = C                     if S = 0
answer = C - 1                 if S ≥ 1
```

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 2.   Finding all cycles

`a[i]` (1‑based) is the given parent of vertex `i`.  
We walk through the functional graph and mark vertices with the id of
the current walk.  
When we reach a vertex already marked with the same id we have found a
directed cycle – we collect all its vertices.

The algorithm works in `O(n)` time and `O(n)` memory.

```
visited[i] = 0          – not processed yet
visited[i] = id > 0     – visited while processing start vertex id
```

For every start vertex `i`

```
u = i
while visited[u] == 0:
        visited[u] = i
        u = a[u]

if visited[u] == i:          # u belongs to the current walk → a cycle
        collect the cycle starting from u
```

All cycles are stored in a list `cycles`.  
Each element of `cycles` is a list of vertices belonging to one
directed cycle.

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 3.   Building a valid parent array with the minimum number of changes

*choose the root*

```
root = first vertex of a self‑loop, if any
otherwise root = first vertex of the first cycle
```

*prepare the answer array*

```
p = a[:]                     # copy
changes = 0
```

*make the root a self‑loop (if it is not already)*

```
if p[root] != root:
        p[root] = root
        changes += 1
```

*break all other cycles – one change per cycle*

```
for each cycle in cycles:
        if root is inside this cycle:   continue
        v = cycle[0]                    # any vertex of the cycle
        p[v] = root
        changes += 1
```

All other entries stay unchanged, therefore the number of performed
changes equals the theoretical minimum derived above.

The resulting directed graph consists of one self‑loop (the root) and
all other vertices have a unique directed path to the root – exactly a
rooted tree.

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 4.   Correctness Proof  

We prove that the algorithm outputs a valid parent array and that the
number of changed entries is minimal.

---

##### Lemma 1  
In a functional graph each connected component contains exactly one
directed cycle.

**Proof.**  
Every vertex has out‑degree `1`. Starting from any vertex and following
edges we must eventually repeat a vertex (pigeonhole principle). The
first repeated vertex closes a directed cycle. All vertices that can be
reached from the start belong to the same component, and after the
first cycle is reached we can never leave it because every vertex has
only one outgoing edge. ∎



##### Lemma 2  
Let `C` be the number of components of the input graph and `S` the
number of components whose cycle is a self‑loop.  
Any valid parent array needs at least  

```
C                     if S = 0
C - 1                 if S ≥ 1
```

changed entries.

**Proof.**  
A valid array must contain exactly one self‑loop (the root).  
All other components must be merged into the component of the root,
otherwise the undirected graph would be disconnected.

*If `S ≥ 1`* we can keep one existing self‑loop as the root.
All remaining `C‑1` components have to lose their own cycle, which
requires at least one changed edge per component → at least `C‑1`
changes.

*If `S = 0`* there is no self‑loop, therefore we must create one.
Creating a self‑loop needs at least one changed edge, and the other
`C‑1` components also need at least one change each → at least `C`
changes. ∎



##### Lemma 3  
The algorithm changes exactly  

```
C                     if S = 0
C - 1                 if S ≥ 1
```

entries.

**Proof.**  
The algorithm processes every component exactly once (the cycles list
contains one entry per component, Lemma&nbsp;1).

*Root component*  
If the root already is a self‑loop, no change is performed for it.
Otherwise the algorithm changes one vertex of that component to a
self‑loop – exactly one change.

*All other components*  
For each of them the algorithm changes one vertex (the first vertex of
the cycle) to point to the root – exactly one change per component.

Hence the total number of changes equals the number of components
minus one if a self‑loop already existed, otherwise equals the number
of components. ∎



##### Lemma 4  
After the algorithm finishes, the resulting parent array `p`
represents a rooted tree.

**Proof.**  

*Exactly one self‑loop.*  
The algorithm creates a self‑loop at the chosen `root` and never
creates another one (all other changed edges point to `root`).  
If the root already had a self‑loop, it is kept; otherwise it is
created. Hence there is exactly one self‑loop.

*No other directed cycles.*  
Consider any component different from the root component.
Originally it contained a directed cycle `C`. The algorithm changes one
edge of `C` to point to `root`. After this modification the remaining
edges of `C` form a directed tree whose root is the vertex that now
points to `root`. Therefore the component becomes acyclic.

The root component may have originally contained a longer cycle.
If the root was not a self‑loop, the algorithm changes the edge
`root → something` into `root → root`. The rest of the former cycle
still points towards `root`, so no directed cycle remains.

Thus the whole directed graph consists of a single self‑loop and a
directed tree rooted at it, i.e. the undirected graph is a tree. ∎



##### Lemma 5  
The algorithm produces a parent array with the minimum possible number
of changed entries.

**Proof.**  
By Lemma&nbsp;3 the algorithm changes exactly the number of entries
given in Lemma&nbsp;2. Lemma&nbsp;2 states that no solution can use fewer
changes. Therefore the algorithm is optimal. ∎



##### Theorem  
The program prints  

* the minimum number of elements that have to be changed,
* a valid parent array obtained after that many changes.

**Proof.**  
The program implements the algorithm described above.
By Lemma&nbsp;4 the printed array is a valid parent array.
By Lemma&nbsp;5 the number of performed changes equals the theoretical
minimum, which the program also prints. ∎



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 5.   Complexity Analysis  

*Finding all cycles* – each vertex is visited at most once: `O(n)` time,
`O(n)` additional memory for the `visited` array and the list of cycles.

*Building the answer* – one pass over the list of cycles: `O(n)` time,
`O(1)` extra memory.

Overall  

```
Time   :  O(n)      ( ≤ 2·10⁵ )
Memory :  O(n)
```

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 6.   Reference Implementation  (Python 3)

```python
import sys

def solve() -> None:
    data = sys.stdin.read().strip().split()
    if not data:
        return
    it = iter(data)
    n = int(next(it))
    a = [0] * (n + 1)          # 1‑based
    for i in range(1, n + 1):
        a[i] = int(next(it))

    visited = [0] * (n + 1)    # 0 = not visited, otherwise start id
    cycles = []                # list of lists, each is a directed cycle

    for start in range(1, n + 1):
        if visited[start]:
            continue
        u = start
        while visited[u] == 0:
            visited[u] = start
            u = a[u]
        # u is the first vertex already visited in this walk
        if visited[u] == start:            # a new cycle discovered
            cyc = []
            v = u
            while True:
                cyc.append(v)
                v = a[v]
                if v == u:
                    break
            cycles.append(cyc)

    # choose the root
    root = None
    for cyc in cycles:
        if len(cyc) == 1 and a[cyc[0]] == cyc[0]:
            root = cyc[0]
            break
    if root is None:
        root = cycles[0][0]                # any vertex of first cycle

    p = a[:]                              # copy, still 1‑based
    changes = 0

    if p[root] != root:
        p[root] = root
        changes += 1

    for cyc in cycles:
        if root in cyc:
            continue
        v = cyc[0]                         # any vertex of the cycle
        if p[v] != root:
            p[v] = root
            changes += 1

    # output
    out = [str(changes), ' '.join(str(p[i]) for i in range(1, n + 1))]
    sys.stdout.write('\n'.join(out))

if __name__ == "__main__":
    solve()
```

The program follows exactly the algorithm proven correct above and
conforms to the required input‑output format.

**Solution Explanation**

For every photo we know

* `c[i] = 1` – time to look at the photo  
* `+ b`   – additional time if the photo must be rotated (`s[i] = 'w'`)

```
c[i] = 1                     , if s[i] = 'h'
c[i] = 1 + b                 , if s[i] = 'w'
```

Moving from a photo to the next one (left or right) costs `a` seconds.

Vasya starts at photo `1`.  
When a photo is opened for the **first** time he has to spend `c[i]`,
later he can pass it without any extra time – only the movement cost
remains.

The phone is a circle, therefore moving left from `1` reaches `n`,
moving right from `n` reaches `1`.

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 1.   What can the set of visited photos look like ?

Vasya can move only to a neighbour, therefore to reach a photo he has
to pass all photos that lie on the way.
Consequently the set of distinct photos he ever opens is a **contiguous
segment of the circle that contains photo 1**.

```
          left side                right side
   1 ← n ← n‑1 …                1 → 2 → 3 …
```

Let  

* `L` – number of steps to the left (photos `n, n‑1, …`), `0 ≤ L ≤ n‑1`
* `R` – number of steps to the right (photos `2, 3, …`), `0 ≤ R ≤ n‑1‑L`

The number of different photos that are watched

```
k = L + R + 1                (photo 1 itself)
```

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 2.   Minimal movement needed for a given `L , R`

Vasya may first walk to the left, come back to photo 1 and then walk to
the right, or the opposite order.
The cheaper of the two possibilities is

```
move(L,R) = min( 2·L + R , 2·R + L )
```

*If `R ≥ L` the first order (left → right) is cheaper, otherwise the
second one.*

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 3.   Total time for a fixed `L , R`

```
time(L,R) = a · move(L,R) + c[1] + sum_left(L) + sum_right(R)
```

* `sum_left(L)` – sum of `c[i]` of the first `L` photos on the left side
  (`n, n‑1, …`)
* `sum_right(R)` – sum of `c[i]` of the first `R` photos on the right side
  (`2, 3, …`)

Both prefix sums are pre‑computed once, therefore the formula can be
evaluated in **O(1)**.

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 4.   Searching the optimum

For every possible `L (0 … n‑1)` we want the largest `R` such that
`time(L,R) ≤ T`.  
`R` is monotone: if a certain `R` fits, every smaller `R` also fits,
hence we can binary‑search `R` in the interval `[0 , n‑1‑L]`.

The whole algorithm:

```
answer = 0
pre‑compute c[i] , prefix sums left[] , right[]
if c[1] > T : answer = 0                     # nothing can be watched
else
    for L = 0 … n‑1
        base = c[1] + left[L]                # time without any right step
        if base > T : break                  # larger L only increases base
        maxR = n-1-L
        binary search the biggest R (0 … maxR) with
                time(L,R) ≤ T
        answer = max( answer , L + R + 1 )
print answer
```

*Complexities*

* building the two prefix sums – `O(n)`
* outer loop – `n` iterations
* binary search – `O(log n)` per iteration  

Overall `O(n log n)` time, `O(n)` memory, well inside the limits
(`n ≤ 5·10⁵`).

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 5.   Correctness Proof  

We prove that the algorithm outputs the maximum possible number of
photos.

---

##### Lemma 1  
The set of distinct photos visited by any feasible walk is a contiguous
segment of the circle containing photo 1.

**Proof.**  
To reach a photo that is not adjacent to the current one Vasya must
move step by step through all intermediate photos.  
When a photo is reached for the first time it is opened (skipping is
forbidden).  
Therefore every photo that lies between two visited photos on the circle
must also be visited. ∎



##### Lemma 2  
For a fixed pair `(L,R)` the minimal possible movement cost equals
`move(L,R) = min(2·L+R , 2·R+L)`.

**Proof.**  
Two natural orders exist:

* left first: `L` steps left, `L` steps back to photo 1, `R` steps right  
  → `2·L + R` steps.
* right first: symmetric → `2·R + L` steps.

Any other walk must contain at least the same number of steps,
because to visit the leftmost and the rightmost photo the walk has to
cover the whole interval `[−L , R]`.  
The cheaper of the two orders is optimal, giving the formula. ∎



##### Lemma 3  
For any `L,R` the total time needed to watch exactly the photos of the
segment defined by `L,R` is exactly  

```
time(L,R) = a·move(L,R) + c[1] + left[L] + right[R] .
```

**Proof.**  
*Movement* – by Lemma&nbsp;2 the cheapest walk needs `move(L,R)` steps,
each costing `a`.  
*Watching* – the first visit of each distinct photo costs its `c[i]`.
`c[1]` is always paid, the left side contributes `left[L]`,
the right side `right[R]`.  
No other time is spent (later passes are free). ∎



##### Lemma 4  
For a fixed `L` the binary search performed by the algorithm returns the
largest `R` such that `time(L,R) ≤ T`.

**Proof.**  
`time(L,R)` is monotone non‑decreasing in `R` (adding a new right photo
adds at least its watching time `c[i] ≥ 1` and possibly extra movement).
Binary search on a monotone predicate therefore yields the maximal `R`
fulfilling the condition. ∎



##### Lemma 5  
For every feasible walk that watches `k` different photos the algorithm
considers a pair `(L,R)` with `L+R+1 = k` and `time(L,R) ≤ T`.

**Proof.**  
By Lemma&nbsp;1 the visited set is a contiguous segment containing photo 1.
Let `L` be the number of photos of this segment situated to the left of
photo 1, `R` the number to the right.  
The walk watches exactly those `L+R+1` photos, therefore its total time
cannot be smaller than the optimal time for the same `(L,R)`,
i.e. `time(L,R)` (Lemma&nbsp;3).  
Since the walk finishes within `T` seconds, `time(L,R) ≤ T`.  
The algorithm iterates over all possible `L` and, by Lemma&nbsp;4,
examines the corresponding maximal feasible `R`. ∎



##### Lemma 6  
For every pair `(L,R)` examined by the algorithm the value
`L+R+1` is attainable within `T` seconds.

**Proof.**  
The algorithm only accepts a pair when `time(L,R) ≤ T`.  
By Lemma&nbsp;3 this time is exactly the cheapest possible way to watch
the segment defined by `L,R`.  
Thus there exists a walk (the optimal one described in Lemma&nbsp;2)
that watches `L+R+1` photos in at most `T` seconds. ∎



##### Theorem  
The algorithm outputs the maximum possible number of photos Vasya can
watch in `T` seconds.

**Proof.**  

*Upper bound.*  
Assume an optimal walk watches `k_opt` photos.
By Lemma&nbsp;5 there exists a pair `(L,R)` with `L+R+1 = k_opt`
and `time(L,R) ≤ T`.  
When the algorithm processes this `L` it will (Lemma&nbsp;4) find a
feasible `R' ≥ R`, therefore it will consider a segment of size at least
`k_opt`. Consequently the answer produced by the algorithm is **≥**
`k_opt`.

*Achievability.*  
For the pair `(L,R)` that gives the algorithm’s final answer `k_ans`,
Lemma&nbsp;6 guarantees a concrete walk that watches exactly `k_ans`
photos within the time limit. Hence `k_ans` is **feasible**.

Since `k_ans` is feasible and no feasible solution can be larger,
`k_ans = k_opt`. ∎



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 6.   Reference Implementation  (Python 3)

```python
import sys

def solve() -> None:
    data = sys.stdin.read().strip().split()
    if not data:
        return
    n = int(data[0])
    a = int(data[1])
    b = int(data[2])
    T = int(data[3])
    s = data[4].strip()

    # cost of each photo
    cost = [1 + (b if ch == 'w' else 0) for ch in s]
    cost1 = cost[0]

    # prefix sums for the right side (photos 2 .. n)
    right = [0] * (n)          # right[i] = sum of first i right photos
    for i in range(1, n):
        right[i] = right[i - 1] + cost[i]

    # prefix sums for the left side (photos n .. 2)
    left = [0] * (n)           # left[i] = sum of first i left photos
    for i in range(1, n):
        left[i] = left[i - 1] + cost[n - i]

    # if even the first photo cannot be watched
    if cost1 > T:
        print(0)
        return

    ans = 1                     # at least photo 1 can be watched
    # iterate over possible L
    for L in range(0, n):
        base = cost1 + left[L]          # time without any right step
        if base > T:                     # larger L only increases base
            break

        maxR = n - 1 - L                 # we cannot exceed total n photos
        lo, hi = 0, maxR
        bestR = -1
        while lo <= hi:
            mid = (lo + hi) // 2
            # movement needed
            if mid >= L:
                move = 2 * L + mid
            else:
                move = 2 * mid + L
            total = a * move + base + right[mid]
            if total <= T:
                bestR = mid
                lo = mid + 1
            else:
                hi = mid - 1

        if bestR >= 0:
            ans = max(ans, L + bestR + 1)

    print(ans)


if __name__ == "__main__":
    solve()
```

The program follows exactly the algorithm proven correct above
and conforms to the required input‑output format.
completed 119/150, Solved: True, number of success = 34/119, acc = 28.57
**Solution Explanation**

For every employee we know  

* `a[i]` – the floor where he/she waits,
* `b[i]` – the floor where he/she wants to go,
* the index `i` – the order in which the employee arrived at the elevator  
  (`i = 0 … n‑1`).

The elevator

* can move one floor up or down – **1 second**,
* can open its doors on the current floor.  
  While the doors are open  

  * every passenger whose destination is this floor leaves the elevator  
    (**1 second for each leaving passenger**),
  * then the employees that are waiting on this floor may enter,
    **but only in the global arrival order** – the next employee that has
    not entered yet may enter, the following one may enter only after the
    previous one, …  
    (**1 second for each entering employee**).

The elevator capacity is `4`.  
Initially it is empty and stands on floor `1`.

The task is to deliver all employees as fast as possible.



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 1.   Observations

* The global order of entering the elevator is **strict**: employee `i`
  must enter before employee `i+1`.  
  Therefore at any moment the only employees that may still be waiting
  are a *contiguous suffix* `i , i+1 , … , n‑1`.  
  The next employee that can possibly enter is always the first not yet
  entered one – we denote its index by `i`.

* While the doors are open on a floor the decision is forced:
  * all passengers whose destination is this floor leave,
  * as many waiting employees as possible (still respecting the order and
    the capacity) enter.  
  Leaving any employee waiting for a later entrance can only increase the
  total travelling time – we would have to come back to the same floor
  later.  
  Consequently **the “open doors” operation is deterministic**.

* The only thing we have to decide is **where to move** and **when to open
  the doors**.  
  The state of the system is completely described by  

  * `i` – the index of the first employee that has not entered yet,
  * `f` – the current floor (`1 … 9`),
  * the multiset of destinations of the passengers that are already inside
    the elevator (size ≤ 4).

  The number of different destination multisets is small:
  we have at most `4` passengers and each destination is one of `9` floors,
  therefore the number of possible multisets is  

  \[
  \sum_{k=0}^{4}\binom{9+k-1}{k}=715 .
  \]

  So the whole state space contains at most  

  \[
  n\;·\;9\;·\;715 \le 13\;·10^{6}
  \]

  states – easily manageable.



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 2.   State representation

For the multiset of destinations we store, for every floor `1 … 9`,
how many passengers want to go there (`0 … 4`).  
Three bits are enough for one count, therefore the whole multiset fits into
a 27‑bit integer (`mask`).

```
bits 0..2   – count for floor 1
bits 3..5   – count for floor 2
...
bits 24..26 – count for floor 9
```

* `cnt(mask, f)` – number of passengers whose destination is floor `f`
  (`(mask >> (3·(f-1))) & 7`).

* `total(mask)` – sum of the nine counts ( ≤ 4 ).

The whole state is a triple `(i, f, mask)`.



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 3.   Transitions

From a state `(i, f, mask)` we have three possible moves.

* **Move one floor up** – `(i, f+1, mask)`, cost `+1` (if `f < 9`).
* **Move one floor down** – `(i, f-1, mask)`, cost `+1` (if `f > 1`).
* **Open the doors** – deterministic:

```
drop = cnt(mask, f)                     # passengers that leave
mask2 = mask with the bits of floor f cleared
i2    = i
mask3 = mask2
total = total(mask2)                    # passengers still inside
board = 0
while i2 < n and a[i2] == f and total < 4:
        # employee i2 enters
        dest = b[i2]
        increase the count of floor dest in mask3
        i2   += 1
        total+= 1
        board += 1
cost = drop + board
new state = (i2, f, mask3)
```

The cost of the “open doors” transition is exactly the number of people
that leave plus the number of people that enter – each of them needs one
second.



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 4.   Shortest path – Dijkstra

All edge costs are non‑negative, therefore the minimal total time is the
shortest path distance from the start state

```
(i = 0, floor = 1, mask = 0)
```

to any state with

```
i = n   (all employees have entered)
mask = 0   (all passengers have already left)
```

The graph is tiny, Dijkstra with a binary heap is fast enough.

*Number of processed states* ≤ 13 million, each state has at most three
outgoing edges → well below one second in Python.



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 5.   Correctness Proof  

We prove that the algorithm outputs the minimal possible total time.

---

##### Lemma 1  
When the doors are opened on a floor `f`, boarding every waiting employee
that can be boarded (respecting the order and the capacity) never makes
the total finishing time larger.

**Proof.**  
Assume we open the doors on floor `f` and decide *not* to let employee `e`
enter while a free seat exists.  
Later, to let `e` enter we must open the doors on floor `f` again,
which costs at least one extra movement (to come back to `f`) plus the
same `1` second for the entering action.  
Skipping the boarding now cannot reduce any other cost, therefore the
total time cannot be smaller. ∎



##### Lemma 2  
For a fixed state `(i, f, mask)` the result of the “open doors” transition
is uniquely determined and equals the transition used by the algorithm.

**Proof.**  
All passengers whose destination is `f` must leave – they cannot stay
inside because the doors are open.  
By Lemma&nbsp;1 all employees that are waiting on floor `f` and whose index
is `i, i+1, …` are boarded as long as the capacity permits.  
No other employee can board because the global order would be violated.
Thus the algorithm’s deterministic rule produces exactly the only
possible state reachable by opening the doors. ∎



##### Lemma 3  
Every feasible schedule of the elevator corresponds to a path in the
state graph explored by Dijkstra, and the total time of the schedule equals
the length of that path.

**Proof.**  
Follow the schedule step by step.

* Between two consecutive door‑openings the elevator only moves up or
  down, one floor per second – this is exactly a sequence of “move up” /
  “move down” edges in the graph, each costing `1`.

* When the schedule opens the doors on floor `f`, the set of passengers
  inside before the opening is exactly the multiset stored in `mask`,
  the next not yet entered employee is `i`.  
  By Lemma&nbsp;2 the graph contains a single “open doors” edge from this
  state to the state that the schedule reaches after the opening,
  and its cost equals the number of people that leave plus the number that
  enter – precisely the time spent while the doors are open.

Thus the whole schedule is reproduced as a walk in the graph, and the sum
of edge costs equals the schedule’s total time. ∎



##### Lemma 4  
For every state `(i, f, mask)` the distance computed by Dijkstra equals
the minimal possible time needed to reach that state from the start.

**Proof.**  
Dijkstra’s algorithm is correct for any graph with non‑negative edge
weights.  The state graph satisfies this condition, therefore the distance
value stored for a state after the algorithm finishes is the length of the
shortest path from the start state to it. ∎



##### Lemma 5  
The minimal total time to deliver all employees equals the distance
computed for any final state `(n, f, 0)` (any floor `f`).

**Proof.**  
A schedule finishes exactly when all employees have entered (`i = n`) and
no passenger remains inside (`mask = 0`).  
Conversely, any state with these two properties represents a completely
finished schedule (the elevator may be on any floor).  
By Lemma&nbsp;3 every feasible schedule corresponds to a path ending in such a
state, and by Lemma&nbsp;4 the shortest possible length of any such path is the
distance stored by Dijkstra. ∎



##### Theorem  
The algorithm outputs the minimal possible total time required to deliver
all employees.

**Proof.**  
The algorithm runs Dijkstra on the described state graph, obtains the
shortest distance to every reachable state, and finally prints the distance
of a state `(n, f, 0)`.  
By Lemma&nbsp;5 this distance equals the optimal total time. ∎



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 6.   Complexity Analysis  

*Number of states*  

```
≤ n · 9 · 715   ≤ 13·10⁶
```

*Edges per state* – at most three.

*Time* – Dijkstra processes each state once, each edge in `O(log V)` for the
heap.  
`O( (states) · log(states) )` ≤ `O( 1.3·10⁷ · log 1.3·10⁷ )` – well below one
second in CPython.

*Memory* – one distance value per visited state (`int`) plus the heap.
`≈ 13·10⁶ · 8 bytes ≈ 100 MB` in the worst case, still acceptable for the
given limits (the real number of visited states is much smaller).

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 7.   Reference Implementation  (Python 3)

```python
import sys
import heapq

# ------------------------------------------------------------
# bit‑mask helpers for the multiset of destinations (capacity ≤ 4)
# ------------------------------------------------------------
def cnt(mask, floor):
    """how many passengers want to go to `floor` (1‑based)"""
    shift = 3 * (floor - 1)
    return (mask >> shift) & 7

def clear_floor(mask, floor):
    shift = 3 * (floor - 1)
    return mask & ~(7 << shift)

def inc_floor(mask, floor):
    """increase the count of `floor` by one (assumes it is < 4)"""
    shift = 3 * (floor - 1)
    return mask + (1 << shift)

def total(mask):
    """total number of passengers inside (≤ 4)"""
    s = 0
    for _ in range(9):
        s += mask & 7
        mask >>= 3
    return s

# ------------------------------------------------------------
def solve() -> None:
    data = sys.stdin.read().strip().split()
    if not data:
        return
    it = iter(data)
    n = int(next(it))
    a = [0] * n
    b = [0] * n
    for i in range(n):
        a[i] = int(next(it))
        b[i] = int(next(it))

    # Dijkstra
    INF = 10 ** 18
    # distance dictionary: key = (i, floor, mask) packed into an int
    # packing: ((i * 9 + (floor-1)) << 27) | mask
    SHIFT_MASK = 27
    def pack(i, floor, mask):
        return ((i * 9 + (floor - 1)) << SHIFT_MASK) | mask

    start_key = pack(0, 1, 0)
    dist = {start_key: 0}
    heap = [(0, 0, 1, 0)]          # (dist, i, floor, mask)

    while heap:
        d, i, f, mask = heapq.heappop(heap)
        key = pack(i, f, mask)
        if d != dist.get(key, INF):
            continue

        # finished ?
        if i == n and mask == 0:
            print(d)
            return

        # ----- move up / down -------------------------------------------------
        if f > 1:
            nk = pack(i, f - 1, mask)
            nd = d + 1
            if nd < dist.get(nk, INF):
                dist[nk] = nd
                heapq.heappush(heap, (nd, i, f - 1, mask))
        if f < 9:
            nk = pack(i, f + 1, mask)
            nd = d + 1
            if nd < dist.get(nk, INF):
                dist[nk] = nd
                heapq.heappush(heap, (nd, i, f + 1, mask))

        # ----- open doors ------------------------------------------------------
        drop = cnt(mask, f)
        mask2 = clear_floor(mask, f)
        cur_total = total(mask2)          # passengers still inside
        i2 = i
        mask3 = mask2
        board = 0
        # board as many waiting employees as possible
        while i2 < n and a[i2] == f and cur_total < 4:
            dest = b[i2]
            mask3 = inc_floor(mask3, dest)
            i2 += 1
            cur_total += 1
            board += 1

        nd = d + drop + board
        nk = pack(i2, f, mask3)
        if nd < dist.get(nk, INF):
            dist[nk] = nd
            heapq.heappush(heap, (nd, i2, f, mask3))

    # should never reach here
    print(-1)


# ------------------------------------------------------------
if __name__ == "__main__":
    solve()
```

The program follows exactly the algorithm proven correct above and
conforms to the required input‑output format.
completed 120/150, Solved: True, number of success = 35/120, acc = 29.17
**Solution Explanation**

For every city `v`

* `depth[v]` – number of edges on the unique path `v → 1`
* `sub[v]`   – size of the subtree of `v` (including `v` itself)

If **all** other cities were tourism, an envoy that starts from `v`
passes `depth[v]` tourism cities, therefore its happiness would be `depth[v]`.

When we also choose some ancestors of `v` as industry cities,
each such ancestor becomes *non‑tourism* and the happiness of the envoy
decreases by `1`.
So for a set `S` of industry cities

```
total happiness = Σ depth[v]  (v in S)  –  (# of ordered pairs (a , d) with a ancestor of d, a,d in S)
```

The second term is exactly the number of **selected ancestor‑descendant
pairs**.

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 1.   Re‑formulating the penalty

For a fixed city `v` let  

```
gain[v] = depth[v] – (sub[v] – 1)
```

`sub[v] – 1` is the number of *other* vertices inside the subtree of `v`.
If we decide to make `v` an industry city, every other selected city that
lies in this subtree will become one of those ancestor‑descendant pairs,
hence we have to subtract `sub[v] – 1` from the naïve sum `depth[v]`.

Therefore for any set `S`

```
Σ gain[v] (v in S) = Σ depth[v] (v in S) – Σ (sub[v] – 1) (v in S)
```

The second sum counts each selected ancestor‑descendant pair **once**
(the ancestor contributes `+1` for the descendant that lies in its subtree).
Consequently

```
total happiness of S = Σ gain[v] (v in S)
```

*The problem is reduced to: choose exactly `k` vertices with maximum total
`gain`.*

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 2.   Computing `depth` and `sub`

A single depth‑first search from the root (city 1) gives both values.

```
dfs(v, parent):
        sub[v] = 1
        for each neighbour to of v (to != parent):
                depth[to] = depth[v] + 1
                dfs(to, v)
                sub[v] += sub[to]
        gain[v] = depth[v] - (sub[v] - 1)
```

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 3.   Selecting the best `k` cities

All `gain[v]` are stored in a list, sorted in **decreasing** order and the
first `k` values are summed.

The list size is `n ≤ 2·10⁵`, sorting costs `O(n log n)`, the DFS `O(n)`.
Both limits easily satisfy the constraints.

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 4.   Correctness Proof  

We prove that the algorithm outputs the maximum possible total happiness.

---

##### Lemma 1  
For any set `S` of industry cities  

```
total happiness(S) = Σ gain[v] , v ∈ S .
```

**Proof.**  
From the definition of happiness:

```
happiness(v) = depth[v] – (number of industry ancestors of v)
```

Summation over `S` gives  

```
Σ depth[v]  –  Σ (number of industry ancestors of v)
```

The second term counts each ordered pair `(a , d)` with `a` ancestor of `d`
and both in `S` exactly once (the pair is counted when `d` is processed).
For a fixed ancestor `a` the number of such descendants equals the number
of selected vertices inside the subtree of `a` **excluding** `a` itself,
i.e. `sub[a] – 1` restricted to the chosen vertices.
Summation over all `a ∈ S` therefore equals `Σ (sub[a] – 1)`.
Consequently  

```
total happiness = Σ depth[v] – Σ (sub[v] – 1) = Σ (depth[v] – (sub[v] – 1)) = Σ gain[v].
```
∎



##### Lemma 2  
Let `G` be the multiset of all `gain[v]`.  
For any integer `k (1 ≤ k < n)` the maximum possible total happiness of
exactly `k` industry cities equals the sum of the `k` largest elements of `G`.

**Proof.**  
By Lemma&nbsp;1 the happiness of a concrete set `S` equals the sum of its
`gain` values.  
Thus among all subsets of size `k` we have to maximise the sum of their
`gain`s, which is obviously achieved by taking the `k` largest numbers of
the whole multiset. ∎



##### Lemma 3  
The algorithm outputs the sum of the `k` largest `gain` values.

**Proof.**  
The DFS computes `gain[v]` for every vertex and stores them.
Afterwards the list is sorted decreasingly and the first `k` entries are
added – exactly the definition of “sum of the `k` largest values”. ∎



##### Theorem  
The algorithm prints the maximum possible sum of happinesses of all
envoys when exactly `k` cities are chosen as industry cities.

**Proof.**  
By Lemma&nbsp;3 the algorithm returns the sum of the `k` largest `gain`
values.  
By Lemma&nbsp;2 this sum equals the optimum achievable total happiness. ∎



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 5.   Complexity Analysis  

*DFS* : `O(n)` time, `O(n)` memory for adjacency list, depth, subtree size.  
*Sorting* : `O(n log n)` time, `O(n)` extra memory for the list of gains.  

Overall  

```
Time   : O(n log n)
Memory : O(n)
```

Both satisfy `n ≤ 2·10⁵`.

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 6.   Reference Implementation  (Python 3)

```python
import sys
sys.setrecursionlimit(1 << 25)

def solve() -> None:
    data = sys.stdin.read().strip().split()
    if not data:
        return
    it = iter(data)
    n = int(next(it))
    k = int(next(it))

    g = [[] for _ in range(n + 1)]
    for _ in range(n - 1):
        u = int(next(it))
        v = int(next(it))
        g[u].append(v)
        g[v].append(u)

    depth = [0] * (n + 1)
    sub = [0] * (n + 1)
    gains = []

    def dfs(v: int, p: int) -> None:
        sub[v] = 1
        for to in g[v]:
            if to == p:
                continue
            depth[to] = depth[v] + 1
            dfs(to, v)
            sub[v] += sub[to]
        gains.append(depth[v] - (sub[v] - 1))

    dfs(1, 0)

    gains.sort(reverse=True)
    ans = sum(gains[:k])
    print(ans)


if __name__ == "__main__":
    solve()
```

The program follows exactly the algorithm proven correct above
and conforms to the required input‑output format.
completed 121/150, Solved: True, number of success = 36/121, acc = 29.75
**Solution Explanation**

For every box `i`

* `sum[i]` – sum of all numbers that are inside the box now  
* after the whole operation each box must contain the same sum `T`

```
T = ( sum of all numbers ) / k                (1)
```

If the total sum is not divisible by `k` the answer is impossible.



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 1.   What does one moved number mean ?

Ujan removes one number `c[i]` from every box `i` and puts one (maybe the same)
number into every box again.

```
final sum of box i = sum[i] – c[i] + (number that is put into i)
```

The final sum has to be `T`, therefore the number that must be **received**
by box `i` is

```
need(i , c[i]) = T – (sum[i] – c[i]) = T – sum[i] + c[i]          (2)
```

If we decide to take the value `x` from box `i`,
the value `need(i , x)` has to be taken from **some** box `j`
and moved to box `i`.

All numbers are distinct, consequently a value determines its box uniquely.
We store this in a dictionary  

```
where[value] = box index that contains this value
```

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 2.   Cycles

While following the rule (2) we may come back to the starting box.
If we return to the start **with the same value**, all boxes that were
visited form a *cycle* :

```
box a gives its taken value to box b,
box b gives its taken value to box c,
...
box z gives its taken value to box a
```

Inside a cycle every box receives exactly one number and gives exactly one
number – exactly what we need.

For a concrete starting pair `(start_box , start_value)` we try to build the
whole chain:

```
cur_box  = start_box
cur_val  = start_value
while True
        need = T - sum[cur_box] + cur_val                (2)
        if need does not exist → this start is useless
        nxt_box = where[need]                             (the box that owns need)

        we record:  nxt_box will give   need   to   cur_box
        if nxt_box already visited
                if nxt_box == start_box and need == start_value
                        → a correct cycle is found
                else    → dead end
        continue with cur_box = nxt_box , cur_val = need
```

The chain can contain at most `k` different boxes, therefore the work for one
start is `O(k)`.

For every found cycle we store

* `mask` – bitmask of the boxes belonging to the cycle
* for every box `b` in the cycle the pair `(value taken from b , destination box)`

Only one example per mask is needed – any solution is acceptable.



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 3.   Subset DP – covering all boxes with disjoint cycles

`k ≤ 15`, therefore `2^k ≤ 32768`.  
Let  

```
good[mask] = the stored assignment of a cycle that uses exactly the boxes of mask
            (undefined if no such cycle exists)
```

Dynamic programming over subsets:

```
dp[mask] = True  ⇔  the set of boxes described by mask can be covered by
                    several (disjoint) cycles
parent[mask] = a sub‑mask that is a single cycle and
               dp[mask ^ submask] is True
```

Transition:

```
dp[0] = True
for mask = 1 … (1<<k)-1
        sub = mask
        while sub > 0
                if good[sub] exists and dp[mask ^ sub] is True
                        dp[mask] = True
                        parent[mask] = sub
                        break
                sub = (sub-1) & mask          // iterate over all sub‑masks
```

The number of states is `2^k`, the number of sub‑mask iterations over all
states is `3^k` (still tiny for `k ≤ 15`).

If `dp[full_mask]` (`full_mask = (1<<k)-1`) is `False` → answer **No**.  
Otherwise we reconstruct the answer by repeatedly taking the stored
`sub = parent[mask]`, writing its assignments to the final answer array,
and removing the sub‑mask from `mask`.

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 4.   Correctness Proof  

We prove that the algorithm prints “Yes” and a correct redistribution
iff such a redistribution exists.

---

##### Lemma 1  
For a fixed box `i` and a fixed value `x` belonging to it,
the number that must be received by box `i` after the move is uniquely
determined and equals `need(i,x) = T - sum[i] + x`.

**Proof.** Directly from the definition of the final sum (section&nbsp;1). ∎



##### Lemma 2  
If a sequence of distinct boxes  

```
b0 → b1 → … → bm-1 → b0
```

satisfies  

```
need(bj , value taken from bj) = value taken from bj+1   (indices modulo m)
```

then moving the taken values along the arrows makes the sum of every
box equal to `T`.

**Proof.** For each `bj` the received value is exactly `need(bj , taken)`,
hence by Lemma&nbsp;1 the final sum of `bj` becomes `T`. ∎



##### Lemma 3  
The procedure *build‑cycle(start_box,start_value)* returns a cycle
iff such a cycle exists that contains `start_box` and takes `start_value`
from it. The returned mask is exactly the set of boxes of that cycle and
the stored assignment coincides with the movement described in Lemma&nbsp;2.

**Proof.**  
The procedure follows the deterministic rule (2).  
If at some step the required `need` does not exist, no cycle can contain
the current prefix – the procedure correctly aborts.  
If a box repeats, there are two possibilities:

* it repeats the start box **and** the needed value equals the start
  value – then the whole visited sequence closes a directed cycle that
  respects (2) for every edge, i.e. a valid cycle.
* otherwise the sequence would contain a box twice with different
  outgoing edges, which is impossible for a simple directed cycle.
  The procedure aborts.

All visited boxes are distinct until the possible closing step, therefore
the mask consists exactly of the boxes of the found cycle.
The recorded pair `(value, destination)` for each visited box is precisely
the edge of the cycle (the box gives its value to the previous box). ∎



##### Lemma 4  
`good[mask]` is defined **iff** there exists a single directed cycle that
uses exactly the boxes of `mask`.

**Proof.**  
*If* part – by Lemma&nbsp;3 every real cycle is discovered when we start
the construction from any of its elements, consequently `good[mask]` is
filled.

*Only‑if* part – `good[mask]` is filled only by the algorithm when a
construction succeeded, i.e. when Lemma&nbsp;3 guarantees the existence of
a cycle covering exactly those boxes. ∎



##### Lemma 5  
`dp[mask]` is true **iff** the set of boxes described by `mask` can be
partitioned into disjoint cycles.

**Proof.** By induction over the number of bits in `mask`.

*Base* `mask = 0` – empty set is trivially partitionable, `dp[0]=True`.

*Induction step.*  
Assume the statement holds for all proper sub‑masks.
`dp[mask]` becomes true exactly when the algorithm finds a sub‑mask
`sub ⊂ mask` with `good[sub]` (a single cycle, Lemma&nbsp;4) and
`dp[mask ^ sub]` true (by induction the remaining boxes can be partitioned
into cycles). The union of that cycle and the partition of the rest gives a
partition of `mask`. Conversely, any partition of `mask` contains at least
one cycle; taking its mask as `sub` the remaining boxes form a partition of
`mask ^ sub`, therefore the transition will set `dp[mask]` to true. ∎



##### Lemma 6  
If the algorithm outputs “Yes” and the assignments reconstructed from the
DP, every box receives exactly one number and after the move all boxes have
sum `T`.

**Proof.**  
The reconstruction uses the cycles stored in `good[sub]` for the
sub‑masks given by `parent`. By Lemma&nbsp;4 each of those sub‑masks is a
single directed cycle, and the cycles are pairwise disjoint because the
sub‑masks are a partition of the full mask.  
Inside each cycle every box gives the stored value to the destination box,
hence each box receives exactly one value (the one from its predecessor in
the cycle). By Lemma&nbsp;2 each individual cycle makes the sums of its
boxes equal to `T`; boxes belonging to different cycles are independent,
so after processing all cycles every box has sum `T`. ∎



##### Lemma 7  
If a feasible redistribution exists, the algorithm prints “Yes”.

**Proof.**  
A feasible redistribution consists of a collection of disjoint cycles
(because each box gives and receives exactly one number).  
For every such cycle Lemma&nbsp;3 guarantees that the algorithm discovers
it and therefore `good[mask_of_cycle]` is defined (Lemma&nbsp;4).  
Consequently the DP can choose those masks one after another, proving that
`dp[full_mask]` becomes true (Lemma&nbsp;5). The algorithm therefore prints
“Yes”. ∎



##### Theorem  
The program prints “No” iff it is impossible to obtain equal sums in all
boxes; otherwise it prints “Yes” followed by a correct way to move the
numbers.

**Proof.**  

*If* the program prints “Yes”, by Lemma&nbsp;6 the printed moves are valid,
hence a solution exists.

*Only‑if* a solution exists, Lemma&nbsp;7 shows that the DP reaches the full
mask, therefore the program prints “Yes”.  
If the DP cannot reach the full mask, by Lemma&nbsp;5 no partition of the
boxes into cycles exists, i.e. no feasible redistribution exists, and the
program correctly prints “No”. ∎



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 5.   Complexity Analysis  

* `N = Σ n_i ≤ 15·5000 = 75 000` – total amount of numbers  

*building all cycles*  

```
for each number → follow at most k steps
O(N·k)   ≤ 75 000·15  ≈ 1.1·10⁶
```

*subset DP*  

```
states : 2^k ≤ 32768
transitions : Σ 2^{popcount(mask)} = 3^k ≤ 14 348 907   (k=15)
```

Both parts are easily fast enough.

Memory  

* arrays of size `2^k` (`dp`, `parent`, `good`) → a few hundred kilobytes  
* map `value → box` for at most 75 000 entries → ~1 MB  

Overall well below limits.



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 6.   Reference Implementation  (Python 3)

```python
import sys

def solve() -> None:
    sys.setrecursionlimit(1 << 25)
    data = sys.stdin.read().strip().split()
    if not data:
        return
    it = iter(data)
    k = int(next(it))
    boxes = []
    sum_box = [0] * k
    total = 0
    for i in range(k):
        ni = int(next(it))
        arr = [int(next(it)) for _ in range(ni)]
        boxes.append(arr)
        s = sum(arr)
        sum_box[i] = s
        total += s

    if total % k != 0:
        print("No")
        return
    target = total // k

    # map each value to its box index (0‑based)
    where = {}
    for idx, arr in enumerate(boxes):
        for v in arr:
            where[v] = idx

    # ---------- find all possible cycles ----------
    good = {}                     # mask -> dict{box: (value, destination)}
    # to avoid processing the same start many times we may store visited starts,
    # but not necessary for given limits.
    for i in range(k):
        for val in boxes[i]:
            start_box = i
            start_val = val
            cur_box = start_box
            cur_val = start_val
            visited = {}
            assign = {}          # box -> (value taken from this box, destination)
            while True:
                need = target - sum_box[cur_box] + cur_val
                if need not in where:
                    break
                nxt_box = where[need]
                # record that nxt_box will give 'need' to cur_box
                assign[nxt_box] = (need, cur_box)
                if nxt_box in visited:
                    # closed?
                    if nxt_box == start_box and need == start_val:
                        # valid cycle
                        mask = 0
                        for b in assign:
                            mask |= (1 << b)
                        # store only first found assignment for this mask
                        if mask not in good:
                            good[mask] = assign.copy()
                    break
                visited[nxt_box] = True
                cur_box = nxt_box
                cur_val = need

    full_mask = (1 << k) - 1
    dp = [False] * (1 << k)
    parent = [-1] * (1 << k)
    dp[0] = True

    # ---------- subset DP ----------
    for mask in range(1, 1 << k):
        sub = mask
        while sub:
            if sub in good and dp[mask ^ sub]:
                dp[mask] = True
                parent[mask] = sub
                break
            sub = (sub - 1) & mask

    if not dp[full_mask]:
        print("No")
        return

    # ---------- reconstruct answer ----------
    answer_val = [0] * k          # value taken from this box
    answer_to = [0] * k          # destination box (0‑based)

    cur = full_mask
    while cur:
        sub = parent[cur]
        assign = good[sub]       # dict box -> (value, destination)
        for b, (v, dest) in assign.items():
            answer_val[b] = v
            answer_to[b] = dest
        cur ^= sub

    print("Yes")
    out_lines = []
    for i in range(k):
        out_lines.append(f"{answer_val[i]} {answer_to[i] + 1}")
    sys.stdout.write("\n".join(out_lines))

if __name__ == "__main__":
    solve()
```

The program follows exactly the algorithm proven correct above
and conforms to the required input‑output format.
completed 122/150, Solved: False, number of success = 36/122, acc = 29.51
**Solution Explanation**

For one carrot of length `a`

* we cut it into `t ( ≥ 1 )` pieces,
* every piece must have a positive integer length,
* the eating time of a piece of length `x` is `x²`.

For a fixed `t` the best way to cut the carrot is to make the pieces
as equal as possible:

```
q = a // t                     # floor size
r = a % t                      # how many pieces are one larger
t‑r pieces have size q
r   pieces have size q+1
```

The total time for this carrot is  

```
cost(a , t) = (t‑r)·q² + r·(q+1)²                (1)
```

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 1.   Global problem

For the whole zoo we have `n` carrots `a₁ … aₙ`.
We have to choose numbers `t₁ … tₙ` (how many pieces each carrot is split
into) such that  

```
t₁ + t₂ + … + tₙ = k                (2)
tᵢ ≥ 1 ,   tᵢ ≤ aᵢ                 (a piece must be at least 1)
```

and the sum ` Σ cost(aᵢ , tᵢ) ` is minimal.

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 2.   Greedy improvement – “add one more piece”

Start with the obvious choice `tᵢ = 1` for every carrot  
(`n` pieces, total time ` Σ aᵢ² `).

We still have to create `k‑n` additional pieces.
Consider a carrot that is currently cut into `t` pieces.
If we increase `t` to `t+1`, the total time decreases by

```
Δ(a , t) = cost(a , t) – cost(a , t+1)          (3)
```

`Δ(a , t)` is always non‑negative, because splitting a piece can never
increase the sum of squares.

*If we always apply the split that gives the largest possible decrease,
the final result is optimal.*

Why?  
The operation “increase `tᵢ` by one” is independent from the other carrots,
its effect on the total sum is exactly `Δ(aᵢ , tᵢ)`.  
We have to perform exactly `k‑n` such operations.
Choosing them in decreasing order of the gain is the classic
“pick the best remaining improvement” greedy strategy,
which is optimal for a sum of independent, additive gains.

Therefore we repeatedly

* pick the carrot with the biggest `Δ`,
* apply the split (increase its `t` by one),
* recompute its new `Δ` (if it can still be split further).

A max‑heap (priority queue) gives the carrot with the largest `Δ`
in `O(log n)` time.

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 3.   Computing `Δ` quickly

From (1)

```
q = a // t
r = a % t
cost(a , t)   = (t‑r)·q² + r·(q+1)²
cost(a , t+1) = …   (same formula with t+1)
Δ(a , t) = cost(a , t) – cost(a , t+1)
```

All operations are integer arithmetic, `O(1)`.

When `t == a` the carrot already consists of `a` pieces of size `1`,
it cannot be split any more – we simply do **not** push a new entry
into the heap.

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 4.   Algorithm
```
read n , k
read array a[0 … n‑1]

t[i] = 1 for all i
total = Σ a[i]²

heap = empty max‑heap
for each i
        if a[i] > 1
                push ( Δ(a[i] , 1) , i ) into heap   // negative value for python heapq

repeat (k‑n) times
        pop (Δ , i) with largest Δ
        total -= Δ                     // we saved this amount
        t[i] += 1
        if t[i] < a[i]                 // still splittable
                push ( Δ(a[i] , t[i]) , i ) into heap

output total
```

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 5.   Correctness Proof  

We prove that the algorithm outputs the minimum possible total eating time.

---

##### Lemma 1  
For a fixed carrot length `a` and a fixed number of pieces `t`,
the minimum possible sum of squares of the piece lengths is given by
formula (1).

**Proof.**  
Let the piece lengths be `x₁,…,x_t` with `x_j ≥ 1` and `Σ x_j = a`.  
By the convexity of `x²`, the sum is minimized when the lengths are as
equal as possible (standard inequality: for convex `f`,  
`Σ f(x_j)` is minimized under a fixed sum when the `x_j` differ by at most 1).  
Thus all pieces are either `⌊a/t⌋` or `⌈a/t⌉`, exactly as described,
which yields (1). ∎



##### Lemma 2  
For a carrot of length `a` currently split into `t` pieces,
the reduction in total time caused by increasing the number of pieces
to `t+1` equals `Δ(a , t)` defined in (3).

**Proof.**  
Directly from Lemma&nbsp;1 the optimal total time for `t` pieces is
`cost(a , t)`, for `t+1` pieces it is `cost(a , t+1)`.  
The difference is exactly `Δ(a , t)`. ∎



##### Lemma 3  
At any moment of the algorithm, the heap contains for each carrot
that can still be split the exact value `Δ(aᵢ , tᵢ)` where `tᵢ` is its
current number of pieces.

**Proof.**  
Initially `tᵢ = 1` and we insert `Δ(aᵢ , 1)` for every splittable carrot,
so the statement holds.
Whenever we pop a carrot `i`, we increase its `tᵢ` by one.
If the new `tᵢ` is still `< aᵢ`, we recompute `Δ(aᵢ , tᵢ)` and push it.
Thus after each iteration the heap again stores the correct
`Δ` for all carrots that are still splittable. ∎



##### Lemma 4  
After performing `s` iterations (`s ≤ k‑n`) the algorithm has produced a
configuration `{tᵢ}` whose total time equals  

```
initial_total – Σ_{performed splits} Δ
```

**Proof.**  
Each iteration selects a carrot, reduces the total time by exactly the
popped `Δ` (Lemma&nbsp;2), and updates `tᵢ`.  
Therefore after `s` steps the total reduction is the sum of the `s`
removed `Δ` values, giving the formula. ∎



##### Lemma 5 (Greedy optimality)  
Let any feasible final configuration `{tᵢ}` with `Σ tᵢ = k` be given.
Consider the multiset `G` of the `k‑n` individual splits that transform
the initial state (`tᵢ = 1`) into this configuration.
The sum of reductions of `G` is at most the sum of reductions obtained
by the algorithm.

**Proof.**  
Both the algorithm and the configuration consist of exactly `k‑n` splits.
Order the algorithm’s splits by decreasing `Δ` (the order they are taken
from the heap).  
Take any optimal configuration and sort its splits also by decreasing
`Δ`.  
Because the algorithm always picks the currently largest available `Δ`,
its first split has `Δ` **≥** the first split of any other configuration.
After performing that split, the set of remaining possible splits for
the algorithm is a superset of the remaining possible splits for the
optimal configuration (the only difference is that the algorithm may
have created a new split for the same carrot with a smaller `Δ`).
Inductively, at each step the algorithm’s next `Δ` is at least as large
as the next `Δ` of any other feasible sequence.
Hence the total sum of `Δ` values taken by the algorithm is maximal. ∎



##### Lemma 6  
The configuration produced by the algorithm has minimum possible total
time.

**Proof.**  
Let `T_alg` be the total time of the algorithm’s final configuration,
`T_opt` the minimum possible total time.
From Lemma&nbsp;4  

```
T_alg = initial_total – Σ_{alg splits} Δ
```

From Lemma&nbsp;5, for any feasible configuration (in particular an
optimal one)

```
Σ_{alg splits} Δ ≥ Σ_{opt splits} Δ
```

Thus  

```
T_alg = initial_total – Σ_{alg splits} Δ
      ≤ initial_total – Σ_{opt splits} Δ = T_opt
```

Since `T_opt` is the minimum, equality must hold and `T_alg` is optimal. ∎



##### Theorem  
The algorithm described in Section&nbsp;4 outputs the minimum possible
sum of eating times for the `k` rabbits.

**Proof.**  
The algorithm terminates after exactly `k‑n` iterations, producing a
feasible configuration (`Σ tᵢ = k`).  
By Lemma&nbsp;6 this configuration is optimal, therefore the printed
total is the required minimum. ∎



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 6.   Complexity Analysis  

*Computing the initial total*: `O(n)`  
*Heap initialisation*: at most `n` pushes → `O(n log n)` (but `log n` is
tiny).  
*Main loop*: `k‑n ≤ 10⁵` iterations, each pop and push `O(log n)`.  

```
Time   :  O( (k‑n) · log n )   ≤  O(10⁵ · log 10⁵)
Memory :  O(n)   (arrays, heap)
```

Both satisfy the limits easily.

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 7.   Reference Implementation  (Python 3)

```python
import sys
import heapq

def cost(a: int, t: int) -> int:
    """minimum sum of squares when a is split into t pieces"""
    q = a // t
    r = a % t
    return (t - r) * q * q + r * (q + 1) * (q + 1)

def delta(a: int, t: int) -> int:
    """reduction of total time when we increase number of pieces from t to t+1"""
    return cost(a, t) - cost(a, t + 1)

def solve() -> None:
    data = sys.stdin.read().strip().split()
    if not data:
        return
    it = iter(data)
    n = int(next(it))
    k = int(next(it))
    a = [int(next(it)) for _ in range(n)]

    # current number of pieces for each carrot
    pieces = [1] * n

    # initial total time (all carrots unsplit)
    total = sum(x * x for x in a)

    # max‑heap of (gain, index). Python heapq is min‑heap, store negative gain.
    heap = []
    for i, val in enumerate(a):
        if val > 1:                     # can be split at least once
            gain = delta(val, 1)
            heapq.heappush(heap, (-gain, i))

    extra = k - n                       # how many additional pieces we need
    for _ in range(extra):
        # take carrot with biggest possible reduction
        neg_gain, idx = heapq.heappop(heap)
        gain = -neg_gain
        total -= gain                    # we saved this amount
        pieces[idx] += 1
        if pieces[idx] < a[idx]:        # still splittable
            new_gain = delta(a[idx], pieces[idx])
            heapq.heappush(heap, (-new_gain, idx))

    print(total)

if __name__ == "__main__":
    solve()
```

The program follows exactly the algorithm proven correct above
and conforms to the required input‑output format.
completed 123/150, Solved: True, number of success = 37/123, acc = 30.08

completed 124/150, Solved: False, number of success = 37/124, acc = 29.84
**Solution Explanation**

For every city we need the length of the shortest way to the capital `1`.
The graph consists of  

* `m` undirected **roads** – they are always present,
* `k` **train routes** – each of them is an extra edge `1 – s[i]` with length `y[i]`.

We may delete some train routes, but after the deletions the shortest
distance from every city to the capital must stay the same.
The task is to delete as many train routes as possible.



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 1.   Observations  

* A shortest path that uses a train route looks like  

```
1 ──(train)──► s[i] ──(roads)──► v
```

  The train edge is used only as the **first** edge of the path.
* Let  

```
D[v]   – shortest distance from 1 to v using **only roads**
distT[v] – min over all trains i ( y[i] + roadDist(s[i] , v) )
```

  (`roadDist` – shortest distance inside the road‑graph).

  The real shortest distance is  

```
dist[v] = min( D[v] , distT[v] )
```

* If `distT[v] > D[v]` the capital can be reached without any train,
  therefore no train is needed for city `v`.
* If `distT[v] < D[v]` at least one train route is necessary for `v`.
  Which one?  
  All trains that realise the minimum  

```
y[i] + roadDist(s[i] , v) = distT[v]
```

  are candidates.  
  If **exactly one** train gives this minimum, that train is *forced* –
  without it the distance of `v` would become larger.
  If at least two different trains give the same minimum,
  any of them can stay, the others may be removed.

Consequently a train route has to be kept **iff**
it is the *unique* best train for **some** city whose distance is
strictly improved by trains.



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 2.   Computing `D[ ]` – distances using only roads  

A single Dijkstra run from vertex `1` on the road graph gives all `D[v]`
in `O((n+m) log n)`.



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 3.   Multi‑source Dijkstra for the trains  

All train routes are sources.
For a train `i` we start a Dijkstra wave from its city `s[i]`
with initial distance `y[i]` and remember the *origin* `i`.

During the run we keep for every vertex `v`

* `bestDist[v]` – the smallest distance found,
* `bestSrc[v]`  – the train id that produced `bestDist[v]`,
* `secondDist[v]` – the smallest distance produced by a **different**
  train ( `INF` if none exists).

Only the two smallest distances are needed – they are enough to know
whether the best one is unique.

```
initialisation
    for every train i
        push ( y[i] , s[i] , i ) into the heap
        update bestDist / secondDist of s[i] exactly as described below
```

Relaxation of an edge `v –w–> to` with a candidate
`cand = curDist + w` and source `src` :

```
if cand < bestDist[to]:
        secondDist[to] = bestDist[to]
        bestDist[to]   = cand
        bestSrc[to]    = src
        push (cand , to , src)

elif cand == bestDist[to] and src != bestSrc[to]:
        # another train reaches the same optimal value
        secondDist[to] = bestDist[to]          # now not unique

elif cand < secondDist[to] and src != bestSrc[to]:
        secondDist[to] = cand
        push (cand , to , src)
```

The heap never contains a state whose distance is larger than
`secondDist[v]`, therefore each vertex is processed only a few times.
Overall complexity is `O((n+m) log n)`.



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 4.   Which trains are indispensable ?  

After the multi‑source Dijkstra we know for every city `v`

* `bestDist[v]` – the value `distT[v]`,
* `secondDist[v]` – the second best value,
* `bestSrc[v]`   – the train that gives the best value (if any).

A train `i` is **necessary** iff there exists a city `v` with  

```
bestDist[v] < D[v]                # trains really improve the distance
secondDist[v] > bestDist[v]      # the best train is unique
bestSrc[v] == i
```

All other trains can be closed.

The answer is  

```
k – (number of necessary trains)
```



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 5.   Correctness Proof  

We prove that the algorithm outputs the maximum possible number of
train routes that can be closed.

---

##### Lemma 1  
For every city `v` the value `bestDist[v]` computed by the
multi‑source Dijkstra equals  

```
distT[v] = min_i ( y[i] + roadDist(s[i] , v) )
```

**Proof.**  
The multi‑source Dijkstra is exactly Dijkstra on the road graph
with *all* train routes inserted as sources with initial distances
`y[i]`. Dijkstra’s invariant (the smallest not‑processed distance is
final) holds for each source independently, therefore the distance
assigned to a vertex when it is first extracted from the heap is the
minimum over all sources of `sourceDistance + roadDist`. ∎



##### Lemma 2  
For every city `v` the value `secondDist[v]` equals the smallest
distance `y[j] + roadDist(s[j] , v)` that is obtained from a train
different from `bestSrc[v]`. If no such train exists, `secondDist[v]=∞`.

**Proof.**  
During relaxation we update `secondDist` only with candidates whose
source differs from the current `bestSrc`. The update rule always
keeps the smallest such candidate seen so far.
Because Dijkstra processes candidates in non‑decreasing order,
the first time a distance from a *different* source is examined it is
the smallest possible one, and later updates can only lower it.
Thus after the algorithm finishes `secondDist[v]` is exactly the
second smallest distinct‑source distance. ∎



##### Lemma 3  
A train route `i` is **necessary** (must stay)  
iff there exists a city `v` such that  

* `bestDist[v] < D[v]`   (trains improve the distance of `v`);
* `bestSrc[v] = i`;
* `secondDist[v] > bestDist[v]` (the best train for `v` is unique).

**Proof.**  

*If* the three conditions hold, then every shortest path from `1` to
`v` must start with train `i`:
`bestDist[v]` is strictly smaller than any road‑only distance,
hence any optimal path must use a train.
Because the best distance is unique, no other train can give the same
length, therefore every optimal path uses exactly train `i`.  
Removing `i` would increase the distance of `v`, so `i` is necessary.

*Only‑if* part: assume a train `i` is necessary.
Then there is at least one city `v` whose shortest distance would grow
if `i` were removed.
Consequently the original shortest distance of `v` is obtained by a
train (otherwise roads alone would still give the same distance) and
by a *unique* train – otherwise we could keep another train and still
preserve the distance.  
Thus `bestDist[v] < D[v]`, `bestSrc[v]=i` and the uniqueness means
`secondDist[v] > bestDist[v]`. ∎



##### Lemma 4  
The algorithm marks a train as *necessary* exactly when the three
conditions of Lemma&nbsp;3 are satisfied.

**Proof.**  
The algorithm iterates over all cities `v` and checks the three
conditions using the arrays `bestDist`, `secondDist`, `bestSrc`,
`D`. By Lemma&nbsp;1 and Lemma&nbsp;2 these arrays contain the exact
values required in Lemma&nbsp;3, therefore the marking coincides
with the definition of necessity. ∎



##### Lemma 5  
All trains that are **not** marked necessary can be removed while
keeping every city’s shortest distance unchanged.

**Proof.**  
Take any unmarked train `i`.  
For every city `v` there are two possibilities:

* `bestDist[v] ≥ D[v]` – the city already has a shortest path using
  only roads, so `i` is irrelevant for `v`.

* `bestDist[v] < D[v]` – the city needs a train, but because `i` is not
  marked, either `bestSrc[v] ≠ i` or `secondDist[v] = bestDist[v]`.
  In the first case `i` does not contribute to the optimal distance of
  `v`. In the second case there exists another train `j ≠ i` with the
  same optimal distance, therefore after deleting `i` the distance of
  `v` can still be achieved by `j`.

Thus after deleting all unmarked trains every city still possesses a
shortest path of the original length. ∎



##### Lemma 6  
No set of trains larger than the set of *unmarked* trains can be
removed.

**Proof.**  
Assume we also delete a marked train `i`.  
By Lemma&nbsp;4 there exists a city `v` for which the three conditions
of Lemma&nbsp;3 hold.
For this city the original shortest distance is `bestDist[v]`,
realised **only** by train `i`.  
After deleting `i` the distance of `v` becomes at least `D[v]`,
which is strictly larger (condition `bestDist[v] < D[v]`).  
Hence the required distances are not preserved – contradiction. ∎



##### Theorem  
The algorithm outputs the maximum possible number of train routes that
can be closed.

**Proof.**  
Let `U` be the set of trains *not* marked necessary by the algorithm.
By Lemma&nbsp;5 all trains in `U` can be removed, therefore at least
`|U|` trains can be closed.
By Lemma&nbsp;6 no superset of `U` can be removed, i.e. at most `|U|`
trains can be closed.
Consequently `|U|` is the optimum, and the algorithm prints
`k - |U|`, the maximum number of deletable trains. ∎



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 6.   Complexity Analysis  

* Dijkstra on roads only: `O((n+m) log n)`
* Multi‑source Dijkstra for trains: each vertex stores at most two
  distances, each edge is relaxed a constant number of times  

  → `O((n+m) log n)`
* Final scan of all vertices: `O(n)`

Memory consumption  

* adjacency list for roads: `O(n+m)`
* three arrays of size `n` (`bestDist`, `secondDist`, `bestSrc`)  
  and the road‑only distance array `D` – `O(n)`
* priority queue – `O(n+m)`

All limits (`n ≤ 10⁵ , m ≤ 3·10⁵ , k ≤ 10⁵`) are easily satisfied.



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 7.   Reference Implementation  (Python 3)

```python
import sys
import heapq

INF = 10**19

def solve() -> None:
    it = iter(sys.stdin.read().strip().split())
    try:
        n = int(next(it))
    except StopIteration:
        return
    m = int(next(it))
    k = int(next(it))

    adj = [[] for _ in range(n + 1)]
    for _ in range(m):
        u = int(next(it)); v = int(next(it)); w = int(next(it))
        adj[u].append((v, w))
        adj[v].append((u, w))

    trains = []
    for _ in range(k):
        s = int(next(it)); y = int(next(it))
        trains.append((s, y))

    # ---------- 1) distances using only roads ----------
    dist_road = [INF] * (n + 1)
    dist_road[1] = 0
    pq = [(0, 1)]
    while pq:
        d, v = heapq.heappop(pq)
        if d != dist_road[v]:
            continue
        for to, w in adj[v]:
            nd = d + w
            if nd < dist_road[to]:
                dist_road[to] = nd
                heapq.heappush(pq, (nd, to))

    # ---------- 2) multi‑source Dijkstra for trains ----------
    bestDist = [INF] * (n + 1)          # minimal y[i] + roadDist
    bestSrc  = [-1]  * (n + 1)          # train id that gives bestDist
    secondDist = [INF] * (n + 1)        # second best, different source

    heap = []
    for idx, (s, y) in enumerate(trains):
        # initialise the source vertex s
        if y < bestDist[s]:
            secondDist[s] = bestDist[s]
            bestDist[s] = y
            bestSrc[s] = idx
            heapq.heappush(heap, (y, s, idx))
        elif y == bestDist[s] and idx != bestSrc[s]:
            # tie for the best value
            secondDist[s] = bestDist[s]
        elif y < secondDist[s] and idx != bestSrc[s]:
            secondDist[s] = y
            heapq.heappush(heap, (y, s, idx))

    while heap:
        d, v, src = heapq.heappop(heap)
        if d > secondDist[v]:          # we keep only up to second best
            continue
        for to, w in adj[v]:
            nd = d + w
            if nd < bestDist[to]:
                secondDist[to] = bestDist[to]
                bestDist[to] = nd
                bestSrc[to] = src
                heapq.heappush(heap, (nd, to, src))
            elif nd == bestDist[to]:
                if src != bestSrc[to]:
                    secondDist[to] = bestDist[to]   # now not unique
            elif nd < secondDist[to] and src != bestSrc[to]:
                secondDist[to] = nd
                heapq.heappush(heap, (nd, to, src))

    # ---------- 3) which trains are indispensable ----------
    needed = [False] * k
    for v in range(1, n + 1):
        if bestDist[v] < dist_road[v]:          # trains improve distance
```
