            if secondDist[v] > bestDist[v]:     # unique best train
                needed[bestSrc[v]] = True

    answer = k - sum(needed)
    print(answer)


if __name__ == "__main__":
    solve()
```

The program follows exactly the algorithm proven correct above
and conforms to the required input‑output format.
completed 125/150, Solved: False, number of success = 37/125, acc = 29.6
**Solution Explanation**

For the numbers `1 … n` we have to build as many pairs as possible.
A pair `{a , b}` is allowed only if  

```
gcd(a , b) > 1                (they have a common divisor larger than 1)
```

Every number may belong to at most one pair.



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 1.  Observations  

*If two numbers share a divisor `p` (prime or not) they are both multiples of `p`.  
So a pair can be built only inside the set  

```
M(p) = { multiples of p not used in another pair }
```

*The smallest prime `2` has the most multiples – all even numbers.
If we first pair the even numbers among themselves we already use the
largest possible amount of numbers, because any even number can be paired
only with another even number (its gcd would contain the factor `2`).

*After all possible even‑even pairs are taken, at most **one** even number
remains unused (the size of the even set can be odd).  
That remaining even number can still be paired with an odd multiple of a
different prime, e.g. `6` (even) with `3` (odd) – their gcd is `3`.

*Therefore the following greedy order is optimal  

```
process the primes in increasing order
    for the current prime p
        take all still unused multiples of p
        pair them arbitrarily (first with second, third with fourth, …)
```

The first prime `2` consumes almost all even numbers,
later primes only see the numbers that are still free, i.e. the odd
numbers and possibly one leftover even number.
No better solution exists – any optimal solution must contain the same
or a smaller number of pairs inside each `M(p)`, and the greedy choice
already uses the maximum possible inside every `M(p)`.



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 2.  Algorithm
```
input n
sieve all primes ≤ n                     // O(n log log n)

used[1 … n] = false
answer = empty list

for each prime p (in increasing order)
        cur = empty list
        for x = p ; x ≤ n ; x += p
                if not used[x]          // still free
                        cur.append(x)

        // pair consecutively
        for i = 0 ; i+1 < len(cur) ; i += 2
                a = cur[i] , b = cur[i+1]
                answer.append( (a,b) )
                used[a] = used[b] = true

output |answer|
output all pairs
```

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 3.  Correctness Proof  

We prove that the algorithm outputs a maximum possible number of groups.

---

##### Lemma 1  
After processing prime `p` the algorithm pairs **all** possible pairs
inside the set `M(p)` (the set of still unused multiples of `p`).

**Proof.**  
`cur` contains exactly the elements of `M(p)`.  
The algorithm pairs them consecutively, i.e. it creates `⌊|M(p)|/2⌋`
disjoint pairs. No more than that many disjoint pairs can exist inside a
set of size `|M(p)|`. ∎



##### Lemma 2  
When the algorithm finishes processing prime `p`,
no optimal solution can contain a pair that uses a number from `M(p)` and
a number that is **not** a multiple of `p`.

**Proof.**  
All numbers of `M(p)` are multiples of `p`.  
If a number `a ∈ M(p)` were paired with a number `b` that is **not**
a multiple of `p`, then `gcd(a,b)` would not contain the factor `p`,
hence `gcd(a,b) ≤ 1` – the pair would be illegal. ∎



##### Lemma 3  
Let `U_p` be the set of numbers still unused after prime `p` has been
processed by the algorithm.
Every optimal solution can be transformed (without decreasing its size)
into a solution that uses exactly the same pairs as the algorithm on the
primes `2,3,…,p`.

**Proof.**  
Induction over the primes.

*Base (`p = 2`).*  
By Lemma&nbsp;1 the algorithm creates the maximum possible number of
pairs among the even numbers. Any optimal solution cannot contain more
even‑even pairs, therefore we can replace its even‑even pairs by the
algorithm’s pairs without losing any pair.

*Induction step.*  
Assume the statement true for the previous prime `p'`.  
Consider prime `p`.  
All numbers that the algorithm pairs now belong to `M(p) ⊆ U_{p'}`.
By Lemma&nbsp;2 any pair of an optimal solution that uses a number from
`M(p)` must use another number from `M(p)`.  
Thus inside `M(p)` the optimal solution can contain at most
`⌊|M(p)|/2⌋` pairs, exactly the number created by the algorithm
(Lemma&nbsp;1). Replacing its pairs inside `M(p)` by the algorithm’s pairs
keeps the total number of pairs unchanged. ∎



##### Lemma 4  
After the last prime (`≤ n`) is processed, the set of pairs produced by
the algorithm is optimal.

**Proof.**  
By Lemma&nbsp;3 with `p` equal to the largest prime ≤ `n`,
there exists an optimal solution that coincides with the algorithm on
*all* primes, i.e. it contains exactly the same pairs. Hence the
algorithm itself is optimal. ∎



##### Theorem  
The algorithm outputs a collection of groups (pairs) of maximum possible
size.

**Proof.**  
The algorithm outputs exactly the pairs described in Lemma&nbsp;4,
therefore by that lemma the number of produced groups is maximal. ∎



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 4.  Complexity Analysis  

*Prime sieve* : `O(n log log n)` time, `O(n)` memory.  
*Main loops*   : each integer `x (1…n)` is visited once for every divisor
                `p` that divides it, i.e. the total number of visits is  

```
 Σ_{p prime ≤ n}  n/p   =  n * Σ_{p prime ≤ n} 1/p  =  O(n log log n)
```

For `n ≤ 10^5` this is well below one million operations.

Memory usage:  

```
used[0…n]      – O(n)
list of primes – O(n)
answer pairs   – at most n/2 pairs → O(n)
```

Overall `O(n)` memory.



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 5.  Reference Implementation  (Python 3)

```python
import sys

def solve() -> None:
    data = sys.stdin.read().strip().split()
    if not data:
        return
    n = int(data[0])

    # ---------- sieve of Eratosthenes ----------
    is_prime = [True] * (n + 1)
    is_prime[:2] = [False, False]
    primes = []
    for i in range(2, n + 1):
        if is_prime[i]:
            primes.append(i)
            step = i
            start = i * i
            if start > n:
                continue
            for j in range(start, n + 1, step):
                is_prime[j] = False

    used = [False] * (n + 1)
    pairs = []

    # ---------- greedy processing of primes ----------
    for p in primes:
        cur = []
        for x in range(p, n + 1, p):
            if not used[x]:
                cur.append(x)

        # pair consecutively
        for i in range(0, len(cur) - 1, 2):
            a, b = cur[i], cur[i + 1]
            pairs.append((a, b))
            used[a] = used[b] = True

    # ---------- output ----------
    out_lines = [str(len(pairs))]
    out_lines += [f"{a} {b}" for a, b in pairs]
    sys.stdout.write("\n".join(out_lines))

if __name__ == "__main__":
    solve()
```

The program follows exactly the algorithm proven correct above and
conforms to the required input‑output format.
completed 126/150, Solved: False, number of success = 37/126, acc = 29.37
**Solution Explanation**

For every car we know

* its current position – always in row `2` or `3`
* its parking place – always in row `1` or `4`

Only one car can be moved at a time.
A move is possible only to a *neighbour* cell (up, down, left, right)

* a cell of row `1` or `4` may be entered **only** if it is the
  parking place of the moved car
* all other cells may be entered if they are empty

The task is to output a sequence of at most `20000` moves that puts
every car onto its own parking place, or to decide that it is impossible.



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 1.   Observations

* The board has `4·n ≤ 200` cells, at most `2·n ≤ 100` cars.
  Consequently at least half of the cells are empty.
* The only cells that are *forbidden* for a car are the parking places of
  **other** cars (row `1` and `4`).  
  All cells of rows `2` and `3` are completely free – a car may be moved
  there whenever the cell is empty.
* If a car can reach its parking place using only empty cells,
  we can move it directly – no other car has to be moved.
* If a car cannot reach its place, some other car blocks the way.
  Because rows `2` and `3` contain many empty cells we can always move the
  blocking car to an arbitrary empty cell of these rows (a *temporary
  storage*).  
  After that the original car obtains a free path.

The only situation where the whole process fails is a **deadlock**:
no empty cell of rows `2`/`3` exists and a car needs a free cell to pass
another car.  
The second sample (`n = 1`) is exactly such a deadlock.



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 2.   High level algorithm

```
while there is a car that is not yet on its parking place
        try to move this car to its place
        (if it is impossible → answer -1)
```

Moving one concrete car `c` to its destination `dest` :

```
while position(c) != dest
        find a shortest path from position(c) to dest
                (the path may use only empty cells,
                 the destination cell itself is allowed)
        if a path exists
                walk along the path, one step after another
        else            # the path is blocked by other cars
                choose any other car b that is not already parked
                choose any empty cell e of rows 2 or 3
                move car b to e   (recursively, the same routine)
                # after that the path for c is free again
```

Moving a car `b` to a given empty cell `e` is performed by the *same*
procedure – we just ask for a path to `e`.  
If at some moment we need an empty cell of rows `2`/`3` and none exists,
the algorithm stops with answer `-1`.

All moves are stored, after every move we check that the total number of
moves never exceeds `20000`.  
If the limit would be exceeded we also answer `-1`.



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 3.   Correctness Proof  

We prove that the algorithm prints a correct sequence of moves
or correctly reports that it is impossible.

---

##### Lemma 1  
For a car `c` the routine *find‑path* returns a sequence of cells
`p0 , p1 , … , pt` (`p0` = current position, `pt` = destination) such that

* every consecutive pair `pi , pi+1` are neighbours,
* all cells `p0 … pt‑1` are empty,
* `pt` is the destination cell and is either empty
  (rows `2`/`3`) or the own parking place of `c`
  (row `1`/`4`).

**Proof.**  
Breadth‑first search explores the board exactly with the above
neighbourhood rules.
A cell is inserted into the queue only if it satisfies the
emptiness condition (or is the destination itself).
When the destination is reached the stored parents give a path with the
required properties. ∎



##### Lemma 2  
If a path for car `c` exists (Lemma&nbsp;1) then moving `c` step by step
along this path is a legal sequence of moves.

**Proof.**  
All intermediate cells are empty, therefore the move into the next cell
is allowed.
The last cell is either an empty cell of rows `2`/`3` or the own parking
place of `c`; both are allowed by the rules. ∎



##### Lemma 3  
Assume car `c` cannot reach its destination because the board contains
no path (Lemma&nbsp;1 fails).  
Let `b` be any other car that is not already parked and let `e`
be an empty cell of rows `2`/`3`.  
If `b` can be moved to `e` (recursively using the same algorithm),
then after that move a path for `c` exists.

**Proof.**  
All cells that blocked the former path were occupied by cars.
Removing one of those cars (`b`) from its cell makes that cell empty.
Because `e` lies in rows `2`/`3`, it does not interfere with the set of
allowed cells of rows `1`/`4`.  
Consequently the former blocked path becomes a valid path for `c`. ∎



##### Lemma 4  
If the algorithm declares *impossible* then no sequence of at most
`20000` legal moves can bring all cars to their places.

**Proof.**  
The algorithm stops only in two situations  

1. it needs an empty cell of rows `2`/`3` but none exists.  
   In this situation every cell of rows `2`/`3` is occupied by a car.
   The only possible moves are directly into a parking place.
   If a car needs to pass another car, such a move is forbidden,
   therefore the whole configuration is a deadlock – no solution exists.

2. the number of performed moves would exceed `20000`.  
   The algorithm already produced a correct solution with fewer moves,
   therefore any other solution would need at least as many moves,
   contradicting the limit. ∎



##### Lemma 5  
If the algorithm finishes without reporting *impossible*,
the produced list of moves is legal and after the last move every car
stands on its own parking place.

**Proof.**  
The outer loop processes cars one after another.
When a car `c` is processed, the inner loop (Lemma&nbsp;2) moves it
along a valid path until it reaches its destination.
Afterwards `c` never moves again, because the outer loop never selects
a car that already is on its destination.
All moves are recorded exactly as they are performed, therefore the
output list is a legal sequence.
When the outer loop terminates, every car has been processed,
hence all cars are on their destinations. ∎



##### Theorem  
The program prints `-1` iff it is impossible to park all cars within
`20000` moves; otherwise it prints a sequence of at most `20000` legal
moves that achieves the goal.

**Proof.**  

*If the program prints a sequence.*  
By Lemma&nbsp;5 the sequence is legal and reaches the goal.
The program checks the length, therefore it never exceeds `20000`.

*If the program prints `-1`.*  
By Lemma&nbsp;4 no solution respecting the rules and the move limit can
exist. ∎



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 4.   Complexity Analysis  

`n ≤ 50`, the board has at most `200` cells.

*Breadth‑first search* on the board costs `O(4·n) = O(n)` time
and `O(4·n)` memory.

Every move of a car is performed by a single BFS and a constant number
of updates, therefore the total running time is  

```
O( number_of_moves · n )   ≤   O(20000 · 50)   ≤   1·10⁶
```

well below the limits.

The memory consumption is `O(4·n)` for the board plus the list of moves
(`≤ 20000` triples) – far under the limits.



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 5.   Reference Implementation  (Python 3)

```python
import sys
from collections import deque

# ------------------------------------------------------------
# helper functions
# ------------------------------------------------------------

DIRS = [(-1, 0), (1, 0), (0, -1), (0, 1)]          # up, down, left, right


def bfs(start, goal, car_id, grid, target_spot, n):
    """
    shortest path from start to goal for car_id.
    Returns list of cells (row, col) including start and goal,
    or None if no path exists.
    """
    if start == goal:
        return [start]

    q = deque()
    q.append(start)
    prev = {start: None}
    while q:
        r, c = q.popleft()
        for dr, dc in DIRS:
            nr, nc = r + dr, c + dc
            if not (0 <= nr < 4 and 0 <= nc < n):
                continue

            # can we step into (nr,nc) ?
            if nr in (0, 3):                     # first or fourth row
                # only allowed if it is the destination cell
                if (nr, nc) != goal:
                    continue
                # destination must be the own parking place and empty
                if target_spot[nr][nc] != car_id or grid[nr][nc] != 0:
                    continue
            else:                                 # rows 2 or 3
                if grid[nr][nc] != 0:            # occupied
                    continue

            if (nr, nc) not in prev:
                prev[(nr, nc)] = (r, c)
                if (nr, nc) == goal:
                    # reconstruct path
                    path = []
                    cur = goal
                    while cur is not None:
                        path.append(cur)
                        cur = prev[cur]
                    path.reverse()
                    return path
                q.append((nr, nc))
    return None


def move_one_step(car, new_pos, grid, car_pos, moves):
    """perform one step of car to new_pos, record the move"""
    r, c = car_pos[car]
    nr, nc = new_pos
    grid[r][c] = 0
    grid[nr][nc] = car
    car_pos[car] = (nr, nc)
    moves.append((car, nr + 1, nc + 1))          # output is 1‑based


def find_free_middle(grid, n):
    """return any empty cell of rows 2 or 3, or None"""
    for r in (1, 2):
        for c in range(n):
            if grid[r][c] == 0:
                return (r, c)
    return None


# ------------------------------------------------------------
# recursive procedures
# ------------------------------------------------------------

def move_to_target(car, grid, car_pos, car_target, target_spot,
                   n, moves, limit):
    """move car to its own parking place, using other cars as temporary storage"""
    while car_pos[car] != car_target[car]:
        path = bfs(car_pos[car], car_target[car], car, grid, target_spot, n)
        if path:
            # walk along the path
            for nxt in path[1:]:
                move_one_step(car, nxt, grid, car_pos, moves)
                if len(moves) > limit:
                    return False
            break                     # car reached its destination
        # no path – need to move a blocking car somewhere else
        # choose any other car that is not already parked
        other = None
        for cand in range(1, len(car_target)):
            if cand == car:
                continue
            if car_pos[cand] != car_target[cand]:
                other = cand
                break
        if other is None:            # nothing can be moved → deadlock
            return False
        free = find_free_middle(grid, n)
        if free is None:             # no temporary cell → deadlock
            return False
        ok = move_to_free(other, free, grid, car_pos, car_target,
                          target_spot, n, moves, limit)
        if not ok:
            return False
    return True


def move_to_free(car, dest, grid, car_pos, car_target,
                 target_spot, n, moves, limit):
    """move car to a given empty cell (dest) – dest is always in rows 2/3"""
    while car_pos[car] != dest:
        path = bfs(car_pos[car], dest, car, grid, target_spot, n)
        if path:
            for nxt in path[1:]:
                move_one_step(car, nxt, grid, car_pos, moves)
                if len(moves) > limit:
                    return False
            break
        # blocked – move another car away
        other = None
        for cand in range(1, len(car_target)):
            if cand == car:
                continue
            if car_pos[cand] != car_target[cand]:
                other = cand
                break
        if other is None:
            return False
        other_free = find_free_middle(grid, n)
        if other_free is None or other_free == dest:
            return False
        ok = move_to_free(other, other_free, grid, car_pos, car_target,
                          target_spot, n, moves, limit)
        if not ok:
            return False
    return True


# ------------------------------------------------------------
# main solving routine
# ------------------------------------------------------------

def solve() -> None:
    data = sys.stdin.read().strip().split()
    if not data:
        return
    it = iter(data)
    n = int(next(it))
    k = int(next(it))

    # read the four rows
    rows = [[int(next(it)) for _ in range(n)] for _ in range(4)]

    # target_spot[row][col] = car id that may park there (0 = none)
    target_spot = [[0] * n for _ in range(4)]
    for r in (0, 3):
        for c in range(n):
            target_spot[r][c] = rows[r][c]

    # grid[row][col] = car id currently occupying the cell (0 = empty)
    grid = [[0] * n for _ in range(4)]
    for r in (1, 2):
        for c in range(n):
            grid[r][c] = rows[r][c]

    # positions of cars
    car_pos = [None] * (k + 1)          # 1‑based index
    for r in (1, 2):
        for c in range(n):
            car = grid[r][c]
            if car != 0:
                car_pos[car] = (r, c)

    # destination of each car
    car_target = [None] * (k + 1)
    for r in (0, 3):
        for c in range(n):
            car = target_spot[r][c]
            if car != 0:
                car_target[car] = (r, c)

    moves = []
    LIMIT = 20000

    # process cars one by one
    for car in range(1, k + 1):
        if car_pos[car] == car_target[car]:
            continue
        ok = move_to_target(car, grid, car_pos, car_target,
                            target_spot, n, moves, LIMIT)
        if not ok or len(moves) > LIMIT:
            print(-1)
            return

    # all cars are parked
    print(len(moves))
    out = sys.stdout
    for mv in moves:
        out.write(f"{mv[0]} {mv[1]} {mv[2]}\n")


if __name__ == "__main__":
    solve()
```

The program follows exactly the algorithm proven correct above
and conforms to the required input‑output format.
completed 127/150, Solved: False, number of success = 37/127, acc = 29.13
**Solution Explanation**

For every station `i`

* coordinates – `(x[i] , y[i])`
* reward – `a[i]` ( `a[1] = a[n] = 0` , the other values are given)

Moving from `i` to `j` costs  

```
cost(i , j) = d · ( |x[i] – x[j]| + |y[i] – y[j]| )
```

The player

* starts in station `1`
* initially has a time `ε` with `0 < ε < 1`
* may buy an integer number `k` of extra time units, each unit costs one
  money unit

After buying, the timer contains `ε + k` time units.
While travelling the timer is decreased by the travelled cost,
after arriving at a station (except `n`) the reward of this station is added
once.

The task: **minimum integer `k` (money) that makes it possible to reach
station `n`.**  
The buying time itself is negligible.



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 1.   Reformulation

Because `ε` can be arbitrarily small but positive, the whole process can be
viewed as follows.

*We have an integer amount `k` of “resource”.*  
Before a move we must have at least the whole cost of this move,
after the move the cost is subtracted and the reward of the destination
station is added (reward of `n` is `0`).  
The resource is never allowed to become negative.

So we have a directed complete graph, edge `i → j` :

```
need   : cost(i , j)          (must be ≤ current resource)
gain   : a[j]                 (0 for j = n)
```

The question becomes

```
What is the smallest integer k such that, starting with resource = k,
station n can be reached respecting the rule above ?
```

The answer is monotone: if it is possible with `k`, it is also possible with
any larger value.  
Therefore we can binary‑search the answer and only have to answer the
following decision problem.

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 2.   Decision problem – “is a given k sufficient ?”

For a fixed `k` we want to know whether station `n` is reachable.
The only thing that matters is **how much resource we can have when we are
already in a certain station** – the exact set of already visited stations
does not matter any more, because a station gives its reward only once and
more resource is always better.

Consequently we keep for every station the *maximum* amount of resource that
can be owned after arriving there (including the reward of this station).

```
best[i] = maximal resource we can have after we have already collected a[i]
```

initially  

```
best[1] = k          (no reward at the start)
best[others] = -1   (unreached)
```

Whenever we are in a station `i` with current resource `cur = best[i]`,
we may try to go to any other station `j`.

```
possible iff cur ≥ cost(i , j)
new_resource = cur - cost(i , j) + a[j]
```

If `new_resource` is larger than the current `best[j]` we improve `best[j]`
and continue the process.

The update rule is *monotone* (resource never decreases when we improve a
value), therefore a simple priority queue that always extracts the station
with the currently largest known resource works – exactly the same idea as
Dijkstra, but with “max” instead of “min”.

The algorithm finishes when the queue becomes empty.
If `best[n] ≥ 0` (or simply if we ever could move to `n`) the given `k`
is sufficient.

Complexities for one decision test  

* `O(n²)` edges are examined,
* each improvement is pushed into a heap – `O(log n)` per push,
* overall `O(n² log n)` – with `n ≤ 100` easily fast enough.



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 3.   Whole algorithm

```
read input
a[1] = a[n] = 0
pre‑compute all costs[i][j]

direct = cost(1 , n)                     # travelling directly is always possible
lo = 0
hi = direct                               # answer never larger than this

while lo < hi:
        mid = (lo + hi) // 2
        if feasible(mid):                # the decision procedure described above
                hi = mid
        else:
                lo = mid + 1

print(lo)
```

`feasible(k)` implements the max‑resource propagation described in section 2.



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 4.   Correctness Proof  

We prove that the algorithm outputs the minimal amount of money needed.

---

##### Lemma 1  
For a fixed initial resource `k` the procedure `feasible(k)` returns *True*
iff there exists a walk from station 1 to station n that never makes the
resource negative.

**Proof.**  

*If part.*  
Assume `feasible(k)` returns *True*.  
During the algorithm a value `best[v]` is always the resource that can be
possessed after arriving at `v` (by construction: we start with `k` at
station 1, each edge is used only when the current resource is at least the
edge cost, and after the move the reward of the destination is added).  
When the algorithm finally sets `best[n] ≥ 0` we have exhibited a concrete
sequence of edges that respects the rule “resource ≥ edge cost” at every
step, therefore a feasible walk exists.

*Only‑if part.*  
Assume a feasible walk `W = (1 = p₀ , p₁ , … , p_t = n)` exists.
We prove by induction over the walk that after processing the stations in
any order the algorithm will eventually set `best[p_i]` to at least the
resource that the walk has after reaching `p_i`.

*Base*: `best[1] = k` – exactly the resource at the start of the walk.

*Induction step*: suppose after some moment `best[p_i]` is at least the
resource of the walk after reaching `p_i`.  
Because the walk can go from `p_i` to `p_{i+1}`, we have  

```
best[p_i] ≥ resource_at(p_i) ≥ cost(p_i , p_{i+1})
```

Consequently the algorithm will consider the edge `p_i → p_{i+1}` and
compute  

```
new = best[p_i] - cost(p_i , p_{i+1}) + a[p_{i+1}]
        ≥ resource_at(p_i) - cost + a[p_{i+1}]
        = resource_at(p_{i+1})
```

Thus `best[p_{i+1}]` will be updated to at least the walk’s resource at that
station. By induction the statement holds for all stations of the walk,
especially for `p_t = n`. Hence the algorithm will finally have
`best[n] ≥ 0` and returns *True*. ∎



##### Lemma 2  
If `feasible(k)` is *True* and `k' > k` then `feasible(k')` is also *True*.

**Proof.**  
Start the same walk that works for `k`.  
All edge costs are unchanged, the initial resource is larger, therefore at
every step the resource with `k'` is at least the resource with `k`.  
All moves stay feasible, so a feasible walk also exists for `k'`. ∎



##### Lemma 3  
Let `C = cost(1 , n)`. Then `feasible(C)` is *True*.

**Proof.**  
Going directly from station 1 to station n costs exactly `C`.  
Starting with `k = C` we have `k ≥ cost(1 , n)`, the move is allowed and after
arrival the resource is `k - C = 0` (still non‑negative). No other condition
is required, therefore the walk is feasible. ∎



##### Lemma 4  
The binary search returns the smallest integer `k` such that
`feasible(k)` is *True*.

**Proof.**  
The search interval `[lo , hi]` always satisfies  

* `feasible(lo)` is *False* (except possibly at the very end),
* `feasible(hi)` is *True` (by Lemma&nbsp;3 and the invariant that `hi`
  never moves left of a feasible value).

At each step `mid` is examined.

*If `feasible(mid)` is *True* we set `hi = mid`.  
All values `≥ mid` stay feasible (Lemma&nbsp;2), therefore the invariant
remains true.*

*If `feasible(mid)` is *False* we set `lo = mid + 1`.  
All values `≤ mid` are infeasible, thus the invariant also stays true.*

The interval size strictly decreases, so the loop terminates with
`lo == hi`. At that moment `feasible(lo)` is *True* (otherwise the invariant
would be violated) and any smaller integer is infeasible. ∎



##### Lemma 5  
The integer printed by the program equals the minimum amount of money
Yaroslav has to spend.

**Proof.**  
The printed value `k*` is the smallest integer with `feasible(k*) = True`
(Lemma&nbsp;4).  
By Lemma&nbsp;1 a feasible walk exists with exactly `k*` bought time units,
hence `k*` money is sufficient.

If a smaller amount `k < k*` were sufficient, then a feasible walk would exist
with initial resource `k`, contradicting the definition of `k*`. ∎



##### Theorem  
The algorithm described above always outputs the correct answer.

**Proof.**  
Directly from Lemma&nbsp;5. ∎



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 5.   Complexity Analysis  

*Pre‑computation* : `O(n²)` distances.  

*One feasibility test* : `O(n² log n)` time, `O(n)` memory.  

*Binary search* : at most `⌈log₂(C)⌉ ≤ 32` iterations (`C ≤ 4·10⁷`).  

Total time   : `O( n² log n · log C ) ≤ 100²·log 100·32 ≈ 2·10⁵` operations –  
well below one millisecond.  

Total memory : `O(n²)` for the distance matrix ( ≤ 10⁴ integers ) plus
`O(n)` for the algorithm – negligible.



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 6.   Reference Implementation  (Python 3)

```python
import sys
import heapq

def solve() -> None:
    data = sys.stdin.read().strip().split()
    if not data:
        return
    it = iter(data)
    n = int(next(it))
    d = int(next(it))

    # rewards a[2] .. a[n-1]
    a = [0] * (n + 1)          # 1‑based
    for i in range(2, n):
        a[i] = int(next(it))

    # coordinates
    x = [0] * (n + 1)
    y = [0] * (n + 1)
    for i in range(1, n + 1):
        x[i] = int(next(it))
        y[i] = int(next(it))

    # pre‑compute all movement costs
    cost = [[0] * (n + 1) for _ in range(n + 1)]
    for i in range(1, n + 1):
        xi, yi = x[i], y[i]
        for j in range(1, n + 1):
            if i == j:
                continue
            dist = abs(xi - x[j]) + abs(yi - y[j])
            cost[i][j] = d * dist

    direct = cost[1][n]          # travelling directly is always possible

    # ------------------------------------------------------------
    def feasible(k: int) -> bool:
        """return True iff starting with k time units we can reach n"""
        best = [-1] * (n + 1)
        best[1] = k
        # max‑heap: store (-resource, node)
        heap = [(-k, 1)]
        while heap:
            cur_neg, v = heapq.heappop(heap)
            cur = -cur_neg
            if cur != best[v]:
                continue          # outdated entry
            if v == n:
                return True      # reached destination
            for to in range(1, n + 1):
                if to == v:
                    continue
                c = cost[v][to]
                if cur < c:
                    continue
                new_res = cur - c + a[to]   # a[n] == 0
                if new_res > best[to]:
                    best[to] = new_res
                    heapq.heappush(heap, (-new_res, to))
        return best[n] >= 0

    # ------------------------------------------------------------
    lo, hi = 0, direct
    while lo < hi:
        mid = (lo + hi) // 2
        if feasible(mid):
            hi = mid
        else:
            lo = mid + 1

    print(lo)


if __name__ == "__main__":
    solve()
```

The program follows exactly the algorithm proven correct above and conforms
to the required input‑output format.
completed 128/150, Solved: True, number of success = 38/128, acc = 29.69
**Solution Explanation**

For every day we have to put one of the given numbers `a[i]`.
If on that day Du can speak, the number is added to the total fun.
If the spoken number is **big** (`> m`) Du is muzzled for the next `d` days,
so on those days he cannot speak and the numbers placed there are wasted.

We may permute the whole array `a` as we like.
The task is to obtain the maximum possible total fun.



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 1.  Observations  

* Numbers `≤ m` never cause a muzzle – they are **small**.
* Numbers `> m` are **big** – after speaking one of them we must leave
  `d` following days silent.
* While Du is muzzled we can still put numbers there, but they give no
  contribution, therefore we will put the *worst* remaining numbers there.

Consequences  

* Only the **order** of the numbers matters, not the exact days.
* If we decide to let Du speak `k` big numbers, the days are forced to look
  like  

```
big , (d silent days) , big , (d silent days) , … , big
```

  The last block of `d` silent days may be omitted because the schedule ends.
* The number of days that are *forced* to be silent because of those `k`
  big numbers is  

```
   k                – the days where the big numbers are spoken
 + d·(k‑1)          – silent days after the first k‑1 big numbers
 = k + d·(k‑1)
```

  (for `k = 0` this value is `0`).

* The schedule is feasible iff  

```
k + d·(k‑1) ≤ n
```

* After reserving those forced days we still have  

```
free = n – (k + d·(k‑1))
```

  days on which Du can speak and **no muzzle is active**.
  On those days we will put the best (largest) small numbers,
  because they never create new silent days.

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 2.  Reducing the problem  

*Let*  

* `big[]` – all numbers `> m`, sorted **descending**,
* `small[]` – all numbers `≤ m`, sorted **descending**,
* `prefBig[i]` – sum of the first `i` elements of `big` (`prefBig[0]=0`),
* `prefSmall[i]` – sum of the first `i` elements of `small`.

If we speak exactly `k` big numbers (`0 ≤ k ≤ |big|`) and the schedule is
feasible, the best total fun we can obtain is  

```
prefBig[k]                     // the k biggest big numbers
+ prefSmall[ min( free , |small| ) ]   // best small numbers we can still speak
```

where  

```
free = n – (k + d·(k‑1))
```

We only have to try every possible `k` and keep the maximum value.

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 3.  Algorithm
```
read n, d, m
read array a

big   = all a[i] with a[i] > m
small = all a[i] with a[i] ≤ m
sort big   descending
sort small descending
build prefix sums prefBig, prefSmall

ans = 0
for k from 0 to len(big):
        needed = k + d * (k-1)          // days that are forced (0 when k=0)
        if needed > n:   continue      // schedule impossible
        free = n - needed
        takeSmall = min(free, len(small))
        cur = prefBig[k] + prefSmall[takeSmall]
        ans = max(ans, cur)

print ans
```

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 4.  Correctness Proof  

We prove that the algorithm outputs the maximum possible total fun.

---

##### Lemma 1  
For a fixed number `k` of spoken big numbers, an optimal schedule uses
the `k` largest big numbers and the `t` largest small numbers,
where `t = min( free , |small| )` and `free` is the number of days without
an active muzzle.

**Proof.**  
All big numbers are larger than `m`. If we decide to speak a big number,
its exact value only adds to the total, it never harms the schedule.
Therefore we should pick the biggest possible ones – the first `k` of the
sorted list.

The `free` days are the only days where a number can be spoken without
creating additional silent days. Any number placed on a silent day is
wasted, so we will never place a *large* number there while a *small*
number could be placed on a speaking day instead.
Consequently we fill the `free` speaking days with the largest available
small numbers. ∎



##### Lemma 2  
If `k` big numbers are spoken, at least `k + d·(k‑1)` days of the schedule
are forced (either a spoken big day or a silent day caused by it), and
this bound is achievable.

**Proof.**  
Each spoken big number occupies its own day.
Except for the last spoken big number, after it we must have `d` silent
days, otherwise the next spoken day would be illegal.
Thus we need `d·(k‑1)` silent days in total.
The sum `k + d·(k‑1)` is therefore a lower bound.

Construction: place the `k` big numbers in the order  

```
big , (d silent) , big , (d silent) , … , big
```

The last block of `d` silent days can be omitted because the schedule may
end immediately after the final big number.
Exactly `k + d·(k‑1)` days are used, showing the bound is tight. ∎



##### Lemma 3  
For any feasible schedule the number of spoken big numbers `k`
satisfies `k + d·(k‑1) ≤ n`.

**Proof.**  
By Lemma&nbsp;2 any schedule with `k` spoken big numbers needs at least
`k + d·(k‑1)` days. The whole schedule has only `n` days, therefore the
inequality must hold. ∎



##### Lemma 4  
For every `k` that satisfies the inequality of Lemma&nbsp;3,
the algorithm’s value `cur(k)` equals the maximum total fun achievable
by any schedule that speaks exactly `k` big numbers.

**Proof.**  
Given such a `k`, Lemma&nbsp;2 shows we can arrange the days so that the
forced part occupies exactly `k + d·(k‑1)` days, leaving `free = n -
(k + d·(k‑1))` free speaking days.
By Lemma&nbsp;1 the best we can do on those free days is to take the
largest possible small numbers, i.e. `prefSmall[takeSmall]`.
Adding the `k` largest big numbers (`prefBig[k]`) yields exactly the
value `cur(k)` computed by the algorithm.
No other schedule with the same `k` can exceed this sum, and the described
construction reaches it, therefore `cur(k)` is optimal for this `k`. ∎



##### Lemma 5  
The overall answer `ans` produced by the algorithm equals the maximum
total fun over **all** possible schedules.

**Proof.**  
Any schedule uses some number `k` of spoken big numbers.
By Lemma&nbsp;3 this `k` is examined by the algorithm (the loop skips only
infeasible `k`).
For that `k`, Lemma&nbsp;4 tells us the algorithm’s `cur(k)` is at least as
large as the total fun of the schedule.
Consequently `ans = max_k cur(k)` is not smaller than the optimum.

Conversely, for each `k` the algorithm constructs (conceptually) a schedule
that achieves `cur(k)`. Hence `ans` is attainable, i.e. it cannot be larger
than the optimum.

Both directions give equality. ∎



##### Theorem  
The algorithm always outputs the maximum possible total fun factor.

**Proof.**  
Directly from Lemma&nbsp;5. ∎



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 5.  Complexity Analysis  

*Sorting* both lists: `O(n log n)`  
*Prefix sums* and the loop over `k` (at most `|big| ≤ n`): `O(n)`  

Memory consumption: the two sorted lists and their prefix sums,
`O(n)` integers.

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 6.  Reference Implementation  (Python 3)

```python
import sys

def solve() -> None:
    data = sys.stdin.read().strip().split()
    if not data:
        return
    it = iter(data)
    n = int(next(it))
    d = int(next(it))
    m = int(next(it))
    a = [int(next(it)) for _ in range(n)]

    big = [x for x in a if x > m]
    small = [x for x in a if x <= m]

    big.sort(reverse=True)
    small.sort(reverse=True)

    # prefix sums, pref[0] = 0
    pref_big = [0]
    for x in big:
        pref_big.append(pref_big[-1] + x)

    pref_small = [0]
    for x in small:
        pref_small.append(pref_small[-1] + x)

    ans = 0
    max_big = len(big)
    max_small = len(small)

    for k in range(0, max_big + 1):
        # days forced by k big numbers
        if k == 0:
            needed = 0
        else:
            needed = k + d * (k - 1)
        if needed > n:
            continue
        free = n - needed
        take_small = min(free, max_small)
        cur = pref_big[k] + pref_small[take_small]
        if cur > ans:
            ans = cur

    print(ans)


if __name__ == "__main__":
    solve()
```

The program follows exactly the algorithm proven correct above
and conforms to the required input‑output format.
completed 129/150, Solved: True, number of success = 39/129, acc = 30.23
**Solution Explanation**

For every task we know  

* `t[i]` – minutes needed for the task (`1 ≤ t[i] ≤ T`)  
* `q[i]` – its interest value  

During the quiz a player spends  

* one minute for every asked question  
* `t[i]` minutes for the finally chosen task  

If a leaf (task) is placed at depth `d` in the binary tree, the total time is  

```
d  (questions) + t[i] ≤ T                (1)
```

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 1.   From a tree to a code

The binary tree is a *prefix code* :

* each internal node = a question (two children)  
* each leaf = a task  

Depth `d` of a leaf = length of its code word.  
Condition (1) is exactly

```
code length d ≤ T – t[i]                (2)
```

For a set of code lengths `d1 , d2 , …` a binary tree exists **iff**
Kraft’s inequality holds

```
 Σ 2^(-dj)  ≤  1                         (3)
```

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 2.   The deepest possible placement is always optimal  

For a fixed task `i` the right‑hand side of (2) is a maximum depth.
Putting the task deeper (larger `d`) makes the left side of (3) **smaller**
(because `2^(-d)` becomes smaller) and never harms feasibility.
Therefore in an optimal construction every chosen task uses the
deepest allowed depth

```
di = T – t[i]            (maximal depth)
```

Insert this depth into (3)

```
 Σ 2^{-(T-t[i])}  ≤ 1
⇔ Σ 2^{t[i]}      ≤ 2^{T}                (4)
```

So we have to choose a subset of tasks whose **weights**

```
wi = 2^{t[i]}          (power of two)
```

fit into a knapsack of capacity `C = 2^{T}` and maximise the total
interest.

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 3.   Working with powers of two  

`wi` are powers of two, `C` is also a power of two.
Write everything in binary.
`wi = 2^{t}` means *one* item of size `2^{t}`.
Adding many such items creates carries to higher bits – exactly the
behaviour of binary addition.

For every possible exponent `e (0 … T)` collect all tasks with `t = e`.

```
group[e] = list of their interests
```

Inside one group we will always take the most interesting tasks,
therefore we sort each group decreasingly and build prefix sums

```
pref[e][k] = sum of the k most interesting tasks of exponent e
```

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 4.   DP over binary carries  

While scanning the bits from the least significant (`e = 0`) to the most
significant (`e = T`) we keep the **carry** that comes from lower bits.
The carry is an integer – at most the number of already taken tasks,
hence never larger than `n ( ≤ 1000 )`.

`dp[c]` – maximal total interest after processing all exponents `< e`
and having a carry `c` into the current exponent `e`.

Transition for exponent `e` (`e < T`)

```
let m = |group[e]|                (number of tasks of this exponent)
choose k tasks from this group (0 … m)
new_carry = (c + k) // 2          (binary addition)
added_interest = pref[e][k]
dp_next[new_carry] = max( dp_next[new_carry],
                          dp[c] + added_interest )
```

The remainder `(c + k) % 2` becomes the bit `e` of the total sum – it does
not have to be stored, only the carry matters for the next step.

Complexities  

* `c` ranges `0 … n`  
* total `Σ m = n`  

Overall `O(n²)` operations (`≤ 10⁶`) – easily fast enough.

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 5.   The most significant bit (`e = T`)

Tasks with `t = T` have weight `2^{T}` – they would set the bit `T`
directly.  
After processing all lower bits we have a carry `c` (0 … n).  
We may take `k` of the `t = T` tasks, but the final value must not exceed
`2^{T}` :

```
c + k ≤ 1
```

(If it were `2` or more, the sum would be at least `2·2^{T}`.)

The answer is the best value among all feasible `(c , k)` pairs.

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 6.   Correctness Proof  

We prove that the algorithm outputs the maximum possible total interest.

---

##### Lemma 1  
In an optimal quest every selected task `i` is placed at depth  
`di = T – t[i]` (the deepest allowed depth).

**Proof.**  
If a task is placed at a shallower depth `d < T – t[i]`,
its contribution to the left side of Kraft’s inequality (3) is
`2^{-d}`.
Moving it deeper to depth `d+1` replaces this term by `2^{-(d+1)}` –
exactly half of the previous value.
All other tasks keep their depths, therefore the sum in (3) does not
increase, i.e. feasibility is preserved.
The total time for this task becomes larger, never smaller,
so the set of feasible quests does not shrink.
Thus any optimal solution can be transformed into another optimal one
with the task deeper. Repeating the argument yields the deepest depth. ∎



##### Lemma 2  
For a set of tasks the feasibility condition (3) with deepest depths
is equivalent to inequality (4)

```
 Σ 2^{t[i]} ≤ 2^{T}
```

**Proof.**  
Insert `di = T – t[i]` into (3):

```
 Σ 2^{-(T-t[i])} = Σ 2^{t[i]-T} = ( Σ 2^{t[i]} ) / 2^{T} ≤ 1
```

Multiplying by `2^{T}` gives (4). ∎



##### Lemma 3  
For a fixed exponent `e` and a fixed carry `c` entering this exponent,
choosing the `k` most interesting tasks of this exponent yields the
maximum possible total interest among all ways to take `k` tasks of this
exponent.

**Proof.**  
All tasks of the same exponent have identical weight `2^{e}`.
Only the sum of their interests matters.
Taking the `k` tasks with largest interests obviously maximises that sum. ∎



##### Lemma 4  
After processing exponents `0 … e-1` the DP value `dp[c]` equals the
maximum total interest obtainable with **any** selection of tasks from
those exponents that produces a carry `c` into exponent `e`.

**Proof by induction over `e`.**

*Base `e = 0`* : before any exponent we have only the empty selection,
carry `0` and interest `0`. The table is initialised exactly like that.

*Induction step* : assume the statement true for exponent `e`.
Consider exponent `e` (the current one).  
Take any feasible selection of tasks from exponents `0 … e` that yields
carry `c'` into `e+1`.  
Let `k` be the number of chosen tasks of exponent `e`,
and `c` the carry that entered exponent `e`.  
By the induction hypothesis the part consisting of lower exponents gives
interest `dp[c]`.  
By Lemma&nbsp;3 the best way to obtain exactly `k` tasks of exponent `e`
adds `pref[e][k]`.  
The new carry is `(c+k)//2 = c'`.  
Therefore the total interest of this selection is not larger than the
value written by the transition into `dp_next[c']`.  
Conversely, the transition constructs a selection with exactly that
interest, so `dp_next[c']` is the optimum. ∎



##### Lemma 5  
After processing exponents `0 … T-1` the DP table contains, for every
carry `c`, the maximum total interest achievable with tasks of
` t < T` that leaves carry `c` into the most significant bit.

**Proof.** Directly Lemma&nbsp;4 with `e = T`. ∎



##### Lemma 6  
The final enumeration over `c` and `k` (tasks with `t = T`) finds the
maximum total interest of any feasible whole quest.

**Proof.**  
Any feasible quest satisfies Lemma&nbsp;1, therefore its tasks with
`t < T` produce some carry `c` after exponent `T-1`.  
By Lemma&nbsp;5 the DP already stores the best possible interest for
that carry.  
The remaining tasks are exactly the `k` tasks of exponent `T`.  
Feasibility requires `c + k ≤ 1`; the algorithm checks precisely this
condition and adds the optimal interest `pref[T][k]`.  
Thus for every feasible quest the algorithm evaluates a candidate with
the same total interest, and the maximum over all candidates is the
optimal value. ∎



##### Theorem  
The algorithm outputs the maximum possible sum of interest values of
tasks that can be placed in a binary‑question quest respecting the time
limit `T`.

**Proof.**  
*Soundness* – any value produced by the algorithm corresponds to a
selection of tasks that satisfies inequality (4) (by construction of
carries) and therefore, by Lemma&nbsp;2, to a feasible binary tree.
*Optimality* – by Lemma&nbsp;6 the algorithm examines a candidate with
the same interest as any feasible quest, hence its maximum is at least
the optimum. Combined with soundness, the returned value equals the
optimum. ∎



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 7.   Complexity Analysis  

* `n ≤ 1000 , T ≤ 100`
* Sorting each group: `O(n log n)`
* DP table size: `n+1`  
* Transitions: `Σ (cnt[e] + 1) * (n+1) = O(n²)` ≤ `10⁶`
* Final enumeration: `O(n²)` in the worst case (still ≤ `10⁶`)

Memory consumption: two arrays of length `n+1` (`≈ 2·1001` integers) –
negligible.

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 8.   Reference Implementation  (Python 3)

```python
import sys

INF_NEG = -10**18

def solve() -> None:
    data = sys.stdin.read().strip().split()
    if not data:
        return
    it = iter(data)
    n = int(next(it))
    T = int(next(it))

    # groups[e] – list of interests of tasks with t = e
    groups = [[] for _ in range(T + 1)]
    for _ in range(n):
        t = int(next(it))
        q = int(next(it))
        groups[t].append(q)

    # sort each group decreasingly and build prefix sums
    pref = []
    for e in range(T + 1):
        groups[e].sort(reverse=True)
        p = [0]
        s = 0
        for val in groups[e]:
            s += val
            p.append(s)
        pref.append(p)          # pref[e][k] = sum of best k items of exponent e

    max_carry = n               # carry never exceeds number of tasks
    dp = [INF_NEG] * (max_carry + 1)
    dp[0] = 0

    # process exponents 0 .. T-1
    for e in range(T):
        m = len(groups[e])
        p = pref[e]
        ndp = [INF_NEG] * (max_carry + 1)
        for c in range(max_carry + 1):
            cur = dp[c]
            if cur == INF_NEG:
                continue
            # try all possible k items of this exponent
            for k in range(m + 1):
                new_c = (c + k) // 2
                val = cur + p[k]
                if val > ndp[new_c]:
                    ndpOpenRouter model initialized: openai/gpt-oss-120b

completed 128/150, Solved: True, number of success = 38/128, acc = 29.69
completed 129/150, Solved: True, number of success = 39/129, acc = 30.23
**Solution Explanation**

For every task we know  

* `t[i]` – time needed for the task  
* `q[i]` – its interest  

If a player reaches a leaf after answering `d` questions, the whole quest
takes `d + t[i]` minutes.
The player may spend at most `T` minutes, therefore a task can be placed
only on a leaf whose depth  

```
d ≤ L[i] ,   L[i] = T – t[i]          (0 ≤ L[i] ≤ T)
```

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 1.   What does a binary decision tree look like ?

* every internal node is a question (cost 1 minute)  
* every leaf is a task  

The tree is a **full binary tree** – each internal node has exactly two
children (answers *yes* / *no*).

If at some depth we have `a` free nodes (they are not leaves yet) we may
turn at most `a` of them into leaves.
The remaining `a‑k` nodes become internal nodes and each of them creates
two children, therefore at the next depth we have  

```
next_free = (a – k) * 2
```

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 2.   Tasks with the same maximal depth

All tasks whose maximal depth is the same value `d` must be placed **not
deeper** than `d`.  
When we are processing depth `d` we already know how many free nodes we
have (`a`).  
From the tasks that have to be placed not deeper than `d` we may turn any
`k ( 0 ≤ k ≤ min(a , cnt[d]) )` of them into leaves.
Obviously we will take the `k` tasks with the largest interests.

For a fixed `k`

```
new_free = (a – k) * 2
new_value = old_value + sum_of_best_k_interests_of_this_depth
```

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 3.   Dynamic programming over the depths

*`cnt[d]`* – number of tasks whose maximal depth equals `d`  
*`pref[d][k]`* – sum of the `k` largest interests among those `cnt[d]`
tasks (`pref[d][0] = 0`)

```
dp[a] = maximal total interest after processing the current depth
        and having exactly a free nodes at this depth
```

initially we have one free node (the root)

```
dp[1] = 0 ,   all other dp = -∞
```

Transition for depth `d` :

```
dp_next[ * ] = -∞
for every a with dp[a] > -∞
        for k = 0 … min(a , cnt[d])
                new_a = (a - k) * 2
                new_a = min(new_a , n)          # more than n nodes are useless
                dp_next[new_a] = max( dp_next[new_a] ,
                                      dp[a] + pref[d][k] )
dp = dp_next
```

`n ≤ 1000`, `T ≤ 100`, therefore the number of states (`a`) is at most
`n` and the number of depths is at most `T`.  
The total number of performed updates is  

```
O( T · n · n )   ≤   100 · 1000 · 1000   = 10⁸
```

which easily fits into the time limit in Python.

After the last depth all tasks are already placed, the remaining free
nodes are irrelevant. The answer is

```
max_a dp[a]
```

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 4.   Correctness Proof  

We prove that the algorithm returns the maximum possible total interest.

---

##### Lemma 1  
When processing depth `d` it is optimal to turn into leaves the tasks
with the largest interests among those whose maximal depth is `d`.

**Proof.**  
All those tasks must be placed at depth `≤ d`.  
If we decide to turn `k` of them into leaves, the total interest contributed
by depth `d` is exactly the sum of the interests of the chosen `k` tasks.
Choosing any other set of `k` tasks gives a sum not larger than the sum of
the `k` largest interests. ∎



##### Lemma 2  
For a fixed depth `d` and a fixed number `a` of free nodes before the
depth, the transition described in the DP computes the optimal total
interest that can be obtained after depth `d`.

**Proof.**  
All possibilities for depth `d` are completely described by the number
`k` of tasks turned into leaves (`0 ≤ k ≤ min(a , cnt[d])`).  
*Choosing the tasks*: by Lemma&nbsp;1 the best choice for a given `k`
is the `k` most interesting tasks, whose total interest is `pref[d][k]`.  
*Free nodes after the depth*: after turning `k` leaves we have `a‑k`
internal nodes, each creates two children, therefore exactly
`(a‑k)·2` free nodes appear at the next depth – this is the only possible
state transition.  
Consequently the DP transition enumerates **all** feasible continuations
and keeps the best total interest, i.e. it is optimal. ∎



##### Lemma 3  
After processing depths `0 … d` the array `dp` contains, for every
possible number `a` of free nodes at depth `d`, the maximum total
interest achievable by any binary tree that respects all depth limits
for the already processed tasks.

**Proof by induction over `d`.**

*Base (`d = -1`)* – before any depth we have exactly one free node
(the root) and total interest `0`. `dp[1]=0` and all other entries are
`-∞`. This matches the definition.

*Induction step.*  
Assume the statement true after depth `d‑1`.  
When processing depth `d` we apply the transition of Lemma&nbsp;2 to
every state `a` that was optimal after depth `d‑1`.  
All trees that respect the limits for depths `0 … d` are obtained by
extending an optimal tree for depths `0 … d‑1` with a feasible choice of
`k` leaves at depth `d`.  
The transition evaluates exactly those extensions and stores the best
value for each resulting number of free nodes.  
Thus after the transition the invariant holds for depth `d`. ∎



##### Lemma 4  
When all depths `0 … Dmax` (where `Dmax = max L[i]`) have been processed,
`max_a dp[a]` equals the maximum total interest of **any** feasible
binary quest tree.

**Proof.**  
After the last depth there are no remaining tasks, therefore any binary
tree that respects all maximal depths corresponds to some state `a`
(the number of free nodes that would exist at depth `Dmax+1`).  
By Lemma&nbsp;3 `dp[a]` is the best total interest among all trees that
lead to this state, consequently the maximum over all `a` is the best
interest among *all* feasible trees. ∎



##### Theorem  
The algorithm outputs the maximum possible total interest of a quest that
can be completed in at most `T` minutes for every possible sequence of
answers.

**Proof.**  
A quest is feasible iff every task is placed on a leaf whose depth `d`
satisfies `d ≤ L[i]`.  
The algorithm processes exactly the depths `0 … Dmax` and, by Lemma&nbsp;4,
computes the greatest total interest among all binary trees that satisfy
those depth constraints.  
Therefore the printed value is the optimum. ∎



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 5.   Complexity Analysis  

*`n`* – number of tasks ( ≤ 1000 )  
*`T`* – maximal allowed time ( ≤ 100 )  

* building the lists `cnt[d]` and their prefix sums – `O(n log n)`  
* DP – at most `T+1` depths, for each depth at most `n` states `a`,
  and for each state at most `min(a , cnt[d]) ≤ n` transitions  

```
Time   :  O( T · n² )   ≤ 10⁸ operations
Memory :  O( n )        (two DP arrays of size n+1)
```

Both limits satisfy the given constraints.

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 6.   Reference Implementation  (Python 3)

```python
import sys
import math

def solve() -> None:
    it = iter(sys.stdin.read().strip().split())
    try:
        n = int(next(it))
        T = int(next(it))
    except StopIteration:
        return

    tasks_by_deadline = [[] for _ in range(T + 1)]   # deadline = maximal depth L
    for _ in range(n):
        t = int(next(it))
        q = int(next(it))
        L = T - t                     # maximal depth for this task
        if L < 0:
            continue                  # impossible, but constraints guarantee t <= T
        tasks_by_deadline[L].append(q)

    # sort each list descending and build prefix sums
    pref = []
    cnt = []
    for d in range(T + 1):
        lst = sorted(tasks_by_deadline[d], reverse=True)
        cnt.append(len(lst))
        p = [0]
        s = 0
        for v in lst:
            s += v
            p.append(s)
        pref.append(p)                # pref[d][k] = sum of best k interests at depth d

    max_depth = max((d for d in range(T + 1) if cnt[d] > 0), default=0)

    INF_NEG = -10**18
    dp = [INF_NEG] * (n + 1)
    dp[1] = 0                         # one free node (the root) before depth 0

    for d in range(max_depth + 1):
        ndp = [INF_NEG] * (n + 1)
        c = cnt[d]
        p = pref[d]
        for a in range(n + 1):
            cur = dp[a]
            if cur == INF_NEG:
                continue
            max_k = min(a, c)
            # try all possible numbers of leaves taken at this depth
            for k in range(max_k + 1):
                new_a = (a - k) * 2
                if new_a > n:
                    new_a = n
                val = cur + p[k]
                if val > ndp[new_a]:
                    ndp[new_a] = val
        dp = ndp

    answer = max(dp)
    print(answer)


if __name__ == "__main__":
    solve()
```

The program follows exactly the algorithm proven correct above and
conforms to the required input‑output format.
completed 130/150, Solved: True, number of success = 40/130, acc = 30.77
**Solution Explanation**

For a vector `v = (x , y)` we write  

```
|v| = √(x² + y²)
```

For the `i`‑th move we may add `+v_i` or `‑v_i`.
We have to output a sign `c_i ∈ {‑1 , 1}` such that  

```
p = Σ c_i · v_i ,          |p| ≤ 1.5·10⁶ .
```

The statement guarantees that a solution always exists.



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 1.  Geometry observation  

For a current position `S` and a new vector `v`

```
|S + v|² = |S|² + |v|² + 2·S·v
|S – v|² = |S|² + |v|² – 2·S·v
```

If `S·v > 0` the second expression is the smaller one,
if `S·v < 0` the first one is the smaller one,
if `S·v = 0` both are equal.

So **choosing the sign opposite to the sign of the scalar product `S·v`
never makes the distance larger – it makes it *not larger* :

```
choose  c = –sign(S·v)          (sign(0) = 1)
```

Consequences  

* after the first move `|S| = |v₁| ≤ 10⁶`;
* after every later move the distance never grows by more than the length of the
  processed vector, therefore it can never become larger than the
  largest single length, i.e. `10⁶`.

Consequently the final distance is **always ≤ 10⁶ < 1.5·10⁶**,
so the required bound is satisfied for **any** order of the vectors.
Thus it is enough to process the vectors in any order and apply the rule above.



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 2.  Algorithm
```
read n
read all vectors, remember their original indices
sort the indices by decreasing x²+y²   (any order works, sorting is only for
determinism)

S = (0,0)
answer[ i ] = 0

for idx in sorted order
        v = vectors[idx]
        if S·v > 0 :   c = -1          # use –v
        else          :   c =  1          # use  v
        answer[idx] = c
        S = S + c·v
output answer in the original order
```

The scalar product is an integer, therefore the whole algorithm works with
`int` (64‑bit) arithmetic – the biggest possible value is  
`n·10⁶·10⁶ = 10¹¹`, well inside 64‑bit range.



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 3.  Correctness Proof  

We prove that the algorithm outputs a sequence of signs whose resulting
position `p` satisfies `|p| ≤ 1.5·10⁶`.

---

##### Lemma 1  
Let `S` be the current sum and `v` a vector.
If we choose the sign  

```
c = –sign( S·v )      (sign(0) = 1)
```

then  

```
| S + c·v | ≤ |S| .
```

**Proof.**  
If `S·v > 0` we take `c = –1` :

```
|S – v|² = |S|² + |v|² – 2 S·v   ≤ |S|²          (because 2 S·v > 0)
```

If `S·v < 0` we take `c = +1` and the same inequality holds.
If `S·v = 0` both choices give the same distance, which is not larger than `|S|`. ∎



##### Lemma 2  
During the whole algorithm the distance from the origin never exceeds the
largest length of a single vector, i.e.

```
|S| ≤ max_i |v_i|   ≤ 10⁶ .
```

**Proof.**  
Initially `S = 0`.  
After the first processed vector `v₁` we have `|S| = |v₁|`.  
Assume after processing some vectors we have `|S| ≤ max_i |v_i|`.  
Processing the next vector `v` we use the sign from Lemma&nbsp;1, therefore
`|S|` does **not increase**. Hence the invariant is preserved. ∎



##### Lemma 3  
`max_i |v_i| ≤ 10⁶   ⇒   |S| ≤ 1.5·10⁶` at the end of the algorithm.

**Proof.** Directly from Lemma&nbsp;2. ∎



##### Theorem  
The algorithm outputs a sequence `c₁ … c_n` such that  

```
p = Σ c_i v_i ,      |p| ≤ 1.5·10⁶ .
```

**Proof.**  
The algorithm produces the signs `c_i`.  
By Lemma&nbsp;2 the distance of the intermediate sum never exceeds `10⁶`,
hence the final sum `p` also satisfies this bound, which is stricter than the
required `1.5·10⁶`. ∎



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 4.  Complexity Analysis  

*Sorting* – `O(n log n)`  
*Processing* – `O(n)`  
Memory – the vectors and the answer, `O(n)`.



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 5.  Reference Implementation  (Python 3)

```python
import sys

def solve() -> None:
    data = sys.stdin.read().strip().split()
    if not data:
        return
    it = iter(data)
    n = int(next(it))
    vec = []
    for i in range(n):
        x = int(next(it)); y = int(next(it))
        vec.append((x, y, i))          # keep original index

    # any order works, we use decreasing length for determinism
    vec.sort(key=lambda t: -(t[0] * t[0] + t[1] * t[1]))

    sx = sy = 0                     # current sum
    ans = [0] * n

    for x, y, idx in vec:
        dot = sx * x + sy * y
        if dot > 0:          # use -v
            c = -1
            sx -= x
            sy -= y
        else:                # use +v
            c = 1
            sx += x
            sy += y
        ans[idx] = c

    # output
    out = ' '.join(str(c) for c in ans)
    sys.stdout.write(out)

if __name__ == "__main__":
    solve()
```

The program follows exactly the algorithm proven correct above and conforms to the required
input‑output format.
completed 131/150, Solved: False, number of success = 40/131, acc = 30.53
**Solution Explanation**

For the `n` different positions let  

```
p1 < p2 < … < pn          (sorted coordinates)
```

`n` is even, therefore  

```
moves of Vova   = moves of Lesha = (n‑2)/2
```

During the whole game exactly `n‑2` positions are banned, the two
positions that stay on the line are the starting places of the
warrior (the smaller one) and the archer (the larger one).

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 1.   From the game to a combinatorial problem  

*Vova* makes his bans first, then *Lesha* reacts.
Think of it as two steps:

1. Vova chooses a set `A` of `(n‑2)/2` positions and bans them.
2. From the remaining `n‑(n‑2)/2 = n/2 + 1` positions Lesha bans
   another `(n‑2)/2` positions.

After step&nbsp;2 only two positions are left.
Because Lesha wants the distance to be as large as possible,
he will keep the two **most distant** positions among the
`n/2+1` positions that survived Vova’s bans.
Consequently the final distance equals the *diameter* (max – min)
of the set that survived Vova’s bans.

So Vova’s problem is:

*choose a subset of size*  

```
k = n/2 + 1
```

*such that its diameter is as small as possible.*

If Vova succeeds with diameter `D`, Lesha cannot obtain a larger
distance – the two remaining points are inside an interval of length
`D`.  
If Vova cannot keep the diameter ≤ `D`, Lesha can force a distance
> `D`.  
Therefore the answer of the original game is exactly the minimal
possible diameter of a `k`‑element subset.

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 2.   Minimal diameter of a `k`‑element subset  

All coordinates are distinct, after sorting they are monotone.
For a fixed subset its diameter is `max – min`, i.e. the distance
between its leftmost and rightmost element.
If a subset is **not** a consecutive block in the sorted order,
there is a gap inside it; moving the elements that lie after the gap
leftwards until the gap disappears never enlarges the diameter.
Hence an optimal subset is always a consecutive block of length `k`.

Thus we only have to examine every window of `k` consecutive points
and take the smallest difference between its last and first element.

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 3.   Algorithm
```
read n and the list x
sort x
k = n//2 + 1
ans = +∞
for i = 0 … n-k
        ans = min( ans , x[i+k-1] - x[i] )
print ans
```

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 4.   Correctness Proof  

We prove that the algorithm outputs the distance obtained when both
players play optimally.

---

##### Lemma 1  
After Vova’s bans exactly `k = n/2+1` positions remain.
Lesha can force the final distance to be the diameter of those
`k` positions.

**Proof.**  
Lesha still has to ban `(n‑2)/2` positions, i.e. all but two of the
`k` survivors of Vova.  
He may delete any two positions he wishes to keep, therefore he can
keep the leftmost and the rightmost of the `k` points.
Their distance is the diameter, and no larger distance is possible
because all remaining points lie inside that interval. ∎



##### Lemma 2  
For a fixed `k` the minimum possible diameter of a `k`‑element subset
of the sorted array `p1 … pn` equals  

```
min_{0 ≤ i ≤ n-k} ( p_{i+k-1} - p_i )
```

**Proof.**  
Take an arbitrary optimal subset `S`.  
Let `a = min S`, `b = max S`. All elements of `S` lie inside `[a,b]`.
If `S` is not a consecutive block, there exists an index `j`
with `p_j ∈ S` and `p_{j+1} ∉ S` while some later element `p_t ∈ S`
with `t>j+1`. Replacing `p_t` by `p_{j+1}` does not increase `b-a`
(because `p_{j+1} ≤ p_t`). Repeating this operation removes all gaps,
producing a consecutive block with the same diameter.
Consequently an optimal subset can be taken as a block,
and its diameter is exactly the difference of its last and first
elements. Scanning all blocks gives the formula. ∎



##### Lemma 3  
The algorithm returns the minimum possible diameter of a `k`‑element
subset.

**Proof.**  
The algorithm evaluates `p_{i+k-1} - p_i` for every `i` from `0` to
`n-k` and keeps the smallest value.
By Lemma&nbsp;2 this value is precisely the minimum achievable
diameter. ∎



##### Lemma 4  
If the algorithm outputs `D`, Vova has a strategy guaranteeing a final
distance ≤ `D`, and Lesha cannot force a distance larger than `D`.

**Proof.**  
Vova bans all positions **outside** the block that attains the
minimum in Lemma&nbsp;3.  
Exactly `n-k = (n-2)/2` positions are removed, which is Vova’s allowed
number of bans.  
The remaining `k` positions have diameter `D`.  
By Lemma&nbsp;1 Lesha’s best possible result is this diameter, i.e.
`≤ D`. ∎



##### Lemma 5  
If the algorithm outputs `D`, no strategy of Vova can guarantee a final
distance `< D`.

**Proof.**  
Assume Vova could force a distance `< D`.  
Then after his bans the `k` surviving positions would have diameter
`< D`.  
But Lemma&nbsp;3 states that the smallest possible diameter of any
`k`‑element subset is exactly `D`. Contradiction. ∎



##### Theorem  
The number printed by the algorithm equals the distance between the
warrior and the archer when both players play optimally.

**Proof.**  
From Lemma&nbsp;4 Vova can ensure a distance not larger than the
algorithm’s output `D`, and Lesha cannot make it larger.
From Lemma&nbsp;5 Vova cannot guarantee a smaller distance.
Therefore the game value is exactly `D`, which the algorithm prints. ∎



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 5.   Complexity Analysis  

*Sorting* – `O(n log n)`  
*Scanning the windows* – `O(n)`  

Memory consumption – the sorted array, `O(n)` integers.

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 6.   Reference Implementation  (Python 3)

```python
import sys

def solve() -> None:
    data = sys.stdin.read().strip().split()
    if not data:
        return
    n = int(data[0])
    xs = list(map(int, data[1:]))
    xs.sort()
    k = n // 2 + 1                     # size of the set that survives Vova's bans
    best = 10 ** 18
    for i in range(n - k + 1):
        cur = xs[i + k - 1] - xs[i]
        if cur < best:
            best = cur
    print(best)

if __name__ == "__main__":
    solve()
```

The program follows exactly the algorithm proven correct above
and conforms to the required input‑output format.
completed 132/150, Solved: True, number of success = 41/132, acc = 31.06
**Solution Explanation**

For the `2·n` numbers we only know that they are the `x`‑coordinates of `n`
points and the `y`‑coordinates of the same `n` points, but we do not know
which number belongs to which coordinate.
We have to split the multiset into two groups of size `n`

* group **X** – the `x`‑coordinates,
* group **Y** – the `y`‑coordinates.

If  

```
xmin = min X ,   xmax = max X
ymin = min Y ,   ymax = max Y
```

the photographed rectangle is  

```
[xmin , xmax] × [ymin , ymax]                (sides are parallel to axes)
```

and its area is  

```
area = (xmax – xmin) · (ymax – ymin)
```

Only the smallest and the largest element of each group influence the
area, the remaining `n‑2` elements are irrelevant.



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 1.   Geometry of an optimal partition  

Sort all numbers

```
a[0] ≤ a[1] ≤ … ≤ a[2n‑1]
```

Consider any optimal partition `S` (size `n`) and its complement `T`.
Let  

```
xmin = a[l] ,   xmax = a[r]          ( l ≤ r ,  a[l] , a[r] ∈ S )
```

All elements of `S` lie between positions `l` and `r` (maybe not all of
them, some of them can belong to `T`).  
If there is an element `a[k]` with `l < k < r` that belongs to `T`,
move it from `T` to `S` (and move any element of `S` that is outside the
interval `[l , r]` to `T`).  
The interval `[l , r]` – therefore the range of `S` – does **not**
increase, while the range of `T` can only become smaller.
Consequently the product of the two ranges never grows.

Repeating this operation we obtain another optimal partition in which
**one of the groups consists of a *contiguous* block of the sorted
array**.  
The other group is then the union of the left part and the right part
(the “prefix” and the “suffix”).

So it is sufficient to try all possibilities where the contiguous block
has length `n`.  
If the block starts at position `i (0 ≤ i ≤ n)` it contains

```
a[i] , a[i+1] , … , a[i+n‑1]
```

*range of the block*  

```
rx = a[i+n‑1] – a[i]
```

The remaining `n` numbers are

```
a[0 … i‑1]   (left part, possibly empty)
a[i+n … 2n‑1] (right part, possibly empty)
```

*range of the remaining numbers*  

```
ry = max( a[2n‑1] , a[i‑1] ) – min( a[0] , a[i+n] )
```

(the two `max / min` are taken only from the existing part).

The answer is the minimum value of `rx·ry` over all `i = 0 … n`.



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 2.   Algorithm
```
read n
read the 2·n numbers into array a
sort a

ans = +∞
for i = 0 … n
        rx = a[i+n-1] - a[i]

        if i == 0          # left part empty
                minY = a[i+n]
        else
                minY = a[0]

        if i+n == 2*n      # right part empty
                maxY = a[i-1]
        else
                maxY = a[2*n-1]

        ry = maxY - minY
        ans = min( ans , rx * ry )
output ans
```

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 3.   Correctness Proof  

We prove that the algorithm outputs the minimal possible area.

---

##### Lemma 1  
For any optimal partition there exists an optimal partition in which
one of the two groups is a contiguous block of the sorted array.

**Proof.**  
Take an optimal partition `S , T`.  
Let `l = min index of an element of S`, `r = max index of an element of S`.  
If some index `k` with `l < k < r` belongs to `T`, exchange the element
`a[k]` with any element of `S` that lies outside `[l , r]` (such an
element exists because `|S| = n` and the interval already contains at
least `r‑l+1 ≥ n` positions).  
The new set `S'` still has size `n`. Its minimum is still `a[l]`,
its maximum is still `a[r]`; therefore its range does not increase.
The range of `T'` cannot be larger than the range of `T` because we
removed a point that was inside the interval `[l , r]` and possibly
added a point outside it.  
Thus the product of the two ranges does not increase, the new partition
is also optimal.  
Repeating the exchange while such a “hole’’ exists finally yields an
optimal partition where all elements of one group lie inside a single
contiguous interval. ∎



##### Lemma 2  
If a group is a contiguous block `a[i] … a[i+n‑1]` then the range of the
other group equals  

```
maxY - minY   with
minY = ( i==0 ? a[i+n] : a[0] )
maxY = ( i+n==2n ? a[i-1] : a[2n-1] )
```

**Proof.**  
The other group consists of the prefix `a[0 … i‑1]` (empty if `i=0`) and
the suffix `a[i+n … 2n‑1]` (empty if `i+n = 2n`).  
The smallest element among them is the first element of the existing
part, i.e. `a[0]` if the prefix exists, otherwise `a[i+n]`.  
Analogously the largest element is `a[2n‑1]` if the suffix exists,
otherwise `a[i‑1]`. ∎



##### Lemma 3  
For every `i (0 ≤ i ≤ n)` the algorithm computes the area of the
rectangle belonging to the optimal partition described in Lemma&nbsp;1
with the contiguous block starting at `i`.

**Proof.**  
The algorithm uses exactly the formulas of Lemma&nbsp;2 for `minY` and
`maxY`, and `rx = a[i+n‑1]‑a[i]` for the range of the block.
Therefore the product `rx·ry` equals the area of that partition. ∎



##### Lemma 4  
The minimal area among all possible partitions equals  

```
min_{0 ≤ i ≤ n}  ( a[i+n-1] - a[i] ) · ( maxY(i) - minY(i) )
```

**Proof.**  
*Upper bound*: for each `i` the partition from Lemma&nbsp;1 exists,
hence the minimum over all `i` is attainable.

*Lower bound*: by Lemma&nbsp;1 every optimal partition can be transformed
into a partition whose one group is a contiguous block.
Such a block must start at some `i (0 ≤ i ≤ n)`.  
Consequently the optimal area is at least the minimum over all those
`i`. ∎



##### Theorem  
The algorithm outputs the minimal possible area of a rectangle that can
contain all `n` favourite stars.

**Proof.**  
By Lemma&nbsp;3 the algorithm evaluates exactly the expression of
Lemma&nbsp;4 for every admissible `i` and stores the smallest value.
Lemma&nbsp;4 states that this smallest value equals the optimum over all
partitions. Hence the printed number is the required minimal area. ∎



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 4.   Complexity Analysis  

*Sorting* `2·n` numbers: `O(n log n)`  
*Loop* over `i = 0 … n`: `O(n)`  

Memory consumption: the sorted array of length `2·n` → `O(n)`.



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 5.   Reference Implementation  (Python 3)

```python
import sys

def solve() -> None:
    data = sys.stdin.read().strip().split()
    if not data:
        return
    it = iter(data)
    n = int(next(it))
    a = [int(next(it)) for _ in range(2 * n)]
    a.sort()
    total = 2 * n
    INF = 10 ** 30
    ans = INF

    for i in range(0, n + 1):
        # range of the contiguous block (size n)
        rx = a[i + n - 1] - a[i]

        # min and max of the remaining numbers
        if i == 0:                     # left part empty
            minY = a[i + n]
        else:
            minY = a[0]

        if i + n == total:             # right part empty
            maxY = a[i - 1]
        else:
            maxY = a[-1]

        ry = maxY - minY
        area = rx * ry
        if area < ans:
            ans = area

    print(ans)


if __name__ == "__main__":
    solve()
```

The program follows exactly the algorithm proven correct above and
conforms to the required input‑output format.
completed 133/150, Solved: True, number of success = 42/133, acc = 31.58
**Solution Explanation**

For every species `i ( 1 ≤ i ≤ k )`

* `a[i]` – number of fishes of this species caught by Alice  
* `b[i]` – number of fishes of this species caught by Bob  

`a[i] , b[i]` are non‑negative integers, only `n+m ≤ 2·10⁵` of them are non‑zero.

The (unknown) weight of a fish of species `i` is `w[i]`  

```
0 < w[1] ≤ w[2] ≤ … ≤ w[k]                (1)
```

Alice’s total weight      `A = Σ a[i]·w[i]`
