Bob’s   total weight      `B = Σ b[i]·w[i]`

We have to decide whether there exist numbers `w[i]` satisfying (1) such that  

```
A > B   ⇔   Σ (a[i] – b[i])·w[i]  > 0                (2)
```

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 1.   Reformulation

Define  

```
d[i] = a[i] – b[i]          (can be negative)
```

The problem becomes:

*choose a non‑decreasing positive vector `w` so that*  

```
Σ d[i]·w[i]  > 0                                    (3)
```

Only the signs of the `d[i]` matter, the exact values of `a[i] , b[i]`
are irrelevant.

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 2.   Geometry of the feasible region  

Constraints on `w`

```
w[i] ≥ w[i‑1]      (i = 2 … k)
w[i] > 0
```

There is **no upper bound** for any `w[i]`.  
The feasible set is a convex polyhedron described by linear inequalities,
hence the maximum of the linear function (3) is attained at an **extreme
point** of this polyhedron.

Because the only restriction is “non‑decreasing”, an extreme point has the
following shape:

* all first `t` components are equal to some small positive value `ε`,
* all remaining components (`t+1 … k`) are equal to a (possibly infinite)
  large value `M`.

Indeed, if a component is strictly larger than its predecessor but not
forced to be infinite, we could decrease it a little and stay feasible –
so it would not be an extreme point.

Therefore it is sufficient to consider weight vectors of the form  

```
w[1] = … = w[t] = ε          (ε > 0, can be arbitrarily small)
w[t+1] = … = w[k] = M        (M > 0, can be arbitrarily large)
```

Plugging this into (3) gives  

```
Σ_{i≤t} d[i]·ε  +  Σ_{i>t} d[i]·M   > 0
```

*If `Σ_{i>t} d[i] > 0` we can choose `M` huge and `ε` tiny – the inequality
holds.*

*If `Σ_{i>t} d[i] = 0` the inequality reduces to `Σ_{i≤t} d[i]·ε > 0`,
which is possible exactly when `Σ_{i≤t} d[i] > 0` (take any ε).*

Consequently **the sign of a suffix sum decides the answer**.

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 3.   Necessary and sufficient condition  

Let  

```
S(t) = Σ_{i = t … k} d[i]          (suffix sum, 1 ≤ t ≤ k)
```

The following statements are equivalent  

* there exist weights satisfying (3)  
* `max_{t} S(t)  > 0`  

Proof sketch  

*If* `max S(t) > 0` – pick the smallest `t` with `S(t) > 0`.  
Set `w[1..t‑1] = ε`, `w[t..k] = M` with `M` large, `ε` tiny → (3) holds.

*Only if* there are feasible weights, consider an extreme point described
above. Its inequality can be true only when the corresponding suffix sum is
positive, otherwise the left side is ≤ 0 for any choice of `ε , M`. ∎



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 4.   Computing the maximal suffix sum  

`k` can be as large as `10⁹`, but only at most `n+m ≤ 2·10⁵` species appear
in the input. For all other species `d[i] = 0`, they do not influence any
suffix sum – the maximum is always attained at a species that actually
appears.

Algorithm  

```
for each fish type x of Alice : diff[x] += 1
for each fish type x of Bob   : diff[x] -= 1          // diff = d

keys = all distinct types in diff, sorted decreasing
suffix = 0
max_suffix = 0
for key in keys (from largest to smallest):
        suffix += diff[key]          // now suffix = S(key)
        max_suffix = max(max_suffix, suffix)

answer = (max_suffix > 0)
```

Complexities  

* building the map – `O(n+m)`  
* sorting the distinct keys – `O(p log p)` where `p ≤ n+m`  
* linear scan – `O(p)`

Overall `O((n+m) log (n+m))` time, `O(p)` memory.

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 5.   Correctness Proof  

We prove that the algorithm prints **YES** iff the required weights exist.

---

##### Lemma 1  
For any weight vector `w` satisfying (1) we have  

```
Σ d[i]·w[i] ≤ max_{t} S(t) .
```

**Proof.**  
Because `w` is non‑decreasing, let `t` be the smallest index with the
maximum weight `M = w[t]`. All earlier components are ≤ `M`, denote the
minimum weight among them by `ε = w[1]` (ε > 0). Then

```
Σ d[i]·w[i] = Σ_{i≤t-1} d[i]·w[i] + Σ_{i≥t} d[i]·M
            ≤ Σ_{i≤t-1} d[i]·M + Σ_{i≥t} d[i]·M
            = M· Σ_{i≥t} d[i] = M·S(t) .
```

Since `M > 0`, the sign of the left side is not larger than the sign of
`S(t)`. Hence the value cannot exceed the largest suffix sum. ∎



##### Lemma 2  
If `max_{t} S(t) > 0` then there exist weights satisfying (3).

**Proof.**  
Choose `t*` with `S(t*) = max_{t} S(t) > 0`.  
Take any `ε > 0` (e.g. `1`) and any `M > 0` large enough such that  

```
M·S(t*)  >  ε·| Σ_{i<t*} d[i] | .
```

Define  

```
w[i] = ε   for i < t*
w[i] = M    for i ≥ t* .
```

The vector is non‑decreasing and positive. Its total weight is  

```
Σ d[i]·w[i] = ε· Σ_{i<t*} d[i] + M·S(t*)  > 0 .
```

Thus (3) holds. ∎



##### Lemma 3  
If `max_{t} S(t) ≤ 0` then no weights satisfy (3).

**Proof.**  
Assume contrary that some feasible `w` makes Σ d[i]·w[i] > 0.
Let `t` be the first index where `w[t]` attains its maximum value `M`.
All later components are also `M`. By Lemma&nbsp;1  

```
Σ d[i]·w[i] ≤ max_{s} S(s) = max_{s} S(s) ≤ 0 ,
```

contradiction. ∎



##### Theorem  
The algorithm outputs **YES** iff there exist positive non‑decreasing
weights `w₁ … w_k` with Alice’s total weight strictly larger than Bob’s.

**Proof.**  

*If* the algorithm prints **YES**, then `max_suffix > 0`.  
By Lemma&nbsp;2 a suitable weight vector exists.

*If* the algorithm prints **NO**, then `max_suffix ≤ 0`.  
By Lemma&nbsp;3 no weight vector can make Alice’s total larger.

Thus the output is correct in both directions. ∎



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 6.   Reference Implementation  (Python 3)

```python
import sys
from collections import defaultdict

def solve() -> None:
    data = sys.stdin.read().strip().split()
    if not data:
        return
    it = iter(data)
    n = int(next(it))
    m = int(next(it))
    k = int(next(it))          # k is not needed further

    diff = defaultdict(int)   # d[i] = a[i] - b[i]

    for _ in range(n):
        x = int(next(it))
        diff[x] += 1
    for _ in range(m):
        x = int(next(it))
        diff[x] -= 1

    # distinct species that actually appear
    keys = sorted(diff.keys(), reverse=True)

    suffix = 0
    max_suffix = 0
    for key in keys:
        suffix += diff[key]          # now suffix = Σ_{i >= key} d[i]
        if suffix > max_suffix:
            max_suffix = suffix

    print("YES" if max_suffix > 0 else "NO")

if __name__ == "__main__":
    solve()
```

The program follows exactly the algorithm proven correct above
and conforms to the required input‑output format.
completed 134/150, Solved: True, number of success = 43/134, acc = 32.09
**Solution Explanation**

For every road its travelling time is either `a` or `b` (`a < b`).

For a set of roads we have to satisfy three conditions  

* the roads are connected – they form a spanning tree,
* the sum of the travelling times of the kept roads is minimum,
* among all spanning trees with the minimum sum the distance from settlement `1`
  to the considered settlement `p` is minimum.

For every `p ( 1 … n )` we have to output that minimum possible distance.
The answer for different `p` may use different spanning trees.



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 1.   What does a minimum‑weight spanning tree look like ?

All `a`‑roads are cheaper than every `b`‑road, therefore a minimum weight
spanning tree uses as many `a`‑roads as possible.
If we give every `a`‑road weight `0` and every `b`‑road weight `1`,
the usual minimum spanning tree algorithm (Kruskal) will first take all
possible `a`‑roads, then the smallest possible number of `b`‑roads.
Consequently

* let the graph consisting only of `a`‑roads have `c` connected components,
* any minimum weight spanning tree contains **exactly `c‑1` `b`‑roads**,
  all other `n‑1-(c‑1)` roads are `a`‑roads.

Inside one `a`‑component we may keep any spanning tree made of `a`‑roads,
and between the components we may keep any spanning tree made of `b`‑roads.
All those trees have the same total weight, they are precisely the
minimum‑weight spanning trees.



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 2.   From a component to another component

Inside one `a`‑component all roads have the same weight `a`.
If we want to travel from vertex `x` to vertex `y` **inside the same component**
the cheapest possible distance is  

```
a  ×  (shortest number of a‑roads between x and y)
```

because we can always keep a spanning tree that contains a shortest
(unweighted) `a`‑path – a tree can contain any simple path.

When we have to leave a component we must use a `b`‑road.
A `b`‑road connects two different `a`‑components, therefore a whole
path from `1` to `p` looks like

```
a‑edges inside component of 1,
b‑edge to another component,
a‑edges inside that component,
b‑edge to another component,
...
a‑edges inside component of p.
```

The whole path uses some number `k` of `b`‑edges.
Because a minimum spanning tree contains **exactly `c‑1` `b`‑edges**, the
path may use **at most `c‑1` `b`‑edges** – the remaining `b`‑edges can be
placed elsewhere to finish the tree.

*If a path uses at most `c‑1` `b`‑edges, it can always be extended to a
minimum‑weight spanning tree.*  
Indeed, the `c` components together with the used `b`‑edges form a connected
subgraph of the component graph; we can add any spanning tree of the
remaining components (still using only `b`‑edges) and obtain exactly `c‑1`
`b`‑edges in total.



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 3.   The real optimisation problem

For every vertex `p` we have to find the cheapest `1 → p` path

* edge costs are `a` (for an `a`‑road) and `b` (for a `b`‑road),
* the path may contain **at most `K = c‑1` `b`‑edges**.

That is a shortest‑path problem with an additional resource
(the number of used `b`‑edges).  
`n ≤ 70`, `K ≤ n‑1 ≤ 69`, therefore a state graph of size `n·(K+1)` is tiny.



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 4.   Dijkstra on the state graph  

State: `(v , k)` – we are in vertex `v` after having used exactly `k`
`b`‑edges (`0 ≤ k ≤ K`).  

Transition over an edge `v – u`

```
if edge is a‑road:
        (v , k)  →  (u , k)   with additional cost a
else (edge is b‑road):
        (v , k)  →  (u , k+1) with additional cost b   (only if k+1 ≤ K)
```

All edge costs are positive, therefore ordinary Dijkstra works.
Complexity  

```
states   :  n·(K+1)   ≤ 70·70 = 4900
edges    :  m·(K+1)   ≤ 200·70 = 14000
time     :  O( (n·K + m·K) log (n·K) )   < 10⁵ operations
memory   :  O( n·K )
```

After Dijkstra finishes

```
answer[p] = min_{0 ≤ k ≤ K} dist[p][k]
```

`answer[1] = 0` (zero distance to itself).

The algorithm is executed once, the whole answer array is printed.



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 5.   Correctness Proof  

We prove that the algorithm outputs the required minimum distances.

---

##### Lemma 1  
Let the graph formed by all `a`‑roads have `c` connected components.
Every minimum‑weight spanning tree contains exactly `c‑1` `b`‑roads,
and any spanning tree with exactly `c‑1` `b`‑roads is minimum‑weight.

**Proof.**  
Give each `a`‑road weight `0` and each `b`‑road weight `1`.  
Kruskal first adds all possible `0`‑weight edges, i.e. a maximal forest of
`a`‑roads – this forest has `c` components.
To connect those components we need at least `c‑1` edges of weight `1`,
and Kruskal adds exactly `c‑1` of them.
Thus any spanning tree with fewer than `c‑1` `b`‑roads cannot be spanning,
and any spanning tree with more than `c‑1` `b`‑roads has larger total
weight (`b-a > 0`). ∎



##### Lemma 2  
Inside a single `a`‑component the smallest possible distance between two
vertices `x , y` that can appear in a minimum‑weight spanning tree equals  

```
a × (shortest number of a‑edges between x and y).
```

**Proof.**  
All `a`‑edges have the same weight `a`.  
Take a shortest (fewest edges) `a`‑path `P` from `x` to `y`.  
Add all other `a`‑edges of the component that are not in `P` arbitrarily,
keeping the graph acyclic – this yields a spanning tree of the component
that contains `P`.  
Consequently the distance inside the tree is exactly `a·|P|`.  
No tree can give a smaller distance because every `a`‑edge costs at least
`a`. ∎



##### Lemma 3  
For any vertex `p` and any integer `k (0 ≤ k ≤ c‑1)`  
the value `dist[p][k]` computed by Dijkstra equals the minimum possible
total travelling time of a `1 → p` walk that uses exactly `k` `b`‑edges
(and any number of `a`‑edges).

**Proof.**  
The state graph exactly represents all such walks:
the cost of a transition equals the travelling time of the used road,
and the second component of the state counts how many `b`‑edges have been
used.  
All edge costs are non‑negative, therefore Dijkstra yields the shortest
path cost for every state, i.e. the minimum total time among all walks
with the prescribed number `k` of `b`‑edges. ∎



##### Lemma 4  
If a `1 → p` walk uses at most `c‑1` `b`‑edges, it can be extended to a
minimum‑weight spanning tree.

**Proof.**  
Let the walk use `k ≤ c‑1` `b`‑edges and visit the set `S` of `a`‑components
that appear on the walk.  
Inside each visited component we keep the `a`‑edges of the walk
(Lemma&nbsp;2 guarantees they can be part of a spanning tree of that
component).  
The `k` used `b`‑edges connect the components of `S` into a tree
(because the walk is simple in the component graph).  
The component graph has `c` vertices, therefore we still have to connect
the remaining `c‑|S|` components.  
Because the whole component graph is connected, we can add any spanning
tree of the remaining components, using exactly `(c‑|S|) – 1` additional
`b`‑edges.  
Total number of `b`‑edges becomes  

```
k + (c‑|S|) – 1 = c‑1 .
```

Thus we obtain a spanning tree with exactly `c‑1` `b`‑edges, i.e. a
minimum‑weight spanning tree (Lemma&nbsp;1). ∎



##### Lemma 5  
For every vertex `p` the algorithm outputs  

```
min   total travelling time of a 1 → p path
over all minimum‑weight spanning trees.
```

**Proof.**  
*Upper bound.*  
Take an optimal minimum‑weight spanning tree `T`.  
The unique `1 → p` path in `T` uses some `k ≤ c‑1` `b`‑edges
(Lemma&nbsp;1).  
By Lemma&nbsp;3 `dist[p][k]` is not larger than the cost of that path,
hence `answer[p] = min_k dist[p][k]` is ≤ the optimum.

*Lower bound.*  
Let `k*` be the value that gives `answer[p]`.  
`dist[p][k*]` is the cost of a walk using exactly `k*` `b`‑edges
(Lemma&nbsp;3).  
Because `k* ≤ c‑1`, Lemma&nbsp;4 tells us that this walk can be extended
to a minimum‑weight spanning tree `T*`.  
The `1 → p` path inside `T*` has exactly the same cost as the walk,
therefore the optimum is ≤ `answer[p]`.

Both bounds coincide, so `answer[p]` equals the required optimum. ∎



##### Theorem  
The program prints, for every settlement `p = 1 … n`,
the minimum possible travelling time from settlement `1` to `p`
after abandoning roads according to the three conditions of the problem.

**Proof.**  
The program

1. finds the number `c` of connected components formed by `a`‑roads,
   computes `K = c‑1`,
2. runs Dijkstra on the state graph described above,
3. outputs `answer[p] = min_{0≤k≤K} dist[p][k]`.

By Lemma&nbsp;5 each `answer[p]` equals the optimum defined in the
statement. ∎



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 6.   Complexity Analysis  

```
n ≤ 70 ,   m ≤ 200 ,   K = c‑1 ≤ n‑1 ≤ 69
states          : (K+1)·n   ≤ 4900
edges in state graph : (K+1)·m ≤ 14000
Dijkstra time   : O( (n·K + m·K) log (n·K) )   < 1·10⁵
Memory          : O( n·K )                     ≤ 5·10³
```

Both limits are easily satisfied.



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 7.   Reference Implementation  (Python 3)

```python
import sys
import heapq

def solve() -> None:
    data = sys.stdin.read().strip().split()
    if not data:
        return
    it = iter(data)
    n = int(next(it))
    m = int(next(it))
    a = int(next(it))
    b = int(next(it))

    # adjacency list: (neighbor, is_b)
    adj = [[] for _ in range(n + 1)]

    # DSU for a‑components
    parent = list(range(n + 1))

    def find(x):
        while parent[x] != x:
            parent[x] = parent[parent[x]]
            x = parent[x]
        return x

    def union(x, y):
        rx, ry = find(x), find(y)
        if rx != ry:
            parent[ry] = rx

    for _ in range(m):
        u = int(next(it))
        v = int(next(it))
        c = int(next(it))
        is_b = (c == b)
        adj[u].append((v, is_b))
        adj[v].append((u, is_b))
        if not is_b:          # a‑edge
            union(u, v)

    # number of a‑components
    comps = set()
    for v in range(1, n + 1):
        comps.add(find(v))
    c_cnt = len(comps)
    K = c_cnt - 1                     # maximal number of b‑edges allowed

    INF = 10**18
    dist = [[INF] * (K + 1) for _ in range(n + 1)]
    dist[1][0] = 0
    pq = [(0, 1, 0)]                  # (cost, vertex, used_b)

    while pq:
        cur, v, used = heapq.heappop(pq)
        if cur != dist[v][used]:
            continue
        for to, is_b in adj[v]:
            nused = used + (1 if is_b else 0)
            if nused > K:
                continue
            w = b if is_b else a
            nd = cur + w
            if nd < dist[to][nused]:
                dist[to][nused] = nd
                heapq.heappush(pq, (nd, to, nused))

    out = []
    for p in range(1, n + 1):
        best = min(dist[p])
        out.append(str(best))
    sys.stdout.write(' '.join(out))

if __name__ == "__main__":
    solve()
```

The program follows exactly the algorithm proven correct above
and conforms to the required input‑output format.
completed 135/150, Solved: True, number of success = 44/135, acc = 32.59
**Solution Explanation**

For every doll we know only one thing – it can contain at most one other doll.
Therefore every chain is a simple line  

```
inner … → a2 → a1          (a1 is the outermost doll of the chain)
```

The allowed operations  

* **put** – we may put a whole *root* chain `A` into a *single* empty root doll `B`  
  (`A` is not inside any other doll, `B` does not contain anything and also is not
  inside another doll).

* **take out** – we may take the doll which is directly inside the outermost doll
  of a chain.  
  After the operation the outermost doll becomes a single empty doll again.

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 1.   What has to be done in the end ?

The required final configuration is

```
1 → 2 → 3 → … → n                (1 is inside 2, 2 inside 3, …)
```

The outermost doll is `n`.  
The only way to build such a chain with the allowed operations is

```
start with the chain (1)                – already a single doll
put it into 2   →   chain (1,2)
put it into 3   →   chain (1,2,3)
…
put it into n   →   chain (1,…,n)
```

So after all dolls are *single* we need exactly `n‑1` **put** operations.

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 2.   How many **take out** operations are necessary ?

If a chain contains `m` dolls we have to take out `m‑1` inner dolls to split it
into single dolls.
For the whole initial configuration

```
total_take_out = Σ (mi – 1) = ( Σ mi ) – k = n – k                (1)
```

(`k` – number of initial chains).

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 3.   Can we keep a part of the initial configuration ?

Assume there exists a chain

```
1 → 2 → … → cnt                (cnt ≥ 1)
```

and **nothing else** is inside `cnt`.  
Then we may keep this whole chain, we do **not** have to separate the
`cnt‑1` inner connections.
All other dolls are still split into single pieces.

* saved take‑out operations = `cnt‑1`
* remaining take‑out operations  

```
take_out = (n – k) – (cnt – 1) = n – k – cnt + 1                (2)
```

* how many puts are still necessary ?

The kept chain already ends with `cnt`.  
Now we put this whole chain into `cnt+1`,
after that into `cnt+2`, …, finally into `n`.

```
puts = n – cnt                                                   (3)
```

*total operations* for this case  

```
answer = take_out + puts
        = (n – k – cnt + 1) + (n – cnt)
        = 2·n – k – 2·cnt + 1                                   (4)
```

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 4.   When is such a chain possible ?

All numbers are distinct and inside each input chain the numbers are already
strictly increasing.
Therefore a chain that starts with `1` and contains only consecutive numbers
must be exactly

```
1 , 2 , 3 , … , m
```

If the first element of a chain is `1` and every element equals its position
(`a[i] = i+1`), then this chain can be kept and `cnt = m`.
Otherwise **no** part can be kept (`cnt = 0`).

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 5.   Final formula

```
if cnt > 0 :   answer = 2·n – k – 2·cnt + 1
else        :   answer = 2·n – k – 1          (all dolls must be split)
```

The formula also works for the already correct whole chain:
`cnt = n , k = 1  →  answer = 0`.

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 6.   Algorithm
```
read n , k
cnt = 0
repeat k times
        read m and the list a[0 … m-1]
        if a[0] == 1 and a[m-1] == m and for all i : a[i] == i+1
                cnt = m                # this is the only possible chain
if cnt > 0
        ans = 2*n - k - 2*cnt + 1
else
        ans = 2*n - k - 1
print ans
```

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 7.   Correctness Proof  

We prove that the algorithm outputs the minimal possible number of seconds.

---

##### Lemma 1  
If all dolls are single, the only way to obtain the final chain needs exactly
`n‑1` **put** operations.

**Proof.**  
A **put** operation always makes the second doll the new outermost one.
Starting with a single doll, after the first **put** the outermost doll is the
second one, after the second **put** the third one, … after `n‑1` puts the
outermost doll is `n`. No other sequence of allowed operations can increase the
length of a chain, therefore at least `n‑1` puts are necessary and the described
construction uses exactly `n‑1`. ∎



##### Lemma 2  
Splitting a chain of length `m` into single dolls needs exactly `m‑1`
**take out** operations, and this number is optimal.

**Proof.**  
Only a **take out** operation can decrease the length of a chain, and each
such operation removes exactly one inner doll. To reach length `1` we have to
remove `m‑1` inner dolls, therefore at least `m‑1` operations are required.
Performing them consecutively (always taking the doll directly inside the
outermost one) achieves the goal in `m‑1` steps, which is optimal. ∎



##### Lemma 3  
If there exists a chain `1 → 2 → … → cnt` and it contains no other dolls,
the minimum number of operations equals formula (4).

**Proof.**  

*Upper bound.*  
*   Keep this chain unchanged – we saved `cnt‑1` take‑outs.
*   All other chains are split into single dolls – by Lemma&nbsp;2 this costs
    `(n‑k) – (cnt‑1)` operations.
*   Afterwards we have the kept chain and `n‑cnt` single dolls.
    Put the whole kept chain into `cnt+1`, then into `cnt+2`, …, finally into `n`
    – exactly `n‑cnt` puts (Lemma&nbsp;1 with a starting chain of length `cnt`).

  Total = `(n‑k‑cnt+1) + (n‑cnt) = 2n‑k‑2cnt+1`.

*Lower bound.*  
Any solution must
*   separate all inner connections except possibly those inside the kept prefix,
    otherwise the final order would be wrong.
    Hence at least `(n‑k) – (cnt‑1)` take‑outs are necessary (Lemma&nbsp;2).
*   After all other dolls are single, the final chain must be built.
    Starting with a chain of length `cnt` we need at least `n‑cnt` puts
    (Lemma&nbsp;1 applied to the remaining `n‑cnt` dolls
completed 136/150, Solved: False, number of success = 44/136, acc = 32.35
**Solution Explanation**

For every word we have to know whether it can be transformed to another word by the
two rules  

* `u  ↔  oo`
* `h  ↔  kh`

The rules may be used any number of times, in both directions and on both
words.

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 1.   Observations

* The two rules never influence each other – one works only with the letters
  `u , o` and the other only with `h , k`.  
  Therefore we can treat them independently.

* **Rule `h ↔ kh`**  
  `kh` can be replaced by a single `h`.  
  The opposite direction only *adds* a `k` **in front of an `h`**.
  Consequently a `k` that stands immediately before an `h` is completely
  irrelevant – it can be deleted or inserted at will.
  After deleting **all** such `k`’s the word contains no substring `kh`
  any more and this form is unique.

* **Rule `u ↔ oo`**  
  Inside a maximal block that consists only of the letters `o` and `u`
  the order does **not** matter.
  Only the total “weight”

```
weight = (# of o) + 2·(# of u)
```

  is invariant, because `u → oo` adds two `o` and removes one `u`
  (weight unchanged) and `oo → u` does the opposite.
  Inside the block we may rearrange the letters arbitrarily,
  therefore every block can be replaced by a canonical one:

```
weight = 2·q + r   (r = 0 or 1)
canonical block =  q times 'u'  followed by  ( 'o' if r = 1 )
```

  This representation is unique.

* The two transformations are independent, therefore a **normal form**
  of a word can be built by

```
1. delete every 'k' that is directly before an 'h'   (rule h ↔ kh)
2. for each maximal o/u‑block replace it by the canonical block
```

Two original words are equivalent **iff** their normal forms are equal.

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 2.   Normalisation algorithm

```
normalize(word):
    # step 1 – delete all “k” before “h”
    stack = empty
    for each character c in word
        if c == 'h':
            while stack not empty and stack.top == 'k':
                  stack.pop()          # delete the preceding k
            stack.push('h')
        else:
            stack.push(c)             # any other character

    # now stack contains the word without any “kh”

    # step 2 – compress o/u blocks
    result = empty string
    i = 0
    while i < stack.length
        if stack[i] is 'o' or 'u':
            weight = 0
            while i < stack.length and stack[i] is 'o' or 'u':
                weight += 1 if stack[i] == 'o' else 2
                i += 1
            result += 'u' * (weight // 2)
            if weight % 2 == 1: result += 'o'
        else:
            result += stack[i]
            i += 1
    return result
```

The word length is at most `20`, therefore the algorithm works in `O(L)`
time (`L` – word length) and `O(L)` memory.

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 3.   Whole algorithm

```
read n
S = empty set
repeat n times
        read word
        S.add( normalize(word) )
print size of S
```

`n ≤ 400`, each word ≤ 20 characters → the program is easily fast enough.

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 4.   Correctness Proof  

We prove that the algorithm outputs the minimal possible number of groups,
i.e. the number of equivalence classes under the given rewrite rules.

---

##### Lemma 1  
After step&nbsp;1 (`kh` deletion) the obtained word contains no substring
`kh` and any word reachable from the original one by the rule `h ↔ kh`
has the same word after repeatedly deleting all `kh`.

**Proof.**  
The rule `kh → h` removes exactly one `k` that stands directly before an `h`.
Our procedure removes *all* such `k`’s, therefore the resulting word cannot
contain `kh`.  
Conversely, any application of `kh → h` can be simulated by our
procedure, because it also deletes that `k`.  
The opposite direction `h → kh` only inserts a `k` in front of an `h`;
later deletions will remove it again.  
Hence after performing deletions until impossible we obtain a unique
word that is reachable from any word of the same `h/kh`‑equivalence class. ∎



##### Lemma 2  
Consider a maximal block consisting only of letters `o` and `u`.
All words that can be obtained from this block by the rule `u ↔ oo`
have the same weight  

```
weight = #o + 2·#u .
```

**Proof.**  
Both elementary replacements change the numbers of `o` and `u` exactly as
described above, and the linear combination `#o + 2·#u` stays unchanged.
Therefore every reachable block has the same weight. ∎



##### Lemma 3  
For a fixed weight `w` all `o/u`‑blocks are mutually reachable.

**Proof.**  
Write `w = 2·q + r` (`r = 0` or `1`).  
The canonical block `U = 'u'*q + ('o' if r = 1 else '')` has weight `w`.  

*From any block to `U`*:  
Repeatedly replace any occurrence of `u` by `oo`. This only increases the
number of `o`’s, never creates new `u`. Afterwards the block consists solely
of `o`. Replace pairs of `o` by `u` as long as possible – exactly `q` times.
If one `o` is left (`r = 1`) we keep it. The result is `U`.

*From `U` to any block*: the reverse sequence of the above operations
produces any desired distribution of `o` and `u` with the same weight.

Thus all blocks with weight `w` are equivalent. ∎



##### Lemma 4  
Step&nbsp;2 of the algorithm replaces each `o/u`‑block by a **unique**
representative of its equivalence class.

**Proof.**  
By Lemma&nbsp;2 the weight of the block is invariant.
The algorithm computes this weight and outputs exactly the canonical block
described in Lemma&nbsp;3, which is the unique representative for this weight.
∎



##### Lemma 5  
Two original words are equivalent (by the given rewrite system) **iff**
their normal forms produced by the algorithm are identical.

**Proof.**  

*If the normal forms are equal*:  
Both words can be transformed to the same intermediate word:
first apply step 1 (delete all `kh`) – Lemma 1 guarantees that this is possible,
then apply step 2 (compress each `o/u` block) – Lemma 4 guarantees that each
block can be turned into the canonical one. Hence the two original words are
equivalent.

*If the words are equivalent*:  
A sequence of allowed replacements transforms one into the other.
All replacements are either of type `kh ↔ h` or `u ↔ oo`.  
Applying Lemma 1 to both words yields the same word without `kh`.  
Inside each `o/u`‑block the weight is preserved (Lemma 2), therefore after
compressing the blocks to the canonical form (Lemma 4) both words obtain the
same normal form. ∎



##### Lemma 6  
The set `S` built by the program contains exactly one element for each
equivalence class of the input words.

**Proof.**  
By Lemma 5 two words belong to the same class ⇔ their normal forms are equal.
The program inserts the normal form of every word into a set, which removes
duplicates. Consequently each class contributes exactly one element to `S`. ∎



##### Theorem  
The program outputs the minimal possible number of groups, i.e. the number of
different names among the given users.

**Proof.**  
By Lemma 6 the size of `S` equals the number of equivalence classes.
A grouping into fewer groups would put two words of different classes together,
which is impossible because they cannot be transformed into each other.
Thus the printed number is minimal and correct. ∎



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 5.   Complexity Analysis

*`L` – length of a word ( ≤ 20 )*  

* normalising one word: `O(L)` time, `O(L)` memory  
* processing all `n ≤ 400` words: `O(n·L) ≤ 8 000` operations  

Both limits are far below the given constraints.

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 6.   Reference Implementation  (Python 3)

```python
import sys

def normalize(word: str) -> str:
    """
    Returns the unique normal form of the word according to the rules:
    - delete every 'k' that stands directly before an 'h' (rule h <-> kh)
    - in each maximal block of only 'o' and 'u' replace it by the canonical
      block having the same weight (#o + 2*#u)
    """
    # ---- step 1 : delete all "kh"
    stack = []
    for ch in word:
        if ch == 'h':
            # while the previous character is a 'k', delete it
            while stack and stack[-1] == 'k':
                stack.pop()
            stack.append('h')
        else:
            stack.append(ch)

    # ---- step 2 : compress o/u blocks
    res_parts = []
    i = 0
    n = len(stack)
    while i < n:
        c = stack[i]
        if c == 'o' or c == 'u':
            weight = 0
            while i < n and (stack[i] == 'o' or stack[i] == 'u'):
                weight += 1 if stack[i] == 'o' else 2
                i += 1
            # canonical representation
            res_parts.append('u' * (weight // 2))
            if weight % 2:
                res_parts.append('o')
        else:
            res_parts.append(c)
            i += 1

    return ''.join(res_parts)


def solve() -> None:
    data = sys.stdin.read().strip().splitlines()
    if not data:
        return
    n = int(data[0])
    words = data[1:1 + n]

    normal_forms = set()
    for w in words:
        normal_forms.add(normalize(w))

    print(len(normal_forms))


if __name__ == "__main__":
    solve()
```

The program follows exactly the algorithm proven correct above
and conforms to the required input‑output format.
completed 137/150, Solved: True, number of success = 45/137, acc = 32.85
**Solution Explanation**

For a subarray `s[l … r]` let  

* `M` – the maximum element of the subarray  
* `S` – the second maximum element (the biggest element that is **not** `M`)

The *lucky number* of the subarray is `M xor S`.

We have to output the maximum lucky number among **all** subarrays of the given
array `s[1 … n]` (`n ≤ 10^5`, all numbers are distinct).

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 1.  When can a pair `(a , b)` be `(M , S)` of some subarray ?

Assume `a > b`.  
Take the **nearest** element larger than `b` on the left side of `b`
(`L`) and on the right side of `b` (`R`).  
If we choose a subarray that contains `b` and `L` (or `R`) but does **not**
contain any element larger than `a`, then inside this subarray

* the maximum is `a` (the larger of the two)
* the second maximum is `b`

Because `a` is the *nearest* larger element, every element between `a` and `b`
is smaller than `b`, therefore `b` really becomes the second maximum.

Consequently **every pair consisting of an element and its nearest larger
neighbour (to the left or to the right) can be the `(M , S)` of some subarray**,
and only such pairs are possible.

So the answer is

```
max  (a xor b)   over all pairs (a , b) where a is the nearest larger element
                 of b on the left or on the right
```

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 2.  Finding nearest larger neighbours in O(n)

A classic monotone stack does exactly this.

* Scan the array from left to right.
* The stack keeps elements in **strictly decreasing** order.
* When the current value `x` is read  

  * while the stack top `< x`  
    * the top element `y` has `x` as its nearest larger element on the **right**  
      → evaluate `y xor x`  
      *pop* `y`
  * after the loop, if the stack is not empty, its top `z` is the nearest larger
    element on the **left** of `x`  
    → evaluate `z xor x`
  * push `x` onto the stack

Every element is pushed once and popped once → `O(n)` time, `O(n)` memory.

During the scan we keep the maximum xor value found.

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 3.  Correctness Proof  

We prove that the algorithm outputs the required maximum lucky number.

---

##### Lemma 1  
For any element `b` in the array, the algorithm evaluates `b xor a`
where `a` is the nearest larger element of `b` on the left (if it exists).

**Proof.**  
When `b` is processed, the stack already contains all previous elements.
All elements larger than `b` stay in the stack because the while‑loop only
removes smaller ones.  
The top of the stack after the loop is the *closest* previous element that is
larger than `b` – exactly the nearest larger on the left.  
The algorithm computes `b xor top`. ∎



##### Lemma 2  
For any element `b` in the array, the algorithm evaluates `b xor a`
where `a` is the nearest larger element of `b` on the right (if it exists).

**Proof.**  
Consider the moment when the nearest larger element `a` (to the right of `b`)
is processed.  
At that time `b` is still on the stack because `b < a`.  
The while‑loop sees `b` as the top element (`b < a`) and therefore pops it,
while simultaneously evaluating `b xor a`. ∎



##### Lemma 3  
For every subarray `s[l … r]` with maximum `M` and second maximum `S`,
the pair `{M , S}` is examined by the algorithm.

**Proof.**  
`M` is larger than `S`.  
Inside the subarray there is no element larger than `M`.  
Consequently, among all occurrences of `S` in the whole array,
the nearest larger element to `S` on the side where `M` lies
must be exactly `M` (otherwise a larger element would be between them,
contradicting that `M` is the maximum of the subarray).  
Thus `M` is either the nearest larger on the left or on the right of `S`.  
By Lemma&nbsp;1 and Lemma&nbsp;2 the algorithm evaluates `M xor S`. ∎



##### Lemma 4  
Every xor value evaluated by the algorithm corresponds to the lucky number
of **some** subarray.

**Proof.**  
Take a pair `(a , b)` examined by the algorithm.
Assume `a > b`.  
If the pair was produced by Lemma&nbsp;1, `a` is the nearest larger element of
`b` on the left.  
Choose the subarray that starts at the position of `a` and ends at the
position of `b`.  
No element inside this interval is larger than `a` (otherwise `a` would not be
the nearest larger).  
Hence `a` is the maximum and `b` the second maximum of that subarray,
and its lucky number equals `a xor b`.  
The same argument works for pairs from Lemma&nbsp;2 (subarray from `b` to `a`). ∎



##### Theorem  
The algorithm outputs the maximum lucky number among all subarrays of the
given array.

**Proof.**  

*Upper bound.*  
By Lemma&nbsp;3, for every subarray its lucky number is among the xor values
examined by the algorithm. Therefore the maximum lucky number cannot exceed
the maximum xor value the algorithm computes.

*Achievability.*  
By Lemma&nbsp;4 each xor value examined by the algorithm is the lucky number of
some subarray. Hence the maximum xor value found by the algorithm is attainable
as a lucky number.

Since both sides coincide, the algorithm’s output equals the required answer. ∎



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 4.  Complexity Analysis  

*Time* : each element is pushed once and popped at most once → `O(n)`  
*Memory*: the stack holds at most `n` elements → `O(n)` (fits easily for
`n ≤ 10^5`)

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 5.  Reference Implementation  (Python 3)

```python
import sys

def solve() -> None:
    data = sys.stdin.read().strip().split()
    if not data:
        return
    n = int(data[0])
    a = list(map(int, data[1:]))

    stack = []          # decreasing stack
    best = 0

    for x in a:
        # nearest larger on the right for popped elements
        while stack and stack[-1] < x:
            best = max(best, stack[-1] ^ x)
            stack.pop()
        # nearest larger on the left (if any)
        if stack:
            best = max(best, stack[-1] ^ x)
        stack.append(x)

    print(best)


if __name__ == "__main__":
    solve()
```

The program follows exactly the algorithm proven correct above and conforms
to the required input‑output format.
completed 138/150, Solved: True, number of success = 46/138, acc = 33.33
**Solution Explanation**

For a prime `p` let  

```
e i = exponent of p in a i          ( v p (a i) )
```

For a pair `{i , j}`  

```
lcm (a i , a j)  contains p with exponent  max(e i , e j)
```

The required answer is  

```
G = gcd over all pairs  lcm (a i , a j)
```

For a fixed prime `p` the exponent of `p` in `G` is

```
g p =  min over i<j   max(e i , e j)                (1)
```

The whole answer is  

```
G = ∏ p ^ g p
```

So we have to compute `g p` for every prime that appears in the input.



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 1.   Computing `g p`

Sort the `n` exponents of `p`

```
e(1) ≤ e(2) ≤ … ≤ e(n)
```

For any pair `{i , j}` the larger exponent is at least `e(2)`,
and the pair consisting of the two smallest elements gives exactly `e(2)`.
Therefore  

```
g p = e(2)                                    (2)
```

`e(2)` is the **second smallest** exponent among the `n` numbers.
Zeros (numbers not divisible by `p`) are also exponents, because
`v p (x) = 0` when `p ∤ x`.

Consequences

* if at least two numbers are not divisible by `p` → the second smallest
  exponent is `0` → `p` does **not** appear in the answer.
* if exactly one number is not divisible by `p` → the second smallest
  exponent is the smallest *positive* exponent.
* if every number is divisible by `p` → the second smallest exponent is
  the second smallest *positive* exponent.

So we only need, for every prime `p`

* `cnt[p]` – how many input numbers contain `p`
* the smallest exponent `min1[p]`
* how many times this smallest exponent occurs `cntMin1[p]`
* the smallest exponent **strictly larger** than `min1[p]` – `min2[p]`

All these values are obtained while we factorise the input numbers.



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 2.   Factoring all numbers

`a i ≤ 200 000`.  
A linear sieve gives the smallest prime factor `spf[x]` for every `x ≤ 200 000`
in `O(maxA)` time.

Factorising one number:

```
while x > 1:
        p = spf[x]
        e = 0
        while x % p == 0:
                x //= p
                e += 1
        (p , e) is a prime factor of the original number
```

The total number of prime factors (with multiplicity) over the whole input
is at most `n·log₂ 200 000 < 7·10⁵`, easily fast enough.



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 3.   Updating the statistics for a prime `p`

```
cnt[p]      += 1
if e < min1[p]:
        min2[p]      = min1[p]
        min1[p]      = e
        cntMin1[p]   = 1
elif e == min1[p]:
        cntMin1[p]  += 1
elif e < min2[p]:
        min2[p] = e
```

All arrays are of size `maxA+1` (`≈ 2·10⁵`).



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 4.   Computing the final answer

For every prime `p` with `cnt[p] > 0`

```
zero = n - cnt[p]          # how many numbers are not divisible by p

if zero >= 2:
        exponent = 0                     # p disappears
elif zero == 1:
        exponent = min1[p]                # smallest positive exponent
else:   # zero == 0, p divides every number
        if cntMin1[p] >= 2:
                exponent = min1[p]        # the smallest exponent occurs at least twice
        else:
                exponent = min2[p]        # second smallest (different) exponent
answer *= p ** exponent
```

The product fits into Python’s arbitrary length integer.



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 5.   Correctness Proof  

We prove that the algorithm outputs exactly  

```
G = gcd_{i<j} lcm(a_i , a_j)
```

---

##### Lemma 1  
For a fixed prime `p` let `e₁,…,e_n` be the exponents of `p` in the input
numbers (zeros allowed).  
The exponent of `p` in `G` equals the second smallest value among
`e₁,…,e_n`.

**Proof.**  
From definition (1)

```
g p = min_{i<j} max(e_i , e_j)
```

Take the two smallest exponents, call them `x ≤ y`.  
For the pair that contains exactly these two numbers we have  
`max = y`. Hence `g p ≤ y`.

For any pair `{i , j}` the larger exponent is at least `y`,
because at most one of the two numbers can have exponent `< y`.  
Thus `max(e_i , e_j) ≥ y` for every pair, consequently `g p ≥ y`.

Both inequalities give `g p = y`, i.e. the second smallest exponent. ∎



##### Lemma 2  
For a prime `p` the algorithm computes the exponent described in Lemma&nbsp;1.

**Proof.**  
Three cases according to the number of zeros (`zero = n‑cnt[p]`).

*`zero ≥ 2`*  
At least two numbers have exponent `0`.  
The second smallest exponent is `0`.  
The algorithm sets `exponent = 0`.

*`zero = 1`*  
Exactly one zero, all other exponents are positive.
The smallest positive exponent is `min1[p]`.  
The sorted list looks like `0 , min1 , …`.  
Hence the second smallest exponent equals `min1[p]`.  
The algorithm outputs `exponent = min1[p]`.

*`zero = 0`*  
All exponents are positive.
If the smallest exponent occurs at least twice (`cntMin1[p] ≥ 2`),
the sorted list begins `min1 , min1 , …` and the second smallest exponent
is `min1`.  
Otherwise the smallest exponent is unique, the second smallest is the
smallest exponent larger than it, i.e. `min2[p]`.  
The algorithm follows exactly this rule. ∎



##### Lemma 3  
For every prime `p` the exponent `exponent` used by the algorithm equals
`g p`.

**Proof.**  
By Lemma&nbsp;1 `g p` is the second smallest exponent among the `n`
exponents of `p`.  
By Lemma&nbsp;2 the algorithm computes exactly this second smallest exponent.
Therefore `exponent = g p`. ∎



##### Theorem  
The number printed by the algorithm equals  

```
G = gcd_{i<j} lcm(a_i , a_j)
```

**Proof.**  
`G` factorises uniquely into primes:

```
G = ∏ p ^ g p
```

For each prime `p` the algorithm multiplies `p` raised to the value
`exponent`. By Lemma&nbsp;3 `exponent = g p`.  
Thus the product built by the algorithm is exactly the product of all
`p ^ g p`, i.e. `G`. ∎



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 6.   Complexity Analysis  

*Building SPF*: `O(maxA)` ≤ `2·10⁵`  
*Factorising all numbers*: each prime factor processed once →  
`O(total number of prime factors) = O(n log maxA)` ≤ `7·10⁵`  
*Updating statistics*: same order as factorisation.  
*Final loop over primes*: `O(maxA)`.

Overall time   `O(n log maxA + maxA)`  ≤ `≈ 10⁶` operations.  

Memory usage: several integer arrays of length `maxA+1`
(~ 2·10⁵) → well below 10 MB.



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 7.   Reference Implementation  (Python 3)

```python
import sys

def main() -> None:
    data = sys.stdin.read().strip().split()
    if not data:
        return
    it = iter(data)
    n = int(next(it))
    a = [int(next(it)) for _ in range(n)]

    max_a = max(a)
    # ---------- smallest prime factor ----------
    spf = [0] * (max_a + 1)
    primes = []
    for i in range(2, max_a + 1):
        if spf[i] == 0:
            spf[i] = i
            primes.append(i)
        for p in primes:
            if p > spf[i] or i * p > max_a:
                break
            spf[i * p] = p

    INF = 10 ** 9
    cnt = [0] * (max_a + 1)          # how many numbers contain the prime
    min1 = [INF] * (max_a + 1)       # smallest exponent
    cnt_min1 = [0] * (max_a + 1)     # how many times min1 occurs
    min2 = [INF] * (max_a + 1)       # smallest exponent > min1

    # ---------- factorise each a_i ----------
    for x in a:
        while x > 1:
            p = spf[x]
            e = 0
            while x % p == 0:
                x //= p
                e += 1
            # update statistics for prime p, exponent e
            cnt[p] += 1
            if e < min1[p]:
                min2[p] = min1[p]
                min1[p] = e
                cnt_min1[p] = 1
            elif e == min1[p]:
                cnt_min1[p] += 1
            elif e < min2[p]:
                min2[p] = e

    # ---------- compute the answer ----------
    answer = 1
    for p in range(2, max_a + 1):
        if cnt[p] == 0:
            continue
        zero = n - cnt[p]
        if zero >= 2:
            continue          # exponent 0, nothing to multiply
        elif zero == 1:
            exp = min1[p]
        else:                 # zero == 0
            if cnt_min1[p] >= 2:
                exp = min1[p]
            else:
                exp = min2[p]
        if exp > 0:
            answer *= pow(p, exp)

    print(answer)


if __name__ == "__main__":
    main()
```

The program follows exactly the algorithm proven correct above
and conforms to the required input‑output format.
completed 139/150, Solved: True, number of success = 47/139, acc = 33.81
**Solution Explanation**

For a permutation `p` of `{1 … n}` we have to satisfy  

```
p[ p[i] ] = n – i + 1          (1)      for every i = 1 … n
```

Define  

* `rev(i) = n – i + 1` – the *reversed* index.  
  `rev` is a permutation itself, it consists of

* `⌊ n/2 ⌋` transpositions `(i , rev(i))`
* one fixed point `m = (n+1)/2` when `n` is odd.

Equation (1) can be written as  

```
p ∘ p = rev                                    (2)
```

So we need a **square root** of the permutation `rev`.

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 1. When does a square root exist ?

*The well known group‑theoretic rule*  

A permutation has a square root **iff** for every even cycle length,
the number of cycles of that length is even.

`rev` contains only cycles of length `2` (the transpositions) and possibly
one cycle of length `1`.  
Therefore a square root exists **iff the number of transpositions is even** :

```
⌊ n/2 ⌋ is even   ⇔   n ≡ 0 or 1 (mod 4)
```

If `n ≡ 2` or `3 (mod 4)` we output `-1`.

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 2. Constructing a square root for admissible `n`

Take two consecutive transpositions of `rev`

```
(i , rev(i))          = ( i , n-i+1 )
(i+1 , rev(i+1))      = ( i+1 , n-i )
```

and join them into one 4‑cycle

```
i → i+1 → n-i+1 → n-i → i
```

In terms of the permutation `p`

```
p[i]         = i+1
p[i+1]       = n-i+1
p[n-i+1]     = n-i
p[n-i]       = i
```

Now check (2) for the four involved positions, e.g.

```
p[ p[i] ] = p[i+1] = n-i+1 = rev(i)
```

and similarly for the other three positions – the condition holds.

We repeat this for every *pair* of transpositions, i.e. for `i = 1,3,5,…`.
If `n` is odd the middle element `m = (n+1)/2` is a fixed point of `rev`,
so we simply set `p[m] = m` (a 1‑cycle, whose square is itself).

The construction uses each number exactly once → `p` is a permutation,
and by the argument above it satisfies (1).

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 3. Algorithm
```
if n % 4 in (2, 3):
        print -1
else:
        create array p[1 … n]
        for i = 1, 3, 5, … , n//2   (step 2):
                a = i
                b = i+1
                c = n - i + 1
                d = n - i
                p[a] = b
                p[b] = c
                p[c] = d
                p[d] = a
        if n is odd:
                m = (n+1)//2
                p[m] = m
        output p[1] … p[n]
```

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 4. Correctness Proof  

We prove that the algorithm prints `-1` exactly when no lucky permutation
exists, otherwise it prints a permutation satisfying (1).

---

##### Lemma 1  
If `n ≡ 2` or `3 (mod 4)` then no permutation `p` satisfies (1).

**Proof.**  
`rev` consists of `⌊n/2⌋` transpositions.
When `n ≡ 2` or `3 (mod 4)`, `⌊n/2⌋` is odd.
A square of any permutation can contain an odd number of 2‑cycles only
if at least one 2‑cycle comes from squaring a 2‑cycle itself,
but squaring a 2‑cycle gives the identity, not a 2‑cycle.
Thus a permutation whose square is exactly `⌊n/2⌋` transpositions
cannot exist. ∎



##### Lemma 2  
For `n ≡ 0` or `1 (mod 4)` the algorithm produces a permutation `p`.

**Proof.**  
The loop processes indices `i = 1,3,5,…, n//2`.  
Each iteration touches the four distinct numbers  

```
i , i+1 , n-i+1 , n-i
```

and assigns each of them exactly one value, forming a 4‑cycle.
Different iterations use disjoint sets of numbers because the intervals
`[i, i+1]` are disjoint and the mirrored numbers are also disjoint.
If `n` is odd, the middle element `m` is not touched by the loop and is
assigned `p[m]=m`.  
Hence every integer `1 … n` receives exactly one image → `p` is a
bijection, i.e. a permutation. ∎



##### Lemma 3  
For every index `i` processed inside the loop the equality
`p[ p[i] ] = n - i + 1` holds.

**Proof.**  
Inside one iteration we have the assignments

```
p[i]       = i+1
p[i+1]     = n-i+1
p[n-i+1]   = n-i
p[n-i]     = i
```

Now compute:

* `p[ p[i] ] = p[i+1] = n-i+1 = rev(i)`
* `p[ p[i+1] ] = p[n-i+1] = n-i = rev(i+1)`
* `p[ p[n-i+1] ] = p[n-i] = i = rev(n-i+1)`
* `p[ p[n-i] ] = p[i] = i+1 = rev(n-i)`

Thus the required condition holds for all four positions. ∎



##### Lemma 4  
If `n` is odd, for the middle element `m = (n+1)/2` the algorithm satisfies
`p[ p[m] ] = n - m + 1`.

**Proof.**  
The algorithm sets `p[m] = m`. Therefore  

```
p[ p[m] ] = p[m] = m = n - m + 1          (because m = (n+1)/2)
```

∎



##### Lemma 5  
When `n ≡ 0` or `1 (mod 4)` the permutation produced by the algorithm
satisfies condition (1) for **all** `i = 1 … n`.

**Proof.**  
All indices belong either to a 4‑cycle handled in Lemma&nbsp;3
or (only when `n` is odd) to the middle element handled in Lemma&nbsp;4.
Thus (1) holds for every index. ∎



##### Theorem  
The program outputs  

* `-1` iff no lucky permutation exists,
* otherwise a lucky permutation.

**Proof.**  

*If the program prints `-1`.*  
That happens exactly when `n % 4 ∈ {2,3}`.
By Lemma&nbsp;1 a lucky permutation cannot exist, so the output is correct.

*If the program prints a permutation.*  
Then `n % 4 ∈ {0,1}`.  
By Lemma&nbsp;2 the printed array is a permutation.
By Lemma&nbsp;5 it fulfills `p[p[i]] = n-i+1` for every `i`.  
Hence it is a lucky permutation.

Both directions are proved, completing the theorem. ∎



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 5. Complexity Analysis  

The loop runs `⌊n/4⌋` times, each iteration does O(1) work.
All other steps are O(n) for initialisation / output.

```
Time   :  O(n)
Memory :  O(n)   (the permutation array)
```

Both satisfy the limits (`n ≤ 10^5`).

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 6. Reference Implementation  (Python 3)

```python
import sys

def solve() -> None:
    data = sys.stdin.read().strip().split()
    if not data:
        return
    n = int(data[0])

    # existence test
    if n % 4 in (2, 3):
        print(-1)
        return

    p = [0] * (n + 1)          # 1‑based

    # build 4‑cycles
    half = n // 2
    i = 1
    while i <= half:
        a = i
        b = i + 1
        c = n - i + 1
        d = n - i

        p[a] = b
        p[b] = c
        p[c] = d
        p[d] = a

        i += 2

    # middle element for odd n
    if n % 2 == 1:
        mid = (n + 1) // 2
        p[mid] = mid

    # output
    out = ' '.join(str(p[idx]) for idx in range(1, n + 1))
    sys.stdout.write(out)

if __name__ == "__main__":
    solve()
```

The program follows exactly the algorithm proven correct above
and conforms to the required input‑output format.
completed 140/150, Solved: True, number of success = 48/140, acc = 34.29

completed 141/150, Solved: False, number of success = 48/141, acc = 34.04
**Solution Explanation**

For every beacon we know  

* its coordinate `a[i]`
* its power `b[i]`

When a beacon is activated it destroys all *still alive* beacons on its left whose
coordinates are inside the interval  

```
[a[i] - b[i] , a[i]]          (inclusive)
```

The beacons are activated **from right to left**.
If a beacon is destroyed earlier it never gets a turn.

We may add one extra beacon **strictly to the right of all existing ones**.
Its position and power can be chosen arbitrarily.
It will be the first (rightmost) beacon that is activated.



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 1.   What does the extra beacon really do ?

Let the extra beacon be placed at coordinate `X` (`X > max a[i]`) and have power `P`.
All original beacons with  

```
a[i] ≥ X - P
```

are destroyed immediately, the others stay untouched.
Because `X` can be arbitrarily large we can realise **any** left border  

```
L = X - P
```

(choose `P` large enough so that `X = L + P > max a[i]`).

Consequences  

* we may delete **any suffix** of the sorted beacons,
* the remaining beacons are exactly a prefix of the sorted list,
* after that the ordinary right‑to‑left activation starts.

So the whole problem reduces to:

```
Choose a prefix of the beacons (maybe empty).
All beacons outside the prefix are already destroyed.
How many beacons will be destroyed during the normal process inside the prefix?
```

If the prefix has length `i` (`0 ≤ i ≤ n`)

```
total destroyed = (n - i)          // the suffix we removed
                + destroyed_inside_prefix(i)
```

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 2.   Survivors inside a prefix

Sort the beacons by coordinate, now `a[1] < a[2] < … < a[n]`.

Consider only the first `i` beacons.
When the rightmost alive beacon `i` is activated

* it certainly survives,
* it destroys every still alive beacon whose coordinate is `≥ a[i] - b[i]`.

All beacons with index  

```
p = first index with a[p] ≥ a[i] - b[i]          (1‑based)
```

are destroyed, the ones with index `< p` stay untouched.
Therefore the number of survivors among the first `i` beacons satisfies

```
survive[i] = 1 + survive[p-1]          (survive[0] = 0)
```

`p` can be found with a binary search (`lower_bound`) in `O(log n)`.

The number of destroyed beacons inside the prefix is  

```
destroyed_inside_prefix(i) = i - survive[i]
```

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 3.   Which prefix is the best ?

The overall number of destroyed beacons for a prefix of length `i` is

```
total(i) = (n - i) + (i - survive[i]) = n - survive[i]
```

`n` is constant, therefore we only have to **maximise `survive[i]`** over all
prefix lengths `i = 0 … n`.

```
answer = n - max_{0 ≤ i ≤ n} survive[i]
```

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 4.   Algorithm
```
read n and the pairs (a, b)
sort the pairs by a
make two arrays A[0…n-1], B[0…n-1]

survive[0] = 0
for i = 1 … n
        target = A[i-1] - B[i-1]
        p = lower_bound(A, target)          // 0‑based index
        survive[i] = 1 + survive[p]          // p corresponds to p‑1 in 1‑based formula
max_survive = maximum of survive[0…n]
print n - max_survive
```

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 5.   Correctness Proof  

We prove that the algorithm outputs the minimum possible number of destroyed
beacons.

---

##### Lemma 1  
Placing the extra beacon can destroy exactly the beacons of a suffix of the
sorted list and no other beacons.

**Proof.**  
The extra beacon destroys all beacons with coordinate `≥ L` where `L = X-P`.
Because the beacons are sorted, the set `{ i | a[i] ≥ L }` is a suffix (maybe
empty).  
Conversely, for any suffix we can choose `L` equal to the smallest coordinate
in that suffix, then pick `X` large enough and `P = X-L`. ∎



##### Lemma 2  
For a fixed prefix of length `i` the number of survivors after the ordinary
right‑to‑left activation equals `survive[i]` computed by the recurrence  

```
survive[0] = 0
survive[i] = 1 + survive[p-1] ,   p = first index with a[p] ≥ a[i] - b[i]
```

**Proof.**  
Induction over `i`.

*Base `i = 0`*: no beacon, `survive[0]=0` – correct.

*Induction step.*  
Assume the statement true for all lengths `< i`.  
During the process the rightmost alive beacon among the first `i` beacons is
exactly beacon `i` (it cannot be destroyed by any beacon to its right, because
there is none inside the prefix).  
Beacon `i` survives and destroys every still alive beacon whose coordinate is
`≥ a[i] - b[i]`. By definition of `p` these are precisely the beacons with
indices `p, p+1, … , i-1`. All beacons with index `< p` are untouched, and
their future behaviour is exactly the same as in a prefix of length `p-1`.  
Hence the total number of survivors is  

```
1 (beacon i) + survive[p-1] .
```

∎



##### Lemma 3  
For every prefix length `i` the total number of destroyed beacons (including the
suffix removed beforehand) equals `n - survive[i]`.

**Proof.**  
The suffix that is removed contains `n-i` beacons, all destroyed.
Inside the prefix `i` beacons, `i - survive[i]` are destroyed (Lemma&nbsp;2).  
Summation gives  

```
(n-i) + (i - survive[i]) = n - survive[i].
```
∎



##### Lemma 4  
Among all possible placements of the extra beacon the minimum number of
destroyed beacons equals  

```
n - max_{0≤i≤n} survive[i].
```

**Proof.**  
By Lemma&nbsp;1 each placement corresponds to a choice of a prefix length `i`.
By Lemma&nbsp;3 the resulting number of destroyed beacons is `n - survive[i]`.
Therefore the optimal placement minimises `n - survive[i]`, i.e. maximises
`survive[i]`. ∎



##### Lemma 5  
The algorithm computes `survive[i]` for every `i` correctly.

**Proof.**  
The algorithm processes `i = 1 … n` in increasing order.
For each `i` it finds `p = lower_bound(A, A[i-1] - B[i-1])`, which is exactly the
first index with `a[p] ≥ a[i] - b[i]` (0‑based).  
Then it sets `survive[i] = 1 + survive[p]`.  
Because `p` is the 0‑based version of `p-1` in Lemma&nbsp;2, this matches the
recurrence of Lemma&nbsp;2, and all needed `survive[·]` values are already known
(`p ≤ i-1`). ∎



##### Lemma 6  
`max_survive` computed by the algorithm equals `max_{0≤i≤n} survive[i]`.

**Proof.**  
The algorithm stores every `survive[i]` (including `survive[0]=0`) and keeps the
maximum while iterating, therefore the final value is exactly the maximum over
all `i`. ∎



##### Theorem  
The algorithm outputs the minimum possible number of beacons that can be
destroyed after adding one extra beacon.

**Proof.**  
By Lemma&nbsp;5 the algorithm obtains the correct array `survive[·]`.  
Lemma&nbsp;6 gives the maximum of this array.  
Using Lemma&nbsp;4 the optimal answer is `n - max_survive`, which the algorithm
prints. ∎



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 6.   Complexity Analysis  

*Sorting* : `O(n log n)`  
*Main loop* : `n` iterations, each binary search `O(log n)` → `O(n log n)`  

Total time `O(n log n)`, memory `O(n)` (arrays of size `n`).

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 7.   Reference Implementation  (Python 3)

```python
import sys
import bisect

def solve() -> None:
    data = sys.stdin.read().strip().split()
    if not data:
        return
    it = iter(data)
    n = int(next(it))
    beacons = [(int(next(it)), int(next(it))) for _ in range(n)]

    # sort by coordinate
    beacons.sort(key=lambda x: x[0])
    a = [p for p, _ in beacons]
    b = [p for _, p in beacons]

    survive = [0] * (n + 1)          # survive[i] for first i beacons
    max_survive = 0

    for i in range(1, n + 1):
        target = a[i - 1] - b[i - 1]
        # first index with a[idx] >= target (0‑based)
        p = bisect.bisect_left(a, target, 0, i)   # we only need to search in prefix
        survive[i] = 1 + survive[p]
        if survive[i] > max_survive:
            max_survive = survive[i]

    # also consider i = 0 (survive[0] = 0) – already covered by initial max_survive = 0
    answer = n - max_survive
    print(answer)


if __name__ == "__main__":
    solve()
```

The program follows exactly the algorithm proven correct above and conforms to
the required input‑output format.
completed 142/150, Solved: True, number of success = 49/142, acc = 34.51
**Solution Explanation**

For every position `i`

* `a[i] = b[i]` – the bit is already correct  
* `a[i] = 0 , b[i] = 1` – we need a **0 → 1** change (call it *type 01*)  
* `a[i] = 1 , b[i] = 0` – we need a **1 → 0** change (call it *type 10*)

The only useful operation is

* **flip** a single wrong bit – cost `1`
* **swap** a *type 01* position with a *type 10* position – after the swap both
  positions become correct, cost `|i‑j|`

If we flip the two mismatching bits separately the cost is `2`.  
Swapping them is better only when `|i‑j| < 2`, i.e. when the two positions are
*adjacent* (`|i‑j| = 1`).  
For distance `2` the cost is the same as two flips, and for larger distances it
is more expensive – we will never use such a swap in an optimal solution.

Therefore the whole problem reduces to:

*We have a list of mismatching positions, each labelled 01 or 10.
Choose a maximum number of **disjoint adjacent pairs** consisting of opposite
labels. Every chosen pair costs `1` instead of `2`, saving exactly `1`.*

The answer is  

```
total_mismatches – number_of_chosen_pairs
```

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### Greedy pairing

Scanning the string from left to right we can greedily take every possible
adjacent opposite pair:

```
i = 0
while i < n-1:
        if a[i] != b[i] and a[i+1] != b[i+1] and a[i] != a[i+1]:
                # a[i] and a[i+1] are opposite mismatches → swap them
                pairs += 1
                i += 2          # both positions are used, skip them
        else:
                i += 1
```

Why is this optimal?
*Any* optimal solution consists of some disjoint adjacent opposite pairs.
If the leftmost pair in that solution starts at position `i`,
our greedy algorithm also takes the pair `(i,i+1)` (the condition is exactly the
definition of such a pair). After fixing it, the remaining part of the string
is independent of the choice, so the greedy step does not hurt optimality.
Repeating the argument proves that the greedy algorithm yields the maximum
possible number of pairs.

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### Algorithm
```
read n, strings a, b
total = number of indices i with a[i] != b[i]

pairs = 0
i = 0
while i < n-1:
        if a[i] != b[i] and a[i+1] != b[i+1] and a[i] != a[i+1]:
                pairs += 1
                i += 2
        else:
                i += 1

answer = total - pairs
print answer
```

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### Correctness Proof  

We prove that the algorithm outputs the minimum possible cost.

---

**Lemma 1**  
Swapping two mismatching positions `i < j` is never cheaper than flipping both
individually unless `j = i+1`.

*Proof.*  
Cost of swapping = `j-i`.  
Cost of two flips = `2`.  
If `j-i ≥ 2` then `j-i ≥ 2`, i.e. swapping is not cheaper. ∎



**Lemma 2**  
In an optimal solution every performed swap is between two adjacent opposite
mismatches.

*Proof.*  
By Lemma&nbsp;1 a swap with distance `≥2` cannot improve the total cost, therefore
an optimal solution never contains such a swap.  
A swap between two equal‑type mismatches would leave at least one position still
incorrect, so it cannot be part of a feasible optimal solution. ∎



**Lemma 3**  
Let `P` be the set of disjoint adjacent opposite pairs chosen by the greedy
scan. Any feasible solution can contain at most `|P|` such pairs.

*Proof.*  
Consider the leftmost index `i` that belongs to a pair in an arbitrary optimal
solution `S`. Because the pair is adjacent and opposite, the greedy condition
holds at `i`, therefore the greedy algorithm also creates a pair `(i,i+1)`.
Both solutions now use the same two indices, and the remaining part of the
string (indices `> i+1`) is independent of this choice.
Applying the same argument recursively to the suffix shows that the greedy
algorithm never uses fewer pairs than `S`. Hence `|P|` is maximal. ∎



**Lemma 4**  
The cost produced by the algorithm equals  
`total_mismatches – |P|`.

*Proof.*  
All mismatching positions are either
* paired in `P` – each such pair is fixed by one swap (cost 1), or
* left single – each needs one flip (cost 1).

If there are `total` mismatches and `|P|` pairs, the number of single positions
is `total – 2·|P|`. Total cost  

```
|P|·1  + (total – 2·|P|)·1  = total – |P|
```
∎



**Theorem**  
The algorithm outputs the minimum possible total cost to transform `a` into
`b`.

*Proof.*  
Let `C_opt` be the cost of an optimal solution.
By Lemma&nbsp;2 any optimal solution consists of some number `k` of adjacent
opposite pairs and flips for the remaining mismatches, therefore  

```
C_opt = total_mismatches – k .
```

By Lemma&nbsp;3 `k ≤ |P|`. Consequently  

```
C_opt ≥ total_mismatches – |P| .
```

From Lemma&nbsp;4 the algorithm achieves exactly `total_mismatches – |P|`,
hence `C_opt` cannot be smaller. The algorithm is optimal. ∎



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### Complexity Analysis  

*Scanning the strings once* – `O(n)` time.  
Only a few integer variables are stored – `O(1)` extra memory.  
`n ≤ 10^6` easily fits into the limits.

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### Reference Implementation  (Python 3)

```python
import sys

def solve() -> None:
    data = sys.stdin.read().split()
    if not data:
        return
    n = int(data[0])
    a = data[1].strip()
    b = data[2].strip()

    total_mis = 0
    for i in range(n):
        if a[i] != b[i]:
            total_mis += 1

    pairs = 0
    i = 0
    while i < n - 1:
        # both positions mismatching and of opposite type
        if a[i] != b[i] and a[i + 1] != b[i + 1] and a[i] != a[i + 1]:
            pairs += 1
            i += 2          # these two indices are used
        else:
            i += 1

    answer = total_mis - pairs
    print(answer)


if __name__ == "__main__":
    solve()
```

The program follows exactly the algorithm proven correct above and conforms
to the required input‑output format.
completed 143/150, Solved: True, number of success = 50/143, acc = 34.97
**Solution Explanation**

For every photo we know its contrast `v[i]`.
We have to split all photos into groups

* every group contains at least `k` photos
* a photo belongs to exactly one group
* the *processing time* of a group = `max contrast – min contrast` inside the group
* the processing time of a whole division = the maximum processing time of its groups  

We have to minimise this value.



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 1.  Observations  

*The order of the photos does not matter.*  
Only the set of contrasts inside a group influences its processing time.
If we sort all contrasts increasingly  

```
a[0] ≤ a[1] ≤ … ≤ a[n‑1]
```

and take a group that is **not** a contiguous segment of this sorted array,
we can always replace it by the segment that contains exactly the same
photos – the new segment has the same minimum and maximum, therefore the
same processing time.
Consequently an optimal division always consists of **contiguous
segments of the sorted array**.

So the problem becomes:

```
Sorted array a[0 … n‑1] .
Split it into segments, each length ≥ k,
such that for every segment   a[right] – a[left] ≤ X
and X is as small as possible.
```

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 2.  Decision problem  

For a fixed value `X` we only have to answer  

```
Is it possible to split the sorted array into segments,
each length ≥ k and each having a[right] – a[left] ≤ X ?
```

If we can answer this in `O(n)` we can binary‑search the minimal `X`
(`0 … max(a) – min(a)` – at most 30‑31 steps).

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 3.  Checking a fixed `X` – linear DP with a difference array  

`good[i]` – *true* if the first `i` elements (`a[0] … a[i‑1]`) can already be
partitioned according to the rules.
`good[0] = true` (empty prefix).

From a position `i` where `good[i] = true` we may start a new group.
Let  

```
r(i) = largest index (exclusive) such that a[r-1] – a[i] ≤ X
```

All possible ends of the new group are `j` with  

```
i + k ≤ j ≤ r(i)
```

and after taking such a group the prefix of length `j` becomes reachable,
i.e. `good[j] = true`.

The transition is a *range update* on the array `good`.
Range updates and point queries can be performed in `O(1)` with a
difference array `add[]` :

```
add[l]   += 1
add[r+1] -= 1          (inclusive range [l , r])
```

During a left‑to‑right scan we keep the current prefix sum `cur`.
`cur > 0`  ⇔  the current position is reachable (`good` is true).

The whole check:

```
diff[0] = 1                     # good[0] = true
cur = 0
r = 0                           # two‑pointer, never moves left

for i = 0 … n‑1
        cur += diff[i]
        if cur == 0: continue          # i is not reachable

        while r < n and a[r] - a[i] <= X:
                r += 1                  # r is exclusive

        L = i + k
        R = r                           # exclusive, but we need inclusive index
        if L <= R:
                diff[L]   += 1
                diff[R+1] -= 1          # R is inclusive, so +1

# after the loop we have to know whether position n is reachable
cur += diff[n]
return cur > 0
```

All operations are `O(1)`, the pointer `r` moves at most `n` times,
therefore the whole test works in `O(n)` time and `O(n)` memory.

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 4.  Whole algorithm  

```
read n , k , array v
if k == 1:                     # each photo may be a single group
        answer = 0
else
        sort v → a
        lo = 0
        hi = a[-1] - a[0]       # maximal possible answer
        while lo < hi
                mid = (lo + hi) // 2
                if feasible(mid):      # the O(n) test described above
                        hi = mid
                else:
                        lo = mid + 1
        answer = lo
print answer
```

`feasible` is the function from section&nbsp;3.

Complexities  

* sorting: `O(n log n)`
* binary search: at most 31 iterations
* each iteration: `O(n)`  

Overall  

```
Time   : O(n log n)          (dominant term is sorting)
Memory : O(n)                (the sorted array + auxiliary diff array)
```

Both satisfy the limits (`n ≤ 3·10⁵`).

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 5.  Correctness Proof  

We prove that the algorithm outputs the minimal possible processing time.

---

##### Lemma 1  
For any optimal division there exists an optimal division whose groups are
contiguous segments of the sorted array.

**Proof.**  
Take any optimal division.  
Consider a group `G`. Let `min = min_{x∈G} v[x]` and `max = max_{x∈G} v[x]`.  
All elements of `G` lie between `min` and `max`.  
If we sort all contrasts, the elements of `G` occupy a contiguous block
(because no element with contrast `< min` or `> max` can be inside that block).
Replacing `G` by exactly this block does not change `min` or `max`,
hence does not change the group’s processing time.
Doing this for every group yields a division into contiguous segments
with the same maximal group processing time. ∎



##### Lemma 2  
For a fixed value `X` the procedure `feasible(X)` returns *true*  
iff there exists a division into contiguous segments, each of length at least
`k`, whose maximal contrast difference does not exceed `X`.

**Proof.**  

*Soundness* (if `feasible` returns true, such a division exists).  
The algorithm maintains the invariant:

```
good[i]  ⇔  the prefix a[0 … i‑1] can be partitioned as required.
```

*Initialization*: `good[0]` is set to true – empty prefix is trivially
partitionable.

*Induction step*: assume `good[i]` is true.
All indices `j` with `i + k ≤ j ≤ r(i)` satisfy  
`a[j‑1] – a[i] ≤ X` by definition of `r(i)`.  
Thus the segment `a[i … j‑1]` is a legal group.
Marking `good[j]` true (via the range update) records that the prefix up to
`j` can be partitioned: the previous partition of the first `i` elements
followed by this new group.
No other positions are ever marked, therefore the invariant holds.

After processing all `i`, `good[n]` is true exactly when the whole array can
be partitioned, i.e. when the function returns true.

*Completeness* (if a suitable division exists, `feasible` returns true).  
Take any valid division and look at its group borders
`0 = b₀ < b₁ < … < b_m = n`.  
We prove by induction that after the loop finishes processing index `b_t`
the algorithm has already set `good[b_t] = true`.

*Base*: `b₀ = 0` – true by initialization.  

*Induction*: assume `good[b_t]` is true.
The group `a[b_t … b_{t+1}-1]` has length ≥ `k` and
`a[b_{t+1}-1] – a[b_t] ≤ X`.  
Consequently `b_{t+1}` lies inside the range
`[b_t + k , r(b_t)]` that the algorithm adds to `good`.
Thus `good[b_{t+1}]` becomes true (maybe later, but before the algorithm
finishes).  

Finally `good[n]` becomes true, therefore `feasible` returns true. ∎



##### Lemma 3  
Let `X*` be the minimal possible processing time.
Binary search returns exactly `X*`.

**Proof.**  
From Lemma&nbsp;2, `feasible(X)` is true **iff** a division with maximal
group difference ≤ `X` exists.
Hence the set `{ X | feasible(X) = true }` is exactly `{ X | X ≥ X* }`,
a suffix of the integer interval.
Binary search on a monotone predicate always returns the smallest `X`
with `feasible(X) = true`, i.e. `X*`. ∎



##### Theorem  
The algorithm prints the minimal possible processing time of a division
into groups of size at least `k`.

**Proof.**  

*If `k = 1`* the algorithm outputs `0`.  
A division into single‑photo groups is allowed, each has difference `0`,
so `0` is achievable and obviously minimal.

*If `k ≥ 2`* :

1. By Lemma&nbsp;1 an optimal division can be assumed to consist of
   contiguous segments of the sorted array.
2. Lemma&nbsp;2 shows that for any integer `X` the function `feasible(X)`
   correctly decides whether such a division with maximal segment
   difference ≤ `X` exists.
3. Lemma&nbsp;3 proves that binary search over `X` using this decision
   procedure returns the smallest feasible `X`, i.e. the optimal
   processing time.

Therefore the printed value equals the optimum. ∎



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 6.  Reference Implementation  (Python 3)

```python
import sys

def solve() -> None:
    data = list(map(int, sys.stdin.read().split()))
    if not data:
        return
    n, k = data[0], data[1]
    v = data[2:]

    if k == 1:
        # each photo may be a separate group
        print(0)
        return

    a = sorted(v)

    # ---------- feasibility test for a given limit ----------
    def feasible(limit: int) -> bool:
        # diff array for range updates, size n+2 to avoid index errors
        diff = [0] * (n + 2)
        diff[0] = 1                     # good[0] = true
        cur = 0
        r = 0                           # two‑pointer, exclusive

        for i in range(n):
            cur += diff[i]
            if cur == 0:                # position i not reachable
                continue

            # enlarge r while the segment [i, r) satisfies the limit
            while r < n and a[r] - a[i] <= limit:
                r += 1

            L = i + k
            R = r                         # r is exclusive, we need inclusive
            if L <= R:
                diff[L] += 1
                diff[R + 1] -= 1

        cur += diff[n]                  # propagate the last pending update
        return cur > 0                  # good[n] ?

    # ---------- binary search ----------
    lo = 0
    hi = a[-1] - a[0]                  # maximal possible answer

    while lo < hi:
        mid = (lo + hi) // 2
        if feasible(mid):
            hi = mid
        else:
            lo = mid + 1

    print(lo)


if __name__ == "__main__":
    solve()
```
