The program follows exactly the algorithm proven correct above
and conforms to the required input‑output format.
completed 144/150, Solved: False, number of success = 50/144, acc = 34.72
**Solution Explanation**

For every step the robot can take  

* the leftmost remaining item with the **left** hand – cost `weight·l`  
  (if the previous step was also left, an additional `Qₗ` is paid)

* the rightmost remaining item with the **right** hand – cost `weight·r`  
  (if the previous step was also right, an additional `Qᵣ` is paid)

All items have to be taken, the order may be chosen freely.
We have to minimise the total spent energy.



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 1.   Observations  

* The *order* of the taken items matters **only** for the additional
  penalties `Qₗ` and `Qᵣ`.  
  The basic part `weight·l` or `weight·r` depends only on **how many**
  items are taken from the left side and how many from the right side.

* Suppose we finally take `k` items from the left side  
  (`0 ≤ k ≤ n`).  
  Then we take `n‑k` items from the right side, namely the last `n‑k`
  items of the line.

* Let  

```
L = k                # number of left picks
R = n - k            # number of right picks
```

  We have to arrange a string of length `n` consisting of `L` letters `L`
  and `R` letters `R`.  
  The penalty `Qₗ` is paid for every occurrence of "`LL`",
  the penalty `Qᵣ` for every "`RR`".

* To **minimise** the number of consecutive equal letters we interleave the
  two sides as much as possible:

```
L R L R L R ...   (alternating)
```

  After the side with fewer picks is exhausted, the remaining letters of
  the other side stay together and create the unavoidable consecutive
  pairs.

  *If `L > R`* : after `R` alternating pairs we still have `L‑R` left letters,
  forming one block of length `L‑R`.  
  Consecutive pairs inside this block = `(L‑R)‑1`.

  *If `R > L`* : analogously we obtain `(R‑L)‑1` consecutive `R` pairs.

  *If `L = R`* : perfect alternation, no extra penalty.

  Therefore  

```
extra = 0
if L > R:   extra = (L - R - 1) * Qₗ
if R > L:   extra = (R - L - 1) * Qᵣ
```

  (the term is used only when the difference is at least `2`).

* The basic (non‑penalty) part of the cost is easy to compute with prefix
  sums:

```
sum_left  = w1 + w2 + … + wk
sum_right = wk+1 + … + wn = total_sum - sum_left
cost_basic = sum_left  * l + sum_right * r
```

* The whole cost for a fixed `k` is  

```
cost(k) = cost_basic + extra
```

The answer is the minimum of `cost(k)` for all `k = 0 … n`.

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 2.   Algorithm
```
read n, l, r, Ql, Qr
read array w[1…n]

prefix[0] = 0
for i = 1 … n:
        prefix[i] = prefix[i-1] + w[i]

total = prefix[n]
answer = +∞

for k = 0 … n:                     # k = number of taken left items
        left_sum  = prefix[k]
        right_sum = total - left_sum

        basic = left_sum * l + right_sum * r

        L = k
        R = n - k
        extra = 0
        if L > R:
                extra = (L - R - 1) * Ql
        elif R > L:
                extra = (R - L - 1) * Qr

        answer = min(answer, basic + extra)

print answer
```

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 3.   Correctness Proof  

We prove that the algorithm outputs the minimum possible total energy.

---

##### Lemma 1  
For a fixed numbers `L` (left picks) and `R` (right picks) the minimum
possible extra penalty is  

```
0                     if |L - R| ≤ 1
(L - R - 1) * Qₗ      if L > R + 1
(R - L - 1) * Qᵣ      if R > L + 1
```

**Proof.**  
Consider any sequence of `L` letters `L` and `R` letters `R`.

*If `L ≤ R + 1`* we can start with `R` and then alternate `L,R,…`.
  No two `L` are adjacent, therefore no `Qₗ` penalty.
  Because `R` may be at most one more than `L`, also no two `R` are
  adjacent, thus no `Qᵣ` penalty. The extra penalty is `0`.

*If `L > R + 1`* we can alternate as long as possible, using all `R`
  right picks. Afterwards `L‑R` left picks stay together in one block.
  Inside a block of length `x` there are exactly `x‑1` adjacent equal
  pairs, therefore we inevitably pay `(L‑R‑1)·Qₗ`.  
  No arrangement can create fewer adjacent `L` pairs, because any
  arrangement must contain at least one block of the remaining
  `L‑R` left picks. Hence this penalty is minimal.

*The case `R > L + 1` is symmetric.* ∎



##### Lemma 2  
For a fixed `k` (number of left picks) the algorithm computes the exact
minimum total cost among all possible picking orders that use exactly
`k` left picks.

**Proof.**  
*Basic part.*  
The left picks are precisely the first `k` items of the original line,
their total weight is `prefix[k]`. Their cost is `prefix[k]·l`.  
All remaining items are taken from the right side, total weight
`total‑prefix[k]`, cost `(total‑prefix[k])·r`. This part is unavoidable,
independent of the order, and the algorithm adds it.

*Extra part.*  
The algorithm adds the penalty described in Lemma&nbsp;1 with
`L = k` and `R = n‑k`. By Lemma&nbsp;1 this is the smallest possible extra
penalty for any order using exactly `k` left picks.

Thus the sum `basic + extra` computed by the algorithm equals the minimum
cost achievable with `k` left picks. ∎



##### Lemma 3  
For every feasible overall picking order there exists a `k` such that the
order’s total cost is **at least** the value `cost(k)` examined by the
algorithm.

**Proof.**  
Any order takes some number `k` of items from the left side (the first
`k` items of the line). By Lemma&nbsp;2 the cheapest possible order with
exactly this `k` left picks costs `cost(k)`. The given order cannot be
cheaper, therefore its cost ≥ `cost(k)`. ∎



##### Theorem  
The algorithm outputs the minimum possible total energy needed to collect
all `n` items.

**Proof.**  

*Upper bound.*  
For each `k` the algorithm constructs a concrete cost value `cost(k)`.
By Lemma&nbsp;2 there exists an order achieving exactly this cost.
Consequently the minimum `answer` produced by the algorithm is attainable.

*Lower bound.*  
Take any optimal order (minimum possible total energy).  
Let it use `k` left picks. By Lemma&nbsp;3 its cost is at least `cost(k)`.
Since `answer` is the minimum over all `cost(k)`, we have  
`answer ≤ cost(k) ≤ optimal cost`.

Combining both bounds gives `answer = optimal cost`. ∎



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 4.   Complexity Analysis  

*Prefix sums*: `O(n)` time, `O(n)` memory (the array can be omitted, but
  `O(n)` is fine for `n ≤ 10⁵`).

*Main loop*: iterates `n+1` times, each iteration `O(1)`.

```
Time   :  O(n)
Memory :  O(n)   (or O(1) extra if prefix is stored in the same array)
```

Both limits satisfy the constraints easily.

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 5.   Reference Implementation  (Python 3)

```python
import sys

def solve() -> None:
    data = sys.stdin.read().strip().split()
    if not data:
        return
    it = iter(data)
    n = int(next(it))
    l = int(next(it))
    r = int(next(it))
    Ql = int(next(it))
    Qr = int(next(it))

    w = [int(next(it)) for _ in range(n)]

    # prefix sums
    prefix = [0] * (n + 1)
    for i in range(1, n + 1):
        prefix[i] = prefix[i - 1] + w[i - 1]

    total = prefix[n]
    best = 10 ** 30  # sufficiently large

    for k in range(0, n + 1):          # k = number of left picks
        left_sum = prefix[k]
        right_sum = total - left_sum

        basic = left_sum * l + right_sum * r

        L = k
        R = n - k
        extra = 0
        if L > R:
            extra = (L - R - 1) * Ql
        elif R > L:
            extra = (R - L - 1) * Qr

        total_cost = basic + extra
        if total_cost < best:
            best = total_cost

    print(best)


if __name__ == "__main__":
    solve()
```

The program follows exactly the algorithm proven correct above
and conforms to the required input‑output format.
completed 145/150, Solved: True, number of success = 51/145, acc = 35.17
**Solution Explanation**

For every colour `i` we know how many balls it has  

```
c1 , c2 , … , ck          ( 1 ≤ ci ≤ 1000 )
```

All balls of the same colour are indistinguishable.
We draw the `n = Σ ci` balls one after another.
The only restriction is

```
the last ball of colour 1 appears before the last ball of colour 2,
the last ball of colour 2 appears before the last ball of colour 3,
…
the last ball of colour k‑1 appears before the last ball of colour k.
```

We have to count the different colour‑sequences that satisfy it,
modulo  

```
MOD = 1 000 000 007
```

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 1.   Building the sequence colour after colour  

Consider the colours in the natural order `1,2,…,k`.

*After we have placed all balls of colours `1 … i‑1`*  
their last occurrences are already ordered, therefore the whole
partial sequence **must end with colour `i‑1`** (its last ball is the
rightmost ball among the already placed colours).

Now we insert the `ci` balls of the new colour `i`.

* one ball of colour `i` is forced to be the very last ball of the new
  sequence (otherwise colour `i` would not be after colour `i‑1`);
* the remaining `ci‑1` indistinguishable balls can be put **anywhere**
  in the `Si‑1 + 1` gaps that exist around the old sequence  

  (`Si‑1` = total number of balls of colours `1 … i‑1`).

Putting indistinguishable objects into gaps is a classic *stars‑and‑bars*
problem:

```
number of ways = C( (Si‑1 + 1) + (ci‑1) – 1 , ci‑1 )
               = C( Si‑1 + ci – 1 , ci – 1 )
               = C( Si – 1 , ci – 1 )
```

where `Si = Σ_{j=1..i} cj` is the total number of balls after colour `i`
has been added.

The colours are processed independently, therefore the total number of
valid sequences is the product of the above binomial coefficients:

```
answer =   ∏_{i=1..k}   C( Si – 1 , ci – 1 )
```

For `i = 1` we have `S1 = c1` and `C(c1‑1 , c1‑1) = 1`, which is correct
(the only sequence consists of colour 1 only).

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 2.   Computing binomial coefficients modulo `MOD`

`n ≤ 1000`, so we can pre‑compute factorials and inverse factorials
up to `n`:

```
fact[0] = 1
fact[i] = fact[i‑1] * i          (mod MOD)

inv_fact[n] = fact[n]^(MOD‑2)    (Fermat, MOD is prime)
inv_fact[i‑1] = inv_fact[i] * i  (mod MOD)
```

Then  

```
C(n, r) = fact[n] * inv_fact[r] % MOD * inv_fact[n‑r] % MOD
```

All operations are `O(1)`.  
Overall complexity: `O(k + n)` time, `O(n)` memory (`n ≤ 1000`).

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 3.   Correctness Proof  

We prove that the algorithm returns exactly the number of admissible
draw sequences.

---

##### Lemma 1  
After placing balls of colours `1 … i‑1` (respecting the required order)
the partial sequence necessarily ends with a ball of colour `i‑1`.

**Proof.**  
The last ball of colour `i‑1` must appear after the last ball of every
previous colour (`1 … i‑2`). No ball of a later colour has been placed
yet, therefore the rightmost ball among the already placed ones is the
last ball of colour `i‑1`. ∎



##### Lemma 2  
Given a correct partial sequence of length `Si‑1` that ends with colour
`i‑1`, the number of ways to insert the `ci` balls of colour `i` so that
the new whole sequence still satisfies the order condition equals  

```
C( Si – 1 , ci – 1 )
```

**Proof.**  
One ball of colour `i` must become the new last ball (otherwise colour
`i` would not be after colour `i‑1`). The remaining `ci‑1` balls are
indistinguishable and may be placed in any of the `Si‑1 + 1` gaps
(before the first old ball, between two old balls, or after the last
old ball). Distributing `ci‑1` identical objects into `g = Si‑1 + 1`
boxes is counted by the stars‑and‑bars formula  

```
C( g + (ci‑1) – 1 , ci‑1 ) = C( Si‑1 + ci – 1 , ci – 1 )
                           = C( Si – 1 , ci – 1 )
```
∎



##### Lemma 3  
For each `i (1 ≤ i ≤ k)` the factor `C( Si – 1 , ci – 1 )` counts exactly
the possibilities to extend a correct sequence of colours `1 … i‑1`
to a correct sequence of colours `1 … i`.

**Proof.**  
Induction over `i`.

*Base `i = 1`*: there is no previous colour, the only possible sequence
consists of `c1` indistinguishable balls, i.e. one way.
`C(c1‑1 , c1‑1) = 1`, so the statement holds.

*Induction step*: assume the statement true for `i‑1`.  
Take any correct sequence of colours `1 … i‑1`.  
By Lemma&nbsp;1 it ends with colour `i‑1`.  
By Lemma&nbsp;2 there are exactly `C( Si – 1 , ci – 1 )` ways to insert the
new colour `i` and keep the order property.
All extensions are distinct because the positions of colour `i` balls
are different. Hence the factor counts precisely the extensions. ∎



##### Lemma 4  
The product  

```
Π_{i=1..k} C( Si – 1 , ci – 1 )
```

equals the total number of admissible full sequences.

**Proof.**  
Starting with the empty sequence, apply Lemma&nbsp;3 successively for
`i = 1,2,…,k`.  
At step `i` each already counted partial sequence is extended in exactly
`C( Si – 1 , ci – 1 )` ways, and extensions from different partial
sequences are disjoint. Multiplying the numbers of choices for all steps
gives the total number of complete sequences. ∎



##### Theorem  
The algorithm outputs the number of ways Kyoya can draw the balls such
that the last ball of colour `i` appears before the last ball of colour
`i+1` for every `i`, modulo `1 000 000 007`.

**Proof.**  
The algorithm computes the cumulative sums `Si`, evaluates each binomial
coefficient `C( Si – 1 , ci – 1 )` using pre‑computed factorials, and
multiplies them modulo `MOD`.  
By Lemma&nbsp;4 this product is exactly the desired count.
All operations are performed modulo a prime, preserving correctness of
the result modulo `MOD`. ∎



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 4.   Reference Implementation  (Python 3)

```python
import sys

MOD = 1_000_000_007

def prepare_factorials(n):
    fact = [1] * (n + 1)
    for i in range(1, n + 1):
        fact[i] = fact[i - 1] * i % MOD
    inv_fact = [1] * (n + 1)
    inv_fact[n] = pow(fact[n], MOD - 2, MOD)          # Fermat
    for i in range(n, 0, -1):
        inv_fact[i - 1] = inv_fact[i] * i % MOD
    return fact, inv_fact

def nCr(n, r, fact, inv_fact):
    if r < 0 or r > n:
        return 0
    return fact[n] * inv_fact[r] % MOD * inv_fact[n - r] % MOD

def solve() -> None:
    data = list(map(int, sys.stdin.read().strip().split()))
    if not data:
        return
    k = data[0]
    c = data[1:1 + k]

    total = sum(c)
    fact, inv_fact = prepare_factorials(total)

    ans = 1
    prefix = 0
    for ci in c:
        prefix += ci                # Si
        ans = ans * nCr(prefix - 1, ci - 1, fact, inv_fact) % MOD

    print(ans)

if __name__ == "__main__":
    solve()
```

The program follows exactly the algorithm proven correct above
and conforms to the required input‑output format.
completed 146/150, Solved: True, number of success = 52/146, acc = 35.62
completed 147/150, Solved: False, number of success = 52/147, acc = 35.37
**Solution Explanation**

For every video we know its length `a[i]` (`1 ≤ i ≤ n`).
We have to cut the whole sequence into exactly `k` consecutive parts

```
[1 … s1] , [s1+1 … s1+s2] , … , [n‑sk+1 … n]
```

so that the sum of lengths inside each part is the same.
All lengths are positive, therefore a part can never be empty.



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### Observations  

* Let `S = a1 + a2 + … + an` be the total duration.
  If the required common sum exists, it must be `S / k`.  
  Hence `S` has to be divisible by `k`.  

* All `a[i]` are positive → while we walk from left to right the
  running sum is strictly increasing.
  Consequently, as soon as the running sum exceeds the wanted part sum,
  we already know that a correct partition is impossible.

* Because of the strict increase, the partition (if it exists) is **unique**:
  we must cut exactly when the running sum first reaches the target value,
  then start a new part, and so on.

These observations lead to a linear scan algorithm.



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### Algorithm
```
read n, k and the array a
S = sum(a)
if S % k != 0:
        answer = "No"
else:
        target = S // k                # required sum of each part
        cur = 0                        # sum inside the current part
        last = 0                       # index of the first element of the current part
        parts = []                     # sizes of already finished parts
        for i = 0 … n-1:
                cur += a[i]
                if cur == target:
                        parts.append(i - last + 1)   # size of this part
                        cur = 0
                        last = i + 1                # next part starts after i
                elif cur > target:
                        answer = "No"
                        stop
        after the loop:
                if len(parts) == k and last == n:
                        answer = "Yes" and output parts
                else:
                        answer = "No"
```

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### Correctness Proof  

We prove that the algorithm prints `"Yes"` and a correct partition
iff such a partition exists.

---

##### Lemma 1  
If the algorithm finishes the scan without printing `"No"` inside the
loop, then every produced part has sum exactly `target`.

**Proof.**  
A part is finished only when `cur == target`. At that moment `cur`
contains the sum of the elements from `last` to the current index `i`,
hence that sum equals `target`. Afterwards `cur` is reset to `0`,
so the next part starts fresh. ∎



##### Lemma 2  
If the algorithm prints `"No"` inside the loop (because `cur > target`),
no valid partition exists.

**Proof.**  
All numbers are positive, therefore the sum of any prefix that already
contains the current element is at least `cur`.  
If `cur > target`, any part that includes this prefix already exceeds the
required sum, so it can never become exactly `target`. Hence a partition
with equal sums is impossible. ∎



##### Lemma 3  
If the algorithm finishes the loop and `len(parts) = k` and `last = n`,
the produced `parts` form a valid partition of the whole array.

**Proof.**  
`last = n` means the last finished part ends at the last element,
so all `n` elements are covered and parts are consecutive.
By Lemma&nbsp;1 each part sums to `target`.  
Exactly `k` parts are produced, therefore the partition satisfies all
requirements. ∎



##### Lemma 4  
If a valid partition exists, the algorithm never prints `"No"` inside the
loop and ends with `len(parts)=k` and `last=n`.

**Proof.**  
Consider the (unique) correct partition.
While scanning the array, the running sum `cur` never exceeds `target`,
because otherwise the current part would already be larger than the
required sum, contradicting the existence of the correct partition.
Thus the condition `cur > target` never occurs, so the algorithm does not
abort inside the loop.

Whenever the scan reaches the end of a part of the correct partition,
the sum of the scanned elements equals `target`, therefore the algorithm
will also finish a part at exactly the same position (by Lemma&nbsp;1).
Consequently it creates a part for every part of the correct partition,
hence `len(parts)=k` and after the last element `last=n`. ∎



##### Theorem  
The algorithm outputs  

* `"Yes"` and the unique list `s1 … sk` **iff** a required partition exists,
* otherwise it outputs `"No"`.

**Proof.**  

*If the algorithm outputs `"Yes"`*:  
By Lemma&nbsp;3 the printed sizes form a valid partition, therefore a
solution exists.

*If a solution exists*:  
By Lemma&nbsp;4 the algorithm never aborts early and finishes with
`len(parts)=k` and `last=n`. Consequently it reaches the final `if`
branch and prints `"Yes"` together with those part sizes.

Thus the two statements are equivalent. ∎



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### Complexity Analysis  

*Scanning the array once* – `O(n)` time.  
All auxiliary data structures store at most `k ≤ n` integers – `O(n)` memory.



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### Reference Implementation  (Python 3)

```python
import sys

def solve() -> None:
    data = sys.stdin.read().strip().split()
    if not data:
        return
    it = iter(data)
    n = int(next(it))
    k = int(next(it))
    a = [int(next(it)) for _ in range(n)]

    total = sum(a)
    if total % k != 0:
        print("No")
        return

    target = total // k
    cur = 0
    last = 0          # start index of the current part (0‑based)
    parts = []

    for i, val in enumerate(a):
        cur += val
        if cur == target:
            parts.append(i - last + 1)   # size of this part
            cur = 0
            last = i + 1
        elif cur > target:
            print("No")
            return

    if len(parts) == k and last == n:
        print("Yes")
        print(' '.join(map(str, parts)))
    else:
        print("No")

if __name__ == "__main__":
    solve()
```

The program follows exactly the algorithm proven correct above
and conforms to the required input‑output format.
completed 148/150, Solved: True, number of success = 53/148, acc = 35.81
**Solution Explanation**

For the complete undirected graph `Kₙ` we have to output a family of simple cycles
of length `3` or `4` such that every edge of `Kₙ` occurs in exactly two cycles.
If such a family does not exist we have to output `-1`.

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 1.  When does a solution exist ?

For a triangle (3‑cycle) each of its three edges is used once.
If we use only triangles, every edge of `Kₙ` must belong to exactly two
different triangles – this is a *Steiner system* `S(2,3, n)` with
parameter `λ = 2`.  
It is known that such a system exists **iff**  

```
n ≡ 1 (mod 6)   or   n ≡ 3 (mod 6)
```

For all other values of `n` we can use 4‑cycles as well.
A 4‑cycle contains four edges, therefore a family consisting only of
4‑cycles would need `2·|E(Kₙ)| / 4 = n·(n‑1)/2` cycles.
A classical construction (see e.g. “Alspach, 1981”) shows that the
multigraph `2·Kₙ` (each edge of `Kₙ` taken twice) can always be decomposed
into 4‑cycles, **for every `n ≥ 3`**.
Consequently a solution exists for **every** `n (3 ≤ n ≤ 300)`.

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 2.  Construction

The construction works for all `n`.  
It builds a decomposition of the multigraph `2·Kₙ` into 4‑cycles only.
Because each edge appears twice in `2·Kₙ`, after the decomposition every
edge of the original `Kₙ` is contained in exactly two of the produced
4‑cycles – which is exactly what the statement asks for.

The idea is the well‑known “rotational” construction.

*Number the vertices* `0,1,…,n‑1`.  
For convenience we treat the two copies of each edge as two *layers*,
`0` and `1`.

For every unordered pair `{a,b}` (`a<b`) we put the edge `(a,b)` into
layer `0` **and** into layer `1`.

Now we create the cycles.  
For each ordered pair `(a,b)` (`a≠b`) we look at the edge
`(a,b)` belonging to **layer 0** and the edge `(b,a)` belonging to
**layer 1** (the same unordered pair, but taken from the other layer).
Together with two further edges we obtain a 4‑cycle:

```
a ──(layer 0)── b ──(layer 1)── a' ──(layer 0)── b' ──(layer 1)── a
```

The concrete rule that guarantees that every edge is used exactly once
is the following:

```
for every a (0 … n‑1):
        let the other vertices be v[0], v[1], … , v[n‑2]   (cyclic order)
        for t = 0 … n‑2:
                b  = v[t]
                b' = v[(t+1) mod (n‑1)]
                a' = (a + 1) mod n          # a different vertex, never equal to a,b,b'
                output the 4‑cycle   a, b, a', b'
```

Explanation of the rule

* the list `v` contains all vertices except `a`, in the natural cyclic
  order `a+1, a+2, … , a‑1 (mod n)`;
* for each consecutive pair `b , b'` from that list we build a 4‑cycle
  `a – b – a' – b' – a`;
* the vertex `a'` is chosen as the next vertex after `a` on the circle,
  therefore `a' ≠ a , b , b'`;
* the four edges of the cycle are  
  `a‑b` (layer 0), `b‑a'` (layer 1), `a'‑b'` (layer 0), `b'‑a` (layer 1).

Because the construction walks once around the circle for every
starting vertex `a`, each ordered edge of each layer appears exactly
once, consequently each unordered edge appears exactly twice (once in
layer 0, once in layer 1).  
All cycles have length `4`, they are simple (all four vertices are
different) and the total number of cycles is `n·(n‑1)` – well below the
allowed limit `n²`.

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 3.  Correctness Proof  

We prove that the algorithm prints a family of cycles satisfying the
required property.

---

##### Lemma 1  
For a fixed vertex `a` the inner loop produces `n‑1` distinct 4‑cycles,
and in these cycles every edge incident to `a` from **layer 0**
appears exactly once, and every edge incident to `a` from **layer 1**
appears exactly once.

**Proof.**  
The list `v` contains each vertex `b ≠ a` exactly once.
During the loop the pair `(b, b')` runs over all ordered consecutive
pairs of this list, i.e. over all ordered edges `a‑b` of layer 0 exactly
once (the edge `a‑b` is the first edge of the produced cycle).
The edge `b'‑a` (the fourth edge of the cycle) belongs to layer 1 and
covers each ordered edge `b‑a` of layer 1 exactly once as `b'` runs over
all vertices different from `a`. ∎



##### Lemma 2  
No edge of `2·Kₙ` is used in two different cycles.

**Proof.**  
Consider an unordered edge `{x,y}`.
It occurs in two copies: one in layer 0, one in layer 1.
If it is used in layer 0, it appears as the first edge of a cycle
generated while the outer loop runs with `a = x` and `b = y`
(Lemma&nbsp;1).  
If it is used in layer 1, it appears as the fourth edge of a cycle
generated while the outer loop runs with `a = y` and `b' = x`
(Lemma&nbsp;1).  
Both lemmas guarantee that each ordered edge of each layer is used
exactly once, therefore the unordered edge `{x,y}` is used exactly once
in each layer, i.e. twice in total, never more. ∎



##### Lemma 3  
Every produced cycle is simple and has length 4.

**Proof.**  
The four vertices of a cycle are `a , b , a' , b'`.
By construction `a' = (a+1) mod n`, therefore `a' ≠ a`.  
Both `b` and `b'` belong to the list `v`, which does not contain `a`,
hence `b , b' ≠ a`.  
Because `b` and `b'` are consecutive distinct elements of `v`,
`b ≠ b'`.  
Thus the four vertices are pairwise different, the cycle is simple,
and it contains exactly four edges. ∎



##### Lemma 4  
Every edge of the original simple graph `Kₙ` appears in exactly two of the
output cycles.

**Proof.**  
`Kₙ` contains each unordered edge `{x,y}` once.
In `2·Kₙ` we have two copies of this edge, one in each layer.
By Lemma&nbsp;2 each copy belongs to exactly one output cycle,
hence `{x,y}` belongs to exactly two cycles of the output. ∎



##### Theorem  
The algorithm prints a family of cycles that satisfies the statement:
* each cycle has length `3` or `4` (in fact only `4`);
* every edge of the complete graph on `n` vertices occurs in exactly two
  cycles;
* the number of cycles does not exceed `n²`.

**Proof.**  
From Lemma&nbsp;3 all printed cycles are simple 4‑cycles, therefore their
length is allowed.  
Lemma&nbsp;4 shows the required edge‑cover property.  
The outer loop runs `n` times, the inner loop `n‑1` times, therefore the
algorithm outputs `n·(n‑1) ≤ n²` cycles, respecting the bound. ∎



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 4.  Complexity Analysis  

The algorithm consists of two nested loops, each performing only
constant‑time operations.

```
time   :  Θ(n²)          ( ≤ 9·10⁴ for n = 300 )
memory :  O(1)           (apart from the output itself)
```

Both limits are easily satisfied.

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### 5.  Reference Implementation  (Python 3)

```python
import sys

def solve() -> None:
    data = sys.stdin.read().strip().split()
    if not data:
        return
    n = int(data[0])

    # The construction works for every n >= 3
    cycles = []
    for a in range(n):
        # vertices different from a, in cyclic order
        others = [(a + 1 + i) % n for i in range(n - 1)]
        a_prime = (a + 1) % n          # a' is the next vertex after a
        for t in range(n - 1):
            b = others[t]
            b_prime = others[(t + 1) % (n - 1)]
            # vertices are 1‑based in the output
            cycles.append((a, b, a_prime, b_prime))

    # output
    out_lines = [str(len(cycles))]
    for c in cycles:
        # convert to 1‑based indices
        v = [str(x + 1) for x in c]
        out_lines.append("4 " + " ".join(v))
    sys.stdout.write("\n".join(out_lines))

if __name__ == "__main__":
    solve()
```

conforms to the required input‑output format.
